# 混元圣记

*The Records of the Sagely Origins of the Primordial Dao*
**Title:** *The Records of the Sagely Origins of the Primordial Dao* (*Hunyuan Shengji*). Nine fascicles. Compiled by Xie Cailan of the Song dynasty. Base text from *Zhengtong Daoist Canon*, "Compendia and Registers" section of the *Dongshen* division. Collated with the *Chongkan Daozang Jiyao* edition.

**Preface to *The Records of the Sagely Origins of the Primordial Dao***

Huaiying once pursued the path of a scholar-official, and thus knew well the reverence due to Master Kong. Later, he doffed his Confucian cap and departed to become a Daoist, applying the same reverence he had shown to Master Kong to the Venerable Master Lao. Thereupon, he composed a work in several fascicles, gathering without omission all accounts of Laozi’s names, transformations, and subtle sayings scattered among the Hundred Schools since the dawn of time, arranging them in order as *The Sagely Records*. Alas! How single-minded and erudite he was—truly a service to the Daoist tradition.

Confucians, enamored of office, cannot devote themselves wholeheartedly to scholarship, and thus often lack the leisure to delve into the chronicles of past ages. Even when they do find the time, they dare not stray from meticulous selection, constrained by the Six Classics and unable to roam freely. Thus, while the Way of our Master Kong endures as boundlessly as Heaven and Earth, and while many have revered him, none have produced a work like Huaiying’s.

Had Huaiying fortunately completed his former calling and not left to become a Daoist, this work would have been written for the Kong lineage—how great would its merit have been! Yet, in ancient times, the Grand Historian Sima Qian once composed the "Hereditary House of Confucius," for he too harbored such an ambition. Critics, however, countered: if the Master’s Way endures as boundlessly as Heaven and Earth, why compare it to the longevity of the feudal lords of the Warring States or the Han? Thus, the *Hereditary House* seems out of place. Moreover, the descendants of the Kong lineage, recording what they had heard, produced works like the *Kong Family Sayings* and *Kong Congzi*, yet Confucians scarcely extolled them. When these texts veered toward exaggerating the Sage’s grandeur with trivialities like "grave goods of lamb and mulberry arrows," some dismissed them as fantastical and unworthy of consideration.

Had Huaiying not become a Daoist and instead composed a work revering the Kong lineage, he would have been bound to adjudicate the Hundred Schools by the *Six Classics*, unable to roam so freely in his erudition. For this, I sigh over Huaiying’s misfortune—his inability to anchor himself in the lineage of Confucius. Yet, in another light, he was fortunate to find refuge in the lineage of Laozi.

Thus, I inscribe this preface.

Huaiying’s surname was Xie, his given name Shouxie, a native of Yongjia. He was my fellow student. Written on a day in the eighth month of the fourth year of the Shaoxi reign (1193) by Chen Fuliang.

**Memorial Presented to the Throne**

The humble servant Shouxie respectfully states: The manifest traces of the Great Clarity rely entirely upon the concordant records of trustworthy histories. The surviving scriptures of vast kalpas await the radiant reign to reach their fullest completeness. Such an era is rare to attain, for the Dao does not operate in vain.

With utmost reverence and trepidation, I, Shouxie, bow my head and prostrate myself. I humbly consider that above the nine heavens lies the fabled Imperial Homeland, while beyond the six directions the sacred canons endure. Without the *Three Tombs*, the proximate legacy of Fuxi and Shennong would have faded; with the emergence of the *Six Classics*, the enterprises of Zhou and Kong flourished. During the flourishing eras of Han and Tang, the supreme teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi were universally esteemed. Sages and worthies arose in leisure, the primordial transformation spread abundantly, the ways of the world prospered in tandem, and this culture did not decline.

The Supreme Sage of the Primordial Origin treasured the great source of the wondrous Dao, giving birth to heaven and earth without revealing its beginnings, spiritually animating ghosts and emperors without exhausting its boundless domain. From the kalpas of *Yan Kang* and *Chi Ming*, he served as the teacher of successive dynasties. By the time of the Shang and the house of Ji, he manifested in divided forms to respond to transformation. Teaching without distinction, he enabled the Western barbarians to attain the Dao. The profound Dao being unfathomable, he appeared as a dragon in the east of Lu.

Yet those who comprehend it are few, so it is no wonder that its discourse remains unexplored. Ancient traditions glimpsed but a part like peering at a leopard through a tube, while later scholars increasingly strayed like sheep lost on divergent paths. The complete corpus of the hidden chamber remains unknown, and this indeed constitutes a deficiency in the teachings’ canon.

Though my talent falls short of entering the inner chamber and my learning shames the gate of Master Zhuan, I dare not gallop in the realm of brush and ink. My efforts extend no further than grazing in the garden of arts and letters, refining my essence to produce but half a crane’s worth, wielding the brush until my hand resembles a tortoise’s. I have gathered the books of the Three Teachings and compiled them into the *Sacred Records*. I have examined and collated the sayings of a hundred schools—

*Records of the Primordial Sage, Volume One*
Compiled by Xie Shoubin, Daoist Master of Observing the Return, of the Song Dynasty

The Most High Lord Lao is the sovereign of the Great Dao, the primordial origin of all teachings. He emerged before the Great Void, arose from the source of the Ultimate Non-Being, traversed eons beyond reckoning, and endures without end, extends without limit. Adapting his teachings to the times, he has served as master across kalpas, concealing and revealing, being and non-being—beyond all measure.

Yet in his manifestations to establish the teachings for the world, his responses and apparitions shine as brightly as the sun and moon; how could they remain unrecorded? However, existing chronicles and biographies are largely fragmentary and incomplete. Works such as Sima Qian’s *Records of the Grand Historian*, Ban Gu’s *Ancient and Modern Register of Persons*, Liu Xiang’s *Arrayed Transcendents*, Ji Kang and Huangfu Mi’s *Accounts of Eminent Scholars*, along with Ge Zhichuan’s *Biographies of Divine Transcendents*, *Collected Transcendents*, and *Compendium of Transcendents*—all are piecemeal, capturing but a fraction of the whole. Yin Wencao’s *Sacred Records* in eight hundred and twenty chapters and Jia Shanxiang’s *Like a Dragon*, divided into a hundred sections, though detailed in their accounts, sprawl with tangential digressions, lose coherence in sequence, and suffer from inconsistent selection, their contradictions multiplying confusion for the reader.

Each time I open these volumes, I cannot but sigh—truly a deficiency in the canon of the teachings. Now, undeterred by my own inadequacy, I have compiled and examined the scriptures and records of the Three Teachings, tracing their origins and evolution, and have arranged the accounts of reverence across the ages into the *Sacred Records*, prefaced by an annalistic chronicle.

As for the earliest era of the Five Primes and the beginnings of the Nine August Ones, before the emergence of the True Writ and when records remain obscure, I have here begun by compiling the historical accounts of Lord Lao’s manifestations in the world, setting forth his annalistic chronicle as follows:

**Heavenly Sovereign**
Huangfu Mi’s *Three and Five Calendars* states: At the inception of heaven and earth, there was the Heavenly Sovereign, twelve-headed, ruling by the virtue of Wood, with years reckoned from *Sheti*. Twelve brothers reigned, each for eighteen thousand years.
Xu Zheng’s *Records of the Three and Five Calendars* states: At the dawn of *Yuanxi*, there appeared a divine personage called the Heavenly Sovereign. At that time, Lord Lao descended into the world, known as the Master of Penetrating Mystery, also called the Great Dharma Master of the Mysterium.
The *Record of the Green Goat Palace*, composed by Yue Pengju, Hanlin Academician and Grand Preceptor of the Tang, states: The Most High Mysterious Primordial Lord Lao responded to the times and descended in manifestation, serving as teacher to the Heavenly Sovereign. At the first separation of the pure above and turbid below, the distance between them was not yet great; at the initial parting of the six directions and eight boundaries, the space between them was not yet far.

The forms of the square and round, above and below, were established; the vast and unrefined state of primordial chaos was determined. Rivers had newly melted yet waters remained murky; earth had just congealed yet stones were not yet firm. The Four Seas were matched to the Four Spirits, and the Five Phases were entrusted to the Five Emperors.

The Earthly Sovereign—Lord Lao descended to serve as his teacher, known as the Venerable Ancient Master. The *Tang Annals* state: At that time, the celestial image was still low, and the earthly form remained thin. The pole was established and positions fixed; transformation was spread and merit was built. Our Most High Lord Lao condescended to descend, serving as teacher to the Earthly Sovereign. He apportioned softness and firmness, regulated cold and heat. The earth grew broader and thicker; the heavens became higher and brighter. His sagely power was boundless, his divine work unfathomable. Ten thousand trees split their husks; ten thousand grasses hooked forth their sprouts. Feathered flocks and furred herds were distinguished in the positions of *dui* and *li*; shelled creatures and scaled kinds were divided among the offices of *kan* and *zhen*.

The Human Sovereign—*The Apocryphal Spring and Autumn* records him as the Nine-Headed Lord—Lord Lao descended to serve as his teacher, known as Master Pangu. The *Records of the Three and Five Calendars* state: Heaven and earth were chaotic like a chicken’s egg; Pangu was born within it. When heaven and earth split open, Pangu was within them, and afterward came the Three Sovereigns. This was the beginning of the Three Powers.

The *Tang Annals* state: After the years of the Azure Sea had passed, and the years of departing the Azure Sea had come, the Two Sages had already governed, and the four borders radiated light. Our Most High Lord Lao, in his vast compassion to relieve the multitudes, served as teacher to the Human Sovereign. The three hundred and sixty rivers first divided their blood vessels; the fifteen thousand strategies suddenly matched odd and even. The spirits of the three *ren* and three *yi* departed the womb from water and wood; the generals of the six *bing* and six *xin* were conceived from fire and wind. After one hundred fifty-six generations and forty-five thousand years, he was first called the Master of Penetrating Mystery, then titled the Golden Portal Emperor Lord, and thirdly named Master Pangu.

The Five Dragons recorded five clans; the Jade Emperor’s *Comprehensive Calendar* recorded the Gou Long era. The Lian Tong era recorded six clans, or was called the Lian Yao era. *The Apocryphal Spring and Autumn*, the *River Chart*, and the *Five Sovereigns’ Calendar* all record the Lian Tong era. The He Xiong era recorded three clans—*The Apocryphal Spring and Autumn* calls it the Han Xiong era. The *Initial Placement of Heaven and Earth Classic* calls it the He Xiong era. The Five Clans era—the *River Chart* states: The Tong era recorded five clans, which is called the Five Clans era. *The Apocryphal Spring and Autumn* includes the She Ti era, and the *Five Clans Record* further lists the Qiang Yi era. The Shu Ming era recorded four clans—the *Placement of Heaven and Earth Classic* calls it the Hou era. The *Jade Emperor’s Calendar* calls it the Xuan Ming era. The Xiu Fei era recorded nine clans—the *River Chart* calls it the Ju Fang clan. The Yin Ti era—the *River Chart* calls it the Ti Ting era. The Shan Tong era recorded seventy-two clans—the *River Chart* calls it the Tong Xing era. The Liu Qi era—*The Hidden Meanings of the Records of the Grand Historian: Annals of the Three Sovereigns* states: The Liu Qi era occurred during the time of the Yellow Emperor, when the nine eras were established.

The *River Chart’s Record of Successive Clans* states: In the most ancient times, the beginning of the people’s birth, male and female mingled their essences, and women gave birth to surnames. The first three heads were called the He Xiong era, known as the Three Generations. After the He Xiong clan perished, descendants passed down records of their lifespans, which was called the Shu Ming era. The Tong era recorded four clans, producing two generations of children. With many men and women, they lived together in connection, which was called the Lian Tong era. They produced one generation of children, and the Tong era recorded five clans, which was called the Five Clans era. The world’s people lived in groups, forming bonds by kind. With many men and women, they divided into nine heads, each with their own dwelling, hence called the Ju Fang clan. They produced thirty-two generations of children. The strong bullied the weak, seeking to devour and harm. Among them was a divine person who governed with authority, hence called the Ti Ting clan. They produced thirty-five generations of children. The Tong era recorded seventy-two clans, hence called the Tong Xing clan. After the Tong Xing clan came the You Chao clan, to whom the nine heads of the world all submitted in reverence. The You Chao clan produced the Sui Ren clan; the Sui Ren clan produced Fu Xi.

The *River Chart* and *Records of the Three and Five Calendars* state: After the Human Sovereign, there were the Five Dragons clan, Sui Ren clan, Da Ting clan, Bai Huang clan, Central clan, Juan Xu clan, Li Lu clan, Li Lian clan, He Xu clan, Zun Lu clan, Hun Dun clan, Hao Ying clan, You Chao clan, Zhu Xiang clan, Ge Tian clan, Yin Kang clan, and Yuan Huai clan, though their surnames and generational orders are unknown.

The *Three Tombs* records the Heavenly Sovereign and Fu Xi clan. The *Divination Texts* state: The Sovereign said: I command you, Xiang, to occupy my Flying Dragon position, to produce my diagrams and texts, and to relay my decrees to the four directions above and below, without error or neglect. Later, the Hao Ying clan presented a calendar to the Lord, saying: The calendar begins with *jia-yin*. The Sovereign said: The *jia* day and *yin* hour—wood rules at *mao*. In the second month, he assembled the people at the Teaching Transmission Terrace to announce the beginning of *jia-yin*. Later, the Nü Wa and Yuan Huai clans assisted the Lord’s right; the Grand Minister Gong Gong and the Minister Huang Gen served above and below. The Flying Dragon Zhu Xiang clan and the Hidden Dragon Hao Ying clan stood at the Lord’s left and right. The Li Lu clan resided in the north; the He Xu clan in the south; the Kun Lian clan in the west; the Ge Tian clan in the east; the Yin Kang clan below. The governors of the nine provinces each led their people, dwelling in the outer regions.

The Sovereign said: Li Lu’s son, occupy my Flying Dragon position. Oversee the nurturing of grasses and trees, open the paths of springs and sources, and do not lose the proper seasons. He said: Da Ting’s son, oversee my dwellings. Observe the people who have no homes, instruct them to lend strength, and together build their residences, so none suffer from cold. He said: Yin Kang’s son, oversee my waters and earth, so the people’s dwellings are not swept away, and they diligently follow the land routes. He said: Hun Dun’s son, occupy my Descending Dragon position, and attend solely to the people. He said: Kun Lian’s son, oversee my blades and axes, and do not let wild beasts, rhinoceroses, tigers, and the like harm the people.

The Sovereign said: If the lords of the four directions all heed my words, the world will know no sorrow; if they cherish the people, their positions will not be imperiled. He further said: Sons, do not cling to ease—find ease only in the people’s ease. When the people are at ease, you are at ease; when the people are in peril, you are in peril. Let this be ever in your thoughts.

According to the *Three Tombs* texts, Fuxi was the Heavenly Sovereign of Later Ages, with Nüwa and Yuanhuai as his successors, and Zhuxiang and Haoying as his descendants. Gonggong and Huanggen served as his ministers, while Lilu, Hexu, Kunlian, Getian, Dating, Yinkang, and Hundun were all his divine attendants. Within the realm, his six ministers divided their duties to govern the state; beyond it, they served as lords of the four directions to shepherd the people—these were the so-called Nine Heads. Yet all were contemporaries, with no distinction of eras. Why, then, do historical records arrange them in sequence? Perhaps after Fuxi’s passing, his descendants and the Nine Heads’ ministers all enjoyed long lives, taking turns as rulers. Or perhaps the descendants of his ministers were enfeoffed as lords, adopting their ancestors’ names as their own.

The *Han Shi Waizhuan* states that since ancient times, over ten thousand clans have performed the Feng and Shan sacrifices on Mount Tai and Liangfu, yet when Confucius observed them, he could not recognize them all. Guanzi also records: "Seventy-two clans performed the Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai in antiquity, of whom I, Yiwu, recognize only twelve." The first among them was the Lord of WuHuai, suggesting that the sequence of pre-Fuxi dynasties is largely unknowable.

The *Spring and Autumn Annals of Hua* records that from the opening of heaven and earth to the capture of the lin, a span of 3,276,000 years elapsed, divided into ten epochs:
1. The Nine Heads Epoch,
2. The Five Dragons Epoch,
3. The Sheti Epoch,
4. The HeXiong Epoch,
5. The LianTong Epoch,
6. The ShuMing Epoch,
7. The XiuFei Epoch,
8. The YinTi Epoch,
9. The ShanTong Epoch,
10. The LiuQi Epoch.

The LiuQi Epoch corresponds to the time of the Yellow Emperor, who established the nine epochs. Counting backward from the thirty-ninth year of Duke Jing of Zhou—the twelfth year of Duke Ai of Lu, the gengshen year when the lin was captured—to the first year of the Yellow Emperor (dinghai), only 3,930 years have passed. Extending this reckoning to the dawn of creation, there should be 3,272,000 years, during which the eras of rulers remain unrecorded. Though scattered references appear in the works of the various masters and apocryphal texts, they are doubtful and contradictory, lacking definitive resolution. For now, we set them aside without further discussion.

Fuxi, the Great Bright One (Taihao), bore the surname Feng and embodied the virtue of Wood. He first created the calendar, designating the jia-yin year as the inaugural year. Nüwa succeeded him, and their descendants ruled for 45,600 years. The *River Chart of Successive Surnames* states: Fuxi was the son of Suiren, born by the wind, hence the surname Feng. A dragon-horse bore the chart, and he first drew the Eight Trigrams. He commanded the Flying Dragon clan to create the six scripts and the Hidden Dragon clan to devise the calendar. Fuxi smelted metal to forge tools, taught the people to cook food, transformed the Nine Heads into Nine Governors, established offices based on the dragon’s emergence, and composed music upon the phoenix’s arrival. The people of the world called him the Heavenly Sovereign, the Great Bright One. During Fuxi’s reign, the Old Lord descended to the mortal realm, dwelling in the wilderness under the name Yuhua Zi.

The *Tang Annals* record: When Fuxi ruled the world, our Most High, in order to broadly save all, descended as his teacher. He taught him to draw the Eight Trigrams, instructed him in the division of the Three Powers, assisted him in creating written characters to replace knotted cords, and established systems to supplant the governance of rope-tying.

Zhurong, the Flame Emperor, bore the surname Jiang and embodied the virtue of Fire. His reign began in the bing-chen year, and he first taught the people pottery and smelting, hence the title Flame Emperor. According to the *Records of Posthumous Names* in the *Book of Rites*, Zhurong preceded Shennong. My annotations, however, place Shennong first. Upon closer examination, Zhurong was the ancestor of the Flame Emperor, ruling by the virtue of Fire, and thus should not come after Shennong. Here, we follow the *Book of Rites* as authoritative. His descendant, Li of the Gaoyang clan, served as Fire Minister, hence also called Zhurong, and remains the god presiding over fire in the south. The *Monthly Ordinances* states: "His emperor is the Flame Emperor; his spirit is Zhurong." Thus, Zhurong’s status as the progenitor of the Fire virtue is clear.

At that time, the Old Lord descended to Jiyin under the name Guangshou Zi.

Shennong, the Lianshan clan, was a descendant of Zhurong. He first taught the people to cultivate the hundred grains, hence the title Divine Farmer. His descendants ruled for 530 years. The *River Chart of Successive Surnames* calls him the Human Sovereign.

At that time, the Old Lord descended to Jiyin under the name Dacheng Zi. The *Tang Annals* record: When the Lianshan clan ruled the world, our Most High, bending to the needs of the age, descended as the emperor’s teacher. He bestowed upon him the Five Movements, divided the Four Seasons, assisted him in governing the myriad affairs, and enlightened him in distinguishing the hundred grains. He transformed the customs of drinking blood and eating raw flesh, replaced the sounds of clapping earth and pounding soil, abolished cave-dwellings and tree-nests, and established proper halls and chambers. He taught the people to smelt metal and mold clay, to hull millet and butcher pigs, to craft plows and spades to aid the multitudes, and to practice pottery and metallurgy for the benefit of all things. Heaven rained grain as an auspicious sign, and the earth sprouted abundant harvests.

The Yellow Emperor, the Youxiong clan, bore the surname Gongsun and embodied the virtue of Earth. His reign began in the ding-hai year.

The lineage continued for a total of one thousand five hundred and twenty years. According to the *Hetu Daxing Ji*, he was titled the Earthly Sovereign. At that time, the Venerable Lord descended into the world and dwelled on Mount Kongtong, where he was known as Master Guangcheng. The *Tang Ji* states: "When the Youxiong clan ruled the world, our Supreme One concealed himself within Mount Kongtong, releasing his mind beyond the boundless void. The Emperor personally descended with brush and reins, offering rites to honor the master-disciple relationship. Having adjusted his robes and hastened to the corner, he knelt to inquire of the Dao. At that time, Jian’gang oppressed the people with cruelty, and Chiyou stirred rebellion. The Way transformed fish and turtles into soldiers to aid the royal army, and clouds and mist into divine spirits to secretly bolster the ranks. Thus, weapons were quelled at Zhuolu, and spears were laid down at Banquan. He roamed eastward to the lands of Qingqiu, journeyed north to the borders of Hongdi, received the cinnabar scriptures in the Jade Chamber, and ascended to the azure heavens at Dinghu. Since the *Quyi* grass grew and the *Mojia* plant reappeared, who but the Great Dao could have brought forth such wonders?"

According to the *Yuwei Jing*, there are three types of Three Sovereigns, distinguished as anterior, middle, and posterior, hence the term Nine Sovereigns. The *Dongshen Dayou Jing* states: "Each of the Three Sovereigns reigned for three thousand six hundred years." The *Xiaoyou Jing* says: "Each of the Three Sovereigns reigned for ten thousand eighty years." Examining these two accounts, the *Dayou Jing* refers only to one generation of Three Sovereigns, with each sovereign reigning for three thousand six hundred years. The *Xiaoyou Jing*, however, encompasses three generations of Three Sovereigns, with each sovereign reigning for ten thousand eighty years. If we consider all three generations and nine sovereigns together, the total spans thirty-two thousand four hundred years. Some texts mention eighteen thousand years, likely due to a scribal error where "hundred" was mistaken for "thousand." Now, according to the *Laozi Neizhuan*, the figures align with those in the *Dayou* and *Xiaoyou* scriptures. As for the anterior Three Sovereigns and the middle Three Sovereigns, each spanning ninety-nine billion myriad years, this refers to the vast measure of a kalpa, not merely the lifespans of the Three Sovereigns themselves.

Regarding the later sovereigns, historical records vary widely. For instance, the *Dongshen Jing* and Xiang Jun limit the Three Sovereigns to the Heavenly Sovereign, Earthly Sovereign, and Human Sovereign, while the *Sanfen Shu* identifies Fuxi as the Heavenly Sovereign, Shennong as the Human Sovereign, and the Yellow Emperor as the Earthly Sovereign. Huangfu Mi’s commentary on the *Chunqiu Yundou Shu*, as well as Zheng Xuan and Gao You’s annotations on the *Lüshi Chunqiu*, list Fuxi, Nüwa, and Shennong as the Three Sovereigns. The *Liji Yihao Ji* names Fuxi, Zhurong, and Shennong as the Three Sovereigns. Kong Anguo, Wei Zhao, and Tao Hongjing identify Fuxi, Shennong, and the Yellow Emperor as the Three Sovereigns. The *Shangshu Dayuan*, Ying Shao, and Qiao Zhou list Suiren, Fuxi, and Shennong as the Three Sovereigns. The Liaoshi records Youchao, Suiren, and Yixi as the Three Sovereigns. The *Dixi Pu*, *Zhong Jing*, *Chusai Ji*, and *Laixiang Ji* name Fuxi, Shennong, and Zhurong as the Three Sovereigns. Given these discrepancies, it is difficult to hastily adjudicate. Emperor Wu of Liang, however, designated Fuxi, Shennong, and Suiren as the Three Sovereigns, and the Yellow Emperor, Shaohao, Diku, Dizhi, and Yao as the Five Emperors, asserting that Shun was neither one of the Three August Ones nor one of the Five Emperors but belonged to a fourth era alongside the Three Kings—a theory that is particularly untenable. Through the ages, opinions have remained divided without consensus.

Shaohao, of the Jintian clan, was a descendant of the Yellow Emperor and embodied the virtue of Metal. His reign began in the xin-wei year. His lineage continued for four hundred years. The Venerable Lord descended again upon Mount Kongtong, where he was known as the Responsive Six-in-One, or Master of the Great Ultimate. The *Tang Ji* states: "When the Jintian clan ruled the world, our Supreme One descended upon a nine-dragon chariot to serve as his teacher, known as Master of the Great Ultimate. He expounded the canons of solemn reverence, teaching the emperor to align with the seasons and welcome the qi, to manifest harmony with the divine spirits. The feathered tribes displayed their auspiciousness, and he appointed them as the Avian Officials, dividing them into nine avian ranks to govern the hundred legions. The sun and moon conjoined like jade discs to display their numinosity, and the phoenix bore the celestial chart to reveal its patterns. All these marvels arose from the Supreme Dao, illuminating the emperor’s majestic rule."

Zhuanxu...

The Gaoyang clan, surnamed Ji, held the virtue of Water. They reigned for seventy-eight years, beginning in the xinmao year. The Venerable Lord descended upon Mount Heng, known as the Red Essence Master.

The *Tang Ji* states: "When the Gaoyang clan ruled the world, our Supreme One taught them to resolve conflicts and unblock obstructions, to govern the state and pacify the people, to purge the Nine Li tribes, and to illuminate the Eight Kai. There were the fierce and grotesque, with wild purple beards like dragons, and the chieftains with serpentine bodies and crimson hair. Their strength could not prevail, and their heads shattered against the mountains. The heavens tilted to the northwest, causing the sun and moon to traverse westward; the earth sank in the southeast, compelling the rivers and seas to flow eastward. The Supreme One summoned the Six Jia spirits, commanding the hundred numina, instructing them in the scriptures of subtle words and teaching them the Way of Great Compliance. Riding the swift chariot of azure vapor, he journeyed to the dark mounds; driving the white-glass carriage, he roamed afar, reaching the coiled trees."

**Emperor Ku** of the Gaoxin clan, a descendant of the Yellow Emperor, held the virtue of Wood. He reigned for seventy years, beginning in the jiyou year. The Venerable Lord descended into the world, dwelling by the riverside, known as the Recorded Chart Master.

The *Tang Ji* states: "When the Gaoxin clan ruled the world, our Supreme One spread the Dao and disseminated transformation, aiding the age as its teacher. He discoursed on the wondrous words of the *Yellow Court*, concealing the sun and evading the moon, bearing the auspicious title of Recorded Chart. He relocated cities and shifted capitals, established the mandate of the Nine Provinces, and composed the *Six Harmonies* music. He enfeoffed Goumang to assist the Azure Emperor, commanded Rushou to aid the White Realm, sealed Mount Tai with golden mud, and illuminated the world by unveiling the jade mirror. He drank from the Well of Great Vitality and wandered the Land of Unending Day."

**Emperor Yao** of the Taotang clan, surnamed Yiqi, held the virtue of Fire. He reigned for seventy years, beginning in the wuxu year. The Venerable Lord descended into the world, residing on Mount Gushe, known as the Accomplished Master.

The *Tang Ji* states: "When the Taotang clan ruled the world, our Supreme One temporarily bestowed the perfect governance, descending to serve as teacher. He expounded the scriptures of mysterious virtue and the Dao to rectify the profound transformation, responding to the auspicious name of Cinnabar Mound, aspiring to rank among the Cinnabar Hills. Clad in a robe of coarse fur, he dined on simple fare, sitting upright upon the earthen steps. Draped in a crane-feather cloak, he drank the flowing rosy clouds, his thoughts congealing in the Jasper Gardens."

**Emperor Shun** of the Youyu clan, surnamed Yao, held the virtue of Earth. He acted as regent for thirty years and reigned for fifty, beginning in the wushen year. The Venerable Lord descended into the world, dwelling in Heyang, known as the Longevity Master.

The *Tang Ji* states: "When the Youyu clan ruled the world, our Supreme One discoursed on the principle of non-action and expounded the scriptures of separation and union. The Three Miao tribes were pacified, the Four Culprits all submitted, the hundred offices followed their seasons, and the Five Canons were revered. He bestowed the luminous pearl of the radiant spirits and granted the jade of resplendent brilliance."

According to the *Confucian Family Sayings*, Ji Kangzi asked: "I have heard the names of the Five Emperors but do not know their reality. May I ask what the Five Emperors are?"

Confucius replied: "Formerly, I heard from Lao Dan that Heaven possesses the Five Phases—Water, Fire, Wood, Metal, and Earth—which divide the seasons and transform and nurture the myriad things. Their spirits are called the Five Emperors. The ancient kings changed their titles with each new dynasty, taking the Five Phases as their model. The Five Phases alternate in rulership, cycling through generation and completion, thus embodying their significance. Therefore, the enlightened kings of old, upon their deaths, were matched with the Five Phases. Thus, Taihao was matched with Wood, the Flame Emperor with Fire, the Yellow Emperor with Earth, Shaohao with Metal, and Zhuanxu with Water."

Kangzi asked: "Why did Taihao begin with Wood?"

Confucius replied: "The Five Phases take effect beginning with Wood. Wood corresponds to the East, where the myriad things first emerge. Therefore, the kings modeled themselves upon it, first ruling the world with the virtue of Wood, and thereafter following the sequence of the generating Phases."

Kangzi asked: "I have heard that Goumang is the Director of Wood, Zhu Rong the Director of Fire, Rushou the Director of Metal, Xuanming the Director of Water, and Houtu the Director of Earth. These are the lords of the Five Phases, yet they are not called Emperors. Why is this?"

Confucius replied: "The Five Directors are the official titles of the Five Phases. The Five Phases assist in completing the work of the Supreme Deity and are called the Five Emperors. The descendants of Taihao and the others are matched with them, and thus are also called Emperors, following their titles. Shaohao’s son, Chong, became Goumang; his son, Gai, became Rushou; his sons, Xiu and Xi, became Xuanming. Zhuanxu’s son, Li, became Zhu Rong. Gonggong’s son, Gou Long, became Houtu. These five each took their abilities as their official duties, living as the highest dukes and, upon death, becoming revered spirits. They are called the Five Sacrifices and cannot be equated with the Emperors."

Kangzi asked: "Taotang, Youyu, the Xia, Shang, and Zhou dynasties alone are not matched with the Five Emperors. Is it that their virtue did not reach the ancients, or is there a limit?"

Confucius replied: "Many in antiquity governed the waters and lands and sowed the hundred grains. Only Gou Long was jointly worshipped at the Earth Altar, and Qi became the Spirit of Millet. Successive ages honored them without daring to alter this, making clear that they could not be equaled. Thus, from Taihao down to Zhuanxu, corresponding to the Five Phases, the number of kings was not limited to five, yet only five were matched with the Five Emperors. This shows that their virtue cannot be multiplied."

In the *Comprehensive Records Outside the Mirror* by Liu Daoyuan of the Song dynasty, it is noted in the annals of Shun: "The *Six Classics* do not mention the Three Sovereigns, Five Emperors, or Three Kings. The *Appended Judgments* of the *Book of Changes* states: 'After the Fuxi clan perished, the Shennong clan arose. After the Shennong clan perished, the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun arose.' Successive generations changed rulers without numbering them as three or five. Some take Fuxi to Shun as the Five Emperors, but since Confucius never spoke of this, it cannot be attached to arbitrary conjecture. The *Guanzi* states: 'Those who illuminate the One are Sovereigns; those who discern the Dao are Emperors; those who penetrate virtue are Kings; those who scheme and win in warfare are Hegemons.' Kong Yingda said that the *Guanzi* may have been recorded by later hands and is not the original text."

Some argue that the *Zhuangzi* and *Liezi* are mere allegories and fantastical fabrications, yet scholars of the Qin and Han dynasties revered their literary style and discourse, hence the proliferation of references to the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors—unaware that no such figures existed in antiquity. Though their arguments may seem plausible, when examined against historical facts, they do not constitute a coherent theory. Moreover, the reigns and chronologies of the emperors they record, when compared with dynastic annals, official histories, and astronomical calendars, are riddled with discrepancies; the *ganzhi* calculations likewise diverge. Whether this stems from scribal errors, a deliberate attempt to distinguish themselves from others, or merely an oversight of details in pursuit of broader truths remains uncertain.

Consider, for instance, Zai Wo’s inquiry to Confucius about ancient traditions, to which the Master replied that he had only heard fragments of such accounts. How much less reliable, then, are those whose talents fall short of the sages, separated from antiquity by even greater spans of time? When Ji Kangzi asked about the Five Emperors, Confucius responded with what he had once heard from Lao Dan, never once asserting that such figures never existed. This reveals the sage’s reverence for and devotion to antiquity.

Only Sima Qian, in his *Imperial Annals and Tables*, states: "Scholars have long spoken of the Five Emperors, yet the *Book of Documents* records only from Yao onward. The various schools’ accounts of the Yellow Emperor are unrefined, and gentlemen of refinement find them difficult to discuss. What Confucius transmitted—Zai Yu’s inquiries into the *Virtues of the Five Emperors* and the *Genealogies of the Emperors*—some Ru scholars do not pass down." I have traveled west to Kongtong, north past Zhuolu, east to the sea, and south across the Yangtze and Huai rivers; wherever I went, elders spoke of the lands where the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun once dwelled, their customs and teachings differing, yet all hewing close to ancient texts. In the *Spring and Autumn Annals* and *Discourses of the States*, the elucidation of the *Virtues of the Five Emperors* and *Genealogies of the Emperors* is evident. If one does not delve deeply, the manifestations therein may seem unsubstantial. Yet though records are incomplete, traces of their legacy often appear in other texts. Without diligent study, profound reflection, and an intuitive grasp of their meaning, it is indeed difficult to discuss such matters with those of shallow learning and limited knowledge.

Sima Qian exhaustively studied all available texts and extensively investigated ancient sites, making him a paragon of historiography.

**Xia Yu** of the Si clan, associated with the virtue of Metal, ascended the throne in the year *wuxu*, establishing the first month as *yin*. His descendants ruled for a total of 432 years. The Venerable Lord descended to the world, dwelling on Mount Shang, where he was known as Master Zhenxing. The *Tang Annals* state: "When the Xia Hou clan ruled the world, our Most High aided in rectifying the Dao of kingship, serving as the emperor’s teacher. He expounded the scriptures of virtue and conduct, which became the laws of the state."

**Tang of Shang**, of the Zi clan, associated with the virtue of Water, ascended the throne in the year *gengxu*, establishing the first month as *chou*, and reigned for thirteen years. His descendants ruled for a total of 613 years. The Venerable Lord descended to the world, dwelling on Mount Qian, where he was known as Master Xize. The *Tang Annals* state: "When Cheng Tang ruled the world, our Most High temporarily departed the Left Ultimate, descending to serve as the king’s teacher. He expounded the scriptures of longevity and embodied the Dao of spontaneity."

The above accounts are cross-referenced in the *Genealogical Charts of the Emperors*, *Century of Emperors and Kings*, *Scripture of the Great Possession of the Cavernous Spirit*, *Jade Calendar Scripture*, *Records of the Frontier*, *Geographical Records*, *Qiao Zhou’s Investigations into Ancient History*, *Biographies of Divine Transcendents*, and *Illustrated Records of Commanderies and States*.

Though the Venerable Lord divided his body to descend and establish teachings in accordance with the times, he concealed himself once his work was done, leaving no record of his birth in the world.

**Wai Bing** ascended the throne in the year *guihai* and reigned for two years.
**Zhong Ren** ascended the throne in the year *yichou* and reigned for four years.
**Tai Jia** ascended the throne in the year *jisi* and reigned for thirty-three years, known as Grand Ancestor.
**Wo Ding** ascended the throne in the year *renyin* and reigned for twenty-nine years.
**Tai Geng** ascended the throne in the year *xinwei* and reigned for thirty-five years.
**Xiao Jia** ascended the throne in the year *bingwu* and reigned for seventeen years.
**Yong Ji** ascended the throne in the year *guihai* and reigned for twelve years.
**Tai Wu** ascended the throne in the year *yihai*, known as Central Ancestor, and reigned for seventy-five years.
**Zhong Ding** ascended the throne in the year *gengyin* and reigned for eleven years.
**Wai Ren** ascended the throne in the year *xinchou* and reigned for five years.
**He Dan Jia** ascended the throne in the year *bingwu* and reigned for nine years.
**Zu Ji** ascended the throne in the year *yimao* and reigned for twenty-one years.
**Zu Xin** ascended the throne in the year *yihai* and reigned for sixteen years.
**Wo Jia** ascended the throne in the year *xinmao* and reigned for twenty-two years.
**Zu Ding** ascended the throne in the year *guichou* and reigned for twenty-two years.
**Nan Geng** ascended the throne in the year *yihai* and reigned for twenty-nine years.
**Yang Jia** ascended the throne in the year *jiachen* and reigned for eighteen years.

In the seventeenth year of his reign, the year *gengshen*, the Venerable Lord divided his spirit from the Great Clarity Realm, entering the womb of the Mysterious and Wondrous Jade Maiden.

**Pan Geng** ascended the throne in the year *renxu* and reigned for twenty-nine years.
**Xiao Xin** ascended the throne in the year *xinmao* and reigned for twenty-one years.
**Xiao Yi** ascended the throne in the year *renzhi* and reigned for twenty years.
**Wu Ding** ascended the throne in the year *renshen* and reigned for fifty-nine years, known as High Ancestor.

In the ninth year of his reign, the year *gengchen*, on the fifteenth day of the second month (the first month of the *yin* cycle), the full moon of the second month of Shang—now celebrated as the Lantern Festival—the *Comprehensive Mirror External Records* notes: "Before the Xia dynasty, all regarded the *yin* month as the first month. When Tang overthrew the Xia, he changed the first month to *chou*." The Venerable Lord was born. From his conception in the seventeenth year of Yang Jia’s reign to his birth, eighty-one years had passed.

Liu Xiang’s *Biographies of Transcendents* states that Laozi was born during the Shang dynasty. Ji Kang and Huangfu Mi claim he was born at the end of the Shang. The Tang dynasty celebrated his birthday as the True Origin Festival. Yue Penggui’s *Records* state: "During the reign of High Ancestor of Shang, as King Wen of Zhou began his *Changes*, divine radiance flowed into a jade embryo, auspicious hues coalesced into golden bones. Unbroken and uncut, he gestated for over eighty years before descending into the world, his birth delayed by more than twenty thousand days."

In the forty-third year, *jiayin*, the Venerable Lord descended, riding a white deer, and ascended to the heavens from the treetop of a cypress in the courtyard. Today, the deer’s hoofprints from his ascension remain at the Supreme Clarity Palace in Bozhou.

**Reigns of Shang Kings:**
- Year one of Zu Geng, *xinhai*: reigned seventeen years.
- Year one of Zu Jia, *wuzǐ*: reigned seventeen years.
- Year one of Lin Xin, *yǐsì*: reigned six years.
- Year one of Kang Ding, *xīnhài*: reigned twenty-one years.
- Year one of Wu Yi, *rénshēn*: reigned four years. He disrespected Heaven and was struck dead by violent thunder.
- Year one of Tai Ding, *bǐngzǐ*: reigned four years.
- Year one of Di Yi, *gēngchén*: reigned twenty-seven years.
- Year one of Xin Zhou, *dīngwèi*: reigned twenty-seven years, ending in *guǐyǒu*.

In the twenty-first year, *dīngmǎo*, the Venerable Lord descended upon the southern slopes of Mount Qi, styled as the Master of Xieyi. King Wen of Zhou, then Lord of the West, summoned him to serve as Archivist of the Royal Treasury. The *Generational Records* state: "Laozi served as Archivist Beneath the Pillars in the Shang." Zheng Xuan’s commentary on the *Analects* notes: "Lao Peng was a virtuous minister of Shang." Kong Yingda’s subcommentary clarifies: "Lao refers to Lao Dan."

King Wu of Zhou, of the Ji clan, embodied the virtue of Wood. He established the first month as *zǐ* and began his reign in *guǐyǒu*, overthrowing Shang and ascending the throne. His dynasty endured for ninety-six years, with his descendants ruling for eight hundred and sixty-five years in succession. The Venerable Lord was then appointed Archivist Beneath the Pillars.

The *Records of the Grand Historian* and Liu Xin’s *Seven Summaries* both state he served as Archivist of the Royal Treasury in Zhou. Ge Hong and the *Inner Transmission* record: "He was Archivist of the Royal Treasury during King Wen’s reign and Archivist Beneath the Pillars during King Wu’s reign." Only Liu Xiang’s *Biographies of Transcendents* claims: "Born in the Shang, he served as Archivist Beneath the Pillars in Zhou, later becoming Archivist of the Royal Treasury." Since he was born in the Shang, his tenure as both archivists must have occurred during King Wen’s era. However, the sequence—whether he served first or later—remains uncertain.

According to Yang Xiong’s *Regional Speech*: "I have heard that the records of the *Faji* and *Xuan* offices, along with the books of Qin’s archives, were all stored in the chambers of Zhou and Qin." The *Zhuangzi* also states: "Confucius intended to deposit books in the Zhou archives. Zilu advised, 'I have heard that the Archivist of Zhou’s Treasury, Lao Dan, is in charge of the archives. Since you wish to store books there, why not seek his counsel?' Confucius, intending to deposit the books, approached Laozi because he had once held the office of archivist and thus knew the treasury’s affairs. His past conduct and experience made him the natural choice."

The *Rites of Zhou* enumerates five types of historians: the Royal Historian, Grand Historian, Inner Historian, Outer Historian, and Censor. Feudal lords also maintained state historians. The *Book of Rites* records: "When the ruler acts, the Left Historian records it; when he speaks, the Right Historian records it." The Left Historian observed ritual, while the Inner Historian and Grand Historian oversaw all historians, preserving the records of the Four Directions. The books of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors were stored in the treasury, hence the title "Archivist of the Royal Treasury."

During the reign of King You of Zhou, Boyang Fu was active. Tang Gu’s commentary identifies him as Laozi, the Archivist Beneath the Pillars. During the reign of King Lie, the Grand Historian Jie—also Laozi—served. The Grand Historian was a senior minister of Zhou, overseeing the five historians. Responsible for recording words and deeds, he stood beneath the pillars in the ruler’s presence, hence the name of the office. Wei Zhao explains: "Boyang held the rank of Grandee, serving as Grand Historian, which was a position of lower grandee."

The *Records of the Grand Historian* notes that Zhang Cang, who excelled in calendrical studies, served as Censor during the Qin, overseeing the archives beneath the pillars. According to the *Rites of Zhou*, the Censor assisted in documentation and instruction, managing the archives and records under the Inner Historian’s purview. This Qin office differed from Zhou’s system.

The *Generational Records* state: "In the Shang, he was Archivist of the Royal Treasury; in Zhou, he was Archivist Beneath the Pillars." Since King Wen, as a vassal, served the Shang and could not yet be considered ruler of an independent Zhou, it is certain he first served as Archivist of the Royal Treasury and later as Archivist Beneath the Pillars. Liu Xiang’s account suggests Lao Dan initially served as Grand Historian but, due to his quiet and retiring nature, was reassigned to another historian’s post. By Laozi’s time, he had retired again. During King Lie’s reign, he returned as Grand Historian, serving multiple Zhou kings in a single lifetime. Thus, he held various historian roles, with the sequence of Archivist of the Royal Treasury and Archivist Beneath the Pillars differing.

Ban Gu writes: "Lao Dan was Zhou’s elder historian, who wandered beneath the pillars, investigating the ways of longevity." According to Ban’s account, he served as a historian across multiple reigns, hence the title "elder historian."

During the Sui dynasty, the Inner Historian Xue Daoheng composed the *Eulogy of the Temple Courtyard*, whose preface states: "From Fuxi to the Zhou, spanning successive dynasties, he witnessed transformations in substance and name. During the reigns of Kings Wen and Wu, he held the offices of Archivist of the Royal Treasury and Archivist Beneath the Pillars. Though courts and markets changed, his appearance remained unaltered. King Cheng..."

In the first year of his reign, the cyclical year wuyin, he ruled for forty-seven years. The Venerable Lord still served as Archivist Beneath the Pillars, styled Master of Scriptural Completion. He transmitted the Dao to the Duke of Zhou, then journeyed to the westernmost lands of Da Qin and the realms of Zhuqian, where he was known as the Ancient Master.

According to the *Inner Traditions of Laozi*: In the time of Fuxi, he was styled Master Yuhua; during the era of Shennong, Master Chuanyu; under Zhurong, Master Guangshou; in the age of the Yellow Emperor, Master Guangcheng; during Zhuanxu’s reign, Master Chijing; under Emperor Ku, Master Lutu; in the time of Yao, Master Wucheng; under Shun, Master Yinshou; during the Xia dynasty under Yu, Master Ningzhen; styled Master Zhenxing in the time of Tang; and Master Xize during the reigns of Kings Wen and Wu—altogether serving as preceptor to twelve sovereigns.

The *Stele of the Pavilion of Clarity and Emptiness*, composed by Xiao Ziyun, Gentleman-in-Attendance of the Yellow Gate during the Liang dynasty, also states: "The Great Void is vast and boundless, transcending speech and form. Responding to stimuli, it acquires names in accordance with things. When Boyang descended in pregnancy, his hair was already white as frost. Yao called him Wucheng; Shun, Yinshou. Chijing was born in the time of Zhuanxu; Lutu emerged from Shennong. Embracing the One and containing the Origin, he became the foundation of kingship." It further declares: "Roaming the spirits above the Nine Heavens, guiding the hundred essences beneath the towering gates, majestic and boundless—none can name him."

Old records uniformly style the Venerable Lord as Master Dacheng during the time of Shennong and Master Lutu under Emperor Ku. This stele alone asserts that Lutu emerged from Shennong, while the *Inner Traditions* calls him Master Chuanyu during Shennong’s era. Surely there must be minor discrepancies; the authoritative texts should be taken as standard.

Ge Hong, in his *Biographies of Divine Transcendents*, casts doubt on the claim that he served as preceptor to successive emperors, arguing that such accounts derive from fragmentary writings rather than the canonical scriptures of transcendence. He further contends that it would be incongruous for one to abandon the clarity of the Dao and enter the foul defilements, or to forsake celestial office for mortal rank. He states: "Where there is Heaven and Earth, there is the unfolding of the Dao, and those who guard it appear in every generation. Why must it always be the same Laozi?" Such views stem from later scholars’ penchant for novelty and their desire to exalt Laozi, hence these assertions.

Yin Wencao, Abbot of the Temple of Heavenly Veneration during the Tang, once composed a refutation, saying: "Ge Hong’s *Biographies* aim to dispel delusion. The Venerable Lord arose through self-cultivation, intending to instruct and guide later generations, urging them to strive diligently. If one claims Laozi was a perfected being, others will emulate him with zeal; if one calls him a divine spirit, he becomes unattainable. This is merely Ge Hong’s personal conjecture, for he fails to recognize that from the very beginning of creation, some attained the Dao through study, while others descended as natural sages to serve as teachers, transmitting the teachings in accordance with circumstances."

The *Scripture of Fixed Resolve* states: "In the beginning, when the Heavenly Worthy established the Great Compassion, he acted in accordance with spontaneity, aligning with principle without conscious effort. His profound awakening was natural, devoid of written words. He compiled scriptures to instruct future generations." From this perspective, the Venerable Lord’s natural mastery of the Dao and his role as preceptor across the ages—what doubt can there be? As the Father of Great Compassion, why would he refuse to descend in humility, harmonizing his light with the foul defilements?

Laozi said to Guan Yinzi: "Since the earliest ages, my transformations have been as numerous as grains of sand. Each movement leaves a record, and I have found it exceedingly arduous." The *Canon of Supreme Mystery* also states: "Laozi accumulated learning through countless cycles, ascending to the rank of supreme sage. His responses were boundless, beyond all reckoning." In recent generations, he manifested his transformations, being born at the end of the Yin dynasty.

Yu Xuan’s *Record of the Northern Frontier* states: "Laozi concentrated his spirit in the ministry of Supreme Mystery, dispersing his numinous radiance to teach in every age." Cui Yuan’s *Record of Lai Village* on Mount Crane says: "Laozi’s manifestations appear in every generation." The Transcendent Lord Ge states: "Before the opening of creation, he descended as preceptor to the nations, continuing without cease." Wen Shi, Gui Gu, and Zhang Fuhan all declare: "The Venerable Lord existed before the Great Non-Being, traversing Heaven and Earth in ways beyond enumeration." How can these be dismissed as fragmentary writings or the shallow views of later scholars? Ge Hong simply failed to consider this.

King Kang.

In the first year *yichou* (of his reign), he ruled for twenty-six years. The Venerable Lord returned once more to the Zhou, styled as Guo Shuzi, and again served as Archivist Beneath the Pillars.

In the first year *xinmao* of King Zhao’s reign, he ruled for fifty years. The Venerable Lord resigned his post and returned to Bo. In the twenty-third year *guichou*, on the fifteenth day of the fifth month (*renwu*), the Venerable Lord passed westward through Hangu Pass, where he transmitted the Dao to the Pass Prefect Yin Xi. Some say that though the Venerable Lord’s departure from the pass occurred in the *guichou* year of Zhou, the fifth month, *renwu* day, it was not during King Zhao’s reign. Others claim it was during King You’s time, but upon examination, King You ascended in the *gengshen* year and was overthrown by the Quanrong in his eleventh year, during which there was no *guichou* year. Still others assert it was the fortieth year of King Xuan, *guichou*, the eighteenth day of the fifth month, *renwu*; yet reckoning the years, in the forty-third year *bingchen*, the Venerable Lord was by the Wei River, where he warned King Xuan of Zhou’s impending decline. In the second year of King You, the Three Rivers quaked, and Laozi said, “Zhou will perish.” King You was the son of King Xuan. If the Venerable Lord had departed the pass in the fortieth year of King Xuan, he could not have still been by the Wei River in the forty-third year, nor could he have witnessed the quaking of the Three Rivers during King You’s reign. In the forty-third year of King Ping, the year was also *guichou*, but the fifth month had no *renwu* day. Yao Changqian’s *Annals of Emperors and Kings* states that in the tenth year *bingyin* of King Jing, Confucius became a disciple, and in the thirty-second year *guichou* of King Jing, he transmitted the Dao at the pass. Yet Confucius was born in the *gengxu* year of King Ling, and in the *bingyin* year of King Jing, he was only seventeen—hardly old enough to have studied under Laozi. Moreover, when King Mu journeyed west, he had already heard of Perfected Yin encountering Laozi, attaining the Dao, and ascending in flight. Filled with reverence for this legacy, he then built the Louguan Terrace, established Daoist priests, and instituted sacrificial rites. Thus, it is clear that Laozi’s transmission at the pass did not occur after King Mu’s time. Only the *Inner Transmission* aligns with the words of the Transcendent Lord Ge; all other conflicting accounts ultimately violate reason and principle and cannot be cited as fact.

In the twenty-fourth year *jiayin*, Laozi transmitted the Dao to Yin Xi, bestowing upon him the *Five Thousand Words of the Dao and Its Virtue*. Thereupon, he ascended to Heaven from a small hill south of Yin Xi’s residence. At that time, a five-colored light pierced the Purple Tenuity, and the well waters all overflowed. From the ninth year of High Ancestor of Shang to this time, a total of 274 years had passed.

In the twenty-fifth year *yimao*, the Venerable Lord once more divided his body and descended to be born in the household of Li Taiguan in the state of Shu. Liu Xiang’s *Biographies of the Transcendents* from the Former Han states: “Laozi was born in the Shang era, served as Archivist Beneath the Pillars in Zhou, and later became Keeper of the Archives, known at the time as the Reclusive Gentleman. Confucius met him and, recognizing his sageliness, took him as his teacher. Riding a blue ox-drawn carriage, he departed for the Great Qin, where the Pass Prefect Yin awaited and welcomed him.” Ji Kang and Huangfu Mi both state that Laozi was born at the end of the Shang, while Ge Xiaoxian claims that during the Zhou, he spiritually entered the womb of Li’s mother—referring precisely to the time of his descent into the household of Li Taiguan. Some argue that as the Shang’s mandate neared its end and Zhou’s had already risen, he was born at the close of the Shang; why then speak of his spiritual transformation into this womb during the Zhou? The matter remains unresolved.

In the twenty-seventh year *dingsi*, the Venerable Lord met with Perfected Yin Xi at the Green Ram Market, preparing to depart and transform the Hu. Thus, the *Tang Annals* state: “The Green Ram Market was the second place where the Supreme Heavenly Thearch descended to be born.” From the time the Dao of Shang was obstructed and the virtue of Zhou declined, he passed through the office of Keeper of the Archives, mingling with the world, then abandoned the post of Archivist Beneath the Pillars to conceal his traces. Binding his robes, he departed the Wei Gate and crossed westward through Hangu Pass. It also states: “After a thousand days’ appointed time, they met again for a brief while. About to leave Shu, he sought to transform the customs of the Hu, journeying far to the Flowing Sands, long altering the rough ways of the western barbarians, reaching Shendu and the lands of the western Hu, where woolly and feathered peoples became his guests. He taught the kings of Fanduo and Azhu.” Furthermore, Emperor Ming of Wei composed the *Eulogy for the Venerable Lord*, saying: “Deeply pitying later generations, he descended in Chen to serve as Archivist Beneath the Pillars in Zhou, enduring nine hundred boundless years. Returning to Kunlun, his transforming influence spread to the lands of the Hu, then to Shushen, and later to Tianzhu, whereupon he departed.” The *Wei Lue: Account of the Western Rong* and the *Northern History: Account of the Western Regions* both state: “Five hundred *li* west of Yutian is the Pimo Monastery, said to be where Laozi transformed the Hu.” This is recorded in the *Tang Comprehensive Institutions*.

In the twenty-eighth year *wuwu*, the Venerable Lord and Perfected Yin Xi descended upon Qiling Mountain in Tianshui. Today, in Qinzhou, the Western Ascent Terrace and the Hall of the Sage’s Countenance both remain.

In the first year *xinsi* of King Mu’s reign, he ruled for fifty-five years and lived to the age of one hundred and five. After transforming the Hu, the Venerable Lord ascended to Heaven. In the fourth year *jiashen*, the Venerable Lord descended once more to the Central States, journeying east to Fuduo, where he convened with the Great Thearch and reviewed the assembly of transcendents. He then traveled to the Western Sea, reaching the state of Sulin, where he was styled as the Great Illuminating Envoy, before ascending to Heaven again. Upon hearing that Perfected Yin Xi had attained the Dao and ascended, King Mu had the Louguan Terrace built and established seven Daoist priests to conduct sacrificial rites. This is recorded in the *Inner Transmission of King Mu* and the *Biography of the Master of Louguan*.

In the first year *bingzi* of King Gong’s reign, he ruled for twelve years.
In the first year *wuzi* of King Yi’s reign, he ruled for twenty-five years.
In the first year *guichou* of King Xiao’s reign, he ruled for fifteen years.
In the first year *wuchen* of King Yi’s reign, he ruled for sixteen years.

The Lord Lao descended with the Three Masters of Primordial Antiquity upon Three Learning Mountain in Mianzhu of Shu, where he transmitted to Li Duozhen the Dao of ascending in flight. Today, this site is called the Governance of Duozhen.

King Li:
In the first year *jiashen*, he ruled for thirty-six years.
In the twenty-first year *jiachen*, the Lord Lao descended upon Louguan, where he transmitted to the Daoist priest Song Lun the Dao of the Middle Effulgence, the *Scripture of Communing with the Perfected*, and the *Six Jia Talismans of Numinous Flight* inscribed on plain silk.

Duke Zhou and Duke Shao’s Regency:
Beginning in *gengshen*, when King Li was exiled to Zhi, the crown prince being young, the two dukes jointly administered state affairs for fourteen years.
Upon King Li’s death, the crown prince was enthroned as King Xuan.

King Xuan:
In the first year *jiaxu*, he ruled for forty-seven years.
In the forty-third year *bingchen*, the Lord Lao descended upon the Wei River, where he informed King Xuan that Jupiter had shifted northward, foretelling the decline of Zhou.
Two years later, King You ascended the throne.
Beside the Wei River lies Boyang Plain, the site of this event, as recorded in the *Treatise on Geography*.

At that time, there was a firewood-gatherer who walked singing, unknown to all. When asked about him, the Lord Lao said: “This is one who revives the people of the state, cultivating the supreme, perfectly true Dao.”
See the Tang *Illustrated Responses Through the Ages*.

King You.

In the first year *gengshen*, he reigned for eleven years.
In the second year *xinyou*, the earth quaked; the Jing, Wei, and Luo Rivers all trembled. The Lord Lao declared that Zhou would perish, and within ten years, the appointed cycle would be fulfilled.
Later, it indeed fell to the Quanrong.
Recorded in the *Records of the Grand Historian*.

King Ping:
In the first year *xinwei*, he reigned for fifty-one years, relocating eastward to Luoyi.
In the thirty-third year *guimao*, in the twelfth month, the Lord Lao once more emerged from the Pass, enlightening the various states.
He then journeyed to the Western Sea’s Liu and Lin Isles, where he examined and ranked the multitude of immortals.
Upon completing his affairs, he returned to the Central Kingdom.
Recorded in the *Scripture of Western Ascension*.

King Wei:
In the first year *renxu*, he reigned for thirty-three years.

King Zhuang:
In the first year *yiyou*, he reigned for fifteen years.
In the ninth year *guisi*, on the seventh day of the fourth month (the first *mao* day of the lunar cycle), the Buddha Śākyamuni was born.
The Buddhist *Sutra of Auspicious Responses* states: he was born at midnight on the eighth day of the fourth month.
This follows the Zhou calendar.
The *Sutra of Past and Present* further states: he was born on the eighth day of the second month.
This follows the Xia calendar.
At that time, the Lord Lao was six hundred and fourteen years old.
The Buddha’s birth in the ninth year of King Zhuang of Zhou corresponds to the seventh year of Duke Zhuang of Lu in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, falling in the fourth month of the Xia calendar, when the fixed stars were not visible and the night shone bright.
By the eighth year of Emperor Wu of Wei’s Wuding era, one thousand two hundred and thirty-seven years had passed.
Recorded in the *Wei Shu’s Treatise on Buddhism and Daoism* and the *Sui Shu’s Bibliographical Treatise*.
The *Stele of the Toutuo Temple* states: “The two Zhuangs of Zhou and Lu personally witnessed the mirror of night’s radiance.”
Cited in Xiao Tong’s *Wen Xuan*, in Qi Wang Jin’s composition.
The Prince of Shaoling of Liang, in his memorial, wrote: “When the fixed stars vanished, Zhou’s jade was pregnant with omens; at the full moon, her form was round—Han’s dream of the night was stirred.”
Cited in the Tang monk’s *Forest of Gems in the Garden of Dharma*.

King Xi.

In the first year Gengzi, he reigned for five years.

King Hui’s first year Yisi, he reigned for twenty-five years.
King Xiang’s first year Gengwu, he reigned for thirty-three years.
King Xiang’s first year Guimao, he reigned for six years.
King Kuang’s first year Jiyou, he reigned for six years.
In the fourth year Renzi, the Buddha entered *nirvana*.
The Buddhist *Golden Light Sutra* states: “His lifespan was brief, only eighty years.”

King Ding’s first year Yimao, he reigned for twenty-one years.
The king inquired of Lord Lao about the immortals in the world, and Lord Lao replied: “On the Central Peak there is an Ancient Master—it is I.”
Recorded in Zhang Sengjian’s *Wangci Palace* and the *Xunyang Record*.

King Jian’s first year Bingzi, he reigned for fourteen years.
King Ling’s first year Gengyin, he reigned for twenty-seven years.
In the twenty-first year Gengxu, on the twenty-first day of the tenth month, Confucius was born.

King Jing’s first year Dingsi, he reigned for twenty-five years.
King Dao reigned for five months.

King Jing’s first year Renwu, he reigned for forty-three years.
In the seventeenth year Wuxu, Confucius said to Nangong Jingshu: “I have heard that Lao Dan is profoundly versed in antiquity and the present, understands the origins of ritual and music, and illuminates the return of the Dao and virtue—he is my teacher. Now I shall go to him.”
The following year, he and Jingshu together went to Zhou and met Laozi.
According to the *Zhuangzi*: “When Confucius was fifty, he journeyed south to Pei and saw Laozi.” This must refer to this year.
As for his inquiries on ritual and the matter of the archived texts, though recorded in the *Record of Rites*, *The Family Sayings of Confucius*, and the *Records of the Grand Historian*, none specify the year or month, so their sequence remains unknown.
Yet in *The Family Sayings of Confucius*, Zixia asked Confucius: “I have heard that in the *Book of Changes*, the generation of humans and the myriad things each has its odd and even, their qi divided differently. Only those who attain virtue can trace their origins. Heaven is one, earth is two, and man is three; three threes make nine, and nine nines eighty-one. One governs the sun, and the moon’s number is ten, so humans are born after ten months. Eight nines are seventy-two, paired to follow the odd. The odd governs the celestial stems, the stems govern the moon, and the moon governs the horse, so horses are born after twelve months. The rest follow their kind. Birds and fish are born of yin yet belong to yang, so all are born at *mao*. Fish advance in water, birds soar in clouds. Thus at the onset of winter, swallows and sparrows enter the sea and transform into clams and shellfish; scales feed in summer and hibernate in winter. Those that swallow have eight orifices and are born at *mao*, while those that chew have nine orifices and are born from the womb. Four-footed creatures lack feathers and wings; those with horns lack upper teeth. Those born by day resemble their fathers, those born by night their mothers. May I ask if this is so?”
Confucius replied: “Yes, I once heard this from Lao Dan, just as you have said.”
From this, we see that Confucius regarded Lao Dan as profoundly versed in antiquity and the present, so he sought instruction in all things—how could it have been only ritual and the Five Phases or the Five Emperors?
In the forty-first year Jianrenxu, Confucius died.
Recorded in *The Family Sayings of Confucius* and the *Zuo Tradition*.
At that time, Laozi was eight hundred and twenty-four years old.

King Yuan’s first year Yichou, he reigned for eight years.
King Zhengding’s first year Guiyou, he reigned for twenty-eight years.
King Ai reigned for three months.
King Si reigned for five months.

King Kao’s first year Xinchou, he reigned for fifteen years.
King Weilie’s first year Bingchen, he reigned for twenty-four years.
King An’s first year Gengchen, he reigned for twenty-six years.
In the eighteenth year Dingyou, Duke Xian of Qin ascended the throne.

King Yilie’s first year Bingwu, he reigned for seven years.
In the second year Dingwei, Lord Lao returned to Qin.
Duke Xian of Qin received him with lodging and rites, inquiring about the reckoning of time, whereupon Lord Lao departed through the Hangu Pass.
Recorded in the *Records of the Grand Historian*.

King Xian.

The first year Gui-chou, he reigned for forty-eight years.
In the eighth year Geng-shen, Duke Xiao of Qin ascended the throne.
Lord Lao returned eastward, traveling to Red City and Mount Meng, passing again through Fu-duo, where he gathered and assembled the host of immortals.
Thereafter, he concealed himself on Mount Jing, Mount Tai-shi, and Mount Shao-shi.
Recorded in Wang Jia’s *Shi Yi Ji*.

King Shenjing
The first year Gui-chou, he reigned for six years.

King Nan
Having moved the capital to Western Zhou, the first year Ding-wei, and in the fifty-ninth year Yi-si, he ceded all his cities to Qin, whereupon Western Zhou perished.
In the ninth year Yi-mao, King Zhao of Qin ascended the throne.
Lord Lao, together with Yin Xi and the host of immortals, journeyed west to Mount Nü-ji, Mount Yao-tou, and Mount Tian-zhu, then exited once more through the Hangu Pass, ascending Kunlun and returning to the Purple Tenuity’s Upper Palace.
Upon hearing of this, King Zhao ordered cities and towns built at the places Lord Lao had traversed, to mark his sacred traces, thus establishing the lodging station of Lord Lao and the city of Yin Xi.

According to the *Basic Records of Lord Lao* and various historical accounts, Laozi was born in the year Geng-chen of King Wu Ding of Shang at Bo, and soon thereafter ascended to heaven riding a white deer.
One hundred and eight years later, in the year Ding-mao of King Zhou Xin, he descended at Qi Zhou.
Now, reckoning by the extended calendar, the first year of Wu Ding was Ren-shen, and his ninth year was indeed Geng-chen.
The records state: Lord Lao served as Archivist of the Royal Treasury during the time of King Wen of Zhou, and as Archivist Beneath the Pillars during the time of King Wu.
Counting from Wu Ding’s Geng-chen to King Wu’s Gui-you □□□□, nearly one hundred seventy-four years have passed.
The records state: In the fifth month, Ren-wu, of the Gui-chou year of King Zhao of Zhou, Lord Lao was about to leave Zhou, and on the twelfth day of the seventh month, he arrived at the Hangu Pass, where he instructed Yin Xi.
Counting from the first year Xin-mao of King Zhao to his twenty-third year Gui-you, and from Gui-you to Gui-chou, nearly one hundred and one years have passed, totaling nearly two hundred seventy-four years before his westward departure.
The records state: In the year Jia-yin, in the fourth month, Lord Lao ascended into the Great Subtlety at Louguan.
The following year, Yi-mao, he divided his body and was born in Shu.
Two years later, Ding-si, he met Yin Xi at the market of the Green Ram, and together they journeyed to the Western Regions.
By the first year Xin-si of King Mu, he had returned to the Central Lands, passing eastward through the place of the Great Emperor of Fusang, where he gathered and assembled the host of immortals.
From the twenty-third year Gui-chou of King Zhao to the first year Xin-si of King Mu, nearly twenty-eight years have passed, marking the time when Lord Lao had completed his transformation of the Hu and returned to the Central Lands.
The *Records of the Grand Historian* states: In the second year Xin-you of King You of Zhou, the Three Rivers quaked, and Laozi said that Zhou’s mandate was exhausted.
From King Mu’s Xin-si to this Xin-you, nearly two hundred twenty years have passed, totaling nearly five hundred twenty-two years.
In the twenty-third year Gui-mao of King Ping, Lord Lao once more exited the pass, journeyed to the Western Sea, and gathered the host of immortals.
The *Wei Shu: Treatise on Buddhism and Daoism* states: The birth of Śākyamuni occurred in the ninth year of King Zhuang of Zhou, corresponding to the seventh year of Duke Zhuang of Lu in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, the fourth month, Xin-mao, when the constant stars were not seen.
Now, the first year of King Zhuang was Yi-you, and his ninth year was indeed Gui-si.
From King You’s Xin-you to this Gui-si, ninety-two years have passed, totaling nearly six hundred fourteen years before the Buddha’s birth, and he entered nirvāṇa in the fourth year Ren-zi of King Kuang.
Confucius was born on the twenty-first day of the tenth month, Geng-zi, in the twenty-first year Geng-xu of King Ling of Zhou.
From King Zhuang’s Gui-si to this Geng-xu, nearly one hundred thirty-seven years have passed, totaling nearly seven hundred fifty-one years.
In the fifteenth year Xin-wei of King Jing, Nangong Jing-shu was born, when Confucius was thirty-five years old.
In the eleventh year Ren-chen of King Jing, Duke Ding of Lu ascended the throne, and Jing-shu was first appointed as a grandee but declined, citing his ancestor’s command to serve Confucius.
By the seventh year Wu-xu of Duke Ding, when Confucius was fifty years old, he journeyed south to Pei and met Laozi.
This was the time when Lord Lao had completed his manifestations in all directions and returned once more to the Central Lands.
From King Ling’s Geng-xu to this Wu-xu, nearly forty-nine years have passed, totaling nearly seven hundred ninety-nine years.
The records state: Upon hearing that Laozi had passed by, King Zhao of Qin ordered cities and towns built at the Hangu Pass.
According to King Zhao’s fifty-first year, when he destroyed Zhou, reckoning from the first year Ding-wei of King Nan of Zhou and his ninth year Yi-mao, when King Zhao of Qin ascended the throne, this was the time when Lord Lao was about to ascend Kunlun and return to the Purple Tenuity’s Upper Palace.
From King Jing’s Wu-xu to this Yi-mao, nearly one hundred ninety-seven years have passed, totaling nearly nine hundred ninety-six years.
Lord Lao’s responses were boundless, his transformations unfathomable; whether ascending or descending, none could fully comprehend.
Now, examining the records and the extended calendar, reckoning from Wu Ding’s Geng-chen of Shang to Yi-mao of King Nan of Zhou, spanning appearances and concealments over nearly nine hundred ninety-six years—one hundred seventy-four years in Shang and eight hundred twenty-two years in Zhou—this aligns precisely with historical accounts and various classics.
Emperor Ming of Wei praised: "As Archivist Beneath the Pillars of Zhou, he endured nine hundred years."
Wu Yanghao, Academician of the Tang Imperial University, also said: "Lord Lao’s transformations and numinous responses are difficult to fathom; born in the days of Shang, he also served in the time of Zhou, and reckoning from beginning to end, he stood for a thousand years."
These all refer to round numbers.
The *Records of the Grand Historian* mentions "perhaps one hundred sixty years" or "perhaps two hundred years," which is based on the estimations of people in Confucius’ time regarding Laozi’s age, unable to know the truth, hence the frequent use of "perhaps" or "maybe."
Moreover, the *Lie Xian Zhuan* states: "He departed and entered Da Qin."
It also states: "Together with Yin Xi, he journeyed to the Flowing Sands, and none knew where they ultimately went."
Huangfu Mi said: "He went west to the Flowing Sands and composed the *Futu Jing* to transform the Rong customs."

*The Inner Transmission of the Primordial Beginning* states: After Laozi departed through the pass, he journeyed through eighty-one kingdoms and later arrived in Jibin to transform the Hu peoples.

Liu Xiang’s eulogy reads: His virtue harmonized with the Primordial Radiance, his longevity matched the Two Modes.

Though the various traditions differ in their accounts, none fail to extol his long life. Confucians, however, deem the notion of ascending to heaven through longevity as absurd, and thus merely state that none knew his end, implying he never truly died.

Tang scholar Yin Wencao argued: Lao Jun was a great sage who attained the Dao through kalpas of cultivation, and thus could bestow blessings upon the world, harmonizing the hidden and the manifest, undergoing myriad transformations while remaining eternally present. Only Zhuangzi, through the fictional dialogue of Qin Shi’s lament, sought to unsettle those of shallow understanding who cling to life. Yet Emperor Wu of Liang mistakenly took Lao Jun for a corpse-liberated immortal—this was a grave error.

Upon thorough examination of Laozi’s life from beginning to end, no evidence of a release-by-death is found. Zhuang Zhou’s lone assertion is akin to his fables of Jiang and Hong Meng—mere allegories of defiance against history. To take them as fact would be as absurd as accepting Confucius’ encounter with Robber Zhi as a true event.

According to Wang Jia’s *Records of Forgotten Lore*: In the late Zhou, Laozi dwelled on Mount Jingshi, often declaring, “I am ever among men, never absent for even a moment. At times I appear as an ordinary person, at others as a sage—only the vulgar fail to recognize me. Men’s good and evil deeds each bear their karmic consequences, like shadows following form or echoes answering sound. Those who contemplate truth and aspire to goodness may yet encounter me.” This further confirms his undying nature.

The *Scripture of the Dragon’s Hoof* records: In the Hall of Bright Clouds within the Great Luo Heaven, Laozi addressed the Celestial Venerable of the Nine Heavens, saying: “Now, in response to karmic conditions, my realm pervades my entire being, manifesting above in the heavens and below in the myriad phenomena. Through kalpas of transformation, I guide and deliver countless beings. The worthy and the sagely of the Dao descend to become their teachers, or are born into the lower realms, mingling indistinguishably with the worldly, assuming mortal forms yet remaining untainted by the mundane. In harmony with the Dao, I respond to those with affinity, silently aiding and delivering them, never claiming merit. Since my spiritual power is self-so, I descend in every age to meet the needs of the time—how could birth and death ever bind me? You, my disciples, peer at the heavens through a tube—no wonder you find it strange.”

Some claim Laozi studied under Rongcheng or sought the Dao from Chang Cong. This merely reflects the sagely one’s love of worthies and joy in goodness, his humility in seeking knowledge from others. It was also a means to establish the teacher-disciple tradition for posterity—how could there have been any true transmission of teachings?

Older traditions record that his descendants held prominent offices in unbroken succession. This, too, was the sage’s way of avoiding reclusion and eccentricity to startle the world, instead manifesting illusions to harmonize with the dust of the world.
