Before the Dao-qi responds, they grow lax—this is humanity’s self-abandonment of the Dao, not the Dao’s abandonment of them. With single-minded sincerity, one may aspire to realization across a hundred or a thousand lifetimes, unwavering and unrelenting. Such devotion is deeply pitiable.

In former times, I encountered the Most High Lord Lao, who revealed to me the *True Scripture of the Dao and Virtue*, teaching governance of state and self, salvation of beings, and propagation of the teachings. It spans heaven and earth, fills the cosmos, penetrates the nine heavens, and pervades the myriad things—its essence is the keystone of practice and cultivation, the foundation of realization, beyond mere words. Yet the world, steeped in superficial deceit, scorns humility as cowardice, dismisses withdrawal as ineptitude, ridicules the renunciation of sagacity as absurdity, and derides the abandonment of benevolence and righteousness as perversity. Such is the delusion of this degenerate age.

The intent of the Mysterious Sages is to restore simplicity and return to the uncarved block, to honor the Dao and expel heterodoxy. Once crooked paths are cleared, the Supreme Dao naturally manifests. When simplicity is established, frivolous competition eases, rendering governance and restraint unnecessary, universal love superfluous, luminous sagacity redundant, and cunning stratagems obsolete. The world, in perfect harmony, returns to the Great Compliance—this is the grand aim of the Mysterious Sages.

Having fulfilled my allotted span, I retired to this mountain, perpetually practicing the Dao of enduring vision and non-action, dispatching transcendent officials to instruct humanity. The Supreme Sages and Lofty Perfected, moved by the impermanence of kalpas, the flux of cosmic cycles, the interplay of yin and yang, and the inexorable march of life and death, now approach the cusp of the Yang-nine and Hundred-six catastrophes. Thus, they diligently descend to teach, to save humanity—especially those who seek the Dao.

Yet those who seek the Dao waver between presence and absence, their minds distracted, scarcely one in ten thousand remaining steadfast. The Celestial Perfected, ever compassionate toward the worldly, dwell among mortals, concealing their radiance and transforming their forms to enlighten all directions. On this mountain, nine descents each possess palaces governed by true officials. Below lie treasures of jade, the five metals, divine mushrooms, and spirit herbs—medicines guarded by the Three Heavens, scriptures concealed by the Most High. Some rest in stone chambers and cloud terraces, others in sheer cliffs and secluded valleys, all overseen by numinous stewards. No venomous or predatory creatures dwell here. These may be used to transcend the world, nurture life, cultivate the Dao, and ascend to perfection.

Since residing on this mountain, you have never ventured to explore its four reaches or gaze upon its towering peaks—thus, you remain unaware. Strive in your practice, and await the day you ride the luminous chariot through the void, then shall you invert the light to fathom its origins and ends."

Thereupon, he commanded his attendants to bestow upon her the two scriptures of the *Dao and Virtue* and the *Chart of Lingering Radiance*, then departed. Thereafter, he descended to the Yellow Court Abbey once every year or three to five times. Years later, Miaoxiang ascended to heaven in broad daylight, and her abbey was renamed the Abbey of Dao-Nurturance. This mountain, where Shun cultivated the Dao, was henceforth called Dao Prefecture. Lord Lao once transmitted the *Dao and Virtue* to Shun here, as attested by the River Earl, who said: "I have guarded this text for one thousand seven hundred years."

During the reign of Yu of Xia, he descended upon Mount Shang, styled Master True Conduct, teaching the Dao of diligence and frugality, and bestowing the *Nine Categories*. He also commanded the divine envoy Xuanyi of Mount Wanwei to present the *Five Numinous Talismans of the Jade Scripture*, the true writ for controlling floods, along with the methods of pacing the void and summoning spirits to subdue demons.

When Yu governed the waters, dredging rivers and channels, Lord Lao dispatched Lady Yunhua to secretly assist him. At one point, Yu halted below Mount Wu, where a sudden tempest arose, shaking cliffs and valleys, its force uncontrollable. Suddenly, Lady Yunhua appeared, and Yu bowed, beseeching aid. She instructed her attendants to confer upon Yu the writ for summoning spirits, then commanded her divine ministers—Kuangzhang, Yuyu, Huanghui, Dayi, Gengchen, Tonglü, and the Giant Spirit—to assist in hewing rocks, dredging waves, and clearing blockages.

Yu once paid homage to Lady Yunhua atop Mount Chongsuo. In the blink of an eye, she transformed into stone, then suddenly soared aloft, dissolving into wispy clouds. At times, she settled gently, coalescing into evening rain; at others, she became a soaring dragon or a circling crane, assuming a thousand forms and myriad postures, beyond mortal reach.

Yu suspected her of being a cunning trickster, no true transcendent. He asked Tonglü: "The root of heaven and earth is the Dao; the instrument of the Dao is the Sage."

The grades of the Sage are the Perfected and the Immortals. Those who are endowed with primordial qi and attain the Dao without cultivation—such as the Lord of Wood and the Queen Mother of Metal—are the ancestral origins of the Two Primordials, the root of yin and yang, the sovereigns of the Immortal Perfected, and the first essence of creation. Lady Yunhua of the Radiant Bloom is the daughter of the Queen Mother of Metal. Having received the writ, she became Lady of the Upper Palace of Cloud Radiance, presiding over the instruction of youthful Perfected beings. Her abode is the Terrace of Jade Reflection, where she appears and vanishes in transformation—this is her constant way. She, too, congeals primordial qi to become Perfected, uniting with the Dao in form. She is not a shape born of womb or nurtured by transformation, but the primordial qi of the Lesser Yin of the Western Flower. Moreover, qi pervades heaven and earth, governs the animate and inanimate, encompasses creation in its vastness, and penetrates to the finest hair. It becomes human in humans, and thing in things—how could it be confined to soaring dragons or cranes, flying geese or phoenixes?

Yu was convinced and went to seek her. Suddenly, he beheld a terrace of cloud and jade, its jasper towers towering, divine officers standing guard in numbers beyond naming. Lions held the gates, celestial steeds opened the paths, venomous dragons and lightning beasts—all Eight Awes—stood ready at the carriage. The Lady sat in repose upon her jeweled terrace. Yu bowed his head and inquired of the Dao.

She summoned Yu to sit and said: "The Sagely Craftsman, at the inception of the great undifferentiated simplicity, divided it into countless forms. Unfurling the great storehouse’s bud, it scattered into boundless things. Thus, pacing the Three Luminaries, he established the shadow’s measure; facing the Nine Regions, he ordered the states and kingdoms. He marked the clepsydra to divide day and night, cold and heat to complete the year’s reckoning, trigram symbols to rectify the directions, mountains and rivers to separate yin and yang. He enclosed cities to gather the people, fashioned tools to defend the masses, devised carriages and robes to distinguish noble and base, and cultivated grains to prepare for famine. All these systems above take their pattern from the stars and emulate the divine Perfected, while below they nourish the realm of form.

Therefore, the sun and moon have their obscurity and brightness, life and death their cold and heat, thunder and lightning their times of emergence and retreat, wind and rain their rhythms of movement and stillness. Primordial qi floats above, while the myriad essences disperse below. The cycles of rise and fall, the turns of order and chaos, the natures of worthy and foolish, the dispositions of good and evil, the qi of firm and yielding, the fates of long and short life, the stations of noble and base, the ranks of high and low, the responses of fortune and misfortune, the appointed times of success and failure—all these are endowed by the Dao, suspended from heaven, and made manifest by the Sage.

Nature arises from heaven, but destiny is fulfilled by humans. Heaven establishes it, but the Dao enacts it. Where the Dao abides, there is being; where it departs, there is nothing. Without the Dao, things cannot endure; without cultivation, the Dao cannot be attained. The Most High has said: 'Attain utmost emptiness, guard deep stillness, and the myriad things will revert of themselves.' This means returning to the Dao and abiding constantly.

The Dao’s workings are endlessly transformative, yet never exhaust their singularity. Thus, heaven partakes of the Threefold Mystery, earth of the Primal Yellow, and humans of virtue and power. Beyond this, there is no Dao. The essentials of long endurance are: heaven preserves its mystery, earth guards its things, and humans nurture their qi—this is how wholeness is maintained. Thus, my destiny lies within me, not in heaven and earth’s slaying, nor in ghosts and spirits’ harm, but in losing the Dao and perishing of myself. Be steadfast! Be diligent! Your merit extends to things, your toil reaches the people, your goodness moves heaven—yet you have not heard of the supreme Dao.

Long ago, at the Gate of Purple Clarity, I received the writ and treasured it through diligence. My master, the Three Primordials Lord of the Dao, said: 'The *Inner Scripture of the Supreme Perfected* is the treasure of the Heavenly Perfected. Sealed in a golden terrace, worn at the Grand Tenuity, it ascends in cloud chariots, guarded by spirit tigers at the gates. Robes are shaken in the jasper chambers, feasts held in the rare groves. The Celestial Duke is ever summoned; to the right, White Mountain is nestled, gazing down upon the great void. Drifting upon the Celestial Ford, one rides clouds and spurs dragons; wandering these famed mountains, one is visited by heaven’s people in their chambers. Ten thousand spirits offer protection, a thousand essences await welcome. In motion, one has the Eight Radiance Jade Chariot; in stillness, one feasts within the Golden Terrace—this is also called the wondrous scripture of the *Jade Girdle and Golden Pendants of the Most High*.'

If you would cross vast seas without a chariot’s wheels, traverse flying sands without a cloud carriage, tread muddy paths without a conveyance, or ford waves without a vessel, then on land you would be hindered by distant barriers, and on water you would fear drifting and sinking. How, then, could you guide the hundred valleys and dredge the ten thousand streams? How perilous and distant this seems!

The Most High, moved by your utmost devotion, will bestow upon you the *True Writ of the Numinous Treasure*. On land, it will command tigers and leopards; on water, it will subdue dragons. It will sever the roots of evil, restrain the fierce, and bring your work to completion. This lies in the Yang Brightness of heaven."

She then commanded her attendant, Ling Ronghua, to bring forth the cinnabar jade coffer, open the precious writ of the Upper Clarity, and present it to Yu. She further ordered the spirits Kuangzhang, Giant Spirit, and others to assist Yu in slaying those who harmed the people and could not be restrained by human strength—executing Fangfeng at Kuaiji, and smelting the Huai River’s god Wuzhiqi at Tortoise Mountain—all by their power.

Later, Yu entered the Yang Brightness Grotto-Heaven and became the Perfected of the Purple Court, concealing the true writ in the ruins of Mount Bao. According to the *Jade Tube of Mount Taixiu*, the Grotto-Heaven of the Law and Music is where the Most High bestowed the *True Writ of the Numinous Treasure* upon Yu, and its traces remain there. Today, the Grotto-Heaven of Linwu in Dongting Mountain within Lake Tai—also called Mount Bao—is where Yu concealed the true writ. In the twelfth year of King Helü of Wu, in the first month, he ascended Mount Bao and commanded the recluse, the Venerable Dragon Might, to enter the mountain’s grotto and explore its depths. There, he saw golden walls and jasper chambers, inscribed: *Hall of the Grand Yin, Separate Palace of the Queen of Heaven*. Upon a jade table within the chamber, he found a plain silk scroll of one hundred seventy-four characters, which he brought back.

King Helü, unable to recognize the script, dispatched an envoy to inquire of Confucius, saying: "A red crow delivered a writ to the king." Confucius replied: "Once, while I traveled by the Western Sea, I heard a children’s rhyme that went: *The King of Wu goes forth to view the trembling lake, / Dragon Might the Recluse, by name, dwells in seclusion. / North he climbs Mount Bao, enters the numinous ruins, / Then to Dongting he goes, to steal Yu’s writ in secret. / Heaven’s great writ must not be unfurled— / This text has long transmitted six hundred springs. / Now forced to take it out, the kingdom’s halls shall fall.* I interpret the rhyme to mean that Dragon Might the Venerable found it in the grotto, brought forth by the red crow—beyond this, I know nothing." The King of Wu, struck with fear, returned the writ.

During the reign of Tang of Shang, the writ descended upon Mount Qian, where he was called Master Bestowed-by-Then, expounded the *Scripture of Longevity*, and taught the Way of reverence and compassion. Today, the site of the Dragon Hall before the Heavenly Blessing Palace on Mount Qian marks the very place.

Though the Old Master had manifested across the ages, no trace of his birth had yet appeared. Wishing to harmonize his light with the dust to establish the teachings of the world, he first commanded the Mysterious Wondrous Jade Maiden to descend and become the daughter of the Yin family of Tianshui, named Yishou, who married the immortal Li Lingfei. The Mysterious Wondrous Jade Maiden was none other than the Supreme Primordial Sovereign. Lingfei was a descendant of Gaoyao, and by the time of Shang, father and son had inherited the Way of cultivating life. His father, Qingbin, lived over a hundred years yet retained a youthful countenance, wandering the Five Peaks and all the mountains. One day, a cloud and dragon descended to welcome him, and he ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Inspired by his father’s ascension, Lingfei refined the Great Dao with diligence and also lived over a hundred years. He ascended to heaven before the Old Master’s birth.

In the seventeenth year of King Yangjia’s reign, the tenth sovereign of the eighteenth Shang dynasty, in the gengshen year, the Old Master divided his spirit from the Great Clarity’s immortal realm, riding the essence of the sun and driving nine dragons, transforming into a five-colored flowing pearl to descend. At that time, Lady Yin was napping when she dreamed the sky opened for several yards, and a host of immortals bore forth the sun. After a long while, she saw the sun gradually shrink, falling from the heavens as a five-colored pearl the size of a pellet. She cupped it in her hands and swallowed it, awakening to find herself with child. Today, the Meteor Altar at the Heavenly Tranquility Palace in Bozhou marks the spot. From then on, her complexion grew ever more youthful, her spirit serene and composed. The chamber where she dwelt harmonized the six qi, winter knew no biting cold, summer no scorching heat. Auspicious light illuminated the room, no evil could assail her, and eighty-one years passed without her noticing their length.

In the ninth year of King Wuding’s reign, the twenty-second sovereign of Shang, in the gengchen year, on the fifteenth day of the second month (the first month of the lunar year), at the hour of mao, the Sacred Mother, grasping a branch of the plum tree, suddenly gave birth from beneath her left armpit. The immortal traditions all record that he remained in the womb for eighty-one years, though the *Inner Tradition* states: "The Sovereign, Preceptor of the High Emperor, was moved by the essence of the sun entering her mouth and conceived. After seventy-two years, he emerged from her left armpit." Though the two accounts differ, there is reason: Yu Xuan’s *Record of the Northern Frontier* states: "Laozi remained in the womb for seventy-two years, and the signs appeared for nine years"—which also totals eighty-one years.

At that moment, the sun’s radiance doubled in brilliance, auspicious clouds shaded the courtyard, ten thousand cranes soared in the sky, and the Nine Heavens rejoiced. Jade maidens knelt to receive him, and nine dragons offered water to bathe his sacred form. The pool from which the dragons emerged became the Nine Wells. According to Fu Tao’s *Record of the Northern Expedition* from the Han: "Within the Old Master’s temple are nine wells, all interconnected, so that drawing water from one causes all nine to stir."

At his birth, he took nine steps, with a lotus blossoming at each step. Pointing to the plum tree, he said: "This shall be my surname." The Old Master had once, in kalpas past, been born in the Li Valley of the Northern Mysterious Jade Kingdom, and thus took the surname Li. Now, born again into the Li family beneath a plum tree, he knew his surname at birth and pointed it out to others—not that he had only now adopted Li as his surname.

His posthumous name was Cong, for the Old Master’s ears rose above his neck, thick and firm, with hanging pearls and three openings within—a countenance of a celestial spirit, unlike that of ordinary men, hence the name. Some write it as *Xiu*, as the characters were interchangeable in Han script. Thus, the *Records of the Grand Historian* also writes it as *Taishi Xiu*, just as *Xiu* in Xiuzhou means "ear." Some write it as *Dan*, a corruption of the sound. Others claim he stuck out his tongue and spoke, hence the name *Ci*—but this is merely a mishearing.

His name was Er, and his courtesy name Boyang. Some call him Boyang Fu, a term of reverence for the aged. Another name was Ya, courtesy name Bozong; another, Zhi, courtesy name Boguang; another, Shi, courtesy name Menggong; another, Chong, courtesy name Ziwen; another, Ding, courtesy name Yuanyang; another, Yuan, courtesy name Boshi; another, Xian, courtesy name Yuansheng; another, De, courtesy name Bowen. According to the *Scripture of the Nine Palaces*, the *Scripture of the Three and Five*, and the *Scripture of the Primordial Epochs*, each person has their own trials and encounters. If one changes their name to align with the sounds of the primordial brilliance, they may extend their years and avoid calamity. Many Daoists of the present age do likewise. The *Arrayed Records* also states: "The Master Li changes his name and courtesy name every two thousand five hundred years." The Old Master remained in the world the longest, hence his many names and courtesy names. He once told Yin Xi: "My surnames and names stretch endlessly across kalpas—none can record them all."

On the ninth day after his birth, the Old Master’s body underwent nine transformations, each time clad in celestial crowns and celestial robes that naturally draped his form. He bore the seventy-two marks and eighty-one excellences. The seventy-two marks included: a head round as heaven, a face radiant as the sun, a coiled rhinoceros horn rising from his brow, a jade bowl’s arch crowning his head. His hair was white as a crane’s, over seven feet long. His eyebrows resembled the Northern Dipper, their color emerald green. His whiskers were tiger-like, his beard dragon-like, white and knotted like silk. His ears bore hanging pearls, with three openings within, rising level with his crown, thick and firm. His eyes were like the Yellow River and the sun and moon, with square pupils and green veins.

The Old Master’s nose bore twin pillars, its bridge high and prominent. His mouth was square as the sea, his lips red as cinnabar. His beard held a purple hue, its fragrance like orchid. His teeth were arrayed like cowrie shells, firm as silver, thirty-two in number, evenly aligned above and below. His tongue was long and broad, patterned like brocade, its sound like jade, its resonance like gold. His forehead rose high and full, his jaw square as a carpenter’s square. His temples bore the sun’s angle and the moon’s depth, his countenance golden, his bearing jadelike, his dragon visage solemn, his phoenix gaze serene. His brow shone with radiant signs, three yang energies ascending. His celestial court was level and vast, his golden coffer brimming. His cheeks bore white marks, his jowls jade-like orbs. His neck had three folds, white as a crane’s, held proudly aloft. His arms hung past his knees, his hands bore ten lines, his fingers long and slender, each inscribed with trigrams. His nails were jade-like, his body bore green down, his chest had recumbent bones, his back the River Chariot. His navel was over an inch deep, his belly soft as silk. His heart bore brocade patterns, his abdomen a dark mark, his eyes wheel-like veins, his feet trod the two fields, his toes held heaven and earth. His height was twelve feet, his body exuded fragrance. His face was square and lustrous, its three divisions aligned. His form was adamantine, his appearance crystalline, his gait like a tiger’s stride, his movement like a dragon’s rush. Such were his marks.

To his left stood the Azure Dragon, to his right the White Tiger; before him the Vermilion Bird led, behind him the Dark Warrior followed. His head was shaded by purple clouds, his feet trod lotus blossoms, his neck bore twin luminaries, five lights shone like the sun. His body held the celestial sphere, his movements illuminated the nine heavens. Possessing the full countenance of an immortal, his radiance and hue were wondrously fair. These eighty-one traits were called his excellences.

He gestated for eighty-one years, fulfilling the supreme yang’s nine-by-nine reckoning. Born with hoary hair, he was thus named the Old Master. The ancients styled their teachers as *zi*, and *zi* was also a general term for men, hence he was called *Laozi*. Another account holds that *lao* denotes longevity, while *zi* signifies youth, thus he was called Old, yet also newly born, hence *zi*. Moreover, his white-haired appearance united the two principles, so he was styled *Laozi*.

Tang scholar Yin Wencao observed: *Laozi* is the embodied Dao. Though its manifestations differ inwardly and outwardly, they arise from its responsive nature, hence its forms may vary. Its inner appellations include the Supreme One, True One, Mysterious One, August One, Primordial One, Imperial One, Grand One, Celestial One, and Orthodox One. The Supreme One is wondrous and without substance; the True One is pure and profoundly still; the Mysterious One is spiritually unobstructed; the August One is supremely luminous; the Primordial One is the chief of the myriad ways; the Imperial One is the sacred lord of the hundred spirits; the Grand One is the great sovereign of long life; the Celestial One is the true ruler of yin; the Orthodox One is the dharma-king of radiant majesty. These nine meanings, though differently named, spring from the same source. Having grasped the mysterious mechanism, they are all called *One*.

Its outer appellations include Yuhua, Dacheng, Guangshou, Guangcheng, Suiying, Chijing, Lutu, Wucheng, Yinshou, Zhenxing, Xize, Xieyi, Yucheng, Jingcheng, and Guoshu, among others. *Yuhua* denotes the Dao’s flourishing transformation; *Dacheng* its universally bestowed mysterious virtue; *Guangshou* its joyful spirit and sustaining abundance; *Guangcheng* its vast life-nurturing merit; *Suiying* its unimpeded responsiveness; *Chijing* its revered numinous cinnabar; *Lutu* its hidden accord with talismanic destiny; *Wucheng* its fulfillment of manifold achievements; *Yinshou* its impartiality and robust health; *Zhenxing* its pure sincerity; *Xize* its benevolent and methodical kindness; *Xieyi* its transformative moral influence; *Yucheng* its harmonious nurturing; *Jingcheng* its enduring and complete orthodox teachings; *Guoshu* its purging of generations of encumbrances. These names arose in accord with his teachings, and as he descended to become the state’s preceptor, they all bore the title *zi*.

He dwelt on the northern bank of the Wo River in Quren Village, Lai Township, Ku County, in the state of Song. According to the *Records of the Historian*, he was a native of Quren Village, Lai Township, Ku County in Chu. Liu Xiang’s *Biographies of Exemplary Women* calls him a man of Chen, while Huangfu Mi’s *Biographies of Lofty Scholars* says he was a minister of Chu who resided on the northern bank of the Wo River. Some say he was from Qiao, and *Lai* may also be written as *Li*. The discrepancies in these accounts stem from the fact that the region was the ancient domain of Fuxi, beside Wanqiu, where Emperor Zhuanxu also established his capital. According to the *Tribute of Yu*, it fell within the jurisdiction of Yingzhou. King Tang of Shang later made it his capital, calling it Bo Shang. When King Wu of Zhou conquered Shang, he enfeoffed the descendants of Shun, naming them Yu, at Wanqiu, thus founding the state of Chen. Xiong Yi’s initial enfeoffment connected his lands to Chen, forming the state of Chu. In the eleventh year of Duke Xuan of Lu, Chu destroyed Chen, but Shen Shu Shi admonished the king, and Chen was restored. In the eighth year of Duke Zhao, Chu again destroyed Chen and made it a county. By the thirteenth year, Chu had re-enfeoffed Chen, but in the seventeenth year of Duke Xiang, Chen was finally annihilated by Chu and became part of its territory. When King Qingxiang of Chu was defeated by Qin, he lost his armies and the capital of Ying, relocating to Chen. After Qin conquered Chu, Chen became part of Yingchuan Commandery. Under the Han, it became the Principality of Huaiyang, with its seat at Chen, governing nine counties, including Ku, Xiang, Qiao, and Li, all under its jurisdiction. During the reign of Emperor Zhang of the Later Han, it was renamed Chen Commandery, and today it is known as Bo Prefecture.

Ku County: Xu Guang notes that during the Jin dynasty, it was called Guyang, which was abolished under the Qi, then reestablished by the Sui. The former city of Guyang in eastern Ku County was the ancient city of Li Township. Northeast of the county lies the ancient city of Xiang, which was Xiang County in Chu. By the end of the Warring States period, Xiang was merged into Ku. In the first year of the Qianfeng era of the Tang, it was renamed Zhenyuan County, and today it is known as Weizhen County. To the north flows the Wo River, and forty *li* to the east stands the ancient city of Qiao, which was Qiao County in the Han.

The elders thus variously refer to Laozi as a man of Chen, Chu, Xiang, or Qiao. Li Township is none other than Lai Township. The *Zuo Tradition* states: "King Cheng of Chu attacked Xu, and the Qin army attacked Li to rescue it." Du Yu notes: "Li was an ally of Chu." The *Treatise on Geography* records: "It belonged to Huaiyang; Wang Mang renamed it Lai Ling." Pei Songzhi’s *Record of the Northern Campaign*, Li Daoyuan’s *Classic of Waterways*, and Kuo Xi’s *Illustrated Gazetteer of the Thirteen Provinces* all write it as *Lai*, but as the place lies near the Wo River, the common folk altered it to *Lai* (濑).

When the Holy Mother had given birth to the Dao-body and was about to return to the heavenly palace to resume the position of Primordial Lord, wishing to demonstrate to the world the Way of master-disciple transmission, she addressed Lord Lao: "Humans are born within Heaven and Earth, some pure, some turbid. Those of pure essence are wise, kind, and benevolent; those of turbid qi are foolish, cruel, and violent. The enlightened cultivate to perfect their nature; the benighted indulge their desires to harm their lives. Nature is the source of the body; life is the root of existence. Thus, those who cultivate and study refine their bodies with the Nine Elixirs, untie the knots of the Five Spirits, guide their qi back to its origin, and sever the roots at the Three Passes. After nine refinements and ten transformations, with a hundred joints illuminated and the knots of the womb dissolved, they come to know their original truth and ascend as perfected immortals. Without cultivation, demonic forces invade the body, a hundred illnesses arise, and death comes before life’s span is complete—such suffering is beyond words.

Immortality arises from the heart’s study; sincerity of heart brings immortality to fruition. The Dao values inner seeking; inner secrecy draws the Dao near. One who attains stillness to study the true, accumulates emptiness to commune with spirits, draws ever closer to immortality. But if the heart races and the spirit labors, the body grows restless and unfocused, movement and stillness dissipate essence, and the ears and eyes grow distracted—then, though one may accumulate years in vain pursuit of the Dao, the Dao only recedes further. Our sojourn in Heaven and Earth is but a fleeting moment. If one can gather qi to nourish the spirit, endure hardship to concentrate the true, and persist long enough, one will attain the Dao and dwell with Heaven and Earth in the Great Void. One who penetrates emptiness and embodies non-being becomes invisible to sight, inaudible to hearing, and merges with the Dao in truth."

Lord Lao said: "Now that I wander the dusty world, can I yet attain perpetual life and deliver others in accordance with the times?"

The Primordial Lord replied: "I possess secret treasures, transmitted only to the sagely. Those who cultivate them may attain everlasting life."

Lord Lao said: "I wish to hear of their attainment."

The Primordial Lord said: "The supreme Dao is profound and abstruse, beyond knowing—neither being nor non-being, neither sound nor form. It cannot be seen, nor grasped. It encompasses Heaven and Earth, yet the perfected person remains boundless. It lies within all bodies, yet none can fathom it. To know its principles is to possess dark virtue."

Lord Lao said: "Does the Dao have means to attain it?"

The Primordial Lord replied: "The Dao is the ultimate truth of emptiness and penetration; the arts are the wondrous techniques of transformation. The Dao employs the arts to aid humanity; humans, through cultivation, unite with the Dao—thus transformations become inexhaustible. The essence of the Dao lies in non-action and spontaneity; the secrecy of the arts lies in talismans and elixirs. Talismans are the numinous script of the Three Luminaries, Heaven’s pledge of faith. Elixirs are the blossoms of the Five Phases, the essence of Earth. Qi is the harmony of yin and yang, the spirit of the myriad things. Though one may master a single practice, one must ultimately rely on talismans, elixirs, and the Dao to complete the work. These are my secret treasures. If you can master them all, you may attain everlasting life and deliver countless beings."

Lord Lao said: "The body is the vessel for attaining the Dao; qi is the root of life’s destiny. When the root is severed, life ends; when the vessel decays, the Dao departs. Now that I wish to cultivate, how may I secure life and exalt the Dao?"

The Primordial Lord replied: "Humans are endowed with a form of flesh and bone, like the unfired clay of a potter. Exposed to wood and fire, it quickly decays; without refinement, life is brief—such is the principle. Even one whose virtue surpasses all beings, whose spirit communes with the Great Mystery, may yet fear the inevitability of old age and death. Why? Because transformation dictates it; it cannot be otherwise. Only elixirs can refine the body; only talismans can summon the spirits. When spirits return, the heart is enlightened and nature at ease; when the body is firm, qi is stabilized and life preserved. Then, one transforms qi into essence, penetrates formlessness, soars through the void, and exists in spontaneity—thus attaining everlasting life. Though the perfected may encounter cataclysm, with Heaven and Earth collapsing, no disaster can reach them. This is why talismans and elixirs are prized."

Lord Lao said: "Those who ingest the divine elixir and attain longevity—is it due to the protection of spirits, or the power of the elixir?"

The Primordial Lord replied: "The merit of longevity lies in the elixir; the elixir’s completion lies in the spirits. Thus, when compounding the elixir, one must rectify the heart and avoid transgressions, so that the numinous spirits may protect it, and the elixir’s creation be assured. When the divine elixir enters the mouth, life becomes boundless. Heaven and Earth witness clearly: the Dao returns to the benevolent. The teeming masses, mere walking corpses, do not believe in the attainability of longevity, dismissing it as empty falsehood. From dawn to dusk, they engage only in acts that hasten death—how can Heaven force life upon them? Indulging desires to the utmost, they perish in an instant. A thousand pieces of gold spent on funerals—what benefit is there? When the divine elixir and the Dao are perfected, one not only attains longevity but may also become a treasure of the age. Those who know this Dao—what need have they for the world? If a ruler offered his kingdom in exchange for my formula, I would not transmit it to the unworthy."

Lord Lao bowed deeply and said: "I wish to hear its essentials."

The Primordial Lord then raised her gaze to the heavens and let out a long whistle. Suddenly, a purple cloud like a canopy descended from the sky, within which five-colored vapors radiated light in all directions. The immortal Juanzi attended her. The Primordial Lord produced the *Divine Charts*, the *Precious Chapters of Transformations*, the methods of *Returning the Elixir*, *Subduing Fire*, *Water Mercury*, and *Golden Liquor*—seventy-two scrolls in all—and bestowed them upon Lord Lao.

The text states:

For the *Xuanbai* to give birth to the *Golden Duke*, the *Great Yang Flowing Pearl* enters the *Flowery Pool* with five taels of *Wenjiang*, *Luochi* salt, and *White Snow*, forming the *Male-Female Five Talismans* and *Nine Elixirs*, which enable one to ascend as a perfected immortal. The Dao lies herein, yet people remain unaware.

The *Five Talismans* are: first, *Xuanbai*; second, *Essence of Gold*; third, *Flying Talisman*; fourth, *Golden Florescence*; fifth, *Essence of the Three-Five Azure Dragon*.

The *Nine Elixirs* are: first, *White Snow Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; second, *Male-Female Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; third, *Golden Liquor Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; fourth, *White Florescence Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; fifth, *Elixir Florescence Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; sixth, *Five-Color Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; seventh, *Mud Mercury Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; eighth, *Essence of Gold Ninefold Cyclically Transformed Elixir*; ninth, *Nine Cauldrons Radiant Ultimate Elixir*. Of these nine elixirs, obtaining even one allows one to attain longevity—there is no need for hasty practice.

The Dao of the divine elixir undergoes three transformations and five cyclical turns, culminating at nine. Those who ingest it become equal to me.

The Dao of immortality does not lie in sacrifices or prayers to spirits, nor in *daoyin* or bending and stretching, nor in recitations or verbose speech, nor in exhausting oneself through intense contemplation. The essence of longevity lies in the divine elixir. Knowing it is exceedingly easy, but achieving it is profoundly difficult—only those who can practice it may endure.

This Dao, once established, grants transcendence. Even I did not attain it naturally but through study.

Lord Lao bowed again, pressing his forehead to the ground, and said: "I have received your gracious instruction on the Nine Elixirs. Yet I have heard that those who seek transcendence without obtaining the *Golden Liquor* labor in vain. I humbly request you reveal its essentials."

The Primordial Lord said: "Your question is most profound. The Nine Elixirs and the *Golden Liquor* are both paths to ascending to heaven, yet among the Nine Elixirs, the *Golden Liquor* is supreme. The reason is this: those who ingest the Nine Elixirs may be received by immortal officials with clouds and dragons after three years, two years, one year, half a year, a hundred days, sixty days, thirty days, ten days, or even three days. But those who ingest the *Golden Liquor* transform upon tasting it—their bodies take on the hue of purple gold, and they instantly grow feathers and wings, ascending to become immortal officials.

To ingest it, one must first observe a prolonged fast and abstain from grains for a full year before partaking. Unless one bears the *Mysterious Thread* within or possesses the name of the deathless, one shall never hear of the Dao of the Nine Elixirs and *Golden Liquor*.

Its method follows the prior process of compounding the golden elixir into a liquid—thus is the Dao complete. This is my secret treasure, containing one thousand two hundred formulas.

Five kalpas ago, before the dust of worlds and the formation of heaven and earth, I received it from the *Primordial Celestial Worthy*. Practicing it, I attained the rank of *Supreme Primordial Lord*. In the past, I transmitted it to the *Perfected Great Sage, Heavenly Emperor, Supreme Lord*, and the *Celestial Lords of Grand Tenuity and Grand Unity*. Now I bestow it upon you—strive diligently."

Lord Lao received the transmission and again inquired: "The teeming multitudes, countless as the grasses, are all bound for the realm of death. If this Dao were broadly bestowed to deliver them, how might that be?"

The Primordial Lord said: "The careless masses, lost in glory and desire, perish like stones cast into rivers—gone, never to return. Their plight is deeply lamentable. Yet the Dao does not operate in vain; it must be entrusted to the true person. This Dao is lofty and wondrous, concealed within the Nine Mysteries, its transmission as rare as the jade terraces of the clouds—once in ten thousand years. It is recorded in the *Mysterious Registers* and *Jade Names*. Unless one is destined by former lives, with the bone and countenance of an immortal, one shall not hear of it. Foolish people err frequently—how could one defy celestial law by recklessly transmitting it?

My teachings are now complete. I shall depart. The *Celestial Harmony Primordial Lord* shall speak with you further."

As her words concluded, a thousand chariots and ten thousand riders, the perfected immortals of the Five Thearchs, surrounded her eight-jewel jade carriage to escort her as she ascended to heaven.

The methods of the *Great Clarity Divine Elixir Scripture* originate from the *Supreme Primordial Lord*. Her divine powers harmonize yin and yang, command wind and rain, advance and retreat the five stars, regulate cold and heat, and harness nine dragons and twelve white tigers. All immortals under heaven revere and submit to her. The life and death of humanity are governed by her—it is said that ingesting the elixir brings this about.

Lord Lao then wandered through mountains and marshes in search of refining the divine elixir. Passing through *Lao Mountain*, he indeed encountered the *Grand Unity Primordial Lord* riding a five-colored striped qilin, attended by ten officials. Lord Lao followed her to inquire of the Dao. The Primordial Lord said: "The essence of the Dao lies in the *Cyclically Transformed Elixir* and *Golden Liquor*." Thereupon, she transmitted the secret formulas.

In later years, on *Li Mountain*, he again met the *Grand Unity Primordial Lord* and expressed gratitude for the divine elixir’s method. The Primordial Lord said: "You are the honored one among all immortals, the master of the ten thousand Dao. The mysterious and numinous secret arts are inherently part of your nature—why should you humble yourself with thanks?"

Lord Lao said: "The common people, ignorant and dying in great numbers, move my heart to tears of blood. Witnessing this sorrow, I wish to provide them with the divine medicine so that all may attain longevity. Would this be permissible?"

The Primordial Lord said: "It cannot be. The Dao of life is most weighty—it must be entrusted to the greatly virtuous, the filial, the sincere, and the faithful. Heaven generates the ten thousand things, some good, some evil. The good deserve to live; the evil deserve to be eradicated. It is not fitting to dispense the medicine so that all may live indefinitely. You already know this—do not transmit it lightly."

The *Sacred Record* praises:

Lord Lao embodies the quiescence of the Great Void,
His virtue draws from the wondrous existence.
He perfected the Dao through kalpas uncounted,
Shaping transformations across ten thousand heavens.
Spontaneous, not through accumulated study,
He descended through nine sovereigns to establish teachings,
Entrusted through successive emperors as their master.
Alone in the Purple Clarity,
Who could precede him?
Now he seeks the *Holy Mother* to entrust his learning,
Follows the *Primordial Lord* to inquire of the Dao,
Desiring to manifest the true Dao’s profundity—
Without study, it cannot be attained.
The lofty and marvelous path of immortals,
Through cultivation, may be grasped.
Thus he honors the master and esteems virtue,
Abides in the feminine and guards the yielding,
Exemplifying the numinous model of the supreme sage,
Bequeathing a wondrous pattern for future ages.
Adapting to circumstances, he teaches,
Rewarding the good and chastising the deluded.
*Wenshi* and *Baopu* were lost before,
Now they are praised in turn thereafter.

Lord Lao, knowing the Dao of immortals must rely on cultivation, wished to bequeath its methods to encourage later generations. Thus, he preserved authenticity and embraced the One, refined cinnabar to restore the primordial, and only then could he ride the void and ascend the heavens, moving between existence and non-existence at will, his whereabouts unfathomable to mortals. One day, he mounted a white deer, trod upon the cypress of the courtyard, and ascended to heaven.

*The Second Scroll of the Sacred Records of the Primordial Sage* concludes here.

---

In the twenty-first year of King Zhou of Shang, the *dingmao* year, on the twelfth day of the first month, the *bingwu* day, Lord Lao, witnessing King Zhou’s tyranny and the people’s suffering, saw that King Wen of Zhou, as the Western Earl, possessed a benevolent heart and would aid the virtuous to deliver the people. He then divided his black-transformed body, rode the chariot of the flying terrace, and descended south of Mount Qi. Eighteen *li* northeast of present-day Fengxiang Prefecture stands the Temple of the Sage’s Advent, the very site of his descent, where the Terrace of the Western Earl’s Reception of the Dao and its remnants remain.

He styled himself *Xieyizi* and expounded the *Scripture of Crimson Essence*, teaching people the Dao of benevolence and trust. The Western Earl, having heard of him, summoned him as the Keeper of Archives. When King Wu overthrew Shang and ascended the throne, he was transferred to the position of Historian Beneath the Pillars and renamed *Yuchengzi*, composing the *Scripture of Wandering Play*. During the reign of King Cheng, he was called *Jingchengzi* and expounded the *Scripture of Vast Transformation*. Later, he transmitted the Dao to the Duke of Zhou and retired to a life of leisure. He journeyed to the westernmost lands of Da Qin and Zhuqian, styling himself *Ancient Master*. In the time of King Kang, he returned to Zhou, called himself *Guoshuzi*, and again served as Historian Beneath the Pillars.

According to the *Genealogical Records of the Ages*: in Shang, he was the Keeper of Archives; in Zhou, the Historian Beneath the Pillars. This is because King Wen, though a lord, still served Shang. Lord Lao, as a hidden dragon in a humble position, nurtured the Zhou’s Dao. By the time of King Zhao, Lord Lao knew the Zhou house would decline, so he transmitted the *Hidden Writings of the Eight Heavens* to the king, bidding him safeguard the nation’s destiny. King Zhao, believing himself to dwell in the course of nature, gradually relaxed his vigilance, indulging in tours and neglecting celestial patterns. Lord Lao, foreseeing the king would not meet a proper end, withdrew the celestial writings and returned to his native place.

Though Lord Lao dwelt in Zhou for a long time, he always prioritized concealment and anonymity, outwardly harmonizing with the world while inwardly maintaining purity and emptiness, never allowing fame or rank to become prominent. The Later Han scholar Bian Shao composed the *Stele Inscription of Lord Lao’s Temple*, stating: "Lord Lao delighted in namelessness, guarded against virtue, shunned high office, and found peace in lowly positions." Its inscription reads:

> Veiled in primordial virtue,
> Embracing emptiness and guarding purity,
> Delighting in lowly station,
> Unmoved by power or emolument.
> Vast and boundless through the ages,
> None knew his true nature.
> In non-action alone,
> The great transformation of Zhou was perfected.
> Advancing and retreating without constancy,
> Interweaving constancy with change,
> Feigning foolishness with wisdom,
> Abundant yet never full.

From this inscription, the sage’s comings and goings may be discerned.

Lord Lao, wishing to enlighten the Western Regions, in the twenty-third year of King Zhao of Zhou, the *guichou* year—the first year of the Heaven of No Limit—on the *renwu* day of the fifth month, harnessed a chariot drawn by a blue ox, its canopy made of thin planks, with Xu Jia as his driver, and set out to cross the Han Pass westward. The Sui scholar Xue Daoheng composed the *Inscription for the Temple Courtyard*, saying:

> Though the Five Peaks are small,
> The Nine Provinces are narrow.
> I shall soar high and wander afar,
> Transcending the distant.
> The blue ox is harnessed,
> The purple radiance floats ahead.
> The gates of mystery open wide,
> The divine transformation flows unseen.

This speaks of his intent to transform the Western Regions. The *Records of the Western Expedition* state: "He crossed the Hangu Pass." Li Kang’s *Inscription for the Hangu Pass* says: "Yin Xi invited Lord Lao to stay, composing two scrolls before concealing them." *Mystic Serenity* states: "He exited the Long Pass westward." Cui Hao says: "Xi served as the Chief of the San Pass." The *Illustrated Classic of Qin* records a city of Yin Xi beside the San Pass, suggesting that during the reign of King Kang of Zhou, Xi may have served as the Chief of the San Pass. Since historical records merely mention "crossing the pass" without specifying the location, later scholars have offered various interpretations, much like the multiple sites of Chao and Xu’s ox-drinking or Bo Yi and Shu Qi’s Mount Shouyang. However, the Long Pass and San Pass both lie in Qin and Long, while the Hangu Pass is located twelve *li* south of Taolin County in Shanzhou, where the ruins of the old pass remain. According to the *Illustrated Classic* and oral traditions, all confirm that the pass Lord Lao crossed was indeed the old Hangu Pass. In ancient times, King Wu of Zhou resided in Haojing, and though King Cheng built Luoyi, he still returned to Western Zhou. Only when King Ping moved east to Luoyi did the capital shift. The *Inner Records of Louguan* state that King Kang appointed Xi as a grandee, while the *Inner Records of Yin Xi* say Xi served as the Pass Chief. If Xi was the Chief of the San Pass during King Kang’s reign, and Lord Lao met him upon exiting the pass during King Zhao’s time, the chronology aligns smoothly. However, the *Inner Records* state that upon exiting the pass, he arrived at Xi’s residence, which was in Zhengwu County west of the Hangu Pass—had he truly exited the pass, Xi’s residence would not have been there. The Hangu Pass lies east of Haojing, and Lord Lao was further east of the Hangu Pass. The *Immortal Duke* interpreted his westward journey as "departing Zhou," hence "crossing west." If one claims Lord Lao met Confucius before exiting the pass, then the crossing would have occurred after the eastern relocation, by which time the lands of the Long and San Passes were already under Qin’s control, making it impossible to refer to the Hangu Pass as the San or Long Pass.

According to the *Genealogical Records of the Ages*, Qin’s distant ancestor Feizi first dwelled in Taiqiu, mingling with the Rong tribes in the lands of present-day Qizhou.

When King Xiao of Zhou dispatched Feizi to raise horses between the Qi and Wei Rivers, his merit earned him an initial grant of land at Qin—specifically, Qinting in Longxi. During the reign of King Li, the Western Rong rebelled against Zhou, and Qin Zhong was sent to suppress them, whereupon he was rewarded with the lands of Taiqiu. Unable to hold them, Qin Zhong returned to Qinting. Later, when Duke Xiang escorted King Ping in his eastern relocation, the king bestowed upon him the lands west of Qi. Qin then hunted eastward to the region between the Ji and Wei Rivers, and reflecting on the origins of his ancestors’ enfeoffment, divined to settle east of Long.

Duke Mu defeated Jin at Hanyuan, extending his borders eastward to the Yellow River. Duke Xian gradually moved east and established his capital at Liyang, while Duke Xiao developed Jique, calling it Xianyang. Tracing this lineage, Qin first dwelled in Qin west of Long, then moved east to the region between the Qi and Wei Rivers. Thus, in Confucius’s time, Long and San Passes lay at the heart of Qin’s territory—why would a pass have been established there?

The reason San Pass bears the city of Yin Xi is that during King Nan’s reign, Lord Lao and the Perfected Master Yin were ascending Kunlun and passed through this route. King Zhao of Qin ordered a city built there to mark the occasion, hence the Wayfarer’s Post of Lord Lao and the City of Yin Xi. Later scholars, seeing Yin Xi’s city at this site, mistakenly assumed San Pass was where he held office. But since the Zhou pass already stood there, one cannot claim that Lord Lao crossed the pass only to reach Yin’s residence.

Verifying the facts, the place where Yin Xi served as magistrate and where Lord Lao encountered him must be fixed at the Hangu Pass. In the first year of the Tianbao era of the Tang, the Heavenly Treasure Numinous Talisman hidden by Lord Lao was unearthed there, prompting an imperial edict to rename Taolin County as Lingbao County and establish the Lingfu Abbey on the site. Emperor Minghuang personally paid homage and composed an inscribed stele erected within the abbey. Later, Emperor Zhenzong of the Song also composed an *Inscription on Crossing the Pass*, carved into stone.

Initially, Yin Xi, aspiring to the Dao and its safeguarding, cherished from youth the *Three Tombs* and *Eight Canons*, excelling in celestial portents and astrological calculations. He built a thatched tower to gaze upon the heavens, contemplating the mysterious Dao, and was thus called the Tower Observer. There, he beheld a purple aura advancing westward from the east, a celestial omen signaling that a sage would cross the pass. He sought appointment as magistrate of the Hangu Pass, and the king consented.

Yin Xi, styled Gongwen, served as a grandee during King Kang’s reign and was later summoned to the eastern court as a guest of honor. According to the *Inner Transmission*, his mother once dreamed in daylight of a crimson mist swirling around her, which she swallowed. When Yin Xi was born, a numinous radiance hovered beside him, and his eyes held the essence of the sun. From childhood, he loved the ancient texts and excelled in celestial divination and esoteric prognostication, observing and scrutinizing all things with penetrating clarity—even spirits could not conceal their truths from him. Broad-minded and heroic, he spurned conventional rites, humbling himself to aid others without seeking fame. His reputation spread far, and he was summoned as a guest of honor to the eastern court before being appointed magistrate of the pass.

Liu Xin’s *Seven Summaries* states: "The Master of the Pass, named Xi, was an officer of the pass." Both the *Records of the Grand Historian* and the *Biographies of the Immortals* refer to him as Yin Xi of the Pass, though it remains unclear whether "Yin" was his surname or his title as chief of the pass. As the *Zhuangzi* records, "Liezi inquired of the Master of the Pass," suggesting "Yin" denoted his rank. Today, those surnamed Yin likely descend from him, implying that Xi was the "Yin" (chief) of the pass, and the surname derived from his office.

However, the Mysterious Wondrous Jade Maiden was born into the Yin family of Tianshui, and both the *Inner Transmission* and *Biographies of the Divine Immortals* refer to him as "Magistrate Yin of the Pass." According to the *Rites of Zhou*, each pass was overseen by two senior officers and four middle officers, with two junior officers per pass handling its opening and closing. Thus, the junior officers managed the gates, while the *Sikuan* (Controller of the Pass) served as chief of all passes. The *Zhuangzi* mentions a "Gate Yin," the *Book of Rites* a "Village Yin," and the *Discourses of the States* states: "When a guest from a rival state arrives, the Gate Yin announces it." Thus, gate chiefs, village chiefs, and pass chiefs were all called "Yin." Since Xi was chief of the passes, he was able to welcome and await Lord Lao.

Yet the *Rites of Zhou* specifies that the pass chief held only the rank of senior officer, not grandee. Liu Xiang’s *Biographies of the Immortals* states: "Yin Xi of the Pass was a grandee of Zhou, skilled in esoteric learning." The *Inner Transmission* says he "excelled in celestial divination and esoteric prognostication." The *Rites of Zhou* lists the *Fengxiangshi* (Astronomer) and *Baozhangshi* (Astrologer), both middle officers, as responsible for celestial observations, while only the Grand Scribe, a junior grandee, oversaw celestial and climatic matters. Since Xi was skilled in astronomy and held the rank of grandee, he may have been Zhou’s Grand Scribe. While at court, he likely held a grandee’s post, and when dispatched, he assumed the duties of a senior officer. Knowing Lord Lao would arrive at the pass, he sought appointment as its magistrate to await him.

Today, in the Hearkening Immortal Hamlet of Shenlong Township, Zhengwu County, south of Mount Zhongnan, stand the thatched tower, solemn altar, and shrine halls—his former residence. According to the *Biographies of the Masters of Louguan* and the *Record of Louguan*, he served as a grandee during King Kang’s reign and, by King Zhao’s time, encountered Lord Lao and attained the Dao, thereafter accompanying the cloud chariot westward. Later, King Mu, revering the traces of his predecessors, built a shrine and expanded the abbey. Thus, its stele inscription reads: "Louguan was established by Yin Xi, Chief of the Pass and Grandee of Zhou during King Kang’s reign. He built a thatched tower, hence the name." It also states: "When King Zhao of Zhou made his western inspection and Duke Wen of Qin hunted eastward, both diverted their chariots to pay homage. Emperor Qin Shi Huang built a temple south of the tower, and Emperor Wu of Han established an abbey north of the observatory. Jin and Song offered inscribed tablets, which survive to this day, while the temple households of Qin and Han have continued unbroken."

From this, it is evident that Lord Lao crossed the pass during King Zhao’s reign. Some claim it occurred during the Eastern Zhou, but this stems from later scholars seeing that Lord Lao remained during Confucius’s time and assuming he had not yet departed. They failed to realize that after transforming the Hu, Lord Lao returned to the Central Plains, crossing the pass multiple times.

Yet as no events were recorded, they were omitted. Now, however, this marks the inception of Master Yin’s reception of the Dao, and moreover lays the foundation for the transformation of the Hu, hence it is particularly inscribed here to make it known.

When Yin reached the pass, he declared: “The yang number culminates in nine, the stellar lodges align with Metal, and the seasons and months jointly govern the law. Within ninety days, a great sage shall pass through the capital.” He first instructed the pass officer Sun Jing: “Should anyone of extraordinary appearance and unusual carriage arrive, do not permit them to pass.” Yin then prepared in advance, observing purification rites, and ordered the road swept for forty *li* on either side, with incense burned along the way to await the entry of the Celestial Perfected.

On the twelfth day of the seventh month, the *jiazi* day, an elder indeed arrived—hoary-haired and keen-eared, riding a white carriage drawn by a green ox. The pass officer said: “Your Excellency has given orders; we humbly request a brief delay.” He entered to inform Yin, who exclaimed: “The Dao has come at last! I shall behold the Sage.” Donning his court robes, he went forth to welcome him, prostrating himself and kowtowing, entreating: “May the Heavenly One deign to tarry awhile in his divine carriage.”

The Elder said: “I am but a poor, lowly old man dwelling east of the pass, with fields to the west. I am merely going to gather firewood—why detain me? Pray, let me pass.” This was the first trial.

Yin again bowed deeply, saying: “How could the Heavenly One be gathering firewood? For long have I heard that the Great Sage would come traveling west. Spare your divine carriage from exposure and fatigue; rest awhile.” The Elder replied: “I have heard that in Jambudvīpa there is an Ancient Master, skilled in entering non-action, eternally abiding in continuity. Thus I ascend my carriage and set forth, passing through your pass. Why do you persist in detaining me?” This was the second trial.

Yin continued: “Now beholding the Heavenly One’s sagely countenance, transcendent and peerless, I see the Supreme One of Heaven. How could the frontier barbarians be worth visiting? I beg you not to dissemble; grant me a moment of compassionate instruction.” During the reign of King Cheng, the Elder had once journeyed to Da Qin, Jambudvīpa, and other lands, styling himself the Ancient Master. The *Scripture of Western Ascent* states: “The Elder ascended west, instructing Jambudvīpa. There was an Ancient Master, skilled in entering non-action, without end or beginning, eternally abiding in continuity—thus he ascended and proceeded.” This refers to the Dao itself.

Master Chongxuan said: “Jambudvīpa lies west of Kunlun, north of the Red River, and is endowed with the qi of pure harmony, naturally bestowing longevity. Yet they had not grasped the essentials of the Dao’s origin or the practice of refinement, so the Elder once ascended to the western extremity to transform and guide them.” When the Elder first crossed Hangu Pass, wishing to test the Pass Master, he first called himself a poor, lowly old man. Yet the Pass Master, recognizing him as the Great Sage, insisted on detaining him. The Elder then claimed to be going to instruct Jambudvīpa, to ascend and proceed to the Ancient Master. But the Pass Master declared him the Supreme One of Heaven, asking how the frontier barbarians could be worth visiting—this is the purport of the first chapter of the *Scripture of Western Ascent*. Whenever a former sage transforms others, they first undergo trials. Having passed all three trials, the Elder then revealed the truth to him: “The Ancient Master is none other than myself. Having transformed Jambudvīpa, I shall now return my spirit to the Nameless, eternally abiding in continuity.” This is the purport of the final chapter of the *Scripture of Western Ascent*.

The Elder asked: “How did you recognize me?” This was the third trial. Yin replied: “Last winter, in the tenth month, the Star of Heavenly Principle moved westward past *Pi*. Within the Dipper are four stars of Heavenly Principle, governing the regulation of all things, where the Elder resides. Thus the *Rhapsody on the Sagely Virtue of the Primal Origin* states: ‘Now shining in the *Gouchen*, now concealing its light in Heavenly Principle.’ *Pi* is the stellar lodge of the true west. From the first day of this month, the harmonizing wind has blown thrice, and an azure qi resembling dragons and serpents has crossed westward—this is the sign of a great sage.”

Yin had long studied the *Book of Changes*, apocryphal texts, and celestial patterns. All his observations, whether gazing upward or examining below, had never failed to prove true, so he knew a sage would cross the pass. Day and night, he meditated without cease. Now, with cause, he had indeed encountered the immortal carriage. He entreated: “Grant me compassionate instruction, to deliver those sunk in darkness.”

Having passed all three trials, the Elder smiled contentedly and said: “Excellent, your recognition of me! I, too, have known you. You possess the vision of spiritual penetration and shall attain deliverance from the world.” Overjoyed, Yin prostrated himself, kowtowing, and asked: “May I inquire the Great Sage’s surname and given name?” The Elder replied: “My surname and given name are vast and distant, spanning kalpas without end—impossible to recount fully. Now I am surnamed Li, named Boyang, and styled Lao Dan.”

Thereupon, Yin led him to the official residence, prepared a seat for his veneration, offered ablutions and grooming, and performed the rites of a disciple. Master Yongli remarked: “Behold Laozi and Yin Xi—did they not awaken to the subtle signs beforehand? Yet they still maintained such humble demeanor, wishing to instruct the world.”

The Elder’s charioteer, Xu Jia, had long been in his service, agreeing to a daily wage of a hundred coins. By the time they reached the pass, the debt amounted to seven million three hundred thousand coins. Seeing the Elder departing far from office, Xu Jia hastened to demand payment. The Elder said: “I intend to journey to the Western Sea, Da Qin, Tiaozhi, Jambudvīpa, Anxi, and other lands. If you drive my carriage, I shall repay you in gold upon our return.” Xu Jia agreed. Upon reaching the Pass Master, they fed the green ox in the wilds.

The Elder, wishing to test him, transformed an auspicious herb into a beautiful woman, who approached the oxherd and flirted with Xu Jia. Enchanted, he wished to stay, thus breaking their prior agreement. He then went to the Pass Master to sue the Elder for his wages. The Elder said to Xu Jia: “You have followed me for over two hundred years. You should have died long ago. I gave you the *Tai Xuan Yang Sheng Fu*, by which you have endured to this day. Why do you not recall this and sue me?” As he spoke, the talisman flew from Xu Jia’s mouth—its cinnabar script as fresh as new—and Xu Jia instantly became a pile of white bones.

Moved by pity for Xu Jia’s death against his true will, Xi knelt and kowtowed to plead for his life, wishing to pardon his offense and restore him to life, and offering to pay the debt on his behalf. The Elder, valuing Xi’s compassionate generosity, said: “Good.” He then cast the *Tai Xuan Yang Sheng Fu* again upon Xu Jia, who instantly revived.

The Elder said: “I do not blame you. You broke our original agreement, and the Dao naturally departed from you—thus you died. Now I shall repay you.” Xi then gave the money to Xu Jia and sent him off with propriety. Xu Jia begged to stay, but the Elder refused. Prostrating himself and striking his cheeks, Xu Jia said: “Having bathed in the sagely grace of your lenient pardon, my scattered bones now see the light again. I shall carve this into my heart and bones, and wish to follow your cloud-borne carriage.” The Elder still refused.

The Elder kept Xi at the Pass for over a hundred days. Xi purified himself, kowtowed twice, and knelt to plead: “A humble child of the dust, I have fortunately encountered a sage. I offer my body and life, and wish to hear the great Dao.”

The Elder said: “Subtle indeed is your question. The great Dao holds to the Three Grades of Authenticity as its rule, taking Oneness as its measure and rectitude as its virtue.”

Xi said: “If the grades of the Dao are three, can one still ascend as an immortal?”

The Elder said: “If you can know the One, all things are complete. Seal off passions and desires, enter the inner chamber, and refine gold to consume the Black—this is the first step. If you can know this and hold fast without losing it…”

Xi leapt with joy and kowtowed, saying: “How does one refine gold?”

The Elder said: “It is a divine method. From the Most True Supreme Lord down, none have attained the Dao except by the Golden Elixir. Treading the Nine Darks and entering the Profound Void, ascending and descending through emptiness, they rise together with the divine luminaries.”

Xi again kowtowed and said: “I wish to hear its essentials.”

The Elder said:
*“Red cinnabar and black mercury form the great elixir’s head,
From red to black is true cultivation.
Extract essence from the red, marrow from the black,
Resolve the red and black—peerless medicine.
Use red to enter black to preserve long life,
Use black to enter red to become a celestial immortal.”*

Xi said: “The Dao is lofty and wondrous indeed. I have not yet studied or mastered it—pray grant me further instruction.”

The Elder said: “The first is lead, and lead is the lord. The second is mercury, and mercury is the minister. If the lead is not true, the mercury will not draw near. If the lead is true, it will not lose its household ministers. The Azure-Waisted Messenger and the Crimson-Blood General unite the two clans, disparate kin dwelling as one. White mercury becomes the brain, yellow sprouts the root. Transform lead into powder, refine mercury into dust. Yin resides in the yang position, yang numbers balance yin. The moon waxes and the sun wanes, cold and heat divide their realms. Open and establish the symbolic forms—brilliant patterns emerge. Only this Dao of mine endures with heaven and earth.”

Xi bowed and said: “I reverently receive your command regarding the essentials of the medicines. But what of the furnace and crucible’s design, the timing of water and fire?”

The Elder sang:
*“Round as three-five, a tenth of an inch,
Mouth four-eight, lips two inches,
Two feet long, thickness even,
Belly three navels, seated warmth descending.
Yin above, yang rushing below,
Head and tail martial, center civil.
Begin at seventy, end at thirty,
Within a hundred, harmonize well.
Yin fire white, yellow sprouts the root,
Coming and going, never leaving the door.
Gradually growing great, nature and temperament pure,
Return to the One, restore the origin.
When one cycle completes, be most diligent,
Guard closely, do not grow confused.
The path is distant, profoundly mysterious—
If you attain this, you will fathom heaven and earth.
At midnight and noon, purify soul and spirit,
Gain long life, enter the immortal village.
Those who delight in the Dao, seek its patterns,
Ponder deeply—no need for debate.”*

He sang again:
*“Sun and moon are the essence of heaven and earth,
The hexagrams revolve in clear cycles.
Three-five before, three-five after,
Five-six thirty, returning to life.
Life and death cycle through twelve,
Twelve divided into twenty-four.
Twenty-four qi mutually shift,
All phenomena, every ounce, lie herein.
One taste of mercury splits into two,
Transforming yin and yang into *ji* and *wei*.
*Ji* and *wei* then pair as husband and wife,
Only then does one perceive heaven and earth within the crucible.”*

He sang once more:
*“The Golden Liquid Returned Elixir flows with immortal splendor,
Soaring high on clouds, ascending to the celestial mounds.
Red and yellow substances form in an instant,
At that time, male and female pearls scatter in profusion.
They can transform and summon traveling kitchens,
Divine youths and jade maidens accompany me.
Entering and exiting without barrier, in harmony with heaven,
Their essence congeals like frost, adept at sinking and floating.
If you reverently follow this, you will surely journey there—
The secrets of the Golden Liquid end here.”*

“When the elixir is complete, fashion gold into eating vessels—this too can grant long life. First test the gold; only when it is formed may you consume it. Thus it is said: the Golden Elixir can be made, and the world can be delivered.”

Thereupon, he bestowed upon Xi the *Tai Qing Eight Talismans Scripture*, the *Tai Qing Observing Heaven Scripture*, the *Nine Capitals Scripture*, the *Divine Elixir Scripture*, the *Golden Liquid Scripture*, and the methods for the Eight Refinements, Nine Cycles, Returned Elixir, and Fire-Taming.

The Elder again said: “If you truly desire to become a divine immortal, first consume the Returned Elixir and Golden Liquid. If you cannot consume the Returned Elixir and Golden Liquid, your suffering will be in vain. You will be bound by wind, cold, rain, damp, and demonic darkness, and the Registrar of Destinies will be unable to save you. Can you not strive? Diligently refine the elixir, guard yourself early, and do not show it to fools, lest they slander and defame the divine Dao, increasing your transgressions. Alas! A walking corpse, though rich with all things under heaven, though noble as a king or marquis, will not fill a hundred years before becoming a basket of earth—how lamentable! To compound the divine elixir is but a moment’s toil. Once achieved, you become an immortal, equal with heaven, freely following your heart’s desires. What boundless joy!”

Xi said: “Refining gold and consuming the Black—are these two different paths?”

The Elder said: “They are the essence of heaven and earth, the root of yin and yang—they are called the Two Qi. The yang dragon and yin tiger, the liquid of wood and the essence of metal—when the Two Qi meet and unite, this is called the Outer Elixir. Holding harmony and refining the storehouse, exhaling the old and inhaling the new, ascending to the Mud Pill Palace, descending to the Cinnabar Field, circulating without cease, morning at the Crimson Palace, gathering from the Five Stones to nourish the hundred spirits—this is the Inner Elixir.”

Holding harmony and refining qi—those who cultivate the Dao and attain the Inner Elixir may prolong their years, while those who attain the Outer Elixir may ascend to heaven. The Three Ones and Nine Contemplations are the essentials of inner cultivation; the Nine Elixirs and Golden Liquid are the ultimate of the Outer Elixir. To practice both together is the decisive path to Daoist attainment.

The Han Celestial Master composed the *Preface to the Scripture of the Golden Liquid Divine Elixir*, which briefly states: The subtleties of the immortal’s pursuit, the wondrous words of the profound, the supreme principle beyond all principles, the great reality that is yet not reality—these have already been fully expounded in the profound tradition, not to be confined by even the slightest partiality.

Laozi said: This is also to expound the record of the empty and tranquil Dao. Its manifestations of numinous virtue, the causes of its arising, the establishment of scriptures and teachings, the recording of extraordinary accounts—all these weave together the great Dao, dissecting the Three Ultimates. First, the great clarity discerns the inverse and the direct; then, all is swept away in the forgetting of dualities. All are called possessors of virtue, yet they are unified in non-dependence. In their comings and goings, none perceive their origin; in the daily use of the hundred clans, they ever excel without knowing why. This is the power by which the Dao reveals itself in clarity and becomes the bridge for the myriad things.

As for the Dao, as for its virtue—tracing the lineage to its source, exhausting the wondrous to its limit, gathering all branches to the true root, converging the ten thousand strands into one essential—it flows ceaselessly without end. As for the work of the Golden Elixir, its numinous depths are lofty, its dark transformations profound. The dragon’s metamorphosis shines with spirit; what was withered and cut off revives, and those who grasp the qi of life know no decay. Ascending the radiant Han to soar beyond, roaming the cloud peaks in freedom—until the face radiates jade light, the body grows wondrous fur, water is spat and fire exhaled, flight is effortless, forms divide into ten thousand changes, and all is done at will. Rivers and mountains are dammed without embankments, peaks and ridges are split without axes or awls. Its numinosity is hard to record, its wonders impossible to lose. Vast is the essence of the numinous—it cannot be fully described.

Xi again inquired: “I have received your teaching on the method of refining gold. May I now ask about consuming the Black?”

The Elder said: “Consuming the Black is the firming of essence. Receive the *Mysterious Scripture of Radiance*; the essential wonders of the life-generating Dao emerge from darkness and enter the hidden. Above, secure the Gate of Clouds; below, fortify the Numinous Root. To know it is very easy; to practice it is very difficult. When the body moves, virtue scatters; when qi strays, the Dao rebels. Essence dissipates, the hun-soul is harmed; when the eyes stir, the po-soul is disordered. Thus the sage stills the body and secures the qi, accumulates essence and restrains vision—then virtue and Dao congeal in emptiness, the hun and po are firmly guarded, the body is one and the spirit ten thousand, and the Dao is accomplished.

“Regularly, after midnight, lie supine with fists clenched, inhale the pure Black through the nose, and slowly exhale through the mouth. Then guard the Three Ones, enter the chamber, and engage in the Nine Contemplations. When the spirit is settled and the Black is at peace, the Dao of immortality is near.” Thereupon he transmitted to Xi the *Central Scripture of the Jade Calendar* in thirty-five chapters.

Xi said: “I beg you to instruct me on the purport of the Three Ones.”

The Elder said: “The Dao of the Three Ones is what immortals hold most sacred. The world has never heard of it—you must keep it secret. Between the eyebrows, one inch inward is the Hall of Light; two inches inward is the Cavern Chamber; three inches inward is the Mud Pill Palace—this is the Upper Elixir Field, wherein dwells the Red Child, named Yuanxian, called Imperial Minister. The heart is the Crimson Palace, the Middle Elixir Field, wherein dwells the Perfected One, named Zidan, called Radiant Firmness. Three inches inward from the navel is the Gate of Life, the Lower Elixir Field, wherein dwells the Infant, named Yuanyang, called Mysterious Valley. Each is three inches in length, all clad in red silk, red robes, and red shoes—these are the Three Ones. Another version says the upper wears purple, the middle red, and the lower yellow, all in the form of infants. Below each sits a golden forest and jade table, and always with a golden furnace and jade spoon they stir the essence to nourish the Upper One and Middle One. Day and night, you must visualize this. If you desire long life, the Three Ones must be made clear. If you can guard the One, the One will also guard you. Qi transforms into essence, essence into spirit, spirit into the Infant, the Infant into the Perfected One, and the Perfected One then into the Red Child—this is the true One.”

He further said: “False paths nurture the body; the true Dao nurtures the spirit. When spirit is true, the Dao is penetrated—one can vanish or abide, the spirit can make the body fly or even move mountains. When the body becomes ashes and dust, how can it be recognized? The ears and eyes, sounds and sights—these are the faults that linger for you. The nose and mouth, their beloved fragrances and flavors—these are your foes. The body is the root of affliction—pain, itching, cold, and heat. The mind is the burden of the body—sorrow, poison, worry, and vexation. If you do not swiftly cast these aside, your will and qi will daily diminish, your lifespan will not extend—this has been the case for a long time. You must first diminish all desires, not let the mind wander, dwell in quietude, and meditate in a purified chamber. Ten thousand scrolls of cinnabar scriptures are not equal to guarding the One. Always take emptiness as your body, non-being as your mind. The body of no-body, the mind of no-mind—this is called guarding the spirit. Guarding the spirit, the mysterious is penetrated—this is called the Dao’s sameness.

“It is like lofty mountains and great marshes—they do not desire insects, fish, birds, or beasts, yet insects, fish, birds, and beasts gather to them. If one can be empty and non-active, not desiring to practice the Dao, the Dao will naturally come to them.” I have seen this in the *Scripture of Western Marvels*.

Thereupon he transmitted to Xi the method of the Nine Refinements of the Golden True Yang, called the Nine Chambers of Contemplation: First, the Contemplation of No Deviant Thoughts; second, the Contemplation of Upright Body; third, the Contemplation of Attaining Rectitude; fourth, the Contemplation of Great Rectitude; fifth, the Contemplation of Utmost Rectitude; sixth, the Contemplation of Body Rectitude; seventh, the Contemplation of True Rectitude; eighth, the Contemplation of Penetrating the Mysterious; ninth, the Contemplation of the Great Mystery. Entering these nine chambers and practicing these nine contemplations, one unites in form with the Great Yang and together attains the Dao.

Xi received all these secrets, bowed twice, and touched his forehead to the ground in reverence, cherishing the profound purport.

Discourse says: The sage’s words are most concise; the primordial principle is not complex. What is recounted above consists of the words with which Lord Lao responded to Guan Yin’s repeated earnest requests—collected in their essentials to instruct later generations. This was no conversation of a single morning or evening.

Xi then resolutely resigned from office. On the twenty-fifth day of the twelfth month, he respectfully invited Lord Lao to his home. Another day, he entreated, saying: "This foolish one knows not what to ask—pray grant further instruction."

Lord Lao replied: "If you wish to cultivate the body and nurture nature, to practice the Dao and discipline form, to refine qi and perfect spirit, and to attain boundless continuity, nothing precedes stillness. Thus, heaven is still to establish constancy, spirit is still to enact transformation, and man is still to sustain life. When about to enter stillness, turn inward in reflection, let the mind harbor no reckless thoughts, the mouth utter no reckless words, the form make no reckless movements. The formless takes form before you; the soundless becomes audible; the tasteless turns sweet; the wordless becomes teaching. Divine radiance descends; fortune and virtue draw near; harmonious qi responds. If you wish to still the spirit, first bathe and fast for twenty-five days, then refine your body until the hun and po are at peace—only then may you enter the chamber.
