Once inside, should a cinnabar serpent alight upon your heart, or the Queen Mother descend, or a Dao-body manifest, know that these are but the spirits within your body testing you. Do not be startled or afraid; if questioned, do not answer; if harmed or offended, do not fear. Simply compose your mind and settle your intent, and the apparition will depart of itself.

After twenty-five, forty-five, sixty, eighty-five, one hundred and five, one hundred and twenty-five, one hundred and fifty, and one hundred and eighty days, spirits will come—all to test you. Remain composed and do not respond; they cannot harm you. If you succumb to fear, the trial will be failed, and the Dao lost. Beware and take heed.

After one hundred and eighty days, the chamber will brighten of itself, and matters of safety and peril, life and death, will all be foreknown. The palaces within your body will each become clearly visible. After two hundred and eighty days, all illnesses will be dispelled. After one year, scars will vanish. After a thousand days, form and spirit will both attain subtlety, and you will become a Perfected One."

Thereupon, he bestowed upon Xi the *Wondrous Perfection*, the *Inner Explication*, the Three Caverns’ True Scriptures of *Great Clarity* and *Highest Clarity*, the talismans and diagrams of *Numinous Treasure*, and the methods of *Great Mystery*, charging him to teach them to the most refined and benevolent, that they might ascend as feathered transcendents and ensure the lineage would not be severed. Xi bowed his head in reverence and gave thanks.

Lord Lao then said to Xi: "I tell you again: the ancient masters were none other than my own manifestations. I once transformed in the land of Jambudvīpa, and now I shall return my spirit to the Nameless, abiding ceaselessly as if present. Now I depart, returning to the One Origin."

As Lord Lao prepared to journey west, Xi kowtowed and begged to accompany him. Lord Lao replied: "I roam beyond heaven and earth, play within the dark mystery, traverse the four bounds and eight extremities, above and below without limit. How could you follow me?"

Xi said: "I would enter fire or the abyss, descend into the earth or ascend to heaven, reduce my body to ashes and forfeit my life—all to follow the Great Immortal."

Lord Lao said: "Those who attain the Dao can see with penetrating vision and hear with penetrating clarity; their transformations are unfathomable, their concealment and manifestation indeterminate. Even they cannot follow me afar. Though your bone-configuration accords with the Dao and you are destined to achieve perfection, you have received the Dao but recently and have not yet attained spiritual communion. How could you transform and follow my sacred body?"

Xi bowed again and said: "May I hear of the Way of spiritual communion and transformation?"

Lord Lao replied: "The qi of the living and the forms of existence are all illusions. That from which creation begins, that which yin and yang transform, is called life and death. To exhaust the reckonings and master change, to shift and alter form, is called transformation and illusion. The Creator’s craft is wondrous, its work profound—thus it is difficult to exhaust or end. The craft of the formed is manifest, its work shallow—thus it arises and perishes in an instant. Only when you know that illusion and transformation do not differ from life and death may you be spoken to of the Way of transformation. If you can diligently cultivate this Way and embody spontaneity, then I may journey with you to transform the kingdoms."

Twenty-eight days later, Xi again burned incense, bowed twice, and entreated: "I have been fortunate to hear the supreme Dao, my joy beyond words. Pray bestow a written work to expound the Dao’s purport and instruct later generations."

Lord Lao said: "Very well. You shall be the leader of all Perfected Ones and, in this land of Wanli beyond the world of the abandoned worthy, transmit and propagate the Great Dao." Thereupon, he bestowed the *Five Thousand Words of the Dao and Virtue*, saying: "I have never lightly bestowed this text upon others. Only once in a thousand years is it transmitted. Strive diligently." Xi received it with joy, held it fast, recited it, and practiced it. The Dao was accomplished, and he transmitted it to later disciples.

According to Liu Xiang’s *Seven Summaries*, when Liu Xiang collated the *Laozi* in the palace library, there were two chapters in the *Laozi* and one chapter in the Grand Scribe’s version. Minister Xiang’s version had two chapters, while the combined internal and external texts comprised five chapters and one hundred forty-two sections. After removing three duplicate chapters and sixty-two sections, he established two chapters and eighty-one sections. The first chapter, the *Dao Classic*, contained thirty-seven sections; the second, the *Virtue Classic*, contained forty-four. This was the original arrangement of the chapters after initial collation, though it is unknown which passages were removed or where the divisions were made. Both the palace text and Xiang’s text refer to two chapters, suggesting a fixed version existed before collation. Later traditions hold that the *Laozi* has eighty-one chapters, corresponding to the supreme number of the Great Yang. The *Dao Classic* is placed above to model heaven, and heaven’s number is odd, hence thirty-seven chapters. The *Virtue Classic* is placed below to model earth, and earth’s number is even, hence forty-four chapters. Yet Ge Hong and others did not alter this original arrangement, instead appending the chapter "The Dao Constantly Does Nothing" to the end of the *Virtue Classic*, stating: "Heaven is completed by the four seasons, hence the upper classic has four nines—thirty-six chapters. Earth is completed by the five phases, hence the lower classic has five nines—forty-five chapters. Together, they correspond to the number nine times nine."

Strictly following the guidelines and maintaining consistency with the previous translation:

---

Yan Junping thus stated: "The Way of Yin is eight, the Way of Yang is nine; combining eight with nine yields seventy-two chapters—forty in the upper section and thirty-two in the lower." Yet his chapter divisions entirely diverge from those of Heshang Gong. This arrangement, too, originates with Junping and lacks any basis. Now, examining the various versions, some present the text as continuous prose, while others divide it into eighty-one chapters, akin to the stanzas of ancient poetry, each marked separately. Such segmentation, however, burdens the text with redundancy, and thus the chosen divisions are clear without requiring each chapter to be titled.

Carefully considering *Liezi*, which cites "The Valley Spirit never dies" and attributes it to *The Book of the Yellow Emperor*, we see that the five thousand characters may derive from an older text. Thus, when *Laozi* states, "What the ancients called 'the crooked shall be whole,'" it is no empty saying—this is its meaning. Hence Confucius, lamenting "transmission, not innovation," and "trusting and loving the ancients," sighed in emulation of Lao Peng.

Examining other texts that quote *Laozi*, some refer to it as the *Mysterious Classic*, while Han Fei’s *Illustrations of Laozi* diverges slightly from the currently circulating version. Such variations are no more surprising than the differing recensions of *The Analects* (Qi and Lu versions) or *The Book of Documents* (ancient and modern texts)—differing accounts are to be expected.

During the Tang, Fu Yi collated numerous versions and tallied their characters, noting: The "Concubine of Xiang Yu" version, discovered when a tomb of Xiang Yu’s concubine was opened in Pengcheng during the fifth year of Qi Wuping, was said to be the "Gazing Toward Anqiu" text. In the Taihe era of the Wei, Daoist Kou Qianzhi obtained the "Master of the Riverbank" version. The family-transmitted version of the Qi recluse Qiu Yue contained 5,722 characters, aligning with Han Fei’s *Illustrations of Laozi*. Additionally, an official version from Luoyang had 5,635 characters; Wang Bi’s version had 5,683 (or 5,610) characters; and Heshang Gong’s version had 5,555 (or 5,590) characters. The commentaries of various schools also vary in length. Yet, as the years passed, each tradition held fast to its transmission, sometimes cross-referencing other versions, resulting in discrepancies.

Sima Qian’s *Records of the Grand Historian* states: "Laozi composed a book expounding the meaning of the Dao and virtue, totaling over five thousand words." Since it does not exceed six thousand, "over five thousand" is accurate. Today, Daoist tradition refers to it as the *Five Thousand Character Text*, merely citing a round number.

Lu Xisheng’s *Commentary on the Dao and Virtue Classic* states: "When the Great Dao was obscured and worldly teachings declined, the realm descended into chaos. At such times, Heaven’s bell summoned the sage, who, grieving that the people had strayed from governance, arose to support the declining and rescue the chaotic. The late Zhou was such an era. Thus, Confucius elucidated the culture of the Three Dynasties to uphold its decline, while Laozi grounded himself in the simplicity of the Three Sovereigns to remedy its chaos—their aims were one. Confucius’ flourishing arose from culture, and culture delights the emotions. Laozi’s flourishing stemmed from simplicity, and simplicity restores nature. The extremes of nature and emotion are not distinct to the sage. The alternation of culture and simplicity is immutable across the ages.

*The Book of Changes* states, 'Manifesting through benevolence'—this refers to teaching through culture. Cultural teaching makes matters evident, and when matters are evident, refined speech is detailed. *The Book of Changes* also states, 'Concealing through application'—this refers to teaching through simplicity. Simplicity’s teaching is subtle, and subtlety is unfathomable; thus, those who fabricate interpretations abound.

Laozi’s flourishing takes the Dao as its essence and names as its function, embodying 'non-action yet nothing left undone,' aligning with the august ultimate. Yang Zhu adhered to Laozi’s essence but fell short, valuing the self and belittling things. Zhuang Zhou misunderstood Laozi’s function and overreached, striving to 'abolish sageliness and discard wisdom.' Shen Buhai and Han Fei misapplied Laozi’s names, leading to oppressive and harsh governance. Wang Bi and He Yan misconstrued Laozi’s Dao, drifting into emptiness and reckless abandon. These six figures are all transgressors against Laozi. Yet the world, deeming Laozi’s principles incompatible with Confucius’, slanders his name by claiming he 'denigrates benevolence and righteousness' and 'abolishes ritual and learning,' or faults his Dao as 'solely relying on purity and emptiness, unfit for governance.' Alas, the world’s delusion runs deep! This has led to Laozi being maligned for a thousand years, his Dao and virtue unfulfilled in his time—rightly so.

Laozi traces the origins of Heaven and Earth, recounts the changes of past and present, first clarifying the Dao and virtue, then expounding benevolence and righteousness, and finally exposing the failures of ritual and learning and the excesses of punishments and warfare—his words arise from the gradual governance of the world. His grasp of essentials and adherence to fundamentals lie in the extremes of nature and emotion. Thus, his Dao begins with the self and mind, takes form in the family and state, and culminates in the world—so comprehensive is it. Yet the deluded still speak thus—is this not a grave slander?

Fu Xi drew the Eight Trigrams, symbolizing the myriad things, exhausting the principles of nature and destiny, and harmonizing with the Dao and virtue. Laozi preceded Heaven and Earth, grounded himself in yin and yang, deduced the principles of nature and destiny, and explored the mysteries of the Dao and virtue—his origins are the same as Fu Xi’s. King Wen observed the movements of the nine and six in the Great Change, valuing firmness and esteeming transformation, yet centering on the mean. Laozi examined the correctness of the seven and eight in the Great Change, embodying softness and guarding stillness, yet unifying them in the great—his lineage aligns with King Wen’s. Confucius traced the legacy of Yao and Shun, modeled himself on Kings Wen and Wu, and guided the people with the teachings of benevolence and righteousness. Laozi emulated Fu Xi, encompassed the Yellow Emperor, and enveloped the world with the transformation of the Dao and virtue—his authority converges with Confucius’. These three noble ones are the pinnacle of sages, yet Laozi transformed and penetrated them, returning to unite with them, investigating the mechanism of ultimate change and exploring the essence of ultimate subtlety—this is what is called the utmost spirit."

Yet Wang Bi held that the sage embodies the Dao, while Laozi had not yet fully embodied it—thus Ruan Ji deemed him a "superior worthy, second only to the sage." This aligns with Fu Si’s view. Could it be that Laozi’s traces of worldly engagement fell short of his own teachings? Such is not the case.

Alas! The sage’s presence in the world follows two paths: one of active advancement, the other of reclusive withdrawal. When the Dao goes unenacted, some risk their lives in ceaseless travel, bearing the world’s sorrows as their own; others conceal their names, drifting like fleeing birds, showing the world it cannot entangle them. Though their ways diverge—one boundless, the other confined—they converge at the same destination. This is the true gate of Dao and virtue, not mere opposition.

Thus, Confucius’ emergence and Laozi’s abiding, Laozi’s silence and Confucius’ speech—these are the utmost expressions of yielding and extending, concealing and revealing. How could those two scholars have grasped this? Sima Qian, in compiling the lineages of all schools, placed the *Dao De Jing* at the forefront—truly discerning root and branch. Yet Ban Gu, in his *Table of Ancient and Modern Personages*, disparaged Laozi by ranking him in the third grade. Though his name may be disparaged, can his Dao be denigrated?

Moreover, Confucius personally met Laozi and marveled at his Dao, saying, "It is like the dragon!" He sought his instruction on ritual, and indeed, no question remained. Later records and traditions failed to plumb its meaning, giving rise to a profusion of heterodox interpretations. For those who transmitted it did not exhaust its source, and thus those who expounded it did not fulfill its essence.

Ah! Had such a transmission not been composed, Laozi’s intent might have nearly vanished. Now, therefore, I have exhaustively traced its subtleties, making it resplendently harmonize with the intentions of all the sagely ones. One who embodies the Great Dao and employs its name, wielding antiquity to govern the present, will bring order as easily as turning a palm.

Of those who have spoken in Laozi’s defense, only the Grand Historian came close. As for governance, only Emperor Wen of Han attained a fraction of his Dao. All other perverse and distorted accounts are unworthy of consideration.

The

The honored position of Supreme Perfected is conferred upon him, along with a purple hibiscus crown, a fluttering azure feathered skirt, vermilion undergarments with green sleeves, a rainbow-hued sash, and a nine-colored tasseled jade tablet. Fifty golden lads and fifty jade maidens are granted to his service, placing him above the Twenty-Four Heavenly Kings, where he oversees eighty thousand immortals.

Xi prostrated himself in reverence, saying: "This humble dust-born child, having encountered the Great Sage, has been granted flesh upon withered bones—a thousand lifetimes of fortune. To serve as a sweeper in your abode or as an attendant on your journeys would be my deepest wish and greatest honor. Yet this exalted rank, these lavish rites—what virtue do I possess to dare accept them?"

The Lord Lao replied: "You have long yearned for the Dao, your hidden virtue has ascended to be heard, and your diligent cultivation has fulfilled its profound work. This is no favor I bestow upon you alone; press onward with resolve."

Xi, unable to decline further, bowed twice and accepted the mandate. From then on, he soared through the void, attending the Dragon Chariot and roaming the eight directions.

Thereupon, the Lord Lao expounded the *Scripture of Primordial Yang* to the Sacred Mother of the Grand Bureau. Her entire household—over two hundred kin—ascended to heaven in a single ascent. The Lord Lao, recognizing the family’s generations of accumulated merit, raised them to the heavens in accordance with celestial decree, manifesting his transformation within their home.

In Chengdu Prefecture, the Green Ram Palace remains, along with the sites of the Descent Birth Terrace and the Primordial Yang Terrace. Yue Penggui, Hanlin Academician and Drafter of Edicts, composed its summary, saying:

Of the eighty-one heavens, they span half the lifespan of the Most High. Six million years mark the dawn of the Primordial Beginning. The Dao, vast and boundless, defies comprehension; its essence, subtle and formless, eludes inquiry. It dwells beyond form and void, before the Great Beginning and the Great Simplicity. Two spirits emerged together, carving open creation; three qi intermingled and advanced, scattering the unhewn into embryonic form. Dark and yellow flowed yet uncoagulated; pure and turbid divided and congealed. The Dao is the ancestor of the ten thousand qi; virtue is the root of the ten thousand things.

It is also said: His body rides the chariot of power, wheels turning beyond the primordial chaos; his hand wields the handle of longevity, directing all before the unfolding of creation. Serene and without action, he abides in effortless contentment. By transforming what is untransformed, the ten thousand things are perfected; by generating what is ungenerated, all living beings attain fulfillment.

Again, it is said: When the hidden depths first opened, the ninefold brilliance steamed without cease; when the Great Primordial unfolded, the six voids flowed without rest. He established the undivided teaching, revered the true and singular origin. First, he wandered in the Azure Epoch, and when the Azure Epoch ended, he later emerged in the Crimson Clarity, which in turn passed away. Ninety-nine myriad billion years are stored in a pellet of cinnabar; the five thousand five hundred layered heavens lie hidden within a cicada’s shell.

At the height of King Gaozong of Yin’s reign and the inception of King Wen of Zhou’s *Book of Changes*, divine radiance infused the jade womb, auspicious qi coalesced into golden bones. Unhewn and unadorned, he gestated for over eighty years; descending in splendor, his birth surpassed twenty thousand days. His feet tread the principle of imperishability; his hands grasp the scripture of eternal life. Gazing down upon the ford of the Qi River, he surveys the era after the primordial chaos. Adapting to circumstances, he established teachings as the model for all ages; responding to change without constraint, he became the paradigm for a hundred kings.

Thus, the Great Dao is the crown of the Three Teachings, and supreme virtue is the ancestral origin of all rulers.

The Green Ram Market, according to the annals, is where the Most High, the Celestial Sovereign of Primordial Mystery, descended a second time. After the Yin Dao declined and the Zhou virtue waned, he traversed the mortal realm with historians, abandoned his post as Archivist, and withdrew into seclusion. Leaving the Wei Gate behind, he crossed the Han Pass to the west, riding a carriage drawn by a green ox, its wheels turning gracefully, and a brush pulled by a white phoenix, soaring freely. Xu Jia held the reins, accompanying the former emperor; Yin Xi divined the winds, knowing the Dao Lord would arrive once more. Though briefly parted, three thousand years had passed since he last appeared as the Crimson Youth; reunited, eight hundred years of life remained in the company of the Yellow and the Old. Secretly, he transmitted the wondrous formulas; subtly, he imparted the esoteric words. A thousand days were renewed in promise, and a single moment of reunion was sealed anew.

Briefly ascending to the Primordial Beginning, he then ascended to the Heaven within Heaven, eternally serving the ancestral master and returning to the image beyond images. Thus he exhorted the Azure Emperor’s Azure Dragon, transforming into the Green Ram in Shu Commandery; riding the purple clouds to the Purple Palace, descending the purple qi upon the royal chambers. The ceaseless sound was about to pause, yet the infant’s milk had yet to come. Suddenly, joy arrived, and the wailing child fell silent. From the body of a crimson infant, he reverted to a white-haired elder, and so on.

Inscribed thus:
At the vast origin’s first unfolding, kalpas stretched boundless.
In the dim and distant, the Great Dao; in the subtle and serene, the image of the Emperor.
The Grand Inception could conceal, the Grand Commencement followed.
Within the dark and yellow shell, clear and turbid had not yet shed.
Before heaven and earth, myriad eons passed.
Teachings were set forth according to the moment, responding to change without hindrance.
The Three Sovereigns grew ever clearer, the Five Emperors ever wiser.
All under heaven knew peace, all living beings were delivered.
The Louguan Temple set the course, the Han Pass bore the inscription.
As guest, he expounded the transformation; in India, he spread his benevolence.
The form beyond form, the sound beyond sound.
Departing, none could send him off; arriving, none could welcome him.
Forced to name the name, forced to call the call:
Above the Great Void, gold forms the city.
Within the boundless darkness, white jade forms the capital.
Resplendent beyond limit, embracing the eight directions.
All nations drink as one, all rivers pour as one.
None can exhaust it, none can fill it.
Those who tread the shallow path dwell long in prosperity and integrity.
Those who tread the deep path attain eternal Great Peace.

It is also said:
He carved open heaven and earth, shaped and refined the primordial chaos,
grasped the sun and moon, enveloped the cosmos.
All phenomena were exhausted, only the Dao endured alone.
Amid the three teachings’ contention, only the Dao stood supreme.

In the ninth month of the fourth year of Zhonghe during the Tang Dynasty, a stele was erected at Qingyang Palace by the Jiedushi of Xichuan, which remains extant today.

*End of Scroll Three of The Sacred Records of the Primordial One*

*Scroll Four of The Sacred Records of the Primordial One*
Compiled by Song Dynasty Master of Observing the Return, the High Scholar Xie Shoubin

The Lord Lao said to Perfected Yin: "You once wished to follow me on a distant journey. Now that your Dao is accomplished, you may tour beyond the eight directions." Thereupon, he exhaled the hidden texts of the eight directions and bestowed them upon him.

Overjoyed, Yin received them and immediately summoned the cloud chariot of the Eight Effulgences. The Lord Lao mounted the chariot, drawn by five-colored divine dragons, bearing the vermilion celestial standard of the Flowing Radiance Yellow Heaven, shaded by the nine-light crane canopy. Twelve guardian officials led the way, divine soldiers wielding banners, while ninety thousand flying immortals followed, controlling the whirlwind wheels. Lions cleared the path, phoenixes flanked the carriage, and they soared through the void. The Perfected One accompanied the Four Heavenly Kings.

They arrived at the Mountain of the Sun’s Cavern and Eternal Brightness, where they grasped the cinnabar branches of Fusang, scattered the vermilion blossoms of Ruomu, gazed upon the Azure Sea, embraced the Eastern Well, passed the Jade Pool Palace, and were received by the Divine King of Yang Valley, the Azure Lad of the Eastern Sea, and the assembled immortals, who offered cinnabar fruits and golden nectar of jade-green ale.

Next, they ascended Ancestral Mountain, beheld the Mushroom Fields, gathered spirit-nourishing herbs, and rested at Penglai Palace. They then journeyed south to Phoenix Mountain, ascended Green Mound, passed Purple Mansion, where the Lady of Great Primordial and the Master of Purple Mansion presented the nine-light sweet dew, the White Essence of King Wen, and the Azure Grove’s White Seeds. This mountain, also called Xiao Mound, yields the nine-light magic mushrooms and the cloth of the Fire Court.

They ascended Longli Mountain, crossed the Crimson Ford, and entered the Great Cinnabar Palace, where the Lady of the Southern Culmen served jasper blossoms, jade wine, and the fiery dates of Red Spirit. Arriving at Crimson Mountain, they observed the Land of Flowing Fire, rested at the Scarlet Mound’s gate, and were offered by the Duke of Great Harmony and Jade Perfection the cinnabar marrow of Flame Ridge, flying elixirs, purple peaches, and the nectar of Lei Mai.

They then traveled west to Tortoise Terrace, entered the Seven Treasures Garden, and beheld the Flying Mystery Purple Script. Passing the Flowing Essence Gate, the Primordial Sovereign of Grand Simplicity, the Golden Mother of the Nine Numina, presented dates inscribed with jade script, their fruits like vases, and commanded her attendants to offer the returning-soul incense, the dark-light azure peaches, and the intertwined golden-purple dates. Next, they crossed the Weak Waters, ascended Phoenix Mountain, embraced the Jade Pool, rested at Kunwu Mountain, where the White Emperor Heavenly Lord presented the seven-famed stone mushrooms, flowing essence jade ointment, and golden-light elixir.

Journeying north to Kongtong Mountain, they passed the Cavern of Shade Palace, where the Perfected Lord of the Northern Culmen offered the thousand-knot divine herbs and the void melons of the Black Cloud Plum. These melons ripen once every four kalpas. Ascending Dark Mound, they observed the Eight Refining Pools of Northern Darkness, rested at the Vast Cold Ruins, and were served by the Immortal Earl of Great Mystery the cinnabar fruits of the Crimson Tree and the three-mystery purple nectar.

They then ascended the Central Marchmount, Kunlun Mountain—this is not the Five Marchmounts of the human realm, but the divine mountain upon the sea. The Eastern Marchmount is called Broad Mulberry, the Southern Marchmount Longli, the Western Marchmount Lovely Agriculture, the Northern Marchmount Vast Wilds, and the Central Marchmount Kunlun.

They toured the Jasper Terrace of the Dark Garden, entered the Hall of Hearing the Wind, beheld the Seven Treasures’ Jasper Grove, listened to the nine-plumed singing phoenix, gazed upon the sun, moon, and stars in radiant exchange, and looked down upon the Four Heavens as if upon their palms. There stood golden terraces and jade towers, palaces of seven treasures, luminous day and night, the abode of the Heavenly Emperor and divine kings. Each face had one hundred and forty gates, with forests of pearls and seven treasures, purple blossoms and cinnabar fruits, jasper leaves and jade flowers. Lutes and zithers harmonized, all attuned to the pitch pipes. The Perfected Ones of the Nine Chambers of Great Mystery dwelled there. All was the natural celestial kitchen; ingress and egress were at will. The heavenly retinue was exquisite beyond compare, the music beyond description—truly the Pure Capital of the cosmos, the grand spectacle of divine perfected beings.

Yin the Perfected, having first received the office of Youthful Perfection, accompanied the Lord Lao on this journey through the five sacred mountains of the mortal realm, the grotto-heavens and palaces, and the watery abodes of the four seas and rivers. Their lords were sometimes called Sacred Emperors, sometimes Perfected Kings, sometimes Dukes or Marquises. In their consultations, they often discussed the Two Modes and Three Radiances, the yin-yang qi and seasons, the length and brevity of kalpas, the rise and fall of emperors’ fates, the fortunes and misfortunes of the people, the ranks and demotions of those who cultivate the Dao and ascend as immortals, the methods of harmonizing the black sequence and averting the Yang’s ninety-six calamitous conjunctions, or the profound principles of the Dao’s origin, its virtue, and the subtleties of the formless and soundless. The Lord Lao answered all as requested, his words arcane and unfathomable.

It was during this western journey to Tortoise Terrace that he expounded *The Scripture of Constant Purity and Stillness* to the Queen Mother of the West. Ge the Immortal Elder said: "I once received it from the Lord Emperor of the Eastern Culmen, who received it from the Lord Emperor of the Golden Gate, who received it from the Queen Mother of the West. The Queen Mother transmitted it orally, without written record. Now I inscribe and record it for the world."

The Lord Lao said to Yin: "I shall now ascend with you to the Jade Dawn to pay court and tour the imperial homeland." As he spoke, the numinous sounds of the Eight Assemblies arose, clouds and darkness gathered on all sides, and they slowly ascended into the void. They traversed the Nine Heavens, where the Lords of the Heavens all came to welcome the Lord Lao. He entered their palaces, where jasper blossoms, jade fruits, moon nectar, cloud wine, and spirit mushrooms were arrayed, their radiance illuminating the seats. Heavenly music resounded in grand assembly, the divine songs surpassing all description. The Perfected Emperors of the Heavens all bowed in reverence and inquired of the natural, supreme, mysterious, and perfect Way of the Primordial One. Thereafter, the Great Perfected of the Upper Prime, the Immortal Duke of the Great Ultimate of the Middle Prime, and the Immortal Earl of the Great Clarity of the Lower Prime arrived, riding cloud chariots of azure haze and yellow essence, drawn by azure rainbows, qilin, and lions. They unfurled canopies of intertwining radiance, phoenixes, and flying cranes, bearing the banners of the Sun Essence and the Perfected Mandate, the standards of the Moon Radiance and the Demon Subduers, and the flags of the Star Light and the Immortal Mandate. Divine immortals and numinous officials attended them.

The assembly numbered one hundred fifty thousand—seventy thousand from the Upper Prime, fifty thousand from the Middle Prime, and thirty thousand from the Lower Prime—each descending in hierarchical order with their cloud chariots, canopies, ornaments, banners, and standards. Bearing incense and flowers, they floated through the void to arrive, prostrating and bowing in welcome as the Lord Lao ascended to the Vermilion City of the Sun Gate and the Jeweled Palace of Elixir Pearls in the Upper Clarity. There, the Lord Lao commanded them to pay homage to the Most High Jade Dawn, the Supreme Lord of the Great Dao.

Then the Dao Lord bestowed upon them twelve treasures: the Ring of Joy’s Unyielding Elixir Fruit, the Hidden Dragon Lingzhi, the Jeweled Bloom Jade Nectar, and nine other divine gifts. Thereafter, following the Lord Lao, they descended to the numinous mountains of Tianshui, proceeding onward to the Western Regions. This is the present-day Mount Qiling in Qinzhou. There stand the Terrace of Western Ascension and the Hall of the Sacred Countenance, both situated beneath the cliffs.

Earlier, when the Lord Lao departed the Zhou, he journeyed west to transform the lands of Da Qin, Anxi, Yuezhi, Wuge, and Tianzhu, where he was known as the Ancient Master. The kings and peoples of those realms embraced his teachings, cultivating goodness, softening their fierce and unruly customs, and exalting the virtues of courtesy and deference. Did he then return to the Middle Kingdom? According to the *Tang Comprehensive Institutions*, Da Qin—also called Lixuan or Fulin—lies forty thousand *li* from Chang’an. Its land is level and vast, comprising over four hundred cities, with dozens of smaller states paying tribute. Its people are tall and resemble those of the Middle Kingdom. Their ruler is not hereditary; rather, the worthy are chosen by consensus. The Lord Lao had long since enlightened these lands, though the world remained unaware, and thus no records survive. Only now, with the Perfected Master Yin’s mission of transformation, have accounts of these events spread to the Middle Kingdom.

Liu Xiang’s *Biographies of the Immortals* states: “The Lord Lao cultivated essence and qi, dwelling in Zhou for many years before departing for Da Qin.” The *Biography of Perfected Master Yin* recounts: “Only when Yin accompanied Laozi across the Flowing Sands did they journey together.” Ge Hong’s *Preface to the Elixirs* notes: “In my youth, I wished to travel to Fulin to witness the customs of the lands beyond the seas. I heard that the people of Da Qin revered the Dao, living in purity and harmony, their laws just and upright. Merchants from the Middle Kingdom were called ‘men of Zhou.’” During the Tang, envoys from Da Qin came to court, wearing crowns and silk robes. They paid homage before the image of the Lord Lao, donated funds to restore a Daoist abbey, and copied the Emperor Minghuang’s annotated *Daode Jing* and *Huangdi’s Classic of the Hidden Talisman* to take back to their land—further proof that the Lord Lao once visited their realm.

Now, accompanied by the Supreme Perfected Master Yin Xi, he once more journeyed to Khotan to spread the teachings, briefly resting in the mountains and valleys near the outskirts. The king of the Pure Harmony realm was originally the Celestial Elder of the Eastern Ultimate, who in a former life ascended to become the King of Wondrous Brahmā. Whenever the Lord Lao passed through his domain, the king would bow from afar and vow: “May the Compassionate One deliver this disciple. In future lives, may I always serve as your disciple and attain the rank of a lofty Perfected.” Later, though he exhausted his celestial blessings and was reborn among mortals, residual merit allowed him to become a ruler of the Qiang and Hu peoples—namely, King Fan Tuo of Khotan.

Once born into the human realm, he forgot his past lives and indulged in boundless slaughter, frequently setting out on hunting expeditions. Unwilling to forsake his earlier vow, the Lord Lao descended to instruct him. As the king was hunting in the mountains, a five-colored radiance pierced the sun. Marveling at the sight, he followed it and encountered the Lord Lao. The king asked, “Who are you, sir?”

The Lord Lao replied, “I am a cultivator of the Dao.”

The Perfected, who penetrate the profound and fathom the subtle, comprehend all celestial and terrestrial tongues, as well as the speech of foreign lands and even the sounds of other beings. Thus, when conversing with the Hu king, the Lord Lao spoke in the language of his realm. The attendant Perfected, however, recorded the exchange in the proper script, which later circulated in the Middle Kingdom as the *Scripture of Transforming the Barbarians*. This is akin to a man of Qi who, though able to speak like a man of Chu, would naturally use the southern dialect when addressing a Chu native—yet when committing the conversation to writing, would revert to the language of Qi. The same hand records both, requiring no translation.

The king asked, “What is this thing called the Dao? Truly, explain it to me.”

Lord Lao said: “The Dao is the primordial darkness of emptiness and nothingness, the undifferentiated spontaneity of the primordial chaos. It has no exterior, no interior, no high, no low. Heaven and earth arise from it, and the myriad things take form through it. It cannot be named, so we force a designation and call it the Dao.”

The king said: “Since I established this kingdom, I have never heard of such matters. From whence have you come to dwell here?”

Lord Lao said: “I am the numinous root of the ten thousand qi, the primordial origin of the Way of divine transcendents. In heaven, I am revered by all the sages; on earth, I am the teacher and lord of the ten thousand states. Above and below heaven, all are my officials. My spirit revolves without limit, and my transformative power shapes the cosmos. I distribute the sun and moon, the constellations and the void. I pace the heavens and measure the earth, composing scriptures and texts. Heaven and earth, the myriad things—all are bound to the Lord of the Dao by fate. All that has form arises from the Dao.

I am born from the formless, grow in the nameless. I descend as the teacher of emperors, following the world’s transformations to instruct it. Now I have come from Zhou.”

Knowing the king to be stubborn and coarse, difficult to transform, and unversed in humble speech, Lord Lao spoke plainly to him, hoping to awaken him to his past causes.

The king said: “I wonder what methods you used to teach people in that land.”

Lord Lao said: “In the Middle Kingdom, I always used the Daoist methods to enlighten them.”

The king said: “What are Daoist methods?”

Lord Lao said: “Daoist methods are the Supreme Numinous Treasure, born before heaven and earth. Vast, it encompasses all; subtle, it embraces all. Without it, heaven cannot shine, earth cannot take form, spirits cannot enter the unseen, and humans cannot live or grow. Thus, the teeming myriad things take the Dao as their root. The snake that attains it becomes a dragon; the bird, a phoenix; the beast, a qilin; the ordinary person, a transcendent.

Those who cultivate it wander freely in the great void, transform their five viscera, alter their appearance, command ghosts and spirits, and conceal or reveal themselves at will—this is the Way of the highest transcendents.”

The king said: “What you call the Numinous Treasure is indeed rare, but my wisdom and strength are shallow and weak. I am unequal to such matters. I wonder what method would be suitable for teaching in this kingdom.”

Lord Lao said: “Observing the customs of your kingdom, I see that the people harbor perversity and rebellion. They slaughter one another, cruelly harming all living things. Lustful and greedy, they daily commit evil deeds, bringing about their own downfall. If you wish to live a full lifespan and die without misfortune, you should practice the method of the *Futu* (Buddha).

It cuts away inner and outer defilements without harming the body, hence it is called *Futu*.”

The king said: “What is this method?”

Lord Lao said: “First, abandon the self and others, and cease the boiling and killing. All living things, however dull, love their bodies and lives. Now, you, O king, are fond of hunting and kill without measure. The Way of Heaven loves life and deeply abhors killing and harm. If you wish to preserve your lifespan, I hope you will cease hunting.”

The king’s countenance changed, and he said: “How strange! Are you trying to disrupt our customs, criticize my hunting, and forbid me from killing? Since I established this kingdom, generation after generation has hunted, and we have still preserved our heavenly years. If you have any needs, I will send supplies. Do not speak such words to confuse my people and make them abandon their livelihoods.” With that, he galloped away.

Later, while hunting, he again came to Mount Qu and once more encountered Lord Lao. He asked: “What crime is there in killing that you forbid my hunting? When the body dies and becomes empty, what retribution will there be?”

Lord Lao said: “Your fondness for hunting disregards the four seasons and fails to avoid the breeding and nurturing of life. How can this not be a violation of Heaven and a cruelty to creatures? By law, it should cut short your blessings and diminish your lifespan. Heaven has spirits who oversee killing and record human transgressions. When your life ends one day, the earthly officials will seize your hun soul and confine it, subjecting it to interrogation and suffering, forever consigning it to the prisons of sin.”

The king said: “Apart from killing, are there other benevolent transgressions?”

Lord Lao said: “The sin of killing is the gravest. Accumulated grievances become karma, and in the five destinies and four modes of birth, the retribution never ceases. Arrogance, violence, indulgence in wine and lust, greed, deceit, and belief in demonic sorcerers—all these are also sins. After death, one enters hell, and the hun soul suffers torment. If you wish to reform and believe in the *Futu*, in life you will enjoy wealth, honor, health, and peace; in death, your hun soul will not be bound, and your joy will be boundless. Is this not good?”

The king said: “Very good. Please explain it to me in detail.”

Lord Lao said: “The *Futu* shaves off hair and beard, wears faded robes, bares one shoulder, and walks barefoot to restrain the body. It upholds precepts to cease evil, eats at midday and repents of past wrongs, and constantly practices cessation and contemplation to discipline the mind. Those who cultivate it may, at best, preserve their bodies and protect the state; at least, they may avoid the苦 realms.”

The king said: “It is good, but my wisdom and understanding are shallow, and my habits are long-ingrained. I cannot uphold precepts, practice cessation and contemplation, nor wear coarse robes and walk barefoot. However, the affairs of the state and the army are weighty, and I still hope for blessings. Might I first practice midday eating, gradually reduce killing and hunting, and thereby transform my people? Would that be acceptable?”

Lord Lao said: “... It is acceptable.”

The king said: “I do not yet understand the method. Please instruct me.”

Wishing to transform the kingdom and make all believe and turn toward the Way, Lord Lao said to the king: “Today, I will prepare a midday meal for you. I hope you will lead your ministers and the common people to come and partake, so that they may observe the method.”

The king said: “However divine you may be, how can you provide for the multitudes of my kingdom?”

Lord Lao said: “I hope you will deign to lead the people here. Do not worry.”

The king then returned to his kingdom and set a date for the gathering. Lord Lao scattered the five clouds in the valleys of the five directions. According to their directional colors, they transformed into palaces, adorned with curtains and canopies. Hundreds of millions of flying transcendents became attendants, and the heavenly kitchens provided countless delicacies.

The king secretly sent men to observe, and when they saw this, they were greatly astonished. The entire kingdom then came to the gathering. After seven days, the assembly dispersed, and Lord Lao bestowed upon the king and his ministers and people all manner of rare treasures and silken fabrics.

The king addressed Lord Lao, saying: “We have witnessed the rites and rituals. We wish to prepare a midday feast and hope to lead our followers to attend.”

Lord Lao replied: “My followers are quite numerous. I fear you may not be able to provide for them.”

The king, astonished, said: “I rule a great kingdom, possessing all things. How could we fail to provide? Let us set a date for the gathering.”

Lord Lao then said to True Man Yin Xi: “The Hu are stubborn and unyielding, their hearts still unrepentant. We may use this gathering to transform them with skillful means.” He then summoned all the transcendents from across the land to attend the assembly. Those who came filled the roads without cease for over a month.

The king’s granaries and treasuries were nearly exhausted before the feast was even half complete, and he began to regret his decision. Summoning his ministers, he plotted: “Why are these Daoist followers so numerous? I prepared this midday feast to benefit the kingdom, yet now they drain my granaries. I fear this is a scheme by neighboring enemies, sending them to harm our land. If not, they must be demonic spirits. If we do not act swiftly, our kingdom will suffer greater ruin. You must burn and kill them at once to preserve our land.”

Thereupon, the Hu amassed firewood in the outer suburbs, their smoke and flames blotting out the sky. They surrounded Lord Lao and his attending transcendents with troops, driving them into the fire. Lord Lao and the transcendents entered the flames with serene composure, appearing and vanishing amidst the smoke, their forms growing ever more radiant.

The king then ordered them cast into the depths of the abyss. As Lord Lao entered the water, the waves surged, and his body emitted a brilliant light. Enraged, the king ordered great cauldrons heated to boil them. Lord Lao calmly entered the seething broth, laughing and conversing as if at ease.

The Hu king, now alarmed, realized they could not be killed. Yet, having already committed such acts, he was ashamed at his failure and hastily dispatched messengers to neighboring kingdoms: “A demonic spirit, sometimes old, sometimes young, ever-changing in form, dwells in the mountains and valleys of my land. I cast it into fire and water, yet it cannot be slain. I fear it will bring greater harm to your kingdoms. I urge you to swiftly raise your armies and join me in exterminating it.”

Within ten days, the Hu forces assembled, surrounding Lord Lao. Drums and horns resounded, bows were drawn, swords unsheathed, and the encirclement closed on all sides. Yet Lord Lao remained at ease within the siege, untroubled and without anger.

Suddenly, wind and thunder converged from all directions, the earth trembled, and the Hu soldiers’ arrows and stones turned back upon them. Spears and halberds shattered, armor and shields fell silent. Terrified, the Hu forces scattered in panic.

The Hu king trembled and addressed his people: “How do we know these elders are not great sages? Now I wish to serve them. What say you?”

The Hu replied: “Great King, since fire, water, boiling, and arrows could not harm them, are they not sages? But we fear their accumulated wrath may yet destroy our kingdom. We urge you to act swiftly.”

The king said: “Indeed. I shall go before them myself, bound and prostrate, to beg for mercy and forgiveness.” Thereupon, he bound himself and knelt before Lord Lao, pressing his forehead to the ground a thousand times, pleading for pardon.

At that moment, Lord Lao, wishing to strengthen their faith, declared: “I am the Most High Lord Lao, born before the limitless, having endured kalpas as numerous as grains of sand. I have delivered countless beings, presiding over the thirty-six heavens, governing the three realms, and commanding all spirits. Retribution for sin and fortune alike proceed from me. I came to transform you out of pity for your transgressions. Yet you, relying on your wealth and pride, refused to comply. You invited me to a midday feast only to humiliate me. Your accumulated evil clings to you—what use is regret now? The divine soldiers of heaven were poised to destroy your kingdom and eradicate your people.”

Before he finished speaking, the Hu king and his followers beheld celestial warriors, divine kings, and armored soldiers beyond count, riding dragons and tigers through the air, their angry voices shaking the earth. The king and his people, terrified and wailing, pressed their foreheads to the ground until they bled, prostrating themselves and pleading: “We were born in the borderlands, wild and foolish by nature, disbelieving in the Dao and its laws, and insulting the Heavenly Venerable. We weigh our crimes and know that even a thousand deaths would be too light a punishment. The annihilation of our entire kingdom would not suffice to repay our debt. We beg only for forgiveness and the gift of life. We vow to uphold your teachings without regret until death, dedicating our kingdom to your service without cease.”

Lord Lao said: “Enough, enough. Even with your foreheads bloodied from kowtowing, I cannot yet be assured. I fear you may yet repent. What pledge can you offer as oath?”

The king replied: “If the Heavenly Venerable and Great Sage deign to pardon our sins and grant us a new beginning, we vow that all men and women of our kingdom shall never marry nor take spouses, shaving their heads and beards to serve your teachings. If we violate this oath, let our deaths stand as witness.”

Lord Lao said: “Very well. Your oath is weighty, and I am moved to pity. Yet your cruelty has endured so long that I have little cause to spare you. To destroy your kingdom and reduce it to dust, sinking it ten thousand fathoms into the earth, would be as effortless as flicking a finger. But I cherish life, so I pardon you. You must adhere strictly to my precepts and never betray them again.”

The king bowed and said: “We reverently accept your command.”

Thereupon, the divine soldiers vanished, and heaven and earth returned to tranquility. Lord Lao then instructed the king: “Now that I have released you from your sins, you must uphold the Buddha’s teachings to enjoy eternal blessings and ever dwell in the human realm.”

The king and his people, filled with awe and joy, pressed their faces to the ground, palms joined in reverence and gratitude. Lord Lao then cast his merciful and benevolent light upon the king’s body. Suddenly, the king recalled his past causes, awakened with remorse, and wept bitterly, tears streaming down his face. He bowed twice, kowtowing, and said: “From kalpas without number, my evil roots have never been severed. Though I have been reborn, I soon fall again, rising and sinking like a bucket in a well. Had I not encountered the Heavenly Venerable’s great compassion, moved by my karmic connections to grant me deliverance, I would have forever languished in the evil destinies without hope of escape. Now I vow to abandon my kingdom and enter the mountains to uphold the Great Precepts, atoning for my people’s sins and praying for their blessings. I hope to be delivered from the world and forever escape the wheel of rebirth.”

The Heavenly Venerable said: “Excellent. If you wish to dwell in the mountains and study the Dao, let your wife, children, and ministers decide for themselves whether to stay or go. Those who remain shall uphold the Five Precepts and the Ten Good Deeds, while those who follow you into the mountains shall observe the Six Precepts.”

The king replied: “Indeed, this is my sincere wish.”

The Heavenly Venerable said: “If you truly desire to hear the Dharma, you must purify yourselves and observe the fast. When the fast is complete, gather together, and I shall transmit the teachings to you.”

The king bowed in gratitude and withdrew. He then relinquished his throne, passing it to his son, and led from his kingdom twelve thousand five hundred men and women—ministers and commoners who had awakened good hearts—to arrive together. They prostrated themselves, kneeling long in reverence to await instruction.

The Perfected Lord Yin, having just returned from partaking in the celestial feast of the Heavenly Emperor, descended with a halo of radiant light crowning his head and lotus blossoms beneath his feet. Accompanied by jade lads and jade maidens, celestial musicians, and clouds of fragrant flowers, he descended from the heavens.

The Heavenly Venerable pointed to the Perfected Lord Yin and said to the Hu king: “This is my disciple, the Supreme Perfected Lord, whom I appoint as your master.”

He then instructed the king and the assembled multitude to first repent the transgressions of their three karmic actions and six senses—all hidden sins and manifest faults, the ten evils and five rebellions, and all misdeeds arising from adherence to heterodox views—committed since time without beginning, as well as the karmic offenses accumulated by their parents and kin across past lives. When their repentance was complete, he said:

“Now, each of you, compose your thoughts and calm your spirits. Listen carefully to my words, for today I shall transmit to you the Five Precepts and the Ten Good Deeds.

The Five Precepts are: first, do not kill; second, do not consume intoxicants; third, do not steal; fourth, do not engage in illicit sexual conduct; fifth, do not speak falsely. Those who uphold these precepts shall be guarded by twenty-five precept spirits.

The Ten Good Deeds are: first, filial piety; second, loyalty; third, compassion; fourth, forbearance; fifth, generosity; sixth, diligence; seventh, offerings; eighth, faith and devotion; ninth, exhortation to virtue; tenth, tranquility and concentration. Those who practice these shall be protected by ten celestial spirits of goodness.”

Having transmitted these, he further expounded to the king and his followers the Ten Precepts, the Eighteen Precepts, the Twenty-Four Precepts, the Two Hundred Fifty Precepts, and the Five Hundred Precepts for Women.

He then instructed them to observe the Three Long Month Fasts in the first, fifth, and ninth months of the year. Additionally, they were to keep the Six Fasts each month on the first, eighth, fourteenth, fifteenth, twenty-eighth, and twenty-ninth days—known as the Fasts of the Four Heavenly Kings. Furthermore, they were to observe the Ten Straight Fasts each month:

On the first day, invoke the Boundless Great Flower Heavenly Venerable to escape the sufferings of the Fire Cart Hell.
On the eighth day, invoke the Supreme Jade Dawn Heavenly Venerable to escape the sufferings of the Dark and Cold Hell.
On the fourteenth day, invoke the Perfected Lord of Transcendent Salvation to escape the sufferings of the Scalding Cauldron Hell.
On the fifteenth day, invoke the Jade Treasure August Heavenly Venerable to escape the sufferings of the Wind and Thunder Hell.
On the eighteenth day, invoke the Heavenly Venerable of Life-Sustaining Salvation to escape the sufferings of the Bronze Pillar Hell.
On the twenty-third day, invoke the Mysterious Perfected Heavenly Venerable of Myriad Blessings to escape the sufferings of the Fire Screen Hell.
On the twenty-fourth day, invoke the Supreme Numinous Void Heavenly Venerable to escape the sufferings of the Dismemberment Hell.
On the twenty-eighth day, invoke the Supreme Wondrous Ultimate Heavenly Venerable to escape the sufferings of the Diamond Hell.
On the twenty-ninth day, invoke the Perfected August Cavern Spirit Heavenly Venerable to escape the sufferings of the Universal Torment Hell.
On the thirtieth day, invoke the Jade Void Luminous August Heavenly Venerable to escape the karmic retributions of the Three Realms.

On these days, the heavens of the ten directions descend to observe the good and evil of humanity. One should fast for a day and a night, abstaining from all but plain food, and recite scriptures to escape the sufferings of the Nine Hells and the karmic retributions of the Three Realms. Those unable to fast may consume the flesh of animals that have died of natural causes.”

Then, addressing the four assemblies, he said: “The king has now received deliverance. I shall proceed to transform other kingdoms.”

The king again bowed deeply and entreated: “Great Sage Heavenly Venerable, now that you are about to journey afar—a reunion most difficult to attain—our entire kingdom has received your grace, and our longing is boundless. We humbly request that you and our master, along with your disciples, remain to practice the Dao for thirty days before we bid you farewell. We implore your consent.”

The Heavenly Venerable replied: “Very well.”

Thereupon, the king, together with his heir, ministers, and people, established a grand Daoist sanctuary, presenting abundant offerings, hymns, and praises. When the appointed day arrived, though they still wished to detain him, the Perfected Lord said: “Excellent, excellent indeed are your offerings. Yet I must attend my master in transforming other kingdoms and cannot remain.”

Then the Hu king gathered his robes, rose, and advanced on his knees. Bowing deeply, he joined his palms and said: “My master is about to leave me, and I have no further counsel to seek. I beseech you to bestow upon me an essential teaching to uphold for the rest of my life.”

The Perfected Lord said: “I shall tell you: though the precepts are not the ultimate Dao, they are indeed the stepping stones to entering it. If you uphold them steadfastly, supreme tranquility will arise in this life, and wisdom will dawn. A nine-tiered terrace begins with heaps of earth; a tree that embraces a man’s arms grows from a tender shoot; a journey of a thousand miles begins with a single step.

He further said: “The customs of your kingdom are fierce and unyielding, and the lingering habits of asura nature remain unremoved. My master’s teachings exalt gentleness and humility, and you must practice non-contention. Thus, none in the world can contend with you. If you can embody non-contention, in this present life you shall be foremost among men, and in future lives you shall attain the fruits of goodness.”

Having received this teaching, the king prostrated himself in gratitude. The Perfected Lord then addressed the king again: “Come forward. Do you know that the form of the body is illusory and that the myriad phenomena are not real? If you seek the joy of quiescent extinction, you must eliminate defilements, cease thoughts, and refrain from perceiving objects of desire. Even a single speck of dust, if clung to, will burden your nature.”

At these words, the king awakened and bowed his head in gratitude, saying: “Indeed, indeed.”

Thereupon, the Perfected Lord, accompanied by the assembled immortals and attendants, followed the Heavenly Venerable out of the borders of Jibin. The entire kingdom’s ministers and people, filled with sorrowful longing, bowed in farewell.

The king then entered the mountains with his followers to cultivate the Dao, instructing one another in turn. The Heavenly Venerable proceeded to the kingdom of Tiaozhi, where the king revered heterodox demonic masters, calling them sages. His disciples, numbering over three thousand, were all supported by the court. For twelve years, they widely practiced sorcery, and the king held them in deep reverence.

One day, the demonic masters said to the king: “There is a great mountain in the kingdom that blocks the path, obstructs the source of streams, and causes floods from time to time. Can you masters remove it?”

The demonic masters replied: “We can. Tomorrow, we shall remove it for the king.”

Just then, the Heavenly Venerable arrived and sat beneath a tree, observing the demonic masters performing their strange arts. They made hand seals, and the mountain trembled slightly.

Perceiving that the king could be converted, the Heavenly Venerable said to Perfected Lord Yin: “The demonic masters are attempting to remove this mountain. If they succeed, the king will believe them to be true sages, and we will no longer be able to convert him.” He then instructed Perfected Lord Yin to restrain the mountain from afar with his spiritual power, and the mountain ceased to move.

The demonic masters said to one another: “Earlier, the mountain moved, but now it does not. Could there be someone with extraordinary arts restraining it?” They hurried to the mountain’s summit and scanned the surroundings. Spotting the Perfected Lord in the distance, they rushed to seize him and brought him before the king.

The king asked: “Who are you?”

The Perfected Lord replied: “A disciple of the Most High.”

The king asked: “Who is the Most High?”

“A sagely being,” he answered.

The king asked: “Where is the Most High now?”

“Having left Jibin to teach the Dao, he is now nearby, beneath the mountain.”

The king immediately ordered his attendants to welcome him. Once the Heavenly Venerable arrived at the palace, the king invited him to sit and asked: “People say the Old Master is a sage. There is a great mountain in my kingdom that obstructs the path and troubles the people. The demonic masters could not remove it. I wish the sage would remove it for me.”

The Heavenly Venerable said: “Very well.”

The king asked: “How many men will be needed?”

“This is a small matter. Why would men be needed?”

Fearing the king would no longer trust them, the demonic masters sought another chance to remove the mountain themselves.

The king said: “Very well.”

Thereupon, the three thousand demonic masters all performed their arts, exhausting their techniques, but the mountain remained unmoved.

The king said: “Your arts are exhausted. Please, Old Master, remove it.”

The Heavenly Venerable took his nine-jointed staff and swept the mountain away as if it were a clod of earth. The waters flowed smoothly, and the land was level. The king bowed his head in reverence, seeking to become a disciple, and no longer served the demonic masters. The entire kingdom’s ministers and people, filled with awe and admiration, all wished to take refuge and receive the teachings.

The Heavenly Venerable returned to the tree beneath which he had sat. The king invited him to stay in the palace, but he refused. The king’s faith deepened, and he crawled forward on his knees, kowtowing and entreating: “I have heard that sages do not go against the wishes of others. I humbly beg you to expound the wondrous Dao.”

The Heavenly Venerable said: “I have heard that true kings are no ordinary men. Their virtue shades all things, their conduct aligns with Heaven and Earth, they enlighten humanity and the heavens, and they rule the kingdom. If the king cultivates the Dao, the land will long endure, armor will be laid aside, weapons stilled, and the people will be at peace. If the Way is absent in the world, the land will collapse, disasters will multiply, and poison will afflict ministers and people. The king should devote himself to serving the Dao and seeking truth. Then he will be free from calamity, his lineage will prosper, neighboring kingdoms will stand in awe, evil will not encroach, his life will be long, and he will stand alongside Heaven. The moon will not suffer nightly eclipses, nor the sun dim by day. Fathers will not mourn their sons, nor ministers betray their lords. The king should embody this and honor the Dao and revere Heaven.”

The king said: “I humbly beg for your compassion, to bestow your teachings and reveal the essentials of the Dao.”

The Heavenly Venerable said: “I fear the king may grow arrogant and easily lose heart, so I dare not lightly impart them.”

Thereupon, the king summoned his ministers and the people, and for seven days and nights they performed purification rites and made offerings. Each held colored silks and gold as tokens of their sincerity, bearing incense and flowers, kowtowing a hundred times, and entreating thrice with earnest devotion: “We have heard that the Great Sage enlightened Jibin, and distant and near kingdoms all received the true teachings. We are weak and unworthy, yet to receive your compassion. We beg you to reveal the great Dao, to enlighten our foolish people, that the entire kingdom may follow it for generations.”

Knowing their hearts were sincere, the Heavenly Venerable said: “I have already instructed my disciple, the Perfected Lord of the Supreme, to serve as teacher to the kings of various lands, expounding the scriptures and precepts, purifying the heart and restraining the form, so that in life they may dwell in peace, free from untimely death, and in death attain liberation, not falling into evil destinies. If the king reverently upholds these, he will gain boundless blessings.”

The king said: “Indeed, indeed.”

The Heavenly Venerable said: “Good. The king may return to the palace, burn incense, and meditate.”

Thereupon, the Perfected Lord manifested a transformation body in the king’s palace hall, seated upon a precious lotus, and bestowed upon the king the Eighteen Precepts. The king rejoiced, joined his palms in reverence, and vowed to abandon his kingdom and worldly attachments, upholding these precepts without fail. The Perfected Lord also expounded the Three Precepts to the king, his ministers, and the people—men and women alike—who had newly awakened, and the Five Precepts to those whose hearts were already subdued.

The king further asked: “The Most High Sage first descended to the East. Did he teach the same Dharma as that which you have now bestowed?”

The Perfected Lord said: “My master adapts his teachings to the world. Long ago in the Divine Continent, he taught the Dao of immortality to enlighten the realm. From the Three Sovereigns to the Five Emperors, all who cultivated it attained immortality.”

The king asked: “What is the difference between the Buddha and the immortals?”

The Perfected Lord said: “The Buddha turns the mind toward the Dao, cutting off the six senses, taking emptiness and quiescence as the essence, and through spiritual awakening of the nature, attains the fruit of Buddhahood. Yet when life ends and form perishes, they ultimately enter quiescence, becoming nothing more than yin spirits. The immortals cultivate truth and realize the Dao, uniting form and spirit in wondrous perfection. They manifest transformations, ascend and descend between being and non-being, and attain eternal life without extinction.”

The Hu king said: “Why not teach this to our foreign lands, that we may share in it?”

The Perfected Lord said: “The Dao of life is most weighty, a secret of Heaven. Without having planted its roots in former lives, how could one easily hear of it?”

The Hu king bowed in gratitude, then built a pure abode and had an image made to honor the Heavenly Venerable. He then accompanied the Perfected Lord to convert other kingdoms, eventually reaching the kingdom of Yutian. The people of Yutian, having heard that the Heavenly Venerable was coming, led the entire kingdom to welcome him at the southern canal mountain, also called Mount Tantai, where they had built a pure abode to invite the Heavenly Venerable to reside.

The Heavenly Venerable resided in quietude, neither eating nor drinking. The king and his ministers sought his counsel morning and evening, entreating him to share his teachings. The Heavenly Venerable instructed them in the methods of fasting and purification.

The king said: “The sagely master speaks only of fasting and purification—are there yet further teachings?”

The Heavenly Venerable replied: “My Dao is boundless, adapting to customs and times, establishing transformations according to the moment. In the Central Lands, I propagate the Supreme Orthodox, Perfect, and Limitless Dao. Those who cultivate it transcend the world and prolong their years; when their practice is complete and merits perfected, they ascend to immortality, riding dragons and driving darkness to ascend to heaven in broad daylight. I expound the thirty-six divisions of the Three Caverns’ true scriptures and the eighteen thousand chapters of the Great Vehicle’s secondary teachings. From emperors to common folk, those who cultivate and uphold them receive rewards and ascend as immortals according to their depth of practice—some become supreme sages, others attain lofty perfection. Their ranks, noble or humble, number thirty-seven grades, and they soar freely, their transformations unfathomable.

There are also the ways of refining golden elixirs and divine cinnabar, the secrets of exhaling and inhaling, swallowing radiance, and nurturing the darkness; the methods of softening the body and harmonizing the spirit, guiding and pulling; the scriptures of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors, the arts of refining and controlling; and the formulas for ingesting divine mushrooms, sacred herbs, malleable gold, flowing water, and jade. As for concealing the heavens and hiding the moon, transforming one’s radiance and refining one’s form, commanding ghosts and spirits, controlling heaven and earth, talismanic charts and precious registers, jade seals and golden writings—myriad scriptures and a thousand canons—all these are used to enlighten later learners. Yet they may only be transmitted to those whose past roots are pure, whose qi is harmonious and unadulterated, who delight in the Dao and rejoice in truth, whose sincerity never wavers, and whose body and mind resonate in accord.

Your people, however, have accumulated lives of rigidity and harshness. It is fitting to temper their bodies and minds with the methods of the Floating Vehicle. If they practice diligently without slackening, after the end of their lives, they will receive blessings and be reborn in heaven.

The Dao’s methods are profound and subtle. Even if I were to teach them to you, they would be difficult to comprehend—not out of my reluctance, but because sentient beings, since time without beginning, have been miserly, greedy, and lustful, insatiable in their consumption, nourishing their pus and blood, deepening their karmic roots. Birth, aging, sickness, and death; noxious winds and poisonous qi; a hundred illnesses assail them. Their rebirths and endowments differ, yet day and night they cycle through ten thousand births and ten thousand deaths. Some remain trapped in the womb, never to be born; others die at the moment of delivery. Some change with every thought at one or two years of age, never knowing a moment’s peace in a single day. This is truly because of their gluttonous consumption of filth, the steaming corruption of flesh and bone, which damages the organs and dissolves even gold and jade, making the body difficult to endure.

I neither eat nor drink, embracing the Dao in its naturalness, transforming without limit, living forever without death. I constantly expound the scriptures and teachings to deliver sentient beings. Yet ordinary people, deluded, cannot believe or apply them. I am ever concerned and grieved for them, and thus I teach them to fast, to lessen their suffering and illness. Those who practice this will obtain boundless blessings.”

The king said: “I have heard of the Buddha, who has recently appeared in Jibin, transmitting the Dharma and delivering people. Is he the sagely master?”

The Heavenly Venerable replied: “This is the teaching of the Floating Vehicle. The kings and peoples of the Western Regions have all taken refuge in it, ceasing to kill and eliminating lust, tempering and softening their natures. They will receive karmic rewards and be reborn constantly among humans and gods. If you now uphold and practice this without slackening, maintaining purity within and without, with a mind illumined and perfectly bright, then sins will not arise, and blessings will naturally grow. Through kalpas without end, you will always know joy.”

The king deeply believed and revered these words, bowing in respect to receive the command.

The *Annals of the Primordial Sage*, Scroll Five, compiled by the Song Dynasty Master of Observing the Return, the Great Master Gao Shi Xie Shouhao.

At this time, the Lord Lao was in the kingdom of Khotan, where he addressed the Perfected Lord Yin, saying: “My transformative teachings have spread throughout these western lands, subduing heterodox doctrines and guiding them to return to the true Dharma. Of all these kingdoms, those already delivered and those not yet delivered—I shall now assemble them all, that they may awaken and enter the Dharma-gate.”

As his words ended, a nine-colored divine radiance illuminated the boundless realms of the West. Wherever the light reached, no distance was too far. In an instant, the city of Vimalakīrti became naturally vast and level, its earth transformed into golden hue. A great Dharma-seat arose, adorned with a hundred treasures. The host of immortals from the Three Realms, the numinous deities of all heavens, assembled together in that moment.

The kings of Khotan, Jibin, Tiaozhi, Anxi, Greater Yuezhi, Zhujuban, Kebantuo, Humiduo, Guduo, Jumi, Xiesu, Xuwuna, Jiuyuejian, Yidan, Wulage, Shifanyan, Hushijian, Hadaluozhi, Persia, Shule, Suiye, Qiuci, Fulin, Yanqi, Gongyue, Seni, Kang, Shi, Cai, Simopan, Cao, He, Greater and Lesser An, Muhu, Wunage, Xunwu, Huoxun, the Western Women’s Kingdom, Daqin, Pāṭaliputra, Dinavuti, Kamaloka, Gandhāra, Udyāna, Dāna, Tamaliṅga, Dulejian, the Arab Empire, Zhifu, Shuman, Damo, Juyao, Cuogu, Jiayemiluo, Jialuo, Bululuo, Nepal, Sri Lanka, Kuśinagara, Vaiśālī, Kapilavastu, Śrāvastī, Campā, Samataṭa, Uḍra, Surāṣṭra, Sindhu, Uliśa, Huli, Sekarika, Mansuman, Niba, Yuediyan, Shemi, the Dwarf Kingdom, Xuanqu, Tuoluoyiluo, Langjieluo, Śrāvastī-Magadha, Linni, and Tianzhu—altogether eighty kingdoms.

According to the *Comprehensive Institutions*: The Saka kings resided in Jibin, west of the Congling Mountains. Their tribes dispersed into several kingdoms. From Shule northwestward, the Xiu-tu and Sun-du tribes were all descendants of the Saka. Sun-du refers to the Brahmin kingdom, also called Magadha. The Han called it *Sun-du*, also known as *Shen-du*. Yan Shigu stated: *Shen-du* is Tianzhu (India). The Saka are the Śākya clan, for the barbarian tongue varies in stress. India comprises dozens of separate kingdoms, such as Śrāvastī and Pāṭaliputra. Though each differs slightly, all are called Tianzhu, hence the Five Indias, also known as the Five Lands of India. In addition, the chieftains of the Qiang tribes were too numerous to count.

From afar, they beheld the wondrous and rare radiance, gazing upon it day and night with joy and praise.

Lord Lao, wielding wondrous spiritual powers, gathered them all and brought them before the Way. Seated in repose upon a jeweled throne, Lord Lao was encircled by the barbarian kings, their queens, and retinues, who each burned incense, scattered flowers, and played music as they circumambulated in reverence and made offerings, beseeching to hear the Dharma.

Lord Lao then expounded the scriptures. When he finished, he further instructed them: "You must always take refuge in the Great Dao and have faith in the Buddha."

At that moment, the kings of Śrāvastī, Magadha, and others—*as the* Tongdian *records, Magadha being a vassal state of India, its capital by the Ajitavatī River, with a mountain called Lingnu, known in the barbarian tongue as Gṛdhrakūṭa, for its peak of blue stone resembles a startled crow*—stepped forward, prostrated themselves, and kneeling in the barbarian fashion, inquired: "We venture to ask: in taking refuge in the Great Dao and having faith in the Buddha, how should one cultivate and what reverence should one observe?"

Lord Lao replied: "Excellent question. Those who follow my teachings must establish pure dwellings, maintain solemnity without doubt, burn incense morning and evening, transmit and receive the scriptural teachings, sustain unbroken mindfulness, awaken themselves and others, cultivate fields of merit, hold fasts and lectures to enlighten, draw near to enlightened masters, compassionately guide later learners, renounce worldly entanglements, refrain from boasting of merit, constantly think of aiding others, wish to behold the enlightened age, dispense Dharma and medicine, desire the Way to spread, avoid false gains, shun vainglory, never deceive the lowly, show reverence to all, succor the aged and orphaned, treat near and far with equality, refrain from wanton killing, forever sever all karmic deeds, and ever uphold the harmony of the Dao. Those who can do thus will gain blessings in this life, ascend to heaven in past lives, and bring joy to future generations."

The kings of Śrāvastī, Udyāna, Magadha, and others all vowed to relinquish their thrones and devote themselves to cultivation. The countless assembly, moved by goodness, bowed their heads in assent and received the teachings with faith.

Master Yin, attending Lord Lao, then returned to the Central Lands. The barbarian multitudes, reluctant to part, entreated repeatedly that they remain in their lands to teach and transform beings. The Master replied: "You see me through form, yet I am fundamentally unmoving—how could there be coming or going? If you must see me in bodily form, then fashion images of my likeness with the five colors, earth, wood, metal, jade, and colored stones, and make offerings in reverence. I shall manifest and expound the Dharma for you."

He further instructed them: "You are all born of the same primordial qi of heaven and earth, each endowed with the true and constant nature. In the future, you shall ultimately attain the supreme, perfectly enlightened, and all-knowing Way. Guard yourselves well, transgress no precepts, and eat no food past midday. When rising after a meal, first recite: *Dwelling in the world as in empty space, like a lotus untouched by water, the mind pure and transcendent above all.* Then bow to the supreme honored one, and only then rise."

The entire assembly rejoiced, prostrated themselves, and received the teachings with faith.

At that time, in those lands there were ninety-six kinds of heterodox teachings that deluded the people, yet none could perceive it. Lord Lao transformed them all, guiding them to the true Way. As Lord Lao prepared to return to the Central Lands, he took leave of the barbarian multitudes. Though they clung to him in reluctance, Lord Lao, not wishing to oppose their good intentions, explained: "I shall briefly return to heaven to arrange the records of humans and spirits, then descend again. After a hundred years, I shall send the Buddha to be born in your lands to teach future generations."

Having spoken, Lord Lao mounted his eight-scene cloud chariot, Master Yin rode a white deer, and the host of immortals astride cranes ascended to heaven amid celestial music and divine attendants.

*According to the* Tongdian’s *General Account of the Western Regions... Khotan lies over two hundred li north of the Congling Mountains, nine thousand eight hundred li from Chang’an, with thirty-two thousand households. It is two thousand eight hundred li northeast to the Protectorate’s seat of governance and fifteen hundred li east to Shanshan. East of the Congling, only these two kingdoms are great. From the Later Han through the Wei, Liang, Later Zhou, Sui, and the Zhenguan era of the Tang, they all dispatched envoys to communicate with China. Of the kingdoms west of Shanshan, the people are mostly deep-eyed and high-nosed, but only this kingdom’s appearance does not resemble the barbarians, being rather like that of the Central Lands.*

From the time of Emperor Wu of Han to the present, imperial edicts, decrees, writs, and tally tokens have all been transmitted and passed down in succession, reverently preserved here. Five hundred *li* to the west of the kingdom stands the Vimalakīrti Monastery, said to be where Lord Lao transformed the barbarians. The assembled Hu gazed up in reverence, prostrating themselves with weeping and lamentation. In an instant, all traces of him vanished, and they each returned to their own lands. Only the king of Jibin, Vindāna, entered the mountains to dwell in a monastery, where he accumulated rigorous practice, upheld the precepts with unwavering discipline, and transformed his people, turning them from evil toward goodness. After his death, he ascended to the Brahma Heaven. Once reborn there, he gained knowledge of his past lives and, recalling his former causes, longed to hear the Great Dao. Lord Lao, not refusing his wish, dispatched Perfected Master Yin to descend and bestow upon him the Dao of True Emptiness and Eternal Quiescence, along with the methods of divine transcendence and liberation.

In the *Book of Later Han*, Pei Kai memorialized Emperor Huan, stating: "Your servant has heard that within the palace, shrines to the Yellow and the Old [Dao] and to the Buddha have been established.
