This Dao is pure and empty, esteeming non-action, cherishing life and abhorring killing, reducing desires and eliminating extravagance. Lord Lao entered the western barbarian lands and first initiated the transformation of the Buddha. The Buddha did not lodge beneath the same mulberry tree for three nights, unwilling to foster long-lasting attachments of affection—such was his utmost refinement. When heavenly spirits sent him beautiful women, the Buddha said, 'These are but leather sacks filled with blood.' And so he did not glance at them. By maintaining such singular focus, he was able to attain the Dao."

Thus it is known that at the founding of the Buddha’s teaching, King Bimbisāra first relinquished his kingdom to enter the mountains for cultivation, and by steadfastly upholding his resolve without breaking the precepts, he was fittingly able in the end to hear the Great Dao and realize Buddhahood.

Emperor Ming of Wei composed the *Hymn to the Scripture of Lord Lao’s Transformation of the Barbarians*:
Before the primal chaos began, Lord Lao was already first,
Existing before the Great Beginning, in the obscurity of primordial darkness.
Without teacher, without ancestor, he was born of spontaneity.
He refined the true and dispersed the uncarved, subtle and mysterious indeed.
Gazing upward to grasp it, it shines resplendent in the celestial heavens;
Looking downward to examine it, it is profound as the abyssal source.
He unfolded the Two Modes to spread transformation,
Illuminated the Three Luminaries to array the heavens.
His nature was without desire, pure and flawless in essence.
His form embraced emptiness and simplicity, wondrous and beyond comprehension.
Stirred, it does not grow turbid; settled, it does not become clear.
Concealed, it is not obscured; revealed, it does not glory.
Who says the heavens are high? Their celestial signs may be marked.
Who says the earth is deep? Its fathomless springs may be dredged.
Yet the Dao—though marked, is not lofty; though dredged, is not profound.
Things receive its form but cannot fathom its source;
Humans are endowed with it but cannot know its entirety.
How splendid is the Dao! It is the ancestor of the myriad things,
The beginning of heaven and earth.
I wish to record it, yet no brush can inscribe it;
I wish to embody it, yet no form can be likened to it.

It further states:
In profound compassion for later generations, he descended in the Chen lineage,
Serving as the Historian of Zhou’s Pillars for nine hundred years.
At the limitless boundary, he returned to Kunlun.
His transformation extended to the barbarian lands, first bestowing teachings upon Shubina,
Then reaching India, whereupon he departed.
His scriptures endure for posterity, everlasting and indestructible.

After Lord Lao transformed the barbarians, he ascended to heaven.
In the fourth year of King Mu’s reign, the jia-shen year, he descended to wander the Eastern Sea,
Reaching Fusang, where he met the Great Emperor and reviewed the ranks of the immortals.
Again, he divided his form to descend upon the Western Sea in the kingdom of King Su-lin,
Where he was titled the Supreme Healing King of Immortal Nectar, the Great Radiant Envoy,
Delivering humans, devas, spirits, and ghosts.
Wherever his teachings had not yet reached, he ensured all beings attained liberation.
Soon after, he ascended to heaven once more.

King Mu, from his youth, was devoted to the Dao of divine transcendents. Moved by his sincerity, the Queen Mother of the West descended to his palace. He also obtained the Eight Steeds, upon which the king rode, with Benrong as his right-hand attendant and Zaofu as his charioteer. Guiding the carriage, they crossed the Weak Waters, where fish and turtles formed a bridge, and thus ascended Mount Chongshan. There, he offered a toast to the Queen Mother at the Jade Pool before returning. He reigned for fifty-four years and lived to the age of one hundred and four.

By this time, Yin Xi had already journeyed to the Flowing Sands. His thatched hut stood on the northern slope of Mount Zhongnan. The king, longing for the sacred traces, commanded his chariot and horses to be prepared. He had an abbey and shrine constructed, and invited Du Wai and six others to become Daoists, serving together in the same rites.

Chong, styled Xuanyi, dwelled in seclusion, chanting and reciting the *Daode*. He constantly guarded the Black Fluid, exhaling and inhaling radiant qi. After more than twenty years, a Perfected suddenly descended to his quiet chamber, accompanied by two attendants bearing a jade casket on either side. Chong bowed in reverence and pleaded, whereupon he was granted a casket containing the *Scripture of the Ninefold Radiance Elixir*. The Perfected said to him: "Lord Lao and Master Wen Shi summoned the Great Emperor at the Eight Halts Mountain in the Eastern Sea to review the ranks of all transcendents. Transcendents from mountain caves and grottoes across the world, no matter how distant, all arrived without fail. At that time, the Earth Controller recommended you for your diligence, and Lord Lao entrusted me to deliver this immortal formula to you." Chong followed the method and ingested it, his body emanating jade-like radiance, his five viscera filled with vitality, his appearance and breath harmonized.

Later, the Perfected Li bestowed upon him the *Supreme Primordial Mysterious and Vast Wondrous Scripture of the Cavernous Void*, which Chong again cultivated, achieving remarkable efficacy. Thus, he untied the knots of his mortal form, preserved his life, and concentrated his true nature. He commanded the hosts of spirits, perceived the mysteries of all things, subdued tigers and leopards, and employed the hundred numina. He traversed the hidden and manifest realms, his depths unfathomable, and lived over a hundred years. In the nineteenth year of King Mu’s reign, the *ji-hai* year, Lord Lao dispatched the Supreme Clarity Primordial Lady to descend and welcome him, bestowing upon him the title of Perfected of the Supreme Ultimate and appointing him as the Transcendent King of Mount Wangwu.

Master Wen Shi’s disciple, Mao Gui, styled Gongdu, studied astronomy from a young age and was also versed in apocrypha and weft texts. Wen Shi transmitted to him over a hundred scrolls of Daoist scriptures and elixir formulas, all accompanied by oral instructions. After Wen Shi ascended as a transcendent, Mao Gui practiced alongside the recluse Du Chong at the thatched abbey. At the age of twenty-eight, he ceased grain, practiced qi circulation, and refined divine elixirs. Upon achieving the Dao and ascending, Lord Lao bestowed upon him the title of Perfected of Supreme Harmony and commanded him to descend and govern the celestial bureaucracy at the Du Yang Palace. Gui often traveled, carrying divine elixirs throughout the world to deliver those with karmic affinity. Sometimes he refined gold and silver to relieve the poor, or administered elixirs to rescue those in peril. All who sought his aid received blessings and benefits.

Li Tuo studied the Dao of longevity. During King Mu’s reign, he came to reside beneath Dragon Bridge Peak in Jintang Mountain, Shu, where he compounded the Ninefold Radiance Elixir. After its completion, he wandered the Five Marchmounts, the Ten Caverns, and over two hundred other sites for more than two hundred years. On the Eastern Sea, he encountered the Flying Yang Lord, who transmitted to him the Dao of Water Jade. Returning to this mountain, he refined the medicine to completion, then departed to Dragon Bridge Peak to refine the Golden Crucible Reverted Elixir. By the time the elixir was complete, he had lived eight hundred years. The people of Shu witnessed him across generations, hence he was called Li Eight Hundred. As he had studied the Dao thrice on this mountain, the world named it Three Studies Mountain.

Tuo’s younger sister, named Zhen Duo, followed her brother in cultivating the Dao and dwelled in Mianzhu. Lord Lao and the Three Ancient Masters descended to transmit to her the Dao of ascension. She ascended to heaven in broad daylight before her brother, and thus her mountain was named Zhen Duo’s Transformation—this was the ancient governance of Floating Mountain.

In the twenty-first year of King Li’s reign, the *jia-chen* year, Song Lun of Luoyang took refuge at the thatched abbey. Embracing unity and harmonizing qi, he withdrew from worldly affairs, daily reciting the two chapters of the *Daode* several times. He ingested Solomon’s seal and white wood, practicing thus for over twenty years. One day, Lord Lao descended to his dwelling, accompanied by six transcendents who assisted the Perfected in recording his deeds. Lun prostrated himself in supplication, whereupon Lord Lao said to him: "I possess the Dao of the Middle Effulgence and the Scripture of Communing with the Real, born at the inception of the Three Origins, issuing from the Court of the Nine Mysteries. The Five Powers converge in felicity, the Six Qi congeal in essence. Bearing the Real and preserving the remote, one attains long life and reverts age. If you can cultivate this, you will swiftly ascend upon clouds, exiting the manifest and entering the void, penetrating the obscure and illuminating the bright. The Three Luminaries will shine together, the Two Blacks will unite in numinosity. Transforming at will, you will ascend to Supreme Clarity. Now I bestow this upon you." Lun bowed and received the teaching. Thereupon, the treasure trove was opened, and the *Numinous Flight Six Jia* and cinnabar talismans inscribed on plain silk were delivered to Lun. After cultivating these, he naturally attained spiritual resonance. Six youths constantly attended and welcomed him. He could perceive all within a thousand miles as if by divine sight, traverse cliffs in a single stride, and cross waves and perilous terrain without need of fords or paths. Sometimes he transformed into a white deer, or into a wagtail, soaring over plains and marshes to test the hearts of men. At over ninety years of age, during the reign of King Jing, he was bestowed the title of Perfected of Supreme Clarity and appointed to govern the records of transcendents on the Central Marchmount.

Peng Zong, at the age of twenty, studied the Dao under Du Chong. He received the *Five Thousand Words of the Daode* and practiced the Way of Feminine Unity. He could extend a single breath for three days and three nights, and recite the *Daode Jing* twice in one breath. During King Li’s reign, at the age of one hundred and fifty, Lord Lao summoned him as a Perfected of Supreme Clarity.

During King Xuan’s reign, Feng Chang served as the Archivist Beneath the Pillars. At forty, he retired from office to enter the Dao, constantly reciting the *Daode Jing* while dwelling on Mount Zhongnan. Lord Lao dispatched the Perfected Peng Zong, riding a white tiger, to descend to his chamber and bestow upon him the *Supreme Hidden Scripture*. By the time of King Ping, he ascended as a transcendent and became the Perfected of the Western Marchmount.

In the forty-third year of King Xuan’s reign, Lord Lao passed through the Wei River valley. The king went to meet him, whereupon Lord Lao said: "The Year Star has entered the north of Yan—Zhou is in decline." Two years later, King You ascended the throne. Today, beside the Wei River, there is a Boyang River—this is the place.

At that time, woodcutters in the outskirts sang as they worked:
*"With golden kerchief, enter Heaven’s Gate,
Call the Long Essence, gather the Dark Spring,
Sound the Celestial Drum, nurture the Muddy Pellet."*
None of the people understood, but Lord Lao said: "These are words of those who preserve the land—its meaning is profound. This is the practice of the Supreme Perfect Dao."

In the second year of King You’s reign, the earth quaked in the Western Zhou, and the Jing, Wei, and Luo rivers all trembled. Lord Lao said: "Zhou is about to perish. The qi of Heaven and Earth does not lose its order; if it loses its order, it is because the people have thrown it into chaos. Yang lies hidden and cannot emerge; yin presses down and cannot steam upward—thus the earth quakes. Now the Three Rivers have truly shaken, for yang has lost its place and been buried by yin. Yang lost and submerged in yin, the sources must become blocked; when sources are blocked, the state must perish. Water and soil nourish the people’s livelihood, but if the soil has no nourishment, the people lack resources—how can Zhou not fall? In ancient times, the Luo dried up and Xia perished; the Yellow River dried up and Shang perished. Now Zhou’s virtue resembles the final years of those two dynasties. Its rivers and sources will again be blocked, and blockage must lead to exhaustion. A state must rely on mountains and rivers—when mountains collapse and rivers dry up, these are omens of ruin. When rivers dry up, mountains must collapse. If Zhou is to fall, it will be within ten years, for this is the reckoning of fate."

That very year, the Three Rivers ran dry, and Mount Qi crumbled. In the third year, King You favored Bao Si. Lord Lao said: "Zhou is doomed." In the decline of Xia, two dragons halted in the palace courtyard and spoke: *"We are the two lords of Bao."* Thereupon, offerings were spread and divination performed. The oracle declared that the dragons’ glory would depart but remain as a relic, so it was sealed in a casket and stored. Through three dynasties, none dared open it, until King Li broke the seal and beheld it. The dragon’s saliva transformed into a glowing lizard, which a young concubine encountered, conceiving a daughter. Fearing the omen, she abandoned the child. During King Xuan’s reign, a rhyme spread: *"A coiled bow, a winnowing basket—truly Zhou’s ruin."* The king heard of a couple selling such items and ordered their arrest and execution. Fearing for their lives, they fled by night and heard the abandoned girl wailing by the road. Moved by pity, they took her in and fled to Bao. A man of Bao, guilty of a crime, offered the girl to the king as restitution—this was Bao Si. The king adored her, and she bore him Bo Fu. He named Bo Fu heir, deposing Queen Shen and the crown prince, Yijiu.

Lord Lao said: "The disaster is complete—nothing can be done." Soon after, Zhou was destroyed by the Quan Rong.

In the thirty-third year of King Ping’s reign, Lord Lao once more emerged from the Pass to transform the various states, then journeyed to the Western Sea, reaching the isles of Liu and Lin. There, he summoned over eighty thousand immortals from the ten directions—including those newly attained to the Dao who governed the underworld, wandering transcendents, and those who had undergone trials of utmost filial piety and loyalty—and reviewed their merits and conduct. He conferred upon them immortal ranks in five grades and immortal offices in twenty-seven ranks, bestowing them according to their attainment, before ascending once more to Heaven.

Lord Lao had parted ways with the Hu barbarians for over a century when King Bintu, once a vassal of the King of the Western Hu, was reborn in the land of Jambudvīpa as a prince—this was the founder of the Buddha’s teaching. In truth, on the ninth year of King Zhuang’s reign, the night of the seventh day of the fourth month in the *guisi* year, he was born from the right flank of Lady Māyā. The fourth month of Zhou corresponds to the present second month. The Buddhist *Sutra of Past and Present* states he was born on the eighth day of the second month; the *Sutra of Causes and Effects* says the seventh day of the third month; the *Sutra of Auspicious Responses* says the eighth day of the fourth month at midnight. According to the *Tang Comprehensive Institutions*, the *Sutras of the Buddha* from the Jin and Song dynasties record: In the kingdom of Lumbinī, the king’s son, the Buddha-to-be, was born. His father was named Śuddhodana, his mother Māyā. The Buddha’s body was golden-hued, his hair like dark silk. Initially, Māyā dreamed of a white elephant and conceived. At birth, he emerged from his mother’s right side. Born with a topknot, he took seven steps upon touching the earth. This kingdom lay within India, where there was also a divine person named Śāriputra.

The *Record of Buddhism and Daoism* in the *History of the Later Wei* states: Śākyamuni was the son of the King of Kapilavastu in India. "Tianzhu" (India) was its general name, while "Jinwei" was a specific designation. At first, Śākyamuni was born from his mother’s right side on the night of the eighth day of the fourth month. At birth, his appearance was extraordinary in thirty-two ways, and thirty-two auspicious signs descended from heaven in response—this is fully recounted in the *Sutra of Origins*. Śākyamuni was born in the ninth year of King Zhuang of Zhou, which corresponds to the summer of the seventh year of Duke Zhuang of Lu in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, when the "weeping star" vanished and the night shone bright. By the eighth year of Emperor Wu of the Wei, 1,237 years had passed.

The *Bibliographic Treatise* of the *History of the Sui* states: Śākyamuni was born on the eighth day of the fourth month in the ninth year of King Zhuang of Zhou, emerging from his mother’s right side. His appearance was wondrous, possessing thirty-two marks and eighty minor characteristics. He renounced the throne, left home to cultivate the Dao, practiced with diligence and devotion, and awakened to omniscience—thus he was called the Buddha, also *Buddha* or *Fo Tuo* in the Hu language. Translated into Chinese, this means "Pure Awakening." His hair and beard were curly, his strength great, and his archery superb. At seventeen, his father arranged his marriage to three daughters of the Gautama clan—Gopā, Yaśodharā, and Mrigajā—who bore him two sons: the elder Rāhula, the younger Ānanda. Twelve years later, while touring the four city gates, he witnessed the sights of birth, aging, sickness, and death. Startled and terrified, he recalled his past causes, abandoned his home, and fled the city. Entering the Snow Mountains, he dwelled beneath an *arura* tree, cultivating the Dao once taught by the perfected Master Yin. The Hu people named the tree *bodhi*, which in Chinese means "the Way." After enduring hardships for six years, his Dao was perfected. His body took on a golden hue, resembling the Buddha’s form, and he was called Śākyamuni. He entered nirvāṇa on the fifteenth day of the second month in the fourth year of King Kuang’s reign, the *renzǐ* year. At that time, the first month was reckoned as the *zi* month; thus, the former second month became the present twelfth month. In the Western Regions, the Buddha’s passing is called *nirvāṇa*. The *Golden Light Sutra* asks: "Why is the Tathāgata’s lifespan so brief, only eighty years?" This is the explanation.

Some claim Lord Lao was born on the fourteenth day of the ninth month in the third year of King Ding’s reign, while the Buddha was born in the twenty-fourth year of King Zhao’s reign and entered nirvāṇa in the thirteenth year of King Mu’s reign. They assert the Buddha’s birth and death preceded Lord Lao’s emergence from the Pass, seeking to disprove the tradition that Laozi transformed the Hu and became the Buddha. However, Liu Xiang’s *Biographies of Immortals* states: Laozi was born in the Shang dynasty. The *Generational Records* say: In the Shang, he was the Archivist of the Royal Treasury; in the Zhou, he was the Archivist beneath the Pillars. Xue Daoheng’s stele inscription states: During the reigns of Kings Wen and Wu, he held the offices of Archivist of the Treasury and Archivist beneath the Pillars. If he were born in King Ding’s time, how could he have served as the Archivist of the Treasury for King Wen of the Zhou? The Buddha’s birth and death are matters of common knowledge in past and present—these cannot be altered.

The *Record of Buddhism and Daoism* in the *History of the Wei* and the *Bibliographic Treatise* of the *History of the Sui* both state that Śākyamuni was born in the ninth year of King Zhuang of Zhou, corresponding to the summer of the seventh year of Duke Zhuang of Lu in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, when the "weeping star" vanished and the night shone bright. By the eighth year of Emperor Wu of the Wei, 1,237 years had passed. Furthermore, Wang Jianqi of the Qi dynasty composed the *Stele of the Toutuo Temple*, which reads: "The two Zhuangs of Zhou and Lu personally witnessed the mirror of the night’s radiance; the two Ming emperors of Han and Jin together inscribed the adornments of cinnabar and azure." The temple and stele still stand in Ezhu, and Xiao Zhaoming once included it in the *Wen Xuan*. The Five Ministers’ commentary states: The Buddha was born in the time of King Zhuang of Zhou and Duke Zhuang of Lu; his teachings came to China in the reign of Emperor Ming of Han and flourished in the reign of Emperor Ming of Jin—hence the phrase "two Zhuangs, two Mings." Xiao Zhaoming was a devout Buddhist; had his words been false, would he have included them in the *Wen Xuan*?

Later scholars, failing to follow the ancients, recklessly fabricated heterodoxies, arbitrarily altering the Buddha’s birth and death to precede him by thirteen reigns and over 340 years. Such deception is egregious. Moreover, since the Eastern Han, numerous Hu monks have entered China, all speaking truthfully. By the time of Yao Qin, over three hundred years had passed without any dissenting views. Yet contentious individuals suddenly sought to overturn the established accounts of their predecessors, insisting on their own theories. Who would believe them?

As seen in the *Treatise on Bibliography* of the *Old唐书*, under the "Masters and Philosophers" (*Zi*) category, the *Dao* of Laozi is listed first, followed by the deluded teachings of immortals, and finally the general catalog of Buddhist schools is appended as a branch of Daoism—no separate category was ever established.

When Ouyang Wenzhong (Ouyang Xiu) compiled the *New唐书*, his accounts of Laozi and the Buddha were exceedingly brief. Only in the *Biography of Tianzhu* (*India*) does he record that during the Zhenguan era, envoys came to offer exotic treasures and a map of their land, along with a request for an image of Lao Jun. Some, failing to comprehend, still ask: "Why did Lao Jun not transform the Hu barbarians and attain the Dao, but instead became a Buddha?" They do not realize that in the language of Tianzhu, *bodhi* is simply the Hu word for *Dao*. Buddhist scriptures also speak of Śākyamuni entering the mountains to cultivate the Dao, and of his attaining the Dao after six years. Thus, *Buddha-Dao* may be used as a general term—just as in China, those who cultivate the Dao and ascend to transcendence do not speak of "attaining the Dao" but of "becoming immortals."

Now, according to the *Devadatta Chapter* of the *Lotus Sutra*: The Buddha declared, "In kalpas long past, I was once a king who, for the sake of seeking the Dharma, relinquished my throne and served an immortal. For a thousand years, the immortal taught me the *Wonderful Dharma Lotus Sutra*. Now, I have perfected the six pāramitās—compassion, joy, and equanimity—attained the thirty-two marks, the eighty subsidiary characteristics, the radiant golden hue, and realized perfect enlightenment."

Thus, an ancient Chan master’s preface to the *Introduction* states: "Śākyamuni himself recounted that in a former life, he abandoned his kingdom and renounced glory to serve an immortal and study the Dao—hence the *Devadatta Chapter*." Zhang Wujin’s *Comprehensive Treatise* also says: "The Buddha’s service to the immortal demonstrates the rarity of encountering a good spiritual friend. The immortal’s teaching of the Buddha shows that the Dharma is transmitted orally, while the Buddha realized it in his mind."

If Gautama, in kalpas long past, already served an immortal, then the Way of Immortals has existed since ancient times. Since the immortal taught him the sutra, enabling him to attain the six pāramitās and realize perfect enlightenment, the immortal may rightly be called the Buddha’s teacher. The Buddha appeared in the world for one great cause and reason, expounding this sutra without falsehood. His integrity—neither deceiving his own heart nor betraying his teacher—lies precisely in directly revealing his origins. This is why he is the Buddha.

Shuanglin Fu Dashi, known in the world as Maitreya Buddha, still revered the Dao above Buddhism, hence he wore a Daoist cap while draped in a kāṣāya. Hanshan of Tiantai, an emanation of Mañjuśrī—who is the teacher of the seven Buddhas—composed a verse:

*My home lies beneath the green cliffs,*
*My garden overgrown, untended.*
*Two or three scrolls of immortal script,*
*Beneath the trees, I read them murmuring.*

Another verse says:
*Hanshan, a mere worm,*
*Body white, head black,*
*Clutching two scrolls—*
*One the Dao, one virtue.*
*Ever wielding the sword of wisdom,*
*To slay the thieves of affliction.*

His *Lament for the World* also states:
*Buried beneath brambles,*
*Dusk falls, how dark!*
*Though you gnaw your iron lips,*
*No way to recite the scriptures of Laozi.*

Observing the sages of old, all knew to honor Laozi and revere the Dao. Yet later scholars, failing to investigate the roots, slander their teachers and betray the Dao—how lamentable!

After Śākyamuni entered nirvāṇa, he ascended to the thirty-second heaven, rising to the Jiayi Heaven and dwelling in the Yan Zhen Palace as the leader of the *Zhongmin* Heaven, known as the *Shanhui Zhenren* (Benevolent and Wise Perfected One). According to the *Benxiang Sutra*: "With all residual habits extinguished and mind-and-consciousness forgotten, he transcended the three realms and ascended into the *Zhongmin*." The *Benshi Sutra* states: "After the Buddha’s passing, he ascended to the thirty-second heaven, eternally abiding in the position of the Kui constellation in the Su Heaven." The Kui constellation has thirty-six stars, of which one is his. The thirty-second heaven marks the boundary of the *Zhongmin* Heaven, called the *Pingyu Jiayi Heaven*. Within it lies a separate palace, named *Yan Zhen*, for the Kui constellation descends from the lower reaches of the Jiayi Heaven.

The *Treatise on Buddhism and Daoism* in the *History of the Later Wei* also records: "The Buddha, having attained the Dao in the Western Hu lands, now presides as the lord of the Yan Zhen Palace in the thirty-second heaven." Li Chunfeng’s commentary on the *Golden Lock and Flowing Pearl* states: "The *Shanhui Zhenren* is the later incarnation of the Buddha of Tianzhu." Could this be said without evidence?

*Record of the Sage of Primordial Chaos, Volume Five* concludes.

**Record of the Sage of Primordial Chaos, Volume Six**
*Compiled by Song Dynasty Master of Observance, the Eminent Scholar Xie Shoubin*

Among Laozi’s disciples was Xin Pi, also known as Jiran, who was the teacher of Fan Li. His Daoist name was Wenzi, and his written work is titled *The Scripture of Penetrating the Mysteries*. He was originally a prince of Jin.

Once, he asked the Old Master: “What are Dao, virtue, benevolence, righteousness, and ritual?”

The Old Master replied:
—To act without contrivance, to be formless, to cultivate the self within and govern others without, to accomplish deeds and establish affairs, and to dwell in harmony with Heaven—this is the Dao.
—To nurture and sustain, to foster and mature, to benefit all without discrimination, and to unite with Heaven and Earth—this is virtue.
—To remain unassuming in great matters, unneglectful in small ones, to love universally without partiality, and to endure without decline—this is benevolence.
—When in a superior position, to aid the weak; when in a subordinate position, to uphold integrity; when prosperous, to refrain from recklessness; when impoverished, to remain steadfast; to follow the principle in all things, never bending for private gain—this is righteousness.
—To be reverent and solemn in high rank, humble and yielding in low station, to retreat in modesty and embrace softness, to be as yielding as the world’s female principle, to stand in timidity, to act as though incapable—this is ritual.

Thus, cultivating the Dao ensures the submission of those below; cultivating virtue ensures their obedience to commands; cultivating benevolence ensures they do not contend; cultivating righteousness ensures their fairness; cultivating ritual ensures their reverence. When all five are cultivated, the household and state are at peace—this is how the sage governs the myriad things.

Without the Dao, those below rebel; without virtue, they resent; without benevolence, they contend; without righteousness, they grow violent; without ritual, they descend into chaos. No state has ever survived the absence of these five.

Wenzi further asked: “What is the foundation of governing a state?”

The Old Master replied: “I have never heard of a ruler whose person was well-ordered yet whose state was in turmoil. When one cultivates stillness to refine the self and frugality to nurture life, the people below are not disturbed, and the populace does not resent. Thus, Heaven covers with the Dao, Earth bears with virtue, the four seasons do not lose their order, wind and rain do not turn tyrannical, the sun and moon remain clear, and the five planets align in measure.

The foundation of governance lies in pacifying the people; the foundation of pacifying the people lies in meeting their needs; the foundation of meeting their needs lies in not seizing from them; the foundation of not seizing lies in moderating expenditures; the foundation of moderating expenditures lies in reducing affairs; the foundation of reducing affairs lies in non-action.

Non-action does not mean refusing to draw near when summoned, resisting when pushed away, ignoring when pressed, or remaining unmoved when stirred—nor does it mean stagnation without flow, or clenching without release. Rather, it means private ambitions do not intrude upon the public Dao, and desires do not distort correct methods. It is to act in accordance with cycles, to achieve in harmony with the times, to delegate responsibilities to subordinates, and to undertake no misguided affairs. Names are self-assigned, categories self-applied, and matters proceed naturally, without originating from the self. Thus, affairs are accomplished without self-praise, merits are established without self-possession—like using a boat to traverse water, a sled to cross mountains, cultivating fields on high ground, or forming ponds in low places. This is not what I call ‘action.’

The ancient establishment of emperors and kings was not to indulge their desires. The sage who ascends the throne does not seek ease and pleasure for himself. For the people of the world, the strong oppress the weak, the many tyrannize the few, the wise deceive the foolish, the brave bully the timid. Those with talent do not teach one another; those with wealth do not share it. Thus, the Son of Heaven was instituted to unify them. Since one person’s wisdom cannot illuminate all under Heaven, the Three Dukes and Nine Ministers were established to assist him. Since distant lands and foreign customs could not receive his grace, the feudal lords were appointed to govern and pacify them. Thus, Heaven’s tasks are all fulfilled, the seasons all respond, officials do not conceal their talents, and the state suffers no neglected benefits.

Therefore, the laws of the former kings decreed: Do not kill the unborn, do not take eggs, do not drain marshes to fish, do not burn forests to hunt. Before the jackals have offered their prey, traps may not be set in the wild. Before the otters have offered their fish, nets may not enter the waters. Before the hawks have struck, snares may not be spread in the valleys. Before the trees have shed their leaves, axes may not enter the forests. Before insects have burrowed, fire may not be used for hunting. Do not kill pregnant creatures, do not take fledgling birds, do not catch fish under a foot long, do not eat dogs or pigs under a year old. Thus, the flourishing of the myriad things is like rising steam. This is how the former kings aligned with the seasons, perfected their preparations, enriched the state, and benefited the people.

When water is muddied, fish gasp; when governance is harsh, people rebel. When superiors are covetous, inferiors harbor deceit; when superiors are demanding, inferiors contend. Cunning and deceit sprout, thieves and bandits multiply. Addressing the branches without tending to the root is like digging a ditch to stop water or carrying firewood to extinguish a fire.

The sage governs with few affairs and brings order; seeks little yet provides abundantly; bestows benevolence without action; commands trust without speech; obtains without seeking; gathers without acting. Preserving authenticity and embracing the Dao, the world follows like a shadow or echo. This is called non-action.”

Wenzi said: “I have heard your teaching on governance through non-action. May I now inquire about instruction without words?”

The Old Master replied: “Heaven attains its height, Earth attains its depth, the sun and moon shine, the stars and constellations gleam—yet none speak. They rectify their Dao, and things arise naturally. The Yin and Yang and the four seasons do not generate the myriad things; timely rain and dew do not nurture grasses and trees. It is the union of spirit and clarity, the harmony of Yin and Yang, that allows things to grow of themselves.

The Dao conceals essence within, lodges spirit in the heart, abides in stillness and tranquility, delights in harmony within the breast, vast and formless, silent and soundless. The government offices seem idle, the court appears empty. There are no recluses, no idle folk, no forced labor, no unjust punishments. The world all looks up to the ruler’s virtue, follows the sovereign’s guidance. Even distant lands and foreign customs, though requiring multiple translations, come of their own accord—not because every household has been visited and every person instructed, but because the ruler extends his sincere heart to all under Heaven.”

Thus, rewarding the good and punishing the evil are matters of government decrees, but what enables their enforcement is the power of sincerity. Though decrees may be clear, they cannot act alone—they must await sincerity. Sincerity takes form within and is understood outwardly in the hearts of people; this is the Way of non-action. When the sage rules from above, embracing the Dao without speech, his beneficence extends to all directions. How vast, then, is the teaching of non-action!

Therefore, when the ruler’s thoughts do not stray in his breast, when his knowledge does not extend beyond the four realms, when he holds sincerity in his heart and acts upon it, timely sweet rains fall, the five grains flourish, the people are nourished with impartiality, awe-inspiring majesty remains untested, laws are few and unobtrusive, prisons stand empty, and the world shares a single custom, none harboring treacherous intent. Thus, when sincerity moves Heaven, auspicious stars appear, yellow dragons descend, soaring phoenixes arrive, sweet springs flow, fine grains grow, rivers do not overflow, and seas do not surge with waves. But if one defies Heaven and ravages the world, the sun and moon grow dim and eclipse, the five stars stray from their courses, the four seasons fall into discord, daylight darkens and night glows, mountains collapse and rivers run dry, winter thunders and summer frosts appear, celestial omens turn ominous, and the state faces peril and ruin. Heaven and humanity share a mutual resonance.

The workings of spirit and clarity cannot be achieved through cunning or force; only the sage harmonizes his virtue with Heaven and Earth, his brightness with the sun and moon, his numinosity with ghosts and spirits, his reliability with the four seasons. Embracing Heaven’s heart, holding fast to its darkness, grasping the balanced and harmonious, he need not leave his hall to traverse the four seas, transforming customs and guiding all to goodness as if it arose from within themselves—this is called spiritual transformation, for it is the practice of the teaching of non-action.

Wenzi bowed twice and asked, “I have reverently received your instruction on the foundation of governing the state. What, then, is the foundation of governing the self?”

The Old Master said, “Come, let me tell you. The highest way is to nurture the spirit; the next is to nurture the form. When the spirit is clear and the mind at peace, all the body’s joints are at rest—this is the foundation of nurturing life. To fatten the flesh and fill the belly, to restrain desires and passions—these are but the lesser aspects of nurturing life.

One who tends to the foundation regulates rest and activity, moderates food and drink, harmonizes joy and anger, and balances movement and stillness. When what lies within is secure, no perverse influences can enter. Human beings are born through the transformations of Heaven and Earth: in the first month, the essence congeals; in the second, blood forms; in the third, the embryo takes shape; in the fourth, the fetus develops; in the fifth, the sinews form; in the sixth, the bones; in the seventh, the body is complete; in the eighth, it stirs; in the ninth, it grows restless; in the tenth, it is born. Once the physical form is complete, the five viscera assume their offices. The liver governs the eyes, the kidneys the ears, the spleen the tongue, the lungs the nose, the gallbladder the mouth. The head is round, modeling Heaven; the feet are square, resembling Earth.

Heaven has its four seasons, five phases, nine divisions, and three hundred sixty days; humans have their four limbs, five viscera, nine orifices, and three hundred sixty joints. Heaven has wind, rain, cold, and heat; humans have taking and giving, joy and anger. Humans are made in the likeness of Heaven and Earth: the sun and moon govern the ears and eyes, while wind and rain correspond to blood and qi. When the sun and moon stray from their courses, they dim and eclipse; when wind and rain come untimely, they bring destruction and disaster. When the five stars stray, the state suffers calamity.

The Way of Heaven and Earth is vast and boundless, yet even it moderates its brilliance and cherishes its spirit and clarity. How, then, can human ears and eyes endure prolonged stimulation without rest, or human sincerity gallop without exhaustion? Thus, the sage guards the inner and does not guard the outer.

Blood and qi are the glory of humanity; the five viscera are its essence. When blood and qi are concentrated within and do not scatter outward, the chest and abdomen are full, and desires are few. With few desires, the ears and eyes are clear, and hearing and sight are keen. When the five viscera remain aligned with the heart and do not stray, the mind prevails and conduct is upright. When spirit and essence are abundant and do not dissipate, one hears all without fail, sees all without obstruction, and accomplishes all without hindrance.

Thus, adorning the outer harms the inner; indulging the emotions exhausts the spirit. Valuing appearances corrupts authenticity; a moment’s forgetfulness of one’s own worth must strain one’s nature. One who cannot walk a hundred paces without thinking of his appearance will burden his form. Therefore, beautiful plumage harms the bones, and lush branches injure the roots. Stillness and tranquility nurture life; harmony, joy, and emptiness uphold virtue. When the outer does not disturb the inner, one’s nature finds its proper place; when stillness does not interfere with activity, one attains peace in one’s position.

To nurture life while engaging with the world, to embrace virtue while cultivating longevity—this may be called embodying the Dao. The Dao shapes and nurtures the myriad things, ordering what has no form. Silent and unmoving, it pervades all, vast and obscure. It is too deep and broad to be considered external, too minute and subtle to be considered internal. It begins in softness and weakness and ends in hardness and strength. It governs what is short and few and completes what is long and many. Thus, a tree ten spans in girth begins as a handful; a terrace of a hundred fathoms begins with a single basket of earth.

Therefore, the Perfected One embodies it through emptiness and simplicity, tranquility and softness, purity and plainness, not mingling with things. Aligning with the Way of Heaven and Earth, he is called the Perfected One. Emptiness is the dwelling of the Dao; simplicity is its essence. Tranquility is its mirror; softness is its function. Purity is its core.

Desires do not burden the utmost emptiness; love and hate do not disturb the utmost simplicity. Unchanging unity is the utmost stillness; adapting to the times is the utmost softness. Not scattering for things is the utmost purity. Thus, when the sage values the world lightly, his spirit is not encumbered; when he regards the myriad things as small, his spirit is not confused. When he equalizes life and death, his mind is not daunted; when he unites with change and transformation, his clarity is not dazzled. Stillness shares virtue with Yin; movement flows with Yang. He is neither distant nor close, aligning with the Dao as his boundary and with virtue as his neighbor. He leans on an unshakable pillar, walks an unbroken path, draws from an inexhaustible storehouse, and learns from an immortal teacher. Thus, there is nowhere he does not succeed, nothing he does not penetrate.”

Wenzi bowed twice and received the teaching.

King Ping of Zhou asked Wenzi, “I have heard that you received the Dao from Lao Dan. Now, though a worthy man may possess the Dao, if he encounters an age of chaos and strife, and seeks to transform a long-troubled people with the power of one man alone, how could this be possible?”

Wenzi replied, “Virtue and the Dao rectify the crooked to make them straight, rouse the disordered to make them well-ordered, and transform the corrupt and ruined to restore their simplicity and purity. They cause virtue to be reborn, and the world to attain peace and tranquility—all hinges upon one man. Thus, the accumulation of virtue makes a king, while the accumulation of resentment brings ruin. Yao and Shun flourished by this principle, while Jie and Zhou perished by it.”

King Ping followed his words, and the world was governed.

Wenzi again studied under the Old Master and received the method of the divine elixir, thereby attaining the rank of an immortal.

Shicheng Qi came from Chu to see the Old Master and said, “I have heard that you are a great sage. For this reason, I did not shrink from the long journey to come here, wishing to see you even if it meant a hundred nights of lodging and repeated inquiry without rest. Yet now I observe that you are no sage: you have more than enough grain stored in rat holes while abandoning your younger sister—this is not benevolence. Before you, food is not fully consumed, yet you ceaselessly accumulate without limit.”

The Old Master remained silent and did not respond. Shicheng Qi withdrew.

The next day, he returned and said, “Yesterday, I spoke rashly against you. Today, my words ring true, yet my heart feels constrained. My form stands upright, and my heart is correct—why then do I feel this way?”

The Old Master said, “Those who are cunning, clever, and spiritually sagely—I have long since cast off such traits. In the past, when you called me an ox, I became an ox; when you called me a horse, I became a horse. If one truly possesses the reality, yet refuses the name given by others, one invites calamity. I wear ordinary clothes, not because I cling to attire.”

Thereupon, Shicheng Qi walked like a crow, avoiding his shadow, and advanced with measured steps to ask, “How should one cultivate the self?”

The Old Master said, “Your bearing is overbearing, your gaze piercing, your forehead protruding, your mouth gaping, and your demeanor rigid—you seem like a horse tethered in place. When you move, you clutch your hair as if it were a trigger; when you observe, you scrutinize with cunning intelligence, and your vision is clouded by excess. All this stems from a lack of trust. At the edge of your domain, there is a thief.”

Shicheng Qi paused, then withdrew.

The Old Master traveled west to Qin. Yang Ziju awaited him at the outskirts and, upon reaching Liang, encountered him. Midway, the Old Master looked up to the sky and sighed, “At first, I thought you could be taught, but now you cannot.”

Yang Ziju did not respond.

Upon reaching their lodging, he brought water for washing, a towel and comb, removed his shoes outside the door, and advanced on his knees, saying, “Earlier, Master, you looked up to the sky and sighed. Your disciple wished to ask, but you were walking without pause, so I dared not. Now that you are at rest, may I inquire about my fault?”

The Old Master said, “Haughty and overbearing—with whom could you dwell? The purest white appears stained; the greatest virtue seems insufficient.”

Yang Ziju’s expression changed abruptly, and he said, “I have received your instruction.”

In the past, when he traveled, innkeepers would greet him, the master of the house would hold his mat, his wife would hold his towel and comb, guests would rise from their seats, and those warming themselves by the fire would yield their place. Upon his return, the innkeepers now vied with him for space.

Another day, he saw the Old Master again. The Old Master said, “The patterns of tigers and leopards, the agility of apes and monkeys—these invite arrows. Why is this so?”

Yang Ziju said, “May I dare ask about the governance of an enlightened king?”

The Old Master said, “His achievements cover the world, yet he seems not to claim them as his own. His transformative influence extends to the myriad things, yet he does not wait to possess virtue. He does not proclaim his name, standing in the unfathomable and roaming in the realm of nothingness.”

The Old Master’s disciple Gengsang Chu, also known as Kangcangzi, had partially attained the Old Master’s Dao. He lived in the north on Mount Weilü. His ministers were arrogant, and the wise departed from him; his concubines were insolent, and the benevolent kept their distance. Only the deformed and the indolent remained with him, serving as his attendants.

After three years, Weilü flourished. Its people said to one another, “When we calculate day by day, Master Gengsang seems insufficient, but when we calculate year by year, he has abundance. Perhaps he is a sage—why not enthrone him as our lord, worship him as our spirit, and honor him as our grain deity?”

Gengsang Chu heard this and, facing south, was displeased. He said, “When spring’s energy arises, the hundred grasses grow; when autumn arrives in due course, the myriad treasures mature. Is this not the result of inherent attainment? I have heard that the perfected person dwells in a humble abode, and the people do not know where to turn. Now, with the petty people of Weilü whispering among themselves, desiring to offer sacrificial vessels to one deemed a sage, I am thus reminded of the Old Master’s words.”

Nanrong Qu asked, “I am already advanced in years. How should I entrust my studies?”

Gengsang Chu said, “Preserve your form, embrace your life, and do not let your thoughts and worries run rampant.”

Qu said, “The eyes and the form—I do not see their difference, yet the blind cannot see themselves. The ears and the form—I do not see their difference, yet the deaf cannot hear themselves. The heart and the form—I do not see their difference, yet the deluded cannot grasp themselves. The form and the form are separate, yet things sometimes seek one another without success. Now you tell me to preserve my form, embrace my life, and not let my thoughts and worries run rampant. I have striven to hear this teaching, and it has reached my ears.”

Gengsang Chu, having exhausted his words, said, “A running bee cannot transform a chrysalis; a Yue chicken cannot hatch a swan’s egg; a Lu chicken, however, is fully capable. Chickens are all chickens—their virtue is not different, yet some can and some cannot. The difference lies in the size of their capacity. Now, my capacity is insufficient to transform you. Why not go south to see Laozi?”

Nanrong Qu then prepared provisions for seven days and seven nights and arrived at the Old Master’s abode. The Old Master said, “Have you come from Chu?”

Qu replied, “Yes.”

The Old Master said, “Why have you come with such a crowd?”

Qu looked back in alarm. The Old Master said, “Do you not understand what I mean?”

Nanrong Qu bowed his head in shame, then looked up and sighed, saying, “Just now, I forgot my own answers and so lost my own questions.”

The Old Master said, “What do you mean?”

After a long while, Qu asked again, “If I am ignorant, people call me foolish; if I am wise, it only brings sorrow to my body. If I lack benevolence, I harm others; if I possess it, it only brings sorrow to myself. If I lack righteousness, I injure others; if I possess it, it only brings sorrow to my own self. How can I escape these three dilemmas?”

These were the afflictions that troubled him, and he wished to ask the Old Master about them through the example of Chu.

The Old Master said, “Earlier, when I saw the questions in your brows and eyes, I had already grasped your heart. Now you speak again and confirm it—why do you cling so stubbornly, as if you had lost your parents and were raising a pole to search for them in the sea? You are a lost man indeed! Bewildered, you seek to return to your original nature yet find no way in. How pitiful!”

Nanrong Qu then asked to withdraw to his lodging. There, he gathered what he loved and cast aside what he despised. After ten days of self-reflection, he returned to see the Old Master.

The Old Master said, “You have cleansed yourself—why then do you remain so troubled? And though your inner state seems to flow, there is still something foul within. Those who are bound by external things cannot grasp and direct them inward, and those who are entangled within cannot mislead and direct them outward. Those who are lashed by both inner and outer forces cannot be upheld by the Dao and its virtue—how much less those who abandon the Dao and act recklessly!”

Qu said, “When a villager falls ill, his neighbors ask about it. If the sick man can describe his illness, he is not yet truly ill. My hearing of the Great Dao is like taking medicine that only worsens the disease. I wish only to hear the classic of preserving life.”

The Old Master said, “Can you embrace the One? Can you not lose it? Can you know good and ill fortune without divination? Can you come to rest? Can you cease striving? Can you abandon seeking in others and seek within yourself? Can you be supple like a fish? Can you be empty like a valley? Can you be like a child? One who wails all day yet makes no hoarse sound has reached perfect harmony. One who gazes all day yet does not strain his eyes has no bias toward externals. One who grasps all day yet does not cramp his hands shares in the virtue of all things. In movement, he does not know where he goes; at rest, he does not know what he does. He flows with things, merging with their waves—this is the classic of preserving life.”

Qu said, “Then is this the virtue of the perfected person?”

The Old Master replied, “No, this is merely what is called ‘the melting of ice and the thawing of frost.’ The perfected person shares food upon the earth and delights together in Heaven. He does not use others for gain or harm, nor does he conspire with them in strangeness, scheming, or affairs. He comes like a fish gliding, goes like a valley echoing—this is the classic of preserving life.”

Qu asked, “Then is this the ultimate?”

The Old Master said, “Not yet. I have told you: can you be like a child? A child moves without knowing what he does, walks without knowing where he goes. His body is like a withered branch, his heart like dead ashes. In such a state, misfortune does not arrive, nor does fortune come. With neither misfortune nor fortune, where can there be human calamity?”

Qu bowed twice, expressed his gratitude, and departed. His spirit became clear and luminous, and for ten days he ate nothing, yet felt as though he had feasted on a grand sacrifice. His wisdom encompassed heaven and earth, and he discerned the finest details as if examining autumn down.

King Ding later asked the Old Master, “Are there any divine immortals in the world today who can be known?”

The Old Master replied, “On the Eastern Marchmount resides Master Zhan Qin; on the Southern Marchmount, Master Kuang Xu; on the Western Marchmount, Yin Xi; on the Northern Marchmount, the August Person; and on the Central Marchmount, the Ancient Master—that is I. Xu, styled Zixiao, was a disciple of mine. He cultivated the Dao on Mount Lu for seven hundred years and ascended to immortality in broad daylight during the reign of King Lie. When the king heard of his name, he sent envoys to summon him. The envoys arrived at his dwelling, but found only an empty hermitage. They then memorialized the throne, requesting that the Southern Zhan Mountain be renamed Jinglu Mountain, and that it also be called Kuangfu after the master’s surname. According to the *Erya’s Explanation of Names*, a ‘fu’ is a mound of earth. This mountain was originally called Southern Zhan, for atop it were the Three Heavenly Zhang. In ancient times, Mount Qian was revered as the Southern Marchmount, while Southern Zhan was considered a reserve of blessings, hence it was also called the Southern Marchmount. In the fifth year of the Yuanfeng era of Emperor Wu of Han, during his imperial tour to sacrifice to the famous mountains and great rivers, he shot a dragon in the Xunyang River. Turning to ask which deity presided over this mountain, Liu Xiang replied, ‘Master Kuang Xu attained the Dao here.’ The emperor then ordered a shrine built at Huhan. The shrine was later included in the official sacrifices. During the Eastern Jin, when Huan Yi governed Jiujiang, he favored Buddhism. At the request of the monk Huiyuan of Mameng, he relocated the master’s shrine to the riverside and converted the site into a monastic dwelling—this is the present Donglin Temple. After the master’s ascension, the Old Master bestowed upon him the rank of Perfected Person and charged him with overseeing droughts and floods in Wu and Chu. He also granted him the Seal of Pestilence, placing him in command of the Eight Divisions of Pestilence Spirits. Thus, he came to be popularly known as Master Kuangfu, the Harmonizer of Pestilence. In the first year of the Jianzhong Jingguo era of the Song dynasty, in the ninth month, he was enfeoffed as the Perfected Person of Serene Illumination. During rituals for averting calamities, imperial prayer tablets were customarily bestowed, and local officials were dispatched to the temple to perform rites.

In the seventeenth year of King Jing’s reign, in the year *wuxu*, Confucius said to Nan’gong Jingshu, “I have heard that Lao Dan is profoundly learned in ancient and modern matters, understands the origins of ritual and music, and clarifies the return of the Dao and its virtue—he is my teacher. I intend to visit him.”

Jingshu replied, “I humbly receive your command.”

He then addressed the Lord of Lu, saying, “Your servant has received the command of our late lord, who said: ‘Confucius is a descendant of sages, though his lineage was extinguished in Song. His ancestor, Fufu He, once held a state but yielded it to Duke Li, while Zheng Kaofu assisted Dukes Dai, Wu, and Xuan, growing ever more reverent with each appointment, as recorded in the inscriptions on the tripods. Zang Sun once said, “If a sage-king were to arise in this age, he would surely have enlightened ministers.” Confucius, though young, is fond of ritual—he is destined for greatness. You must take him as your teacher.’ Now Confucius is about to journey to Zhou to observe the legacy of the former kings and examine the ultimate principles of ritual and music. This is a grand undertaking. Might my lord provide him with carriage and provisions? I request permission to accompany him.”

The Duke said, “Very well.” He then provided Confucius with a carriage, two horses, and one servant boy, and permitted him to accompany Shusun Jing. The following year, they arrived in Zhou, where Confucius met the Old Master. The Old Master said, “Have you come, sir? I have heard that you are a worthy man from the north. Have you attained the Dao?”

Confucius replied, “Not yet.”

The Old Master asked, “Where then have you sought it?”

Confucius said, “I sought it in the measures and numbers, but after five years I had not attained it.”

The Old Master asked, “And where else have you sought it?”

Confucius said, “I sought it in the principles of yin and yang, but after twelve years I had not attained it.”

The Old Master said, “Indeed. If the Dao could be offered as a gift, no one would fail to present it to their lord. If the Dao could be advanced, no one would fail to offer it to their kin. If the Dao could be proclaimed to others, no one would fail to tell it to their brothers. If the Dao could be given to others, no one would fail to bestow it upon their descendants. The reason it cannot be so is no other: without an inner master, it cannot be established; without outward rectitude, it cannot be put into practice. What issues from within is not received without; the sage does not emerge. What enters from without finds no master within; the sage does not conceal himself.”

Confucius withdrew. Before long, he again met the Old Master, bowed, and asked, “Earlier, in our leisurely conversation, I dared to inquire about the Great Dao.”

The Old Master said, “When the noble man finds the right time, he mounts his carriage; when he does not, he drifts like tumbleweed. I have heard that the good merchant hides his treasures as if empty, and the noble man of great virtue appears simple-minded. Rid yourself of your pride, excess, extravagant desires, affected manners, and wayward ambitions—none of these benefit your person. What I have told you is only this.”

Another day, Confucius again met the Old Master and spoke of benevolence and righteousness. The Old Master said, “If chaff gets in one’s eyes, the four directions seem to shift; if fleas and mosquitoes bite the skin, one cannot sleep all night. If you, sir, could ensure the world does not lose its simplicity, then heaven and earth would naturally have their constancy, the sun and moon their brightness, the stars and constellations their order, birds and beasts their flocks, and plants their rootedness. You wish to spread virtue and act, following the Dao in your pursuit—you have already arrived. Why then do you clamor for benevolence and righteousness as if beating a drum to seek a lost child? Alas, you would disorder human nature! The magpie does not bathe daily to be white, nor the crow dye itself black to be dark. The simplicity of black and white is not worth distinguishing; the spectacle of fame and reputation is not worth expanding. As the saying goes, when the spring dries up, fish find themselves on dry land, moistening one another with their dampness, wetting one another with their spittle—better they had forgotten one another in the rivers and lakes.”

Confucius returned and did not speak for three days. Zigong, finding this strange, asked, “Master, when you met the Old Master, what advice did he give you?”

Confucius said, “Ah! Of birds, I know they can fly; of fish, I know they can swim; of beasts, I know they can run. For those that fly, I can use a net; for those that swim, I can use a line; for those that run, I can use a snare. But as for the dragon—it coalesces into form, scatters into patterns, rides the clouds and ascends to heaven—this I cannot fathom. Today, when I saw the Old Master, he was like the dragon. My mouth gaped and could not close; my spirit was confounded, and I knew not where to abide. What advice could I give the Old Master?”

Yang Jie’s preface to the *Record of Laozi* states: “Laozi existed before heaven and earth, and none know whose son he was. He aged after heaven and earth, and was forcibly named the father of a single dawn. Born with hair as white as blossoms, he lived long. Though weak, he could speak—not from learned instruction. Though solemn, he could be as tender as an infant, devoted to softness. Conceived in the womb of Mother Yin, he descended in Renli. His descendants followed, as do all people. He took the surname Li without attachment. His ears were large, his eyes broad; his forehead bore the sun’s angle, his temples the moon’s depth. Two pillars, three gates—grasping ten, treading five—he was the manifestation of a divine person. He studied under Rongcheng, sought the Dao from Changcong, having no fixed teacher. In the time of Fuxi, he was Yuhua Zi; in the time of Shennong, Dacheng Zi; in the time of Zhu Rong, Guangshou Zi; in the time of Shangyang, Chijing Zi; in the time of Di Ku, Lutu Zi; in the time of Tang Yao, Wucheng Zi; in the time of Yu of Xia, Zhenxing Zi; in the time of Tang of Shang, Xize Zi; in the time of King Wen of Zhou, Xieyi Zi.”

It further states: “As Grand Scribe, he became in heaven the Golden Gate Emperor, the Supreme Radiance Lord, the Divine Treasure Venerable—having no fixed name. Whether as Hidden Scribe or Pillar Scribe, he did not choose his office. When Zhou declined, he departed through the pass, retiring in due time. The wondrous essence of the Dao and virtue, the divine and numinous chapters—these are the laws of heaven and humanity. He revolved and governed creation, inhaling and exhaling yin and yang, ascending and descending with the stars, regulating the nine transformations. He refined metals and stones, commanded ghosts and spirits, trod upon shadows and soared through clouds, vanished his form and concealed his radiance. He attained long life and enduring vision, aiding the good and relieving distress, transcending the myriad transformations. The world knows not whether this is so or not, and thus calls him the Reclusive Noble Man.”

Confucius said, “Ah! Of birds, I know they can fly; of fish, I know they can swim; of beasts, I know they can run. But as for the dragon—it coalesces into form, scatters into patterns, rides the wind and clouds to ascend to heaven—this I cannot know. When I saw Laozi, he was like the dragon.” From ancient times to the present, many have spoken of Laozi, but none have matched Confucius’ words.

Zigong asked Confucius, “Is there truly a man who dwells like a corpse yet appears like a dragon, whose voice is like thunder yet whose depth is like an abyss, whose movements are like those of heaven and earth? Might I also behold such a one?”

Confucius said, “The white egret wanders freely, and all desire to see it. Towering like heaven, rumbling like thunder, bursting forth like rain—each bows their head. Zigong, how could you behold such a one?”

Zigong then sought an audience with the Old Master by invoking Confucius’ name. The Old Master was sitting in meditation when he responded faintly, saying, “My years have advanced and passed away. What would you admonish me with?”

Zigong stood trembling, unable to compose himself. The Old Master said, “Young man, you may return. Your teacher is named Qiu—only after admonishing him for three years may you be taught.”

Zigong returned, and Confucius said, “You have been shaken.” Zigong held his silence, unable to respond.

When Confucius later visited the Old Master, his disciples followed. The Old Master asked, “Who were those who came before?” Confucius replied, “Zhong You, brave and mighty; next, Zeng Shen, filial; next, Yan Hui, benevolent; next, Zhuan Sunshi, martial.”

The Old Master said, “I have heard that in the south there is a bird called the phoenix, adorned with sacred tassels of benevolence, trust on its left and wisdom on its right, strength in its talons and courage at its fore—yet even this is but a lesser being.”

The Old Master assisted in the burial of a man from the Daxiang clan, with Confucius following. When an eclipse occurred, the Old Master said, “Qiu, halt the bier by the roadside and cease wailing to observe the omen.” When the darkness passed, they returned and then proceeded with the burial.

Confucius asked, “A bier cannot be taken back—why halt during an eclipse?” The Old Master replied, “A bier should not depart too early nor rest too late; only criminals and those hastening to a parent’s funeral travel by starlight. How can one know the eclipse will not last until nightfall?”

Confucius asked, “For a child who dies young, is a coffin and burial rites used?” The Old Master said, “Long ago, I saw Shi Yi’s son die young, and the grave was distant. The Duke of Shao asked him, ‘Why not encoffin and prepare the body in the palace?’ Shi Yi replied, ‘How dare I?’ He consulted the Duke of Zhou, who said, ‘Why not?’ Thus, Shi Yi did so. The use of coffins and rites for young children began with him.”

Confucius asked, “After three years of mourning, when the final wailing is complete, are there no exceptions for military affairs?” The Old Master said, “In the past, Duke Boqin of Lu made an exception—this was due to the rebellion of the Xu Rong.”

Confucius asked, “In ancient times, when an army marched, the ancestral tablets were carried along—why?” The Old Master said, “When a lord departs his state, the Grand Steward takes the tablets of the ancestral temples to accompany him. For sacrifices to ancestors, the invocator welcomes the tablets of the four temples. When the tablets enter or leave the temple, the road is cleared as a ritual.”

Confucius asked, “I have heard that in ancient sacrifices to Heaven, the Five Emperors were embodied as divine effigies—is this so?” The Old Master said, “Heaven has the Five Phases—Wood, Fire, Metal, Water, and Earth—each governs its season, nurturing and assisting the Supreme Emperor in creating all things. These true spirits are called the Five Emperors.”

Confucius said, “May I ask who the Five Emperors are?” The Old Master replied:
“The Azure Emperor of the East, Weilingyang, wields the compass to govern spring.
The Vermilion Emperor of the South, Chipiaonu, wields the scale to govern summer.
The White Emperor of the West, Baizhaoju, wields the square to govern autumn.
The Black Emperor of the North, Shiguangji, wields the weight to govern winter.
The Yellow Emperor of the Center, Han Shuniu, wields the plumb line to govern the four seasons.
Thus, when ancient kings changed dynasties, they followed the Five Phases, renewing the Five Virtues in succession, cycling endlessly. Therefore, when a sage king dies, he is matched in sacrifice to one of the Five Phases. Thus, Tai Hao is matched with Wood, Yan Di with Fire, the Yellow Emperor with Earth, Shao Hao with Metal, and Zhuanxu with Water.”

Confucius said, “How profound! The Dao is truly difficult to practice. I have offered the Dao and submitted myself to the rulers of this age, yet none have accepted me. How hard it is to practice the Dao!”

The Old Master said, “Those who expound are swept away by eloquence; those who listen are confused by words. Knowing these two, the Dao cannot be entrusted. What you speak of—those men and their bones have long decayed; only their words remain. The *Odes*, *Documents*, *Rites*, and *Music* are but the remnants of the former kings—how could they be the essence? The remnants are what the feet tread upon, but the essence is not the tread. The white egret gazes at its mate, their pupils unmoving, yet they are transformed by the wind. The male insect calls from the upwind, the female responds from downwind, and they are transformed by the wind. All things become male and female and are transformed by the wind. Nature cannot be altered, fate cannot be changed, time cannot be halted, and the Dao cannot be obstructed. If one attains the Dao, there is nothing from which one can be lost.”

Confucius withdrew to his lodging and did not emerge for three months. He then returned to see the Old Master and said, “I have grasped it. The fledgling crow and magpie, the fish that sprays foam, the slender-waisted wasp that transforms—if a younger brother exists, the elder weeps. For too long, I have not been transformed by humanity; how could I transform others?”

The Old Master said, “Good. You have grasped it.”

One day, Confucius went again to see the Old Master. The Old Master had just bathed and was letting his hair dry, standing so still he seemed not of this world. Confucius withdrew and waited. After a while, he saw him and said, “Was I dazed? Was it truly so? Just now, Master, your form seemed like withered wood, as if you had forgotten things and stood apart from humanity.”

The Old Master said, “I wander in the beginning of things.”

Confucius asked, “What does this mean?”

The Old Master replied, “The mind is weary and cannot know; the mouth is shut and cannot speak. I once tried to discuss what lies beyond. The utmost yin is solemn and silent, issuing from heaven; the utmost yang is blazing and radiant, bursting forth from earth. The two intermingle and harmonize, and all things are born. The principle of creation is unseen. Growth and decline, fullness and emptiness, darkness and light—day by day, month by month, they change. There is action, yet no sign of achievement. Life has its sprouting; death has its return. Beginning and end revolve without end, and none know where it ceases. If this is not it, then what is the origin?”

Confucius said, “May I ask about wandering in this?”

The Old Master said, “To attain this is the utmost beauty and utmost joy. To attain the utmost beauty and wander in the utmost joy is called the Perfected Person.”

Confucius said, “I wish to hear the method.”

The Old Master said, “Herbivorous beasts do not hasten to change their marshes; aquatic creatures do not hasten to change their waters. They undergo small changes yet do not lose their great constancy. Joy and anger, sorrow and delight do not enter their breasts.

The world beneath heaven is the unity of the ten thousand things. If one attains this unity and becomes identical with it, then the four limbs and hundred parts of the body will be but dust and grime. Life and death, beginning and end will be as day and night, yet nothing can disturb them—how much less the distinctions of gain and loss, fortune and misfortune?

To cast off servitude is like casting off a body of mud and mire. What is noble lies in servitude; what is noble lies in the self and does not lose itself in change. Moreover, the ten thousand transformations have never had a limit—what is there to trouble the heart?

Those who practice the Dao have resolved this.”

Confucius said, “The great person’s virtue matches heaven and earth, yet still borrows the utmost words to cultivate the heart. Among the ancients, which gentleman could escape this?”

The Old Master said, “Not so. Water’s relation to purity is effortless, and its talent is natural. The Perfected Person’s relation to virtue is uncontrived, and things cannot depart from it. The sky’s height is self-so; the earth’s thickness is self-so; the sun and moon’s brightness is self-so—what cultivation is there?

Confucius departed and told Yan Hui, saying, “In my relation to the Dao, am I not like a gnat in vinegar? Without the Master’s lifting of my covering, I would not have known the great completeness of heaven and earth.”

When Confucius was about to return to Lu, the Old Master saw him off, saying, “I have heard that the rich and noble give gifts of wealth to see others off; the humane give gifts of words. Though I cannot be rich and noble, I have presumptuously taken the name of the humane to offer you words as a parting gift.

Nowadays, among scholars, those who are keenly perceptive and deeply discerning yet near to death are those who delight in criticizing others. Those who are broadly eloquent and vastly learned yet endanger themselves are those who expose others’ faults. As a son, do not assert the self; as a minister, do not assert the self.”

Confucius said, “I respectfully receive your teaching.”

After returning from Zhou to Lu, his Dao grew ever more lofty, and disciples from distant regions came to follow him—numbering three thousand!

According to the *Ying Shen Yue Zhang* in the Tang dynasty’s *Kong Miao* rituals: “Through profound brilliance, he inquired of the Old Master to explore the truth. Three thousand disciples, five hundred worthies—his rules endure for a hundred ages, his temple stands for ten thousand years. With pure sincerity, they offer sacrifice, playing music to welcome the spirit.”

It is said that the *Da Le* once had these words, though their origin is unclear.

Wang Liao’s eulogy says: “Non-being has its origin; emptiness is the source of reality. Laozi’s clarity and stillness lodge intent in wordlessness. Thus he became Confucius’ teacher, preserving the One to endure long.”

Lu Shiheng’s eulogy says: “Kong Qiu, of profound sagacity, matched heaven and magnified it. His influence fanned the mysterious current; his grace ran deep as sacred treasure. Awakening, he yearned for Zhou; speaking, he modeled himself on the Old Master. This is no empty claim.”

Su Shi’s *Ancient History* says: “Confucius taught people with benevolence and righteousness, and governed the world with ritual and music. The changes of benevolence, righteousness, ritual, and music are boundless, yet he declared, ‘My Dao is unified by one thread.’ If there were nothing to unify it, then acting according to change would be righteousness, and the hexagrams would have divisions that do not cohere.

The *Book of Changes* says, ‘That which is above form is called the Dao; that which is below form is called the instrument.’ The *Analects* says, ‘The noble person ascends; the petty person descends.’ Yet Confucius called himself one who ‘studies below to ascend above.’ Sweeping and responding, the *Book of Songs*, the *Book of Documents*, ritual, and music—all were what he studied. The noble person thereby ascends to the Dao; the petty person thereby obtains the instrument. Ascending to the Dao, the myriad changes attain unity; obtaining the instrument, one has constancy and does not waver. This is how Confucius attained both.

Now, Confucius’ character was all-embracing, so he showed people the instrument while concealing the Dao, making it easy for the enlightened to enter while preventing the unenlightened from being dazzled. Laozi’s self-cultivation was profound, so he showed people the Dao while omitting the instrument, making it easy for the enlightened to enter while not troubling himself over the unenlightened. In essence, both were devoted to the Dao, differing only in the order of their teachings.”

I have heard my elder brother Zizhan say: “The Grand Historian says that Zhuangzi wrote *The Fisherman*, *Robber Zhi*, and *The Old Fisherman’s Basket* to slander Confucius’ followers and clarify Laozi’s teachings. This is to know only the coarse side of Zhuangzi. I believe Zhuangzi’s words all grant reality in substance while withholding it in form, outwardly rejecting while covertly aiding. In his slander of Confucius, he never failed to subtly reveal his intent.”

The Grand Historian also says: “Shen [Buhai] and Han [Fei] were lacking in kindness, both deriving from the intent of the Dao and virtue.” Now, Shang Yang governed Qin with law; Shen Buhai governed Han with techniques. Statutes were displayed in government offices; punishments were engraved in the people’s hearts—this was called law. Assigning offices based on ability, holding officials accountable to their titles, wielding the power of life and death, and assessing the competence of ministers—this was called technique. Han Fei’s learning combined and employed both Shen and Shang’s methods. Where law reached, even the sagely and wise were not used. Where technique was wielded, even father and son did not trust one another. This was what Shen and Han called Laozi’s Dao, but in truth it was not.

A minister from Chen visited Lu and had a private audience with Shusun. Shusun said, “There is a sage in our state—do you know him?”

The minister said, “Is it not Kong Qiu?”

“Yes,” he replied.

“How do you know he is a sage?”

Shusun said, “I have heard that Yan Hui and Kong Qiu can discard the heart and employ the body.”

The minister from Chen said, “There is also a sage in our state—do you not know him?”

“Who is the sage?” he asked.

“Lao Dan’s disciple has one named Kang Cangzi, also called Gengsang Chu. His book is titled *The True Scripture of the Cavern Spirit*. He has attained the Dao of shame and can see with his ears and hear with his eyes.”

Shusun reported this to the Lord of Lu, who heard it with great astonishment. He sent his highest minister with generous gifts to invite him.

When Kang Cangzi arrived in Lu, the Lord of Lu humbly requested his instruction. Kang Cangzi said, “Those who transmitted this have spoken falsely. I can see and hear without relying on my ears and eyes, but I cannot reverse the functions of ears and eyes.”

The Lord of Lu said, “This is most extraordinary! What is the nature of your Way?”

Kang Cangzi replied, “The body aligns with the heart, the heart aligns with qi, qi aligns with spirit, and spirit aligns with the formless. With resolute presence and resonant harmony, though distant beyond the eight wilds or near as one’s brows and lashes, I must know it. Yet I do not know whether it is my seven apertures and four limbs that perceive it, or my heart and six viscera that comprehend it—it is simply self-knowing.”

The Lord of Lu was greatly pleased. Later, he told this to Confucius, who smiled but did not respond.

The Grand Steward Dang of Shang visited Confucius and asked, “Is Qiu a sage?”

Confucius said, “How would Qiu dare claim sagacity? Qiu is merely one who has broadly studied and retains much knowledge.”

The Grand Steward said, “Were the Three Kings sages?”

Confucius said, “The Three Kings were skilled in employing wisdom and courage. As for sagacity, Qiu does not know.”

He asked, “Were the Five Emperors sages?”

Confucius said, “The Five Emperors were skilled in employing benevolence and righteousness. As for sagacity, Qiu does not know.”

He asked, “Were the Three Sovereigns sages?”

Confucius said, “The Three Sovereigns were skilled in following the times. As for sagacity, Qiu does not know.”

The Grand Steward was greatly startled and said, “Then who is a sage?”

Confucius changed his expression and said, “In the West there is a man who may be a sage. He governs without disorder, is trusted without speaking, and transforms without instruction. Vast and boundless, the people cannot name him. Qiu suspects he may be a sage, but does not know whether he is truly a sage or not.”

The Grand Steward silently thought, “Is Kong Qiu deceiving me?”

Laozi harmonized his light with the dust, engaged in non-action, and practiced the teaching without words. Thus Confucius admired his sagacity and went to study under him. Moreover, if Kang Cangzi is already called a sage by the multitude, how much more so his master!
