Lu bordered the Eastern Sea, while Zhou lay to its west. At that time, the Old Master was in Zhou, and Confucius resided in Lu, so he pointed to that direction, alluding to his virtue and extolling his sagacity—this was surely no deception.

Later generations mistakenly identified him as the Buddha. Yet by that time, the Buddha had long since passed away, and his language and script differed from those of China. What would Confucius have gained from him? Moreover, Confucius distinguished the Central States from the barbarians, despising those with unkempt hair and left-lapelled robes. According to the principles of the *Spring and Autumn*, those who adopted barbarian rites were demoted. Would he have abandoned the sages of China to study and revere them?

In ancient times, King Wen resided beneath Mount Qi, where he was known as the Earl of the West. King Wu said, “It was my cultured father who became illustrious in the Western Lands.” Zhou lay in the west of China, so it also referred to itself as the West.

When Boyi and Shuqi dwelled in Guzhu, they admired King Wen’s sagely virtue and said to each other, “We have heard there is a man in the West who seems to possess the Way. Let us go and observe him.” They then traveled to the southern slopes of Mount Qi.

The *Book of Songs* says:
*“For whose grace do we long?
The fair one of the West.
That fair one—
The man of the West.”*
This is from the “Airs of Wei.” Zheng’s commentary states it expresses longing for the Zhou court.

Again, Qi Jiang said to Duke Wen of Jin, “The books of the West contain this: ‘If the sun and moon do not remain in their places, who can attain peace?’” This too is from the *Book of Zhou*.

Examining the ancient references to the “West,” they invariably point to Zhou. Guzhu was a state in the west of Liaoxi, while Lu, Wei, and Qi were eastern feudal lords. Thus they all referred to Zhou as the West, never extending the term to the far reaches beyond the Flowing Sands.

As for the Yin-Yang masters who speak of certain spirits and baleful influences residing in the West, they refer to the space within walls—how could they mean distant lands?

Moreover, when Confucius traveled south to Pei and met the Old Master, the Old Master called him a worthy of the North—how could this refer to the lands of the Xiongnu or the like?

In the second year of King Lie of Zhou, the Old Master passed through Qin. Duke Xian of Qin heard of this and went out to the suburbs to welcome him, providing lodging and offering ritual inquiries. He said, “Our humble state is remote and isolated, and I am unworthy. Yet you have graced us with your presence. I dare not trouble you with matters of military campaigns, but may I inquire about the celestial reckoning?”

The Old Master said, “How could an old man like me know such things?”

The Duke said, “I wish to hear it. Please do not decline.”

The Old Master said, “Qin originally united with Zhou but later separated. After five hundred years, they will reunite. Seventy years after that, a hegemon or king will emerge. Beyond this, it cannot be foretold.”

The Duke was pleased and said, “I wish you would stay a while longer so that I may serve as your disciple.”

The next day, the Old Master departed without taking leave and exited through the Hangu Pass.

During the reign of Duke Xiao of Qin, the Old Master returned east and traveled with Master Yin and others to Mount Meng in the Red City of Shangyu. He revisited the abode of the Great Emperor of Fusang, where he assembled the host of immortals. Later, he concealed himself in Mount Jingshi of Luoyang.

At that time, five yellow-haired old men—some riding cranes, others clad in feathers, holding staffs of green bamboo—gathered to discuss the reckonings of heaven and earth. No one in the world recognized them.

The Old Master composed scriptures totaling a hundred thousand words. Beside him stood two divine beings, holding a golden flask filled with ink as thick as lacquer. When the ink was sprinkled on wood or stone, it formed seal and clerical script.

Wang Jia’s *Record of Forgotten Tales* states: “Once, the Old Master resided on Mount Jingshi and discussed the reckonings of heaven and earth with five old historians, composing scriptures totaling a hundred thousand words. Two divine beings from the kingdom of Futi emerged with a golden vessel containing ink as thick as lacquer. When sprinkled on wood or stone, it formed seal and clerical script. When the ink in the golden flask was exhausted, the two wished to refine their hearts and distill their blood to replace it.”

The five elders were the Five Heavenly Emperors of Indra. Mount Jingshi is Mount Taishi.

During the reign of King Zhao of Qin, the Old Master journeyed with Perfected Lord Yin and various immortals through the mountains of Nüji, Difei, and Tianzhu. They then exited westward through the Hangu Pass, crossed the Flowing Sands, ascended Kunlun, returned to the Upper Palace of Purple Tenuity, and finally came back to the homeland of the Supreme Mystery. When the king heard of this, he regretted not having witnessed it and ordered the construction of cities and towns at the western foothills where the Old Master had passed, to mark the sacred proximity. Today, the Old Master’s Pavilion and Post Station and Yin Xi’s City remain, their ancient ruins still extant.

King Xiaowen of Qin hunted between Ba and Han and personally visited the Louguan Terrace to pay homage and offer sacrifices. In the tenth year of King Nan’s reign, the cyclical year *bingshen*, the Old Master dispatched envoys to summon the recluse Shen Xi. Xi, originally from Wu Commandery, had studied the Dao in Western Shu and was able to relieve the suffering of others. One day, while traveling with his wife, Madam Jia, to visit their daughter-in-law, Zhuo Kongning, they encountered a procession on the road: one dragon carriage, one tiger carriage, and one white deer carriage, each drawn by a team, with a grand retinue of crimson-robed attendants bearing insignia. They asked Xi, “Are you the scholar-recluse Shen Xi?” Startled, Xi replied, “Yes.” The riders said, “Scholar, your hidden merits have benefited all beings, and your heart has never forgotten the Dao. From youth to now, your conduct has been flawless. The Yellow Thearch and the Old Master have now sent immortal officials to welcome you. The one in the white deer carriage is Attendant Gentleman Bo Yan; the one in the azure dragon carriage is Transcendent Lord Sima Sheng, Master of World-Transcendence; and the one in the white tiger carriage is the Welcoming Envoy Xu Fu.” In an instant, three feather-robed immortals bearing tablets presented Xi with a white jade register, its borders of blue jade and its characters of cinnabar, inscribed with red script. The Attendant Gentleman of the Azure Vault then carried Xi away to ascend into heaven. At the time, farmers working the fields all witnessed this. Soon, a great mist arose, and when it dispersed, only Xi’s oxcart and ox remained

Amidst the resonant clanging of bronze and iron, the four walls radiated a luminous glow, inscribed with writings. The Old Master stood over ten feet tall, his body emanating a dazzling brilliance too bright to gaze upon directly. He commanded a Jade Maiden to hold a golden tray bearing a jade cup filled with elixir, which he bestowed upon Xi, saying, “This is the divine cinnabar—those who drink it shall not die.” Each received a single *daogui* measure. They were instructed to drink in silence, bow without offering thanks, and were then given two dates, each as large as a chicken’s egg. The Old Master further presented Xi with a talisman and a single scroll of immortal methods, saying, “For now, return to the mortal realm to relieve the suffering of others. If you wish to ascend again, hang this talisman at the tip of a pole, and I shall dispatch envoys to welcome you.” With these words, Xi suddenly felt as though he had fallen into a deep sleep, only to awaken upon the earth.

Empress Dowager Dou, afflicted with illness, once sent envoys to summon Xi. He remained among mortals even into the reign of Emperor An, but later ascended to heaven once more. The First Emperor of Qin built a shrine to the Old Master south of the Louguan Terrace, endowing it with temple households. He personally composed an invocation, which read:

*“The Great Dao flows boundless,
Ever near to left or right.
When the Old Master departs, he wanders west;
When he returns, he glances east.
I now attend to Penglai and Yingzhou—
May your response be swift as echo or shadow.”*

The First Emperor performed the rites himself, and suddenly, the Old Master descended before his seat. Overcome with awe and joy, the emperor prostrated himself repeatedly, entreating the Way with utmost reverence. The Old Master smiled but made no reply, instead bestowing upon him the *Guilin Sutra* and talismans for commanding ghosts and spirits. The emperor pleaded incessantly, yet the Old Master vanished in an instant. The First Emperor returned in disappointment, though the invocation he had composed remained preserved in writing.

*Annals of the Primordial Sage, Scroll Six* (End)
*[Note: The character “文” was originally written as “父”; amended according to the *Jiyao* edition.]*

*Annals of the Primordial Sage, Scroll Seven*
*Compiled by the Song Dynasty Master of Observing Revival, the Eminent Scholar Xie Shoubin*

The Old Lord, pitying the decline of the Zhou and the growing corruption of customs, divided his body to dwell in the world and save the age. He returned near the banks of the Shan and Yellow Rivers, concealing his name and lineage. People thus called him the Old Man of the River, or the River Master.

He once transmitted the Dao to An Qisheng, who later passed it to Ma Mingsheng. Ma Mingsheng taught Yin Changsheng, who in turn taught Master Er Zhu. Thereafter, none knew to whom it was further transmitted. Ma Mingsheng was also recorded as originally surnamed He, styled Junxian. While serving as a county clerk capturing bandits, he was gravely wounded and near death when he encountered the Perfected Lady of Supreme Truth, who revived him with medicine. Junxian, feeling indebted, begged to serve her, hoping only to learn her method for treating sword wounds, unaware she was an immortal. After years of diligent service, the Lady, seeing his reverence and that his spirit remained as vigorous as before, said to him: "The Dao I practice cannot be transmitted except to lofty perfected ones; it is not something to teach beginners. An Qisheng possesses the method of the Golden Fluid and Returning Elixir. Tomorrow he will come, and I shall entrust you to him. Following him for long, you will attain the Dao." The next day, An Qisheng indeed arrived, and the Lady commended Junxian to him. Junxian followed An Qisheng, serving him for nearly twenty years before receiving the oral instructions for the elixir. After refining and ingesting it, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

Yin Changsheng, a kinsman of Empress Yin of the Later Han, was fond of the Dao from youth. Hearing that Ma Mingsheng had attained the Dao of transcending the world, he served as his bondsman for over a decade. Mingsheng did not teach him the Dao but only discussed mundane matters. Of the twelve who served alongside him, all grew resentful and left, save for Yin, whose reverence only deepened. Mingsheng then told him: "You alone are truly fit to receive the Dao." He erected an altar, cut a covenant in blood, and transmitted to him the oral instructions for the divine elixir. Later, on Mount Pingdu in Zhongzhou, Yin refined and ingested the elixir, ascending to heaven in broad daylight. In his own preface, he wrote: "In the first year of *Yanguang* of the Han, north of Xinye Mountain, I received the essential instructions for the divine elixir. Having attained the Dao and departed the world, I concealed myself in the famous mountains. Should any obtain this, they shall be ranked among the perfected and move freely between past and present. Why seek the common world’s method for immortality? The essence lies in the divine elixir. Practices such as circulating qi, guiding and pulling, bending and stretching, or consuming herbs and plants may extend life slightly but cannot eternally transcend to become a divine immortal. If you wish to hear it, this is the essential teaching: it is the fruit of accumulated study, not the work of spirits. Superior scholars strive diligently; inferior ones laugh, deeming it impossible. Those who know the divine elixir shall enjoy enduring vision and eternal existence." Thereupon, he inscribed three copies of the elixir scripture on yellow silk and golden tablets, concealing one each in Mount Song, Mount Hua, and Mount Sui. Another copy on silk he entrusted to his disciples, to be transmitted through the generations.

An Qisheng also separately transmitted the Dao to Mao Xigong, who taught Le Xiagong, who taught Le Jugong, who taught Master Gai. According to the *Records of the Historian*: "Among the clan of Yue Yi was Le Jugong, who was skilled in cultivating the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. His original master was the Old Man of the River, though his origins were unknown." The Old Man of the River taught An Qisheng, who taught Mao Xigong, who taught Le Xiagong, who taught Le Jugong, who taught Master Gai. Master Gai then taught in Gaomi and Jiaoxi, becoming the master of Chancellor Cao of the Han. It is also said: "In the first year of Emperor Hui’s reign, Cao Shen governed Qi. He summoned all the elders and scholars to ask how to pacify and unite the people. The scholars, numbering in the hundreds, each gave different answers. Unsure whom to follow, Cao heard that Master Gai was skilled in the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi and sent a messenger with generous gifts to invite him. Master Gai expounded that governing the Dao valued clarity and stillness, so the people would naturally settle. Expounding further, Cao adopted his methods and governed Qi for nine years, bringing peace and unity to the state."

Emperor Wen of Han and Empress Dowager Bo both favored the teachings of *Laozi*. They decreed that all princes and ministers must recite and study it. The *Records of the Historian: Biographies of Confucians* states: "Emperor Wen favored penal statutes and names; Emperor Jing demoted Confucian teachings; Empress Dowager Dou favored the Yellow Emperor and Laozi." The *Book of Han: Biographies of the Imperial Relatives* states: "Empress Dowager Dou favored the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, but Emperor Jing and the imperial relatives were not permitted to esteem them." Given this, it was Emperor Jing who favored them, so why is Emperor Wen mentioned? Perhaps the Empress Dowager’s preference also stemmed from Emperor Wen’s own fondness.

Ban Gu’s *Postface to the Biography of Yang Xiong* states: "Lao Dan composed two chapters on emptiness and nothingness, disparaging benevolence and righteousness, rejecting ritual and learning. Yet later generations who favored him esteemed it above the *Five Classics*. Even Emperors Wen and Jing of the Han spoke thus, though Emperor Wen’s preference for *Laozi* was not as fervent as his regard for penal statutes and names." He frequently sought and dispatched recluses to interpret its meaning. Someone reported: "By the riverside lives an old man, whose name no one knows. He is called the River Master and recites the two chapters of the *Daodejing*." The emperor heard this and sent an envoy to inquire about the scripture’s purport. The old man said: "The Dao is honored, virtue is noble; this cannot be asked from afar." The emperor then personally visited him. Descending from his carriage with utmost reverence, the emperor found the old man sitting arrogantly in a thatched hut. Undeterred, the emperor said: "All under heaven is the king’s land; all within the seas are the king’s subjects. Among the four great things in the realm, the king is one. Though you possess the Dao, you are still my subject. Why not humble yourself? Why so haughty?" The old man clapped his hands and laughed, leaping tens of feet into the air, halting in the void. Looking down, he replied: "Above, I do not dwell in heaven; in the middle, I am not burdened by men; below, I do not reside on earth. What subject am I?"

The emperor then realized he was a divine being and immediately prostrated himself in reverence, bowing his head in apology: "I, with my meager virtue, have inherited the ancestral legacy. My talents are small for so great a task, and I fear I am unworthy. Though I govern worldly affairs, my heart is ever devoted to the Dao and virtue. Only through ignorance have I failed to perceive proper ritual. I humbly beg the Dao Lord to instruct me."

The old man then bestowed upon the emperor two volumes of plain silk scriptures, saying: "Study these deeply, and your doubts will resolve themselves. There is no need for further discourse." He continued: "Since I composed these texts, over seventeen hundred years have passed. They have been transmitted to only three others—now including you, four in all."

As he finished speaking, clouds and mist gathered on all sides, and heaven and earth darkened. The old man vanished without a trace.

This account appears in the *Records of the Grand Historian* and Ge Hong’s *Traditions of Divine Transcendents*. Today, the temple of the River Earl stands north of Shan Prefecture, along with Emperor Wen’s Terrace for Gazing at Transcendents, both still extant.

The *Traditions of Divine Transcendents* states that the River Earl rose into the void and departed amid mist, though some dismiss this as fanciful. They argue that if Laozi expounded the scripture during the reign of King Zhao of Zhou, fewer than seventeen hundred years would have passed by the time of Emperor Wen, making the claim impossible. However, Bian Shao’s *Stele Inscription for Laozi’s Ancestral Hall* asserts that Laozi had served as teacher to emperors since the time of Fuxi. The *Inner Traditions* records that he was a teacher during the reign of Shun, known as Master Yin Shou, and composed the *Scripture of Dao and Virtue*—thus, its antiquity is well established. Though Yin Xi requested Laozi to write the text, why could Laozi not have revisited his own ancient teachings?

The two volumes of plain silk scriptures the old man bestowed are none other than the authentic *Scripture of Dao and Virtue*. Having received such radiant grace, the heart and spirit awaken of themselves—what need is there for further explanation?

The *Laozi* commentary attributed to the River Earl in later times is likely a misattribution by later scholars, who mistook the author for the commentator and thus falsely ascribed it to him. Liu Zhiji of the Tang, in his *Three Teachings Pearl Grove*, also stated that there is no commentary on the *Laozi* by the River Earl. Before the Qin and Han dynasties, classical texts were never annotated—how much less so for the Way, which is said to be "easily known"? Why would one need to add explanations oneself?

Emperor Wu of Han, fond of the Dao and enamored of transcendence, frequently worshipped at famous mountains, hoping for an encounter with immortals. Yet his ceaseless military campaigns and insatiable desires betrayed his aspirations. Laozi then commanded the Queen Mother of the West and the Upper Prime Lady to descend together into his court, assessing his sincerity and instructing him according to his capacity.

The Queen Mother said to him: "If you wish to cultivate yourself, you must first nurture your qi. The *Perfect Scripture of Great Transcendence* speaks of the Way of Increasing and Transforming: 'Increasing' means augmenting essence, and 'Transforming' means altering form. One who can both increase and transform is recorded in the registers of highest transcendents; one who fails in both will not escape death and calamity.

To practice the Way of Increasing and Transforming, constantly contemplate the Numinous Treasure. 'Numinous' is spirit, and 'Treasure' is essence. You need only refine your essence and solidify your qi. Qi transforms into blood, blood into essence, essence into spirit, spirit into fluid, and fluid into marrow. Practice this tirelessly, and spirit and essence will overflow.

In the first year, you will augment your qi; in the second, your blood; in the third, your essence; in the fourth, your vessels; in the fifth, you will transform your marrow; in the sixth, your sinews; in the seventh, your bones; in the eighth, your hair; and in the ninth, your form. When your form is transformed, you will undergo metamorphosis; through metamorphosis, you will attain the Dao; and attaining the Dao, you will become a transcendent."

The Upper Prime Lady said to the emperor: "Do you truly love the Dao? I hear you have frequently summoned masters of esoteric arts, sacrificed to mountains and rivers, and worshipped at sacred altars—your efforts have indeed been diligent. Yet your diligence has not borne fruit, and there is a reason for this.

Your inborn nature is violent, lustful, extravagant, cruel, and predatory. These five traits are the saws and knives that sever your body, the axes and hatchets that cleave your life. Though you profess a desire for longevity, you cannot cast aside these five faults. Your labors are in vain, bringing only minor benefits to sustain yourself.

From this day forward, cast off these five natures and return to gentleness and goodness. Be clear in your duties, attentive to those below, broaden your thoughts, and cultivate generosity. Show compassion to the poor and orphaned, lighten taxes, and cherish your body. Constantly perform hidden virtues, rescue those in peril, and deliver the dying from calamity. Abandon lust, nurture your spirit; forsake extravagance, embrace utmost frugality. Diligently observe fasts, moderate your diet, sound the Heavenly Drum, drink Jade Nectar, bathe in the Flowery Pool, and strike the Golden Beam. Practice these disciplines, and there may yet be hope."

She then dispatched her attendant, Ji Luorong, to Mount Fu Guang to seize the Azure Perfected Youth and obtain the *Divine Flying Methods of the Six Jia from the Left and Right*, a text composed by the Supreme One of the Three Heavens, intending to bestow it upon the emperor.

The Azure Perfected Youth reported back: "A Chang said he received your instruction and, responding to the Mother’s summons, visited the household of Liu Che. I did not expect the Celestial Numinous One to descend into such foul impurity. I wonder how you have been faring of late?"

Ji Luorong arrived and said: "The Venerable Mother wishes to obtain the twelve sections of the *Golden Book of Secret Characters*—the *Divine Flying Methods of the Six Jia Spirits from the Left and Right*—to bestow upon Liu Che. I have sealed one copy to deliver as a token."

Yet A Chang continued: "Though Che has the heart for it, he is no transcendent’s material. Should we not fear that these teachings will be leaked to a mere walking corpse? Recently, in the emperor’s presence, I have heard many speak thus: 'Mountain ghosts wail in the thickets, lonely souls howl in distant lands. Armies are raised, and clans are rewarded for merit, yet the toil of war is forgotten, and white bones lie abandoned. The common folk are harassed, and cruelty runs rampant. The Supreme One has already made his crimes manifest, and resentment is visible in the heavens. Clamor fills the air—he will certainly not attain transcendence.'

I dare not disobey your command."

The Queen Mother laughed and said: "Many speak thus of this man, but the emperor need not be entirely dismissed."

Those who earnestly aspire to the Dao of transcendence must cultivate sincerity and focus their thoughts, observe fasting to reflect on their transgressions, and thereby eliminate a hundred faults. By restraining the self and returning to goodness, honoring the true spirits, and preserving authenticity while guarding unity, one may, in a single month, erase a thousand faults. Che has long yearned for the Dao, his heart ever diligent; he has repeatedly prayed at sacred mountains, seeking deliverance. Weighing his merits against his faults, they nearly balance. Yet from this day forward, he must diligently refine his resolve and sincerity, heed the words of the Lady of Primordial Yang, and no longer indulge in extravagance, cruelty, or tyranny—lest the multitudes suffer and perish, and the wronged souls of destitute ghosts raise cries of desecration, while the blood-soaked dead are forgotten despite their meritorious deeds.

Then the Lady rose, grasping a jade tablet inscribed with phoenix patterns, and, gazing heavenward, intoned: "The Nine Heavens stretch boundless, Mount Tai radiates its numinous power; the spirits illumine the subtle and profound, the pure and void shine bright. Those who refine their essence attain marvels, those who guard their qi attain life. When devotion to the Dao reaches its peak, stillness evokes the true, and accomplishment follows. To exhaust the spirit and debase the form is to invite disgrace; to calm the essence is to preserve youth and flourish.

She bestowed upon Che the *Lingfei* scripture, along with the Six Ding Talismans for summoning the attendant spirits of the Celestial Radiance, by which one may tread the void, conceal one’s form, attain longevity, and preserve vision—restoring youth and retaining vitality. Should he violate or disclose these teachings, calamity will swiftly overturn him; should he defy the celestial truth, he will surely sink into the depths of darkness. Let him beware of disaster—I dare to admonish you, Liu Che."

Having spoken thus, the Lady plucked forth the ritual implements she had bestowed and displayed them. The Queen Mother of the West then conferred upon him the *Chart of the True Forms of the Five Peaks*. The Emperor bowed to receive it and, with a jeweled casket, enshrined it within the Pavilion of the Ministering Beam.

For six years after receiving the teachings, the Emperor felt his spirit and qi clear and unbound, believing himself visited by divine transcendents and certain of attaining transcendence. Relying on this, he neglected to cultivate utmost virtue, instead constructing palaces and pavilions, exhausting the myriad people, and ceaselessly waging war—so that the roads swelled with lament and resentment. By the eleventh month, day *yi-you*, of the first year of the *Tai-chu* era, heavenly calamity struck the Pavilion of the Wutong Beam. The *Chart of the True Forms*, the *Lingfei Scripture and Record* in twelve scrolls, and all fourteen volumes of his own received and composed texts—along with their caskets—vanished entirely. Though he repeatedly entreated the Queen Mother, she never descended again.

In the eighth month, day *ren-chen*, of the second year of the *Tian-han* era, the Venerable Lord once more dispatched Wei Shuqing to meet the Emperor. At the time, the Emperor was idly residing in his palace hall when suddenly he beheld a figure in feathered robes and star-crowned cap, riding a cloud-borne chariot drawn by white deer. Startled, he asked who it was, and the figure replied: "I am Wei Shuqing of Zhongshan." The Emperor said: "If you are indeed a man of Zhongshan, then you are my subject—come forward and speak with me." Shuqing suddenly vanished without a trace. The Emperor deeply regretted this and immediately dispatched the Minister of Liang to Zhongshan to seek him out. Though he could not be found, the minister brought back his son, Du Shi, to meet the Emperor.

The Emperor asked: "Where is your father now?"
Du Shi replied: "My father has long been devoted to the Dao of transcendence, shunning worldly affairs. He abandoned his family and departed forty years ago, saying he would enter Mount Taihua."

The Emperor then sent Liang Bo with Du Shi to Mount Hua to search for him. At the foot of a sheer cliff, they beheld Shuqing playing a game of *bo* with several companions upon the rock, enveloped in purple clouds, with a forest of white jade and several transcendents holding pennants and staffs standing behind them. Du Shi gazed upon them and bowed twice. Shuqing asked: "Why have you come?"
Du Shi replied: "The Emperor deeply regrets that he was unable to speak with you the other day. He has now sent the emissary Liang Bo with me, wishing to see you again."
Shuqing said: "Previously, I was sent by the Most High to inform him of the great calamity’s time and the methods to avert peril and extend the dynasty’s fate. Yet he, arrogant and self-exalting, failed to recognize the true Dao and instead sought to make me his subject. Unworthy of such discourse, I departed. Now I must join with the Central Yellow and Grand Unity to stabilize the celestial mandate. I shall never return."

Liang Bo returned and reported this. The Emperor regretted it deeply.

The Grand Historian Sima Tan was especially versed in the Way of Laozi. In his *Essential Pointers of the Six Schools*, appended to the *Records of the Grand Historian*, he wrote: "The Daoists enable one to unify the spirit and act in accord with the formless, suffusing the myriad things. Their teachings proceed by following the great harmony of yin and yang, adopting the virtues of the Confucians and Mohists, and distilling the essentials of the Logicians and Legalists. Adapting to the times and responding to things, they establish customs and enact affairs—nothing is unsuitable. Their principles are concise and easy to grasp; their affairs few, yet their achievements many.

Those who follow yin and yang make one constrained and full of fears; the Confucians are broad but lack essentials, laboring much yet achieving little; the Mohists are frugal but difficult to follow; the Legalists are stern but lack benevolence; the Logicians are overly exacting and prone to losing the truth. Only the teachings of Laozi are called the Great Way: 'Act through non-action,' and 'There is nothing that is not done.' In practice, it is easy; in discourse, it is hard to know. Taking emptiness and non-being as its root and following along as its function, it forms no fixed patterns and has no constant shape, thus able to succor the conditions of the myriad things. It does not precede things, nor does it follow them, and so can be the master of the myriad things. With method yet without method, acting in accord with the times; with measure yet without measure, harmonizing with things. Worthiness and baseness become self-evident; black and white take form. Whatever one desires to employ, what affair cannot be accomplished? Thus it accords with the Great Way, blending in profound obscurity, illuminating the world, then returning to non-illumination.

What gives rise to people is the spirit; what it relies upon is the form. When the spirit is overused, it is exhausted; when the form is overtaxed, it decays. When spirit and form separate, death ensues. The dead cannot be revived; the separated cannot be rejoined. Thus the sage values them. From this perspective, the spirit is the root of life, and the form is its vessel. Without first stabilizing the spirit and saying, 'I have the means to govern the world,' how can it be done? Therefore, the Yellow Emperor and Laozi come before the Six Classics."

From the *Records of the Grand Historian*.

The *Han Dynasty Bibliographic Treatise* states: "The Daoist school grasps the essentials and holds to the root, preserving purity and emptiness, maintaining humility and weakness—this is the art of the ruler facing south."

Zhao Rong and Ouyang Xiu, in the *Comprehensive Catalogue of the Chongwen Library*, wrote: "The Daoist school is rooted in purity and emptiness, abandoning vigor and desire, maintaining a tranquil self-possession. Thus it says: 'I practice non-action, and the people transform themselves; I cherish stillness, and the people rectify themselves.' Though the sage faces south to govern, this cannot be altered. Yet some fail to fathom its root, casting aside benevolence and righteousness to return to spontaneity, taking 'following along' as its function—this the Confucians criticize."

In the fifth year of the *Chu-yuan* era of Emperor Xiao-yuan, on the third day of the fourth month, day *ding-chou*, the Venerable Lord, by the command of the Heavenly August Emperor, bestowed upon the transcendent Mao Ying a divine seal and jade tally. Ying, styled Shushen, was a native of Nanguan in Xianyang. Born in the fifth year of Emperor Jing’s reign, day *bing-shen*, at the age of eighteen he abandoned his family to study the Dao. He entered Mount Chang to gather mushrooms and herbs for sustenance, constantly reciting the *Dao De Jing* and the *Book of Changes*. After six years, hearing that the Perfected King of the Western City was accomplished in the Dao, he went directly to seek him as his teacher. Scaling lofty peaks and traversing perilous terrain, braving brambles and dangers, he finally encountered him and served as his attendant for seventeen years, never daring to slacken. The Perfected King made Ying responsible for managing his robes, books, charts, and silk cords.

Three years later, the Perfected King took Ying with him in a nine-canopied carriage to the White Jade Tortoise Terrace to pay homage to the Queen Mother of the West.

The Perfected King bowed deeply and withdrew. The Queen Mother of the West said, "Perfected One, you now bring a mortal with you to ascend the numinous terrace—is this not laborious?" The Perfected King smiled but did not answer. Then, glancing at Ying, he rose to bow and request esoteric essentials. Ying kowtowed twice and said, "This humble one, a lowly and base creature, a remnant of withered bones, dares to seek the span of accumulated moons with the fleeting quality of a morning mushroom. Thus, I have endured the perilous depths of dark forests, coveting the life bestowed by my teacher. The Perfected King, pitying my arduous path, has granted me the essentials of bodily cultivation and the method of absorbing qi. Now, my teacher deigns to instruct me further—ought I not seek greater benefits from the *Book of Changes*? Who could have foreseen that today I would encounter such an august presence? I humbly beseech the art of eternal life, to awaken this walking corpse."

The Queen Mother bestowed upon him the *Scripture of the Jade Girdle and Golden Pendants*, along with the *Mystery of the Supreme Ultimate and the Perfected True*. After three years of practice, the Perfected King further granted him a dose of the Ninefold Cyclically Transmuted Elixir, saying, "Your Dao is now accomplished; you may return. Later, you shall be entrusted with immortal office in the lands of Wu and Yue." Ying then took his leave and returned to serve his parents. In his hometown, those who had died unjustly—even those long buried—were all commanded by the Earth Bureau to be restored to life.

He remained at home for fifty-three years, living in obscurity. After both his parents passed, he observed mourning rites with proper decorum. His two younger brothers, Mao Gu and Mao Zhong, both served in the Han court. Gu became the Grand Administrator of Wuwei, and Zhong the Grand Administrator of Shangjun; they assumed office on the same day, and the carriages and horses of those seeing them off filled the roads. By then, Ying had already received the Perfected King’s secret decree regarding his ascension. He privately told those bidding farewell, "Though I shall not hold office as a *erqian shi*, I too shall have a divine appointment. Next year, on the third day of the fourth month, will you all come to visit as you do today?" The crowd joyfully agreed.

In the fourth year of Emperor Yuan’s *Chuyuan* reign, this came to pass. The following year, those seeing him off gathered as promised, their numbers several times greater than before. On that day, the Lord Lao, by the command of the Celestial August Emperor, dispatched the Embroidered-Robe Envoy of the Hidden Realm Palace, Ling Guangziqi, to confer upon Ying the divine seal and jade insignia. The Lord Emperor of the Grand Tenuity likewise sent the Left Palace Attendant of the Three Heavens, Guan Tiao, to bestow upon him an eight-dragon brocade chariot and a robe of purple feathers. The Supreme Lord of the Great Dao dispatched the Harmonious Dawn Gentleman, Shi Shumen, to present Ying with the Golden Tiger True Talisman and the Flowing Gold Bells. The Sacred Lord of the Golden Watchtower commanded the Perfected of the Great Ultimate, the Orthodox Unity Supreme Mystery Jade Gentleman Wang Zhong, and Bao Qiu, among others, to grant him the four-jointed Swallow’s Embryo, the Flowing Brightness Divine Mushroom, the Ever-Shining Numinous Flying Night-Glowing Cave Herb, bidding him to bow, consume them, fasten the seal, don the robe, straighten his cap, face north with the talisman girded, and stand grasping the bells.

The four envoys addressed Ying: "He who consumes the four-jointed Hidden Mushroom of the Great Ultimate shall be ranked as a Perfected Minister. He who consumes the Jade Mushroom Swallow’s Embryo of the Golden Watchtower shall be ranked as Director of Destinies. He who consumes the Flowing Brightness Golden Bloom of the Eastern Palace shall be ranked as Director of Records. He who consumes the Ever-Shining Numinous Flying Herb shall be ranked as Perfected Earl. He who consumes the Night-Glowing Cave Herb shall oversee the duties of the Left and Right Censors. You have consumed them all—your lifespan shall equal that of heaven and earth, and you shall become the Director of Destinies, Supreme Perfected of the Eastern Marchmount. You shall govern the gods and immortals of the Wu and Yue regions, and command the mountain sources of the lands east of the Jiang."

With these words, the envoys departed. In an instant, the Lords of the Five Directions each descended in chariots of their directional colors, accompanied by their retinues, to receive the Celestial August Emperor’s decree. They bestowed upon Ying the register of Director of Destinies and Supreme Minister of the Eastern Marchmount, adding the Nine Bestowals. The plaque was carved from purple jade, inscribed in yellow gold, its text reading:

*"Of Ying’s nature, void and distant, limpid and bright, he has from youth been steeped in the profound and mysterious. From his earliest years, he let his hair loose upon the northern mountains, calming his heart amid forests and marshes, refining his essence in pursuit of the divine. Ascending peaks and traversing valleys, he arduously sought the gates of his masters, casting off form and severing ties, offering his body to the ten thousand fords. His cinnabar sincerity, unconstrained and natural, followed the course of heaven."*

Thereupon, he sought out master artisans and was granted the sacred writ. He sheared his hair and swore solemn vows, severed his locks and mutilated his body, girded himself with rope and rejoiced in solitude, unashamed of hunger and cold. Wherever he went, he followed only the Dao; what he preserved was his true nature. His sincerity shone before the August Emperor, moving the Great Mystery to deep emotion.

Thus, he is now invested with the rank of Grand Primordial Perfected, assuming the office of Supreme Minister of the Eastern Marchmount and Director of Destinies. With a heart level and upright, he holds fast to his resolve as to metal and stone. His cinnabar heart outshines the multitude; his spirit abides in lofty radiance. In recompense, he is granted the Jade Mace and Green Pennant of the Eight Might, that he may subdue the sources of rivers and marshes, overawing the ten thousand spirits.

Through frost and snow in forests and valleys, he savored the mysterious and gazed upon the true. His thoughts stirred within secluded peaks; his heart’s sincerity was revealed. In recompense, he is granted the Purple Yak-Tail Standard and the Lotus-Blossom Crown of Splendor, that he may summon and command the myriad spirits, seal mountains, and call forth clouds.

Abandoning his home, he journeyed alone, leaving kin to delight in immortality. He endured hardship and tempered his resolve, winter-bare upon the mountains and rivers. In recompense, he is granted the Embroidered Feather Robe and Vermilion Flying Sleeves, that he may ascend the celestial stairs with ease, embracing his mandate among the Jade Perfected.

Treading deep marshes and scaling perilous cliffs, his heart remained steadfast in its aspirations, never faltering in fear. In recompense, he is granted the Mottled Dragon Chariot and White Tiger Cushion, that he may roam and feast in the vast heavens, soaring to the imperial court.

Pushing through brambles to merge with the light, enduring cold and frost, his heart sought the radiant Perfected, unwavering and unafraid. In recompense, he is granted the Curved Spirit Canopy and Jade Pavilion, that he may wander and gaze through the Nine Palaces, his spirit serene in warmth and secrecy.

Rejecting profit and fleeing glory, his heart knew no weariness. Concealing his form in secluded peaks, he meditated in stillness amid the myriad forests. In recompense, he is granted the Flowing Gold Fire Bells and Twin Pearl Moonlight, that he may ascend to the Great Ultimate and communicate his voice to the Upper Clarity.

With a heart pure and lofty, he remained untouched by excess or defilement. Unadorned and luminous, his inner and outer being were level and smooth. In recompense, he is granted the Brocade Banner and Embroidered Banner-Staff, that he may summon the Six Yin Jade Maidens to attend his chariot.

His kindness extended to all things; his hidden virtue nurtured the myriad beings. Even the tiniest creatures were the object of his care. In recompense, he is granted the Phoenix and Luan Pipes, the Golden Bell and Jade Chime, that he may harmonize his spirit in the empty halls, delighting the Perfected and dancing with the spirits.

Hunger and thirst nurtured his spirit; hardship and toil sought the true. The myriad things could not sway his resolve; a thousand evils could not corrupt his purity. Thus, he is bestowed the Purple Grove’s Riches and Jade Nectar of Golden Infants, that his lifespan may equal the Three Luminaries, his name inscribed upon the Cinnabar Jade Tablet.

His bearing led the pure and mysterious; his purple weft illumined the eight directions. His heart blazed with double fire; his spirit leapt in the great auroras. Truly the eldest among the Perfected, he is thus styled Grand Primordial. His nine virtues now complete, he has moved the accumulated essence of the Great Tenuity. Heaven and humanity, in empty whiteness, converge without design. Thus, nine recompenses are granted to requite his past devotion.

His heart and spirit now aligned with his aspirations, the Four Numinous Ones find their abode. His cinnabar sincerity shines forth; his grasp of the true remains unshaken. Upright and steadfast, his mirror reflects even the subtlest details. Thus, he is appointed Prime Minister of the Supreme Ministry, overseeing the Eastern Marchmount, and further charged with the office of Director of Destinies, to manage the registers and records. He is assigned forty Jade Lads and forty Jade Maidens to attend him in his comings and goings from the Great Tenuity, where he receives his duties at the Great Ultimate. His administrative palace is the Vermilion City Jade Grotto. Let him govern with diligence and report all matters, great and small.

At the age of one hundred and two, having received his mandate, he mounted his dragon chariot and turned to address those present:

The true Dao conceals its traces, never seeking outward display. Yet in my present actions, I dazzle the eyes and ears—this I do to entice and encourage my two younger brothers, that they may follow and emulate me, and further, that all under heaven with hearts inclined toward the Way may know its direction.

My two brothers, upon hearing of my departure, may resign their offices and abandon their emoluments to seek me out. For now, I shall temporarily reside on Mount Juqu east of the Jiang River.

Having spoken thus, he ascended his feathered chariot. Banners and canopies billowed in dense array as he harnessed dragons and tigers, floating away into the void. His two brothers, upon learning of his ascent to immortality, both abandoned their posts and returned home. In the first year of *Yongguang*, they crossed the Jiang to seek him on Mount Juqu. He taught them the method of transforming withered bones into flourishing life. The two brothers practiced with utmost diligence, and after twenty-four years, he further petitioned Lord Wang to bestow upon them the Nine-Turned Returning Elixir. Having ingested it, they attained the Dao.

In the third year of *Yongshi* of Emperor Cheng, the *dingwei* year, he led his two brothers to the Golden Gates, where they petitioned the Sage Lord to receive the scriptures. Thereupon, the Supreme Lord dispatched a messenger from the Vermilion Palace to present the Purple Writs to Lord Zhong, saying:

*"The Writ of the Supreme Heavenly Perfected states: Mao Gu of Xianyang, dwelling at the Southern Pass, styled Jivei, is destined to attain immortality and hold the position of Registrar of Destinies, concurrently overseeing the earthly perfected. He is charged with recommending those of the Dao, for their lifespans are intertwined with his. Wherever he governs with reverence, the laws of the four extremities shall be upheld. His administrative palace shall be the Terrace of the Vermilion Terrace, governing Mount Juqu in Danyang. Let him be diligent in his duties and report all matters, great and small."*

It further stated: *"Zhong and his younger brother Zhong, though late in returning to rectitude, possess pure and comprehensive talents. Their thoughts are subtle yet their sincerity is thorough, cutting off the deceptions of the Six Heavens. Their brilliance stands out clearly. Now they are appointed to oversee the Three Offices of Life-Preservation, establishing their names and governing Mount Tai. They shall record life and death, holding the rank of Earthly Immortals. As luminaries of the Nine Palaces, they shall exhort and instruct the young, guide those who are nearly perfected, teach and train the female officials, transmit wondrous numina, govern the hundred spirits, supervise the refinement of essences, inspect the sources of waters, regulate the flows of rivers and seas, seal and guard the golden valleys, keep the registers of jade liquors, oversee the planting of dragon mushrooms and cavern herbs, and the radiant night grasses. Their governance shall be at Mount Liangchang, at the mouth of the Northern Cavern, guarding the gates of the Yin Palace."*

When the messenger had finished delivering the writs, Zhong and his two brothers took leave of the Golden Gates and returned to their place of governance.

In the second year of *Yuan Shou* of Emperor Ai, the *gengshen* year, on the eighteenth day of the eighth month, the *jiyou* day, the Lord of Destinies took leave of his two brothers, saying: *"Now I depart to assume my duties and shall no longer visit you frequently. However, on the eighteenth day of the third month and the second day of the twelfth month each year, be sure to invite my master and the Supreme Numinous Red Perfected of the Southern Marchmount to visit this mountain. Those who are devoted to the Dao shall await me here, and I shall surely instruct them."*

Thereupon, he and Lord Wang mounted their chariots together and departed for the Vermilion City Jade Grotto.

Old traditions record that the Lord of Destinies received his investiture and ascended in flight only after his two brothers received their writs at the Golden Gates, but I privately suspect this account is in error. For instance, when he ascended Mount Juqu in his feathered chariot, bearing the purple-tasseled staff, accompanied by hundreds of civil and military officials—how could this have occurred before receiving his mandate? If he ascended in the *jiwei* year, how could he have led his two brothers to the Golden Gates?

In the second year of *Heping* of Emperor Xiaocheng, the *jiawu* year, Lord Lao manifested himself and descended, wandering by the Quyang Abyss in Langye Commandery. Today, in Guizhou, there is Mount Quyang with the Divine Han River; in Dingzhou, there is also Mount Quyang with the Divine Han River; in Haizhou, there is Quyang City, north of which lies the Feathered Pool; in Shouzhou, there is Quyang City and the Northern Han River. Gan Ji and his disciple Guan Chong were both from Langye, and their activities took place in Quyang of Haizhou.

At that time, a man of Beihai named Gan Shi (later changed to Ji), devoted to the Dao, suddenly contracted a severe illness. For over a decade, no medicine could cure him. Morning and night, he burned incense and lamented to Heaven, praying for deliverance. Lord Lao, moved by his sincerity, sent the immortal Bo He to pose as an old medicine-seller in the market. Gan Ji approached him, and Bo said: *"If you truly wish to be cured, come at cockcrow tomorrow to the north of the great bridge, beneath the magnolia tree by the Ye Ford. There I shall teach you the method to cure your illness."*

At cockcrow the next day, Gan Ji went to the place, but Bo had already arrived. Angrily, he said: *"If you do not wish to be cured, leave. Come again at midnight."* Thereupon, Gan Ji arrived at the first watch and waited long until Bo appeared. He produced two scrolls of plain silk, bound with blue heads and vermilion eyes, titled *The Blue-Headed Writs of Peace*. Handing them over, he said: *"This is the* Taiping Jing *of the Supreme Lord Lao. By obtaining this scripture, you shall not only be cured of your illness but also attain longevity and transform the world."*

Gan Ji bowed and received the scrolls, leaping with joy and moved to tears. His illness was instantly cured. Lord Lao descended again and personally instructed him in its essentials. He then compiled the previous scriptures into one hundred and seventy scrolls.

As recorded in the *Book of the Later Han*: *"Initially, during the reign of Emperor Shun, Guan Chong of Langye presented at court, offering the divine scriptures his master Gan Ji had obtained by the waters of the Quyang Abyss—one hundred and seventy scrolls, all on silk, with vermilion borders, blue heads, and vermilion eyes, titled* The Blue-Headed Writs of Peace*. It was said that Ji had personally received them from Lord Lao. These are the* Taiping Jing *of the Daoist tradition."*

The scripture is divided into ten sections according to the ten celestial stems: *jia*, *yi*, *bing*, *ding*, *wu*, *ji*, *geng*, *xin*, *ren*, and *gui*, each section containing seventeen scrolls. *Piao* refers to bluish-white silk, and *su* to fine silk, bordered in vermilion. *Dao* denotes the headings, and *mu* the titles.

The *Taiping Jing* states: *"In my scriptures, the good are all marked with blue heads and vermilion eyes, aligning with my Dao—this is the trust of vermilion and blue. Blue represents life, benevolence, and the heart. Red represents the Great Yang, the true color of Heaven."* Gan Ji grasped its essentials and thereafter cultivated his body and nurtured his nature, dispelling disasters and curing illnesses—all without fail.

The essentials state: *"When a person is born, Heaven bestows upon them spirit, Earth bestows upon them essence, and the harmonious center bestows upon them [qi]. If one can preserve essence, cherish spirit, and guard the Mysterious, within they will attain longevity, and without they will bring the realm to Great Peace."*

It further states: *"A minister’s conduct should be to love their ruler above all. Those who desire long life—if they are children—should think of their parents, fearing their aging, and guide their intentions toward the Dao, constantly seeking rare formulas and extraordinary arts to serve their lord and kin. As disciples, they should remember their masters’ grace, for though one is born of parents, it is through masters that virtue and power are perfected, and through rulers that honor and glory are attained. Ever mindful that their lord, father, and master will age, and having no means to repay them, they should study further and serve the worthy, seeking rare formulas and wondrous methods to benefit them. Such are the most loyal ministers, filial children, and obedient disciples of the Highest Antiquity and August Sovereign. If all people act thus, the world will attain Great Peace."*

*"Those with heart do only good, their words and deeds sincere and substantial. They examine themselves strictly, purify their hearts, and reform their conduct. Moved by this, those above bestow Heaven’s grace, and they, in turn, express gratitude and remain steadfast without ceasing, thereby attaining immortality."*

The formula states: *"My character ‘eleven’ is named ‘Scholar.’ *Bing-wu* and *ding-wei* are the ancestral beginnings. Four mouths govern affairs and the principles of all things. The ‘Child’s Turban’ uses the horn to govern the right. The ‘Hidden Dragon’ must not be employed—*kan* corresponds to *si*. Those who behold it live long. Dwelling between Heaven and Earth, one merely lives, but governing a hundred thousand people, immortality awaits. Those who skillfully cure illness need not seek recompense. There is no joy like the Long Peace Market, making people’s lives as long as the Queen Mother of the West, akin to the four seasons cycling endlessly. Ninety characters and strategies are transmitted to the fangshi. These divine elixirs and marvelous strategies may be offered to one’s lord, father, and master."*

Furthermore, [the scripture] expounded the regulations and precepts for *jijiu* (libationers) and entrusted them to Gan Ji. Thereafter, Ji revered the scriptures, practiced the Dao, and disseminated them among his disciples, establishing male and female officials to broadly transform the people, drawing followers from all directions.

In the second year of *Yuanhe* during the reign of Emperor Zhang of the Later Han, the Lord Lao descended again and admonished Ji, saying: *"I previously transmitted the Dao to you to assist the state and sustain life, worrying for the myriad people. Yet of late, male and female disciples have usurped my titles, coveting wealth, indulging desires, acting arrogantly, envying the worthy, and obstructing talent, engaging in mutual slander—none of this is as it should be. Thus, I have come to speak with you."*

Ji prostrated himself, touching his head to the ground a hundred times, and said: *"Yes, yes! May the Most High pardon past transgressions and permit this servant to cultivate future goodness. From this day forward, I know not how to save the male and female disciples from their misfortunes."*

The Lord Lao said: *"Listen well, listen well! Though a person may live ten thousand years, without upholding the precepts, what difference is there from ancient wood or enduring stone? Better to die as a person of virtue and power for a single day while upholding the precepts than to live in violation of them. Those who uphold the precepts supplement the celestial offices and ascend as *shi jie* immortals. Though worldly people may be kings, dukes, or even emperors, if they commit grave sins, it avails nothing for their souls and spirits. How can this not be grievous? Clearly uphold my precepts to instruct them."*

Thereupon, he expounded one hundred and eighty precepts. Having finished, he further told Ji: *"The immortals and sages of ancient times all attained the Dao through this. The Dao is originally formless; it is perfected through the master. The Dao must not be abandoned, nor the master slighted."*

Ji bowed deeply, received the command, and expressed his gratitude with repeated bows.

According to the *Biography of Pei Kai* in the *History of the Later Han*: Kai, during the reign of Emperor Huan, again memorialized, saying: *"Formerly, Gong Chong presented divine books that focused on revering Heaven and Earth, taking the Five Phases as their foundation. They also contained methods for strengthening the state and increasing descendants, their texts being easily understood and aligning with the classics. Yet Emperor Shun did not implement them, and thus the imperial line did not flourish."* Kai’s words were often forthright and direct, but the emperor did not adopt them.

The *Commentary of Crown Prince Zhanghuai* cites the *"Rise of Emperors and Kings"* chapter of the *Scripture of Great Peace*, which states: *"A perfected person asked a divine person: ‘I wish to enable emperors and kings to swiftly attain Great Peace. May I hear how?’ The divine person replied: ‘Simply follow the Way of Heaven and Earth without losing their distinct divisions, and Great Peace will be swiftly attained.’*

*‘*Yuanxi’ has three names: Great Yang, Great Yin, and the Harmonious Center. Forms have three names: Heaven, Earth, and Human. Heaven has three names: sun, moon, and stars, with the North Pole as the center. Earth has three names: mountains, rivers, and level land. Humans have three names: father, mother, and child. Governance has three names: ruler, minister, and people. If these three constantly support one another as if of one mind, not losing even the slightest measure, sharing the same concerns and uniting as one family, Great Peace will be swiftly attained, and longevity will be unquestionable."*

When Emperor Ling ascended the throne, he deemed Kai’s words correct. The Grand Mentor Chen Fan recommended Kai as *fangzheng* (upright and principled), but he did not accept the appointment. His hometown revered him, and whenever a new governor arrived, he would be the first to pay respects.

During the *Qingping* era, he was summoned along with Xun Shuang and Zheng Xuan (or Zheng Yuan) as *boshi* (erudites), but he did not go.

Fan Ye’s commentary states: The ancients said, "He who speaks well of Heaven must have verification in man." Zhang Heng likewise declared: "Astronomy, calendrical calculations, yin-yang divination, and prognostications—these are what we must urgently attend to today." Only men like Lang Yi and Pei Kai could look up to observe the heavens and look down to examine the earth, correlating them with human affairs. When fortune and misfortune, good and ill omens duly manifested, they drew upon them to clarify moral teachings. This is how the unfolding of the Dao/Way proved beneficial to the age, and what later generations should take as their measure.

Ji Hou later took up residence in Kuaiji. When Sun Ce pacified the Jiangdong region, seeing that the people revered Ji as a divine being, he invited him to be a guest and kept him in his army. Many officers and soldiers fell ill with epidemics, but whenever Ji spat water upon them, they would recover. The officers and soldiers all venerated Ji, bowing to him each day before paying their respects to Sun Ce. Enraged, Sun Ce arrested Ji and reproached him, saying: "Heaven has long been parched, and the waterways are dry. Yet you sit idly in your boat, playing the sorcerer, bewitching my officers and soldiers and disrupting my ranks. Today I shall do away with you." He bound Ji and exposed him, demanding that he summon rain. "If you can move Heaven to send a great downpour by midday, I shall pardon you. If not, you will be executed." Soon clouds gathered, and rain poured down in torrents. Sun Ce nevertheless put Ji to death. His officers and soldiers wept, wishing to recover his body for burial, but suddenly his corpse vanished. Soon after, Sun Ce was wounded by assassins sent by Xu Gong. Gazing into a mirror, he saw Ji’s reflection. Sun Ce cried out in terror, hurled the mirror away, and died as his wound burst open. Ji continued to move among people for over a hundred years before ascending as an immortal.

The above account is not sourced in the *Yuan* registers; it is briefly recorded in the *Preface to the Scripture of Great Peace* and the main text of the *Scripture of Great Peace*.

In the third year of the *Yongchu* era of Emperor An, on the *jiyou* day of the second month (Year of the Rooster), the Venerable Lord descended upon Mount Tai. He dispatched the Mount Tai emissary Ya Yu with chariots and cavalry to summon Liu Tu, a virtuous scholar of Jiangxia, intending to have him collate and verify the registers of all under Heaven. When Tu arrived, he saw the Venerable Lord seated facing south upon the throne. The Most High resided in the east, facing west; the Nine Heavens’ Immortal Lords resided in the south, facing north; and the Eight Poles’ Heavenly Lords resided in the west, facing east. The Venerable Lord commanded Tu and his officials to collate and verify the names and karmic registers of all under Heaven, completing the task in three days. Wishing to show Tu the retribution of sins and blessings, the Venerable Lord ordered Ya Yu to lead Tu into the earthly prisons. They passed through eighty-one hells, where Tu saw his father suffering in torment. Upon emerging, he pleaded with the Venerable Lord on his father’s behalf, and the Venerable Lord commanded his release. Ya Yu then led Tu to the heavenly realms, where he beheld the Golden Terrace and Jade Palace within the Great Clarity Palace. Music arose spontaneously, and many immortals, clad in azure robes and holding golden tablets, chanted and recited scriptures. After the tour, Tu was sent back. Through this experience, he attained the Dao/Way, became a Daoist priest, and later ascended as an immortal.

This account appears in the *Rhapsody on the Sage’s Chronicle in the Tang History* and the *Yingyuan Diagram*.

In the first year of the *Han’an* era of Emperor Shun, on the fifteenth day of the first month (Year of the Horse), the Venerable Lord descended upon Mount Heming in Shu, where Zhang Ling was cultivating the Dao/Way. Riding a cloud chariot and attended by a host of immortals, he summoned Zhang Ling before him through divine messengers and said: "Your ancestor Zhang Liang accumulated merit and virtue, bestowing blessings upon his descendants. Your destiny is to become a Daoist priest." He then bestowed upon Zhang the newly revealed *Secret Registers of the Orthodox Unity Alliance of Might*, instructing him to sweep away the residual darkness of the Six Heavens and purify the world through transformation. He also taught him the methods of paying homage to the sun and moon, and the techniques of the *High Ascent* and *Yuyi* and *Jielin* rituals, commanding him to practice them. From then on, the people flocked to him as their teacher, and his disciples numbered in the tens of thousands. When the people suffered from illnesses or official calamities, they would offer rice, silk, cloth, hemp, miscellaneous utensils, paper, brushes, firewood, and the like to atone for their sins. They would also submit written confessions of their transgressions, repent, and swear never to repeat them, whereupon their misfortunes would dissolve. Thus, the people all delighted in following him, and none dared to do wrong.

At the outset, his successor was named **Dao-ling**, styled **Fu-Han**, born in the fifteenth day of the first lunar month of the *jiawu* year (10 CE) during the reign of Emperor Guangwu’s *Jianwu* era, in the Tianmu Mountains of Yuhang. At the age of seven, he could recite the *Dao De Jing*. Later, he became a scholar, mastering the Five Classics and well-versed in the writings of the *He* and *Luo* Rivers, as well as celestial patterns. In the second year of Emperor Zhang’s *Yuanhe* era (*yiyou*, 85 CE), he was summoned as a *boshi* (erudite) but declined the appointment. At fifty-two, he declared that Confucian learning was of no benefit to longevity and lamented: *"How fleeting is human life! Old age and death draw near, and I cannot endure the suffering of life and death again."* He then dispersed his disciples and pursued the Dao of long life, retreating to Yunjin Mountain—now known as Longhu Mountain in Xinzhou.

In the fourth year of Emperor He’s *Yongyuan* era (*renchen*, 92 CE), he was appointed *Jianyidafu* (Remonstrating Grand Master) but declined due to illness. The emperor again summoned him with a third-rank seal and cord, a four-horse carriage, and the title of *Taifu* (Grand Tutor), enfeoffing him as Marquis of Jixian. Dao-ling told the envoy: *"Convey my gratitude to the Son of Heaven: in the Nine Heavens above, in the realm of Nothingness, the Golden-Portal Lord of the Imperial Palace—I have already become his subject. Only through purity and restraint can the world govern itself. What need has it of me?"* He then withdrew to Mount Song, where he fasted, meditated on the Dao, and constantly recited the *Dao De Jing*. After nine years of cultivation, his sincerity moved Lord Lao, who dispatched a divine messenger to inform him: *"Within the stone chamber of the Central Peak lie the ancient celestial texts of the Three Sovereigns, the* Huangdi Jiuding Great Elixir Scripture*, and the* Five Marchmounts True Forms Map*. You may go and receive them."* Entering the stone chamber, he indeed obtained the celestial treasures and the *Huangdi Jiuding* elixir methods. However, his family had long been poor, lacking the means to refine the elixir. Hearing that the people of Shu were simple and teachable, and that the region abounded in famous mountains, he led his disciples into Shu and secluded himself on Mount Heming.

There, he encountered Lord Lao, who provided the necessary ingredients. Following the method, he refined the elixir for three years. Upon its completion, he dared not consume it, telling his disciples: *"The divine elixir is now ready. If I take it, I shall ascend to become a Perfected. Yet I have not rendered great service to the world. I must first eliminate harm and promote benefit for the state, aiding the people, before I consume the elixir, ascend lightly, and serve the Three Realms—lest I feel remorse."* Soon after, Lord Lao sent the Pure Harmony Jade Maiden to teach him the method of exhaling and inhaling pure harmony. After a thousand days of practice, he could inwardly perceive his five viscera and outwardly gather the myriad spirits. He then took three steps and nine strides, treading the Dipper and the Qian trigram, wielding his power to subdue demonic forces wherever they manifested. He battled the demonic lords of the Six Heavens, seized the Twenty-Four *Zhi*, transformed them into blessed precincts, and renamed them *Huayu* (Realms of Transformation), demoting their former masters to the rank of *yin* officials.

Previously, tens of thousands of demonic spirits in Shu had roamed freely, holding markets in broad daylight, spreading plagues, and afflicting the people for generations. After the great demons of the Six Heavens were subdued, Dao-ling banished their hordes to the barren lands of the northwest. He swore an oath with them: *"Humans rule by day, ghosts by night. Yin and yang are distinct, each with its own domain. Those who violate this shall face the laws of the* Zhengyi*, and be punished without mercy."* Thus, the realms of the living and the dead were separated, and their paths diverged. To this day, the "Ghost Market" at Qingcheng in western Shu, along with the stele of Dao-ling’s oath to the spirits and the celestial and terrestrial stones bearing the sun and moon, remain. The oath stipulated that the spirits could only emerge when heaven and earth aligned and the sun and moon shone brightly.

On the first day of the fifth month, Lord Lao descended again, bestowing upon Dao-ling the following scriptures: nine scrolls of the *Taiqing Zhongpian Jingmu*, thirty-six scrolls of the *Jinye Danjing*, twenty-four scrolls of the *Taiqing Zhongjing* and *Shizhong Miyao*, the *Xuannü Mimiao Jing*, *Suzhen Jing*, *Shiyin Jing*, *Jiuding Bianhua Lianzhen Xuandong Zhi Fa Kaiming Dajing*, *Yuce Shanji Tulu*, *Shanhai Dajie Heluo Tuchen*, the inner and outer *Huangting*, the inner and outer *Taiqing*, *Yuce Youjing*, *Wuxing Jingwei Dongjing Muyu Jing*, *Tianjing*, *Dijing*, *Mingjing*, *Shanjing*, the *Bianjing Jing*, *Hejing Neishi Jing*, *Jiutian Yuanpu*, *Jiutian Yuan Dongjiu Zhen Zhongjing*, *Wulao Baojing*, *Danjian Heilu*, *Shangxia Zhaipin Jiewen*, *Taiyi Jinye Jing*, *Zhenyi Shouxuan Jing*, *Sanyuan Zhongtai Suling Zhongjing*, *Jinque Dijun Qingtong Neijing*, *Dongxuan Zhenjing*, *Sanhuang Neiwen*, *Xuanhua Sidao Chengbai Guan Jie*, *Sanwu Feibu Chengxuan Jing*, *Jiashan Tu*, *Mingjin Dilu*, and the *Gui Li Zhufa*—totaling nine hundred and thirty scrolls—as well as seventy scrolls of talismans, making one thousand scrolls in all.

Lord Lao further commanded Dao-ling to attend his dragon-drawn chariot to the Seven-Treasure Terrace of Kunlun’s Banfeng and Jincong. There, the Jade Dawn Great Dao Lord was in residence. Lord Lao presented Dao-ling to pay homage, whereupon the Jade Lad was dispatched to bestow upon Dao-ling the seal and cord of the Celestial Master, entrusting him with the governance of the twelve hundred officials. He also granted him the male and female swords, the *Eryi Jiaotai* crown, the *Quxie Qi*, the *Yulie* robe, talismans, registers, contracts, and commandments, along with scriptures and precepts, charging him to assist the state, protect destiny, nurture life, and suppress evil.

After the rites, Dao-ling returned. In the first year of *Jiankang* (*jiashen*, 144 CE), the Celestial Master moved to Mount Yuntai in Guizhou. On the fifteenth day of the tenth month, Lord Lao descended once more, bestowing upon him the *Zhengyi Mengwei Jing*, the secrets of the Three Actions and Six Penetrations, the scriptures of the Three Caverns, and the methods of the two seasonal fasts, all revealed through the celestial characters of the *Lingbao Wupian* as the essential and wondrous scriptures.

Now proclaiming the teachings to the ten directions’ celestial and human beings: on all *jiazi* days, *gengshen* days, the eight seasonal nodes, the Three Primes, the Five La Festivals, and the Three Assemblies, all must bathe, fast, and maintain abstinence, offering sacrifices, reciting invocations, and praying to atone for transgressions, erase entries from the Black Registers, and perform the rites of the Nine Ancestors’ Merit Feast. The above is recorded in the *Biography of the Celestial Master* and the *Illustrated Records of Shu*.

In the second year of *Jianhe* (*wuzǐ*, 148 CE), on the seventh day of the first month, the Celestial Master took Zhao Sheng as his disciple. Twelve divine maidens descended, transforming a bitter spring into a salt well. Later, the commandery was named *Ling* to commemorate his merit, and it was later renamed *Xianjing* Salt Well, now known as Longzhou.

In the first year of *Yongshou* (*yǐwèi*, 155 CE), on the seventh day of the first month, Lord Lao descended again, commanding the Celestial Master to travel together to Chengdu. Lord Lao rode a dragon chariot, while the Celestial Master mounted a white crane, alighting amidst five-colored clouds at the site where the Jade Maiden of Taihao refined the elixir. Earth spirits surged forth, jointly bearing a jade platform over ten feet high to serve as a sacred seat. There, Lord Lao expounded the methods of the Northern Dipper for excising death and inscribing life. By the fifteenth day, he further revealed the Southern Dipper’s powers of refining the hun-soul and forging the po-soul. The *Scriptures of the Northern and Southern Dippers* were later composed by the two attendant perfected ones, Shu and Mi, based on these events.

After Lord Lao departed, the jade platform remained, sinking into the earth to form a well, now located within the inner city of Chengdu, called the Jade Platform Abbey. Later, they traveled to Lutang Mountain, where Lord Lao bestowed upon the Celestial Master the *Texts for Subduing the Six Heavens and Beheading Demons*.

In the third year of *Yongshou* (*bǐngshēn*, 157 CE), the Celestial Master was one hundred and twenty-three years old. On the ninth day of the ninth month at noon, Lord Lao dispatched envoys of the Five Emperors bearing jade tablets, descending upon Mount Yuntai to confer upon him the title of *Zhengyi* Perfected One. Soon after, five-colored cloud-dragons arrived to welcome him, and together with his disciples Wang Chang and Zhao Sheng, he ascended to heaven. This is recorded in the preface to the *Scriptures of the Northern and Southern Dippers*.

In the eighth year of *Yanxi* (*yǐsì*, 165 CE), on the *jiazi* day of the eighth month, the Emperor dreamed of Lord Lao descending in a jeweled canopy chariot to the palace courtyard. He then dispatched envoys to offer sacrifices at his birthplace in Ku County of Chen Commandery. The Emperor ordered Bian Shao, the Chancellor of Chen, to compose an inscription, which read:

Lord Lao served as the Keeper of Archives for Zhou, and during the reign of King You, when the Three Rivers trembled, he admonished the ruler with the lessons of Xia and Yin’s decline, using the principles of yin and yang. Confucius, born in the twenty-first year of King Ling of Zhou, studied ritual with Lao Dan. Calculating his age, Lao Dan was already over a hundred when Confucius sought his instruction. One hundred and twenty-nine years after Confucius’ death, the Grand Scribe Tan of Zhou was identified as Laozi, though none knew his ultimate fate. His two-chapter work declares that the reason heaven and earth endure is that they do not live for themselves. From the dawn of humanity, bodies have succeeded one another, and the meaning of life and death is thus evident. Some speak of the "valley spirit that does not die," referring to the mysterious female, and from this, later seekers of the Dao expanded upon it, asserting that Laozi’s separation and union with the primordial qi marked the beginning and end of the Three Luminaries. Observing the heavens, he crafted prophecies, ascending and descending with the Dipper stars, changing nine times with the sun, waxing and waning with the seasons, measuring the Three Luminaries, with the Four Spirits at his side. By visualizing the cinnabar field and the Purple Chamber of the Great One, the Dao is perfected, immortality achieved, and one sheds the mortal coil to transcend the world. Since the eras of Fuxi and the Yellow Emperor, he has served as the teacher of sages.

Ban Gu, however, claimed that Laozi "abandoned sagacity and rejected knowledge," calling ritual the root of chaos, and thus diverged from Confucius. In his *Han History’s Table of Ancient and Modern Persons*, he applied standards to rank Laozi below Confucius, grouping him with Zixi of Chu. These two assessments differ greatly—such is the saying: "Those whose ways differ cannot make plans together."

In the eighth year of *Yanxi*, on the *jiazi* day of the eighth month, the Emperor, revering virtue and magnifying the Dao, embracing vastness and brilliance, cultivating spirit and nurturing nature, with aspirations to soar among the clouds, turned his heart toward the Yellow Emperor and emulated the example of Gaozong, dreaming of Laozi and honoring him with sacrifices. At that time, Bian Shao, the Chancellor of Chen, oversaw the state’s rites. Though his talent was modest and his thoughts shallow, unable to fathom the perfected one or distinguish right from wrong, he relied on written records to assert that Laozi embraced emptiness, guarded stillness, delighted in namelessness, upheld non-virtue, shunned high office, and found peace in lowly positions. He left Confucius with words of benevolence, fled the world to live in seclusion, changed his name and surname, and feared being recognized.

The sun marks time by brightness and darkness, the moon completes itself through waxing and waning. The origins of growth and decline, the gates of fortune and misfortune—heaven’s way despises fullness and favors humility. Thus, Laozi’s exaltation in the present and the sacrifices offered to him by the people of this age stem from his fleeing emolument and dwelling in obscurity, diminishing himself again and again, manifesting the purity of emptiness and non-being, existing before heaven and earth, preserving authenticity and nurturing nature, thereby attaining the Five Blessings.

With reverence, I compose and inscribe this, its words reading:

Ah, the mysterious power,
Embracing emptiness, guarding purity.
Delighting in lowly positions,
Holding office without scheming.
As a plumb line, it straightens;
Bent, it can coil.
Facing the Three Rivers,
He vented his grievances and scattered his indignation.
Perceiving the moment, he acted,
Waiting at the suburbs, he emerged radiant.
The fortune of reclusion,
Fleeing the world, concealing his name.
Holding to the One without fail,
Bringing peace to all under heaven.
Dwelling in thickness, not thinness,
Abiding in substance, containing glory.
Taking models as weighty,
Gold and jade as light.
Cutting off desires, abandoning cravings,
Returning to infancy.
Vastly traversing the ages,
None knew his true nature.
Essentially, through non-action,
Great transformation is accomplished.
Advancing and retreating without constancy,
Interweaving constancy within.
Taking knowledge as ignorance,
Empty yet not full.
The measure of the great man,
Beyond the judgment of the ordinary.
The ninth-rank scholar,
How could it burden his name?
Shining like the sun and moon,
Aligned with the Five Stars.
Entering and exiting the cinnabar chamber,
Ascending and descending the Yellow Court.
Turning his back on the vulgar,
Discarding splendor, concealing his form.
Enveloping the primordial, transforming spirit,
Inhaling and exhaling the purest essence.
The world could not fathom him,
Knowing neither his life nor death.
Celestial and human ranks offer sacrifices,
Illuminating his numinous power.
Admiring his extended years,
I carve this stone to honor him.

In the twelfth month, the Emperor again dispatched the Palace Attendant Guan Ba to Ku County to offer sacrifices to Lord Lao. In the ninth year, he constructed the Zhuolong Palace within the Fanglin Garden, designating the literary chamber as an altar. He adorned it with pure gold clasps and sounding vessels, arranged a seat beneath a floral canopy, and employed the music of the suburban sacrifices. The Emperor personally worshipped Lord Lao, with the Buddhist figures of the Floating Image tradition as co-recipients of offerings. This is recorded in the *Book of Later Han*.

According to the *Account of the Western Regions* in the *Book of Eastern Han*: the kingdom of Tianzhu, also called Shenyin Gandhara, practiced the Dao of the Floating Image. During the reign of Emperor He, envoys were sent to offer tribute. By the second year of Emperor Huan’s Yanxi era, in the fourth month, they frequently arrived from beyond Rinan to present offerings.

Legend holds that Emperor Ming dreamed of a towering golden figure with a radiant halo around his neck. When he inquired of his ministers, one replied that in the West there was a deity named Buddha, whose form stood sixteen feet tall and shone like gold. Thereupon, the Emperor dispatched envoys to Tianzhu to inquire about the Buddha’s Dao and law, and had his image painted in China.

In the second year of Emperor Ling’s Guanghe era, on the first day of the first month of the year *jiwei*, Lord Lao commanded the Heavenly Emperor of the Nine Heavens to descend with the Three Caverns Dharma Master Xu Laile and others upon Mount Tiantai. Lord Lao rode in an eight-scene jade chariot, accompanied by ten thousand attendants, with the Orthodox Unity Perfected One in attendance.
