Lord Lao styled himself the Supreme Primordial Unity Perfected One, and appointed Zhen Dingguang as the High Mystery Dharma Master of the Cavern Scripture. He ordered the Jade Gentleman of the Immortal Attendants, Wang Sizhen, to unfurl the Nine Radiance Jade Report and present the *Cavern Mystery Great Cavern Numinous Treasure Scripture* in thirty-six sections to the immortal Ge Xuan, along with two grades of Upper Clarity purification rites.

The first was the secluded fast, where one severs worldly ties, cultivates stillness, and models the deportment of the void—entering sageliness and ascending emptiness. The second was the pure altar fast, where one reverently follows the true rites: first delivering the nine ancestors, then one’s household, and finally oneself.

These were the three registers and seven grades of purification rites: the three registers were the Gold Register fast, which harmonizes yin and yang and safeguards the nation’s destiny; the Jade Register fast, which protects empresses, consorts, dukes, and noble clans; and the Yellow Register fast, which delivers the nine lineages and seven ancestors, forever freeing them from the suffering of eternal night.

The seven grades were: the Sun Illuminating Perfection fast, which transcends the departed and resolves all karmic entanglements; the Sun Natural fast, which universally seeks blessings for sentient beings, atones for transgressions, and cultivates the Dao for immortality; the Three Primes fast, which atones for violations of precepts and resolves judgments before the Three Officials; the Eight Nodes fast, which repents the sins of one’s ancestors and oneself, erasing the Black Record; the Cavern Spirit fast, which values purity and simplicity as the highest, seeking immortality and protecting the nation; the Instruction fast, which esteems clarity and plainness, curing illnesses and averting calamities; and the Mud and Charcoal fast, which values austerity as merit, repenting transgressions and seeking blessings.

Along with these, he bestowed the *Sutra of the Wheel of Admonition and Precepts* in forty-five scrolls, the *Boundless Penetrating Mystery*, the *Turning Spirit into Samadhi*, and other scriptures upon Ge Xuan, charging him to propagate them in the world.

He declared: "You should constantly contemplate the Highest Great Dao King of the Vacuous Perfected One, the Lord of the Great Thousand Worlds, known as Laozi, who is the Great Dharma Master of the Mysterious Center. Ge Xuan, styled Xiaoxian, was born in the seventh year of Emperor Huan’s Yanxi era, on the eighth day of the fourth month of the year *jiachen*. His father, Ge Han, served as a Secretary. When Xuan was born, a certain Zhi Daoji, a master of the lofty Dao, said to his father: 'Yesterday, I heard that the Master of Penetrating Mystery descended from the Great Canopy Heaven. Your family shall become the patriarchs of divine immortals—this child is no ordinary one.'

His father replied: 'The transformations of immortals are unfathomable. I would be content if he merely lived a long life as my heir.'

Xuan grew up refined and intelligent, his nature and understanding exceptionally bright. He perused all the classics, masters’ works, philosophers, and histories without exception. At the age of fourteen, having lost both parents, he suddenly sighed: 'The world possesses an eternal, deathless Dao—why not study it?' Thereupon, he wandered to renowned mountains, seeking out extraordinary persons. Ingesting the essence of mushrooms and trees, he received the *Scripture of the Nine Elixirs and Golden Liquid* from the immortal Zuo Ci.

Xuan diligently observed the purification precepts, excelled in healing illnesses and exorcising ghosts, and practiced breath control and guiding exercises. Through concentrated meditation on the Dao, he moved Lord Lao to descend personally and bestow upon him scriptures and methods. He also revealed the celestial court’s decrees on the karmic conditions of worldly beings, namely the *Tract on Response and Retribution*. He further said: 'When merit fills three thousand, one ascends to heaven in broad daylight. With surplus goodness, one descends seated upon a cloud chariot. Ceaselessly propagating the Dao, one attains deathlessness.'

Xuan bowed his head in reverence and gratitude.

Today, at the Tongxiang Abbey on Mount Tiantai, the site of the Dharma Wheel Courtyard where the Three Perfected Scriptures were transmitted, as well as the Interrogation Altar built by the immortal lord’s spirit laborers, still remain.

On the first day of the tenth month of the second year of Sun Hao’s Chiwu era, the day *jiazi* at noon, Lord Lao commanded the Heavenly Emperor of the Nine Heavens to present the Golden Tablet of the Supreme Three Heavens, bestowing upon Xuan the title of Left Immortal Duke of the Great Ultimate. The decree read:

'Ge Xuan of Jurong County in Danyang Commandery of Yangzhou: in past lives, you practiced austerities, accumulated merit over kalpas, sacrificed your body in almsgiving, and rescued those in peril. You turned the wheel of the sacred realm, refined spirit and preserved essence, exhausted your will in the source of the Dao, and served your master with reverence. Your diligent cultivation was unceasing, deeply moving the Supreme. Your name is inscribed in the Mysterious Capital, and your rank is fixed among the Upper Immortals. Thus, your wisdom and intelligence were born into a virtuous lineage. Your talents are jade-like and refined, your fragrance like orchids in bloom. Your celestial disposition is transcendent and outstanding, towering above the crowd. Your Daoist counsel is profound and majestic, unmatched among your peers. You renounced worldly glory, embraced simplicity, and upheld authenticity. You focused your heart on the true immortals, embraced the primordial and guarded the One. You let your hair loose in the forests and mountains, dwelling in a cliffside abode. Delighting in the mountains and forests, you contemplated the lotus blossoms. You fasted past noon, never missing a day of purification. You burned incense and recited scriptures, never neglecting your bows and rites. With a compassionate heart, you delivered others, tirelessly working for their salvation. Your will pursued the subtlest traces, and the great Dharma was thoroughly mastered. You taught both the wise and foolish, explaining all without exception. Humble and amiable, you always wore a joyful countenance.'"

Now bestowed upon you are crimson brocade robes, skirts of flying silk, celestial feathered garments, a lotus-dawn crown, the Golden Perfected Divine Tiger Staff, and the command of demonic maidservants—whatever you will, you may summon the seas and call forth the mountains.

For your profound studies of hidden scriptures, your long fasts and quiet contemplation, your focused and unwavering dedication, you are now granted the Eight Radiance Jade Chariot, to ride dragons and mount the clouds, with a halo of light encircling your neck, and golden seals with jade inscriptions, to repay your past virtues. Thus shall you be of the Daoist gate for generations to come. May later worthies look up to you, upholding the Dharma-wheel. The Most High’s venerable teachings are expounded by the Three Heavens.

Your embodiment of virtue is vast and far-reaching; you instruct and transform both Daoists and laity, universally aiding all beings. Your great attainment of wondrous enlightenment has earned you a name among the lofty perfected, and even the mighty demons submit in reverence. O towering sage, radiant and majestic, your grace fills the ten directions, and all beings attain their desires. Now you are bestowed the rank of Most High Jade Capital Supreme Left Immortal Lord, overseeing the officials and retinues of the Six Heavens and the Great Demon Kings of the Three Realms. You are granted five hundred jade lads and five hundred jade maidens as attendants, bearing in your left hand the incense and flowers of the Nine Luminaries, and in your right the *Supreme Cavern Mystery Scriptures* of the Most High. With two thousand dragon-riders, you shall roam the Nine Palaces of the Upper Clarity, thrice a year ascending to the Golden Towers of the Jade Capital in the Supreme Mystery Metropolis. The Most High confers this mandate, and the Heavenly Emperor receives the decree to carry it out. Proceed according to the ancient statutes of the Three Heavens.

At that time, the śramaṇas Śākyamuni’s disciple Daowei and Zhu Falan, having heard from afar of the Immortal Lord’s attainment, came to seek him as their teacher. They bowed and inquired: “We have heard that the Dao has no before or after, no past or present. We wonder whether the Most High Lord Lao and Prince Siddhārtha’s paths are the same or different.”

The Immortal Lord replied: “In essence, there is no distinction of high or low, noble or base, form or name.”

Thereupon, he established an altar on Mount Tiantai and transmitted to Daowei and Falan the *Chart of the Five Sacred Peaks’ True Forms*, saying: “This is the token of the Most High’s command. Carry it as you travel to the famous mountains of the eight directions, and the Three Realms shall welcome you.” He also bestowed upon them the *Most High Golden Elixir Scripture*, the *Five Spontaneous Treasures of the Numinous Jewels*, the *Three Sovereigns’ Inner Writ of the Great Existence*, the *Celestial Master’s Golden Book of Jade Characters*, and other texts totaling ten scrolls, along with a stalk of *taiji* dragon-script magic fungus. He charged the immortal officials to wear them on their persons and commanded his chamber disciple Zheng Siyuan to impart the oral instructions in full. He further gifted them a grain of immortal medicine, enabling them to abstain from grains and forsake the five flavors, so they might wander freely among the famous mountains.

Daowei and the others, having received the sacred scriptures and divine medicine, felt neither hunger nor thirst. They wished to remain a while longer to serve the sagely master, but the Immortal Lord said: “If your heart holds me, I shall always be visible to you. There is no need for attachment. You should ascend Mount Song; after five years, the perfected ones of that mountain will transmit to you the secret instructions, and your Dao shall be accomplished.”

The Immortal Lord once remarked to the various Confucians discussing the classics: Zhang Zhao asked, “‘Transmitting but not creating, trusting and loving the ancients, I humbly compare myself to Old Peng’—what is the meaning of this?” The Immortal Lord replied: “Laozi expounded the Dao of spontaneity, transforming the world, while Confucius journeyed through the states, extending benevolence to the people. Their ultimate aim is one and the same.”

Taizi Deng once asked the Immortal Lord: “Zhuangzi said, ‘Beyond the Six Directions, the sage acknowledges but does not discuss; within the Six Directions, the sage discusses but does not debate.’ Why is this?” The Immortal Lord replied: “Within the Six Directions, bound by forms, images, and the reckonings of yin and yang, one may still make inquiries. Beyond the Six Directions, where human sight rarely reaches, in the vastness beyond the void, the sage refrains from discourse, hence ‘acknowledges but does not discuss.’” Deng praised this answer.

In the *jiazi* year of the seventh cycle, at the age of eighty-one, on the fifteenth day of the eighth month, the Most High commanded his attendants to ride dragon-unicorns, bearing the Nine Radiance Staff and the Cloud Banner, harnessing nine dragons and the Eight Radiance Cloud Chariot, with canopies of blossoming shade and banners of dragon maidens and tiger flags, to welcome the Immortal Lord. The Immortal Lord reined in his cloud steed, halting his chariot in the Great Void, and composed three five-character poems to leave behind for the world.

The first:
*The perfected of old left teachings,*
*Moved by those lost in doubt.*
*Those who reject the Dao for heresy*
*Bring calamity upon themselves.*
*When the body dies and the name perishes,*
*What regret can there be?*
*Great sins cannot be absolved;*
*Who shall purge this poison?*
*In the unseen, the time of emergence is unknown;*
*Only when the kalpa ends may it cease.*
*Reincarnated in poor and lowly families,*
*Some become servants again.*
*With senses impaired or incomplete,*
*They beg in the marketplace like broken jade.*
*Unaware of the retribution for accumulated sins,*
*They blame the gods and resent Heaven.*
*The Great Dao is ever non-action;*
*Its vastness begins with goodness.*
*Now I, towering and revered,*
*Have earned this through my deeds.*
*The Three Realms bow in homage;*
*I dwell at ease within the Purple Palace.*
*Halting my chariot in the void,*
*I rein in dragons and phoenixes.*
*At parting, I leave these plain words,*
*Roughly outlining the wondrous record.*

The second:
*Now I ascend to Heaven,*
*Leaving behind the scholars with reluctance.*
*The Great Dao embodies emptiness and nothingness,*
*Yet within stillness, essence resides.*
*Those who observe call it obscure,*
*But within the profound, clarity shines.*
*Do not say the Dao is empty and false;*
*The fault lies in lack of sincerity.*
*Why not climb the famous mountains*
*And recite the* Cavern Real Scriptures*?*
*With each recitation and chant,*
*The mysterious sound pierces the Great Clarity.*
*The Most High’s golden countenance radiates;*
*The assembled immortals respond in unison.*
*From the ten directions, incense and flowers scatter;*
*Banners of fragrant sandalwood smoke rise.*
*The Royal Consort plays the Nine Shao;*
*Phoenixes and simurghs sing mournfully.*
*Dragon chariots obscure the sky in welcome;*
*Canopies gleam in the distant void.*
*Beyond the desolation of the kalpa’s measure,*
*The Lord of Śakra descends to the courtyard in reverence.*
*The Eight Kings offer the cinnabar elixir;*
*Embracing the current, their bodies become light.*
*Carefree, they wander beyond being and non-being,*
*Illuminating the names and forms of humanity.*
*Divine lads attend on either side;*
*Spontaneously, they pay homage to the myriad spirits.*

The third:
*Soaring in the Eight Radiance Chariot,*
*The flying wheels traverse the Celestial Capital.*
*Wandering leisurely in the Seven Treasures Palace,*
*Together we delight in eternal splendor.*
*Seven generations of ancestors ascend to the Hall of Blessings;*
*Former worthies, too, attain rebirth.*
*Kings and lords who are faithful and reverent*
*Shall ultimately become the world’s pillars.*
*If you can uphold sincerity,*
*You too shall ascend to the heavens.*

The Great One embodies the supreme virtue,
All things beneath receive its completion.
Roaming freely through mountains and rivers,
Exhaling and inhaling the numinous essence.
Refining qi in union with the subtle and serene,
Silently chanting the *Dao and Virtue* chapters.
With single-minded grasp of the mysterious void,
Hidden resonance now manifests in full.

Riding a chariot drawn by nine dragons,
Lightly ascending on purple mist.
A brief sojourn at Penglai Palace—
In an instant, already ascended to heaven.
Majestic, the assembly of perfected beings;
Vast and distant, they traverse the empty mystery.
Their bodies endure beyond kalpas,
Golden countenances ever fresh as the sun.

Should one fathom the manifold wonders,
Lightly they rise as divine immortals.

The song concluded, the cloud-borne chariot slowly ascended into heaven and departed.

In the first year of Xianxi of the Chenliu King of Wei, the jiashen year, Lord Lao descended and appeared in Longyou’s Lintao Commandery, Xiangwu County. His body stood thirty feet tall, clad in white robes, with flowing silver hair and a golden crown. He addressed Wang Shi of the county, saying: “The world shall soon attain Great Peace.” When Emperor Wu received the abdication, the realm was indeed unified, and he ordered the construction of the Taiping Abbey at the site of the apparition—formerly the residence of Li Zong.

As recorded in the *Book of Jin*:
Dai Yang of Wuxing was twelve years old when he died and revived after five days. He recounted that in death, a celestial envoy appointed him as wine steward, bestowing upon him a tally and thread, along with attendants and a banner-bearing retinue. He was led to ascend Penglai, Kunlun, Jishi, Taishi, Chang, Lu, Heng, and other sacred mountains, only to be later demoted and sent back. Thereafter, Dai Yang developed a passion for the Daoist arts, mastering divination, astrology, and numerology. At the end of the Wu dynasty, he served as a court scribe but foresaw the dynasty’s fall and feigned illness to avoid office. Once, passing through Lai Xiang, he visited the shrine of Lord Lao. The landscape stirred memories—all was as he had seen in death. He asked the shrine keeper, Ying Feng: “Over twenty years ago, was there a man who rode past the shrine of Lord Lao without dismounting, only to fall from his horse and die before reaching the bridge?” Feng confirmed the event, and all details matched Dai Yang’s account. Thus it became clear that he had been sent back to the mortal realm as a punishment.

The Daoist Liang Chen cultivated the Dao at Louguan, refining his spirit for years. In the second year of Yongxing of Emperor Huai of Jin, the yichou year, on the fifth day of the fifth month, Lord Lao commanded the Perfected One of Grand Harmony, Yin Gui, to descend to his chamber. There, he transmitted the methods of refining qi and concealing one’s form, along with the *Sun and Moon Yellow Splendor Upper Scripture*, *Water and Stone Returned Elixir*, *Six Jia Secret Talismans*, and other scriptures. Liang Chen thereafter gained the ability to fly and transform. Three years later, his elixir perfected, he ascended to heaven in broad daylight.

Later, Emperor Daowu of Northern Wei revered the words of *Laozi*, reciting them tirelessly. He frequently summoned princes and ministers to personally expound its teachings. During the Tianxing era, the Gentleman of Ceremonies, Dong Mi, presented dozens of *Scriptures on Ingesting Immortality Elixirs*, prompting the establishment of the post of Immortal Studies Academician.

In the first year of Tianxing of Emperor An of Jin, the renyin year—the fifth year of Hong Shi of Yao Qin—the immortal Cheng Gongxing traveled through Qin territory and encountered the Daoist Kou Qianzhi. He became Kou’s disciple and retreated to Mount Hua. Suddenly, the mountain spirit appeared, telling Gongxing: “Lord Lao is about to descend and inspect the sacred peaks. You should intensify your purification and devotion.” Gongxing, struck with awe, redoubled his incense offerings and gazed heavenward in anticipation.

On the twenty-third day of the eleventh month, the wuzi night, the Purple Yang and Wen Chang constellations within the Dipper’s bowl blazed open. Lord Lao descended upon a white qilin, his feet treading lotus blossoms. A pair of banners rose thirty feet high, attended by a throng of perfected immortals and jade maidens. As his chariot alighted, Lord Lao turned to Gongxing and said: “You now dwell upon the Central Marchmount; you shall attain immortal deliverance.” Gongxing bowed twice, and when he rose, Lord Lao had vanished.

The following year, the guimao year, in the second month, he relayed the appointed day to his master, Kou Qianzhi. Together, they passed through the pass and settled on the heights of Mount Song’s Shaoshi Peak.

Lord Lao descended again in the Gushui hamlet of Henan, halting his chariot in midair. He addressed Gongxing: “Last year, I appeared on the eastern cliffs of Mount Hua. Now you have come to reside in the Song-Shao region. Since arriving here, what are your thoughts?” Gongxing prostrated himself in reverence. Lord Lao said: “The Song and Shaoshi peaks, the Yellow and Luo rivers—this is the abode of immortals, the abyss of soaring dragons. You must not depart far.” He then commanded a jade maiden to fetch medicine and bestow it upon Gongxing. Gongxing bowed twice, knelt, and consumed it. Lord Lao said: “By ingesting my medicine, your five viscera shall be replenished, your divine mind illuminated, and nothing shall escape your perception. Formerly, I appointed Zhang Daoling as Celestial Master, succeeding Gan Ji. After Zhang Daoling ascended to heaven, demonic forces resurged, and the orthodox lineage of China was severed. Who but your master is fit for this task?”

As recorded in the *Book of Later Wei*:
According to the *Treatise on Buddhism and Daoism* in the *Book of Wei*, during the reign of Emperor Shizu, the Daoist Kou Qianzhi, styled Fuzhen, was the younger brother of Kou Zan, the Governor of Nanyang Commandery, and a thirteenth-generation descendant of Kou Gou. From youth, he harbored a passion for immortality and a heart detached from the mundane. In his early years, he practiced the arts of Zhang Lu, ingesting elixirs and medicinal herbs for years without effect. Yet his hidden sincerity reached the heavens.

There was an immortal named Cheng Gongxing, of unknown origin, who came to work as a hired laborer at the home of Kou Qianzhi’s maternal aunt. When Qianzhi visited his aunt, he saw Gongxing—robust in appearance, tireless in labor—and requested to take him on as a replacement servant. He brought Gongxing home and tasked him with clearing the southern thorn fields. Qianzhi sat beneath a tree, calculating, while Gongxing labored diligently, occasionally glancing at the calculations. Qianzhi said: “Just focus on your work. Why do you keep looking at this?” After two or three days, Gongxing returned to observe again, and this continued repeatedly. Later, Qianzhi encountered difficulties in calculating the seven luminaries and was lost in confusion. Gongxing asked: “Master, why are you troubled?” Qianzhi replied: “I have studied calculation for years, yet recently my *Zhou Bi* calculations fail to align. I am ashamed of this. It is not something you would understand, so why ask?” Gongxing said: “Master, try arranging them as I instruct.” In an instant, the calculations fell into place. Qianzhi marveled, unable to fathom Gongxing’s depth, and begged to become his disciple.

Gongxing firmly declined, insisting only on becoming Qianzhi’s disciple. Before long, he said to him: “Master, if you truly intend to study the Dao, could you follow Gongxing into reclusion?” Qianzhi joyfully agreed. Gongxing then instructed Qianzhi to purify himself through fasting for three days, after which they entered Mount Hua. There, Gongxing had Qianzhi reside in a stone chamber while he went out to gather herbs, returning to give them to Qianzhi. After consuming the herbs, Qianzhi no longer felt hunger. Later, Gongxing took Qianzhi to Mount Song, where three layered stone chambers stood, and had him dwell in the second. After some years, Gongxing said to Qianzhi: “After I depart, someone will bring you medicine—simply consume it without doubt or fear.” Soon after, someone arrived with medicine, but it consisted of venomous insects and foul-smelling things. Terrified, Qianzhi fled. When Gongxing returned and inquired, Qianzhi recounted what had happened. Gongxing sighed in regret: “Master, you are not yet ready for immortality—you may only become a teacher to emperors.” Gongxing served Qianzhi for seven years before suddenly declaring: “I cannot stay much longer. Tomorrow at noon, I shall depart.” After Gongxing’s passing, he instructed Qianzhi to bathe himself, saying that someone would come to receive him. Gongxing then entered the third stone chamber and died. Qianzhi personally performed the ablutions. The next day at noon, someone knocked at the stone chamber. When Qianzhi emerged, he saw two youths—one holding ritual robes, the other a bowl and staff. He led them to Gongxing’s body, whereupon Gongxing rose upright, donned the robes, took the bowl and staff, and departed.

Earlier, a man from Baocheng in Jingzhao named Wang Hu’er had lost his uncle, who possessed extraordinary spiritual powers. The uncle once took Hu’er to a separate ridge of Mount Song, where they gazed upon a vision of golden halls and jade chambers. Among them stood a particularly resplendent pavilion, empty and unoccupied, inscribed with the words: *Hall of Cheng Gongxing*. Perplexed, Hu’er asked about it, and his uncle replied: “This is the hall of the immortal Cheng Gongxing. Once, he lost seven rooms in a fire and was demoted to serve as Kou Qianzhi’s disciple for seven years.” Only then did they realize that Qianzhi’s sincerity had reached far, and that Gongxing was indeed an immortal who, having completed his sentence, had departed.

Gongxing served Qianzhi for seven years before ascending as an immortal, while Qianzhi remained on Mount Song, steadfast and unwavering in his devotion. In the tenth month of the second year of the *Shenrui* era of Emperor Mingyuan of the Northern Wei, on the day *yimao*, two divine beings suddenly appeared before Qianzhi. Clad in kingfisher-feathered robes and crowned with purple-gold diadems, they rode dragons and bore tablets, announcing: “The Most High Lord Lao has arrived.” Soon after, the sound of celestial music drew near. Looking up, Qianzhi saw a jade-hubbed chariot, its wheels forged of *vajra*, drawn by nine dragons, its majesty resplendent. Divine attendants filled the void, and the procession alighted upon the mountain’s peak. Five immortal officials stood in attendance before him. Qianzhi witnessed the sudden opening of five celestial gates, celestial music resounding as the Most High Lord Lao sat upon a silver-flowered throne. He commanded the immortal minister Wang Fangping to bring Qianzhi forward and stand before him, saying: “In the year *xinhai*, I received a memorial from Zhao Hongzheng, the *Zhenling* Governor of the Immortal Assembly of Mount Song’s Sacred Peak, stating: ‘Since the passing of Celestial Master Zhang Daoling, those on earth who cultivate goodness have lacked a teacher. As you embody upright principle and conduct in harmony with nature, your talents suit you to serve as a model. You are thus fit to assume the role of teacher.’ I have come to observe you and now appoint you to the office of Celestial Master, bestowing upon you the *Yunzhong Yinsong Xinke Zhi Jie* (New Precepts of the Cloud-Chanting Rites). I possess scriptures and precepts, hidden since the dawn of time, which now, by the turning of fate, must be revealed. You shall proclaim my new precepts, rectify my teachings, assist the state and sustain the mandate, transforming the multitudes. Abolish the corruptions of the Three Zhangs—the levies of grain and coin, and the arts of yin-yang union.”

The Most High Lord Lao lingered in the clouds for three days, bestowing upon Qianzhi nine scrolls of scriptures and precepts, with ritual propriety as their foundation, supplemented by the practices of *qi*-absorption and inner refinement. He also dispatched twelve jade maidens, including Changrong, to teach him the secrets of *qi*-absorption and guiding-and-pulling. Thereafter, Qianzhi’s *qi* flourished, his body grew light, and his complexion became radiant.

On the fifteenth day of the first month of the second year of the *Taichang* era, the year *dingsi*, the Most High Lord Lao descended again, bestowing seventy scrolls of new precepts and talismans, collectively titled *Bingjin* (Simultaneous Advancement). In the eighth year of the *Guihai* era, on the fifteenth day of the tenth month, he further dispatched his great-great-grandson, the Perfected Master of the Pastoral Palace, Li Puwen, to descend upon Mount Song and summon Qianzhi into the immortal palace. There, he conferred upon Qianzhi the four registers of *Tian’gong Taizhen Taibao Jiuzhou Zhenshi* (Celestial Palace’s Supreme Perfected and Treasured Master of the Nine Provinces), *Zhigui Shi* (Master of Governing Spirits), *Huamin Shi* (Master of Transforming the People), and *Jitian Shi* (Successor to the Celestial Master), along with immortal crowns, celestial robes, talismans, charts, and altar rituals. He also bestowed the *Taiping Sujing* (Fundamental Scripture of Great Peace) in one hundred scrolls, all inscribed in authentic celestial script with large characters, personally transcribed by the Perfected Master. He commanded Qianzhi to present the scriptures to the state and propagate their teachings in the year *jiazi*. Additionally, he granted six celestial fruits, the consumption of which allowed Qianzhi to abstain from cooked food.

As recorded in the *Wei Shu: Shilao Zhi* (History of the Wei: Treatise on Buddhism and Daoism), Li Puwen was a great-great-grandson of the Most High Lord Lao. Formerly residing in Sanggan of Daijun, he attained the Dao during the reign of Emperor Wu of Han and became the Master of the Pastoral Palace, governing the administration of thirty-six earthly spirits and humans, with a domain spanning over 180,000 *li*—a number extending through one cycle of the *He* (River Chart). Within this domain, there were 360 regions, each spanning ten thousand *li*. Mount Song’s jurisdiction encompassed the vast and level lands of Guanghan, which were entrusted to Qianzhi. He issued a proclamation: “I reside in the Celestial Palace, expounding the true Dharma. You, at the age of twenty-two, have spent ten years in youthful ignorance, leaving twelve years for teaching and transformation. Though your merits are not yet great, you have labored in instruction. Now, I promote you to the inner office of *Taizhen Taibao Jiuzhou Zhenshi* (Supreme Perfected and Treasured Master of the Nine Provinces), *Zhigui Shi* (Master of Governing Spirits), *Huamin Shi* (Master of Transforming the People), and *Jitian Shi* (Successor to the Celestial Master). Persevere without slackening, and in accordance with your efforts, I shall further promote you, bestowing the *Tianzhong Erzhen Taiwen Lu* (Register of the Supreme Script of the Two Perfected Ones of the Celestial Center), enabling you to summon and command the hundred spirits and transmit them to your disciples.”

The *Lu* (Registers) comprise five ranks: first, the *Yinyang Taiguan* (Grand Offices of Yin and Yang); second, the *Zhengfu Zhenguan* (Perfected Offices of the Rightful Courts); third, the *Zhengfang Zhenguan* (Perfected Offices of the Rightful Chambers); fourth, the *Suguan Sanguan* (Officials of the Lodged and Dispersed); and fifth, the *Bingjin Lusheng* (Concurrent Disciples of the Registers). The altar positions, rites of obeisance, vestments, and ceremonial forms each follow graded distinctions, totaling over sixty scrolls, collectively titled the *Lutu Zhenjing* (True Scripture of Registers and Charts). These I entrust to you for reverent observance, that you may assist the *Taiping Zhenjun* (Supreme Perfected Lord of Great Peace) of the Northern Realm.

The method of the *Tian’guan Jinglun* (Tranquil Wheel of the Celestial Offices) enables one to undertake and complete constructions, thereby transcending to the rank of True Immortal. Yet the people of the world now face the descent of the kalpa’s decline, making the propagation of the teachings exceedingly difficult. Simply instruct men and women to establish altars and perform morning and evening rites of obeisance. If a household has a stern patriarch, his merit extends to former generations; among them, those who cultivate the body, refine the elixir, and study the arts of longevity become the *Zhongmin* (Seed People) of the True Lords.

The methods for preparing medicines—including the formulas for refining the Golden Elixir and the Eighty-Three Nectars of *Yunying* (Cloud Radiance)—each possess essential secrets. Several chapters were inscribed by the hand of the Supreme Master Li Jun, while the remainder were written by the Perfected Scribe Zhao Daofu of the Rightful True Bureau. The ancient script is inscribed along the margins, interspersed with seal and clerical scripts, its phrases concise yet discerning, graceful and well-wrought. Its overarching principles align with the rites of the world, prioritizing those of virtue and trustworthiness, with the diligent following thereafter.

It further states that between the Two Modes (Heaven and Earth) lie the Thirty-Six Heavens, each governed by one of the Thirty-Six Offices. The highest of these is the *Wuji Zhizun* (Ultimate Venerable One), followed by the *Zhida Zhenzun* (Supreme Perfected Venerable One), then the *Tianfu Dizai Yinyang Zhenzun* (Perfected Venerable One of Heaven’s Cover and Earth’s Support, of Yin and Yang), and next the *Hongzheng Zhenzun* (Venerable One of Vast Rectitude), whose surname is Zhao and given name Daoyin. He attained the Dao during the Yin dynasty and is the Master of *Musu* (Pastoral Earth). Since the advent of *Musu*, the company of immortals—including *Chisong* (Red Pine), *Wang Qiao*, *Han Zhong*, *Zhang Anshi*, *Liu Gen*, and *Zhang Ling*—along with more recent transcendents, have all served as his attendants.

*Musu* commanded Qian to become his disciple, binding him in fraternal fellowship with the host of divine immortals. Matters of the netherworld, which the world does not comprehend, Qian explained to them one by one. In the first year of the *Shiguang* reign (jiazi year) of Emperor Taiwu, the Master, obeying the edict, presented these writings to the court. The four directions responded with unanimous reverence, though the imperial court had not yet fully embraced them. The Minister of Education, Cui Hao—a scholar of vast learning—marveled at the teachings and accordingly became the Master’s disciple. He then memorialized the throne, stating:

*“I have heard that when a sage receives the Mandate of Heaven, auspicious omens respond. Yet the* He Tu *and* Luo Shu *conveyed their messages through the patterns of beasts and insects—nothing compares to the present encounter between divine and mortal, where the Master’s hand has inscribed resplendent words of profound and wondrous import, unparalleled since antiquity. Though Emperor Gaozu of Han was a paragon of sagacity, the Four Hoary Ones still refused to bend their principles. Now, a transcendent of pure virtue has arrived unbidden—this is truly the celestial sign that Your Majesty walks in the footsteps of the Yellow Emperor and Fuxi, answering Heaven’s decree. How can such a divine mandate be dismissed as mere worldly discourse?”*

The Emperor joyfully approved and accepted the memorial, promptly ordering officials to journey to the mountain, present offerings, and welcome the remaining disciples dwelling there.

The Emperor, seeing that the Master partook of no earthly sustenance, accorded him even greater reverence and respect. Upon beholding the talismans and registers, his heart swelled with devotion and faith. Thereupon, he established the Terrace of the Mysterious Capital, erected the Palace of Serene Wheels in Heaven, and convened a grand assembly in accordance with ritual. The Master and his disciples all donned immortal robes and celestial crowns, their luminous presence resplendent. The Emperor wished to bestow upon the Master and his disciples high ranks and titles, but they firmly declined, accepting only the rites due to those beyond the mundane realm. By imperial decree, the Master and his disciples were ranked above princes and dukes, yet they refused, insisting on the proper forms of address. Thus, the new teachings were proclaimed and disseminated throughout the realm, greatly propagating the transformation of purity, stillness, and non-action.

Separately, one hundred and twenty disciples of virtuous conduct were selected from noble families to become Daoists. In the seventeenth year, the gengchen year, the Master established a terrace upon the lofty peaks to perform the Daoist rites and pray for blessings upon the Emperor. The Lord Lao commanded that the Emperor be granted the title "True Lord of Grand Peace," bestowing upon him a crown, robes, talismans, and registers. The Master returned and presented this in full report, whereupon the reign title was changed to "True Lord of Grand Peace," and a general amnesty was declared throughout the empire.

In the third year, the renwu year, Qianzhi memorialized: “Now that Your Majesty governs the age as the True Lord, having established the rites of the Palace of Serene Wheels in Heaven—an achievement unparalleled since antiquity—it is fitting that you ascend to receive the talismanic writ, thereby manifesting your sagely virtue.” The Emperor assented and personally approached the ritual terrace to receive the talismanic threads. His ceremonial procession was fully arrayed, with banners and standards all turned toward the color of blue-green, in accordance with Daoist tradition. Thereafter, each succeeding emperor, upon ascending the throne, received the registers in like manner.

*From the* History of the Later Wei.

Volume Seven of *The Sacred Records of the Primordial Origin* concludes.

Volume Eight of *The Sacred Records of the Primordial Origin*
Compiled by Xie Shoubin, Grand Master of Observing the Return of the Song Dynasty

In the third year of the Jiande reign of Emperor Wu of Zhou, on the bingzi day of the fifth month, the Buddhist teachings were abolished, and all scriptures and images were destroyed.

The Emperor further deliberated on abolishing the Daoist teachings and summoned the Daoist priest Yan Da to inquire: “Which is superior, Dao or Buddhism?”
Da replied: “The host is superior, the guest inferior.”
The Emperor asked: “How does one distinguish host from guest?”
Da said: “Buddhism originated in the Western Regions—is it not the guest? Dao resides in the Central Lands—is it not the host?”
The Emperor said: “If the guest returns west, does the host not see them off?”
Da replied: “If the guest returns, it benefits the lands of the Hu; if the host remains, it does no harm to the Middle Kingdom. Those who depart are not pursued, and those who stay preserve themselves—is this not acceptable?”
The Emperor praised his response, yet having already initiated the deliberation, he proceeded to abolish both, leaving only the temples of the Five Sacred Mountains.

On the bingwu day of the sixth month, as the Emperor was about to hold court, he suddenly saw a yellow-robed messenger descending from the sky, holding a jade tablet inscribed in cinnabar, who proclaimed: “A decree from the Most High.”
Startled, the Emperor unconsciously bowed.
The messenger presented the decree, saying: “To Yuwen Yong: The teachings of the Great Dao and its scriptures have endured since antiquity, preserving and regulating Heaven and Earth, delivering and saving living beings. What concern is it of yours that you rashly abolish them? As a ruler of the age, you have scorned the Mandate of Heaven and cut yourself off from it. It is not that Heaven has abandoned you—why do you persist in evil, hastening your own punishment?”
The Emperor read it again and again. The messenger said: “The Mandate of Heaven is not constant; delay is not permitted.” He then retrieved the decree and soared into the sky.
The Emperor remained shaken for days before restoring the Tongdao Abbey in the capital, appointing Yan Da to oversee it. He also summoned masters of the Way from the mountains to lecture on the *Daodejing*.

On the wuwu day, he issued an edict: “The Great Dao is vast and profound, formless yet boundless, embracing both emptiness and existence, its principles reaching into the deepest mysteries. Yet as paths diverge, their sources grow distant; purity and simplicity scatter, and forms and instruments fall into discord. Thus, the Three Mohists and Eight Confucians, the reds and purples, vie in competition; the Nine Schools and Seven Summaries propagate conflicting doctrines. The Way, concealed in minor achievements, has long been thus. Without a unifying return, disputes will never cease.
Now, let a Tongdao Abbey be established in the capital. The subtle words of sages, the canonical teachings of former worthies, the golden precepts and jade inscriptions, the secret traces and mysterious texts—all that can nourish the common people and uphold the teachings—should be broadly expounded and unified as one. Thus, those who grasp at clods of earth may recognize the towering majesty of Mount Song, and those who cling to gravel may awaken to the vast clarity of the Bohai Sea—is this not fitting?”
He then ordered the compilation of texts from the Nine Schools, selecting those aligned with the Dao, and incorporated them into the *Daoist Canon*.

In the seventh year of the Daye reign of the Sui, the xinwei year, Emperor Yang personally led an expedition against Liaodong. Qi Hui, a Daoist of the Louguan Abbey, said to his disciples: “The Way of Heaven is about to change; I shall yet witness it, though it will be within a few years.”
Someone asked: “What will the future hold?”
He replied: “A descendant of the Old Lord shall rule the world. Thereafter, our teachings will flourish greatly, though I fear this frail body may not endure long.”
Several years later, the Sui Dynasty indeed fell into chaos.

In the thirteenth year of the Daye reign, the dingchou year, the Old Lord descended upon Mount Zhongnan and spoke to the mountain dweller Li Chunfeng: “The Duke of Tang shall receive the Mandate of Heaven.”
Thereafter, Chunfeng pledged allegiance to the Tang. *From* The Golden Lock and Flowing Pearl.

When Emperor Gaozu of Tang first raised righteous troops in Jinyang, his daughter, Princess Pingyang—the wife of Chai Shao—also raised troops to respond, stationing them at the Yishou Palace. Hui, foreseeing the advent of the true lord, contributed all the abbey’s resources and provisions to her army.
When the Emperor reached the Putong Pass, Hui rejoiced, saying: “This is the true lord! He shall surely pacify the Four Quarters.” He then changed his name to Pingding in response and dispatched over eighty Daoists to the pass to welcome him.
The Emperor praised him and issued a decree: “Now that the righteous banner is raised in the east and the headquarters opened in the west, the establishment of offices and division of duties depend upon loyalty. The Daoist Qi Pingding, who has renounced the world to seek the truth, forsaken fame and departed from the俗, embracing tranquility and rejecting glory, untroubled by seclusion, has yet devoted his robes and resources to supply military attire, and apportioned his grain to sustain the army. His loyalty and integrity are commendable; he deserves recognition. Pingding shall be appointed Grand Master for Splendid Happiness with Silver and Gold, and those below him shall be granted ranks accordingly as Grand Masters for Splendid Happiness with Silver and Blue, in reward for his righteousness.”
Pingding firmly declined, saying: “This humble servant, in welcoming the sagely lord, reveres the root of the Dao. The yellow cap does not value gold and purple; the Mysterious Teachings esteem silver and blue. Though I receive this singular favor, I fear it is not fitting.”
The Emperor said: “Master, accept for now. Once we take the capital, further arrangements shall be made.”

At that time, the Sui general Song Laosheng was stationed at Huoyi to resist the righteous troops. As continuous rain had fallen for ten days, supplies could not be transported, and Emperor Gaozu ordered a retreat. The Prince of Qin vehemently remonstrated, and the retreat was halted.
An old man in white approached the military gate and said: “I am the spirit of Mount Huo. The Most High Old Lord has sent me to inform the Duke of Tang: The rain shall cease in the eighth month. Take the road southeast of Huoyi, and I shall aid your army.”
Emperor Gaozu said: “This spirit does not deceive. How could Zhao Yuanxu fail me?”
On the xinsi day of the eighth month, Emperor Gaozu led his troops to Huoyi, beheaded Laosheng, and pacified the region.

On the eighth day of the eleventh month, envoys were dispatched to the Louguan Abbey to perform a jiao ritual and pray for blessings.

That evening, white clouds like a canopy shaded the altar grounds, intertwining with the rising incense. A pair of white deer also approached the palace gates, let out several long calls, and then departed. The event was swiftly reported by courier. The next day, the capital was indeed captured, and the era name was changed to Yining.

In the first month of the first year of Wude in the Tang Dynasty, Liu Wu Zhou dispatched Song Jingang with an army of one hundred thousand to invade Jin and Jiang. Prince of Qin, Li Shimin, was stationed in Jiangzhou. A local of Jiangzhou, Ji Shanxing, a man of simple and upright character, had taken refuge in Datong Fort in Linfen County of Jin. The villagers sent Shanxing to Yangjiao Mountain to scout for bandits. As he reached the ridge to the northwest, between two persimmon trees, he saw a man riding a white horse approaching from the slope. Shanxing found this strange and turned to run back to the sweet pear tree below, but the man had already arrived. From behind, he called out to Shanxing: "Stop, do not be afraid." Turning his head, Shanxing saw an old man with white hair and beard, dressed in plain robes and a black cap, holding a red whisk, riding a white horse whose tail and hooves were all crimson. Two young attendants in green, their heads wrapped in red cloth, stood by his sides. The old man said to Shanxing: "Tell the Tang Emperor Li: Now that sagely governance is achieved, the state and altars shall endure. He should establish the Anhua Palace in the east of Chang’an and install Daoist images there, and the realm will know great peace." With these words, he soared into the sky and vanished.

On the night of the twenty-seventh day of the fourth month, Shanxing was at home when it seemed someone summoned him. The next morning, he found himself unknowingly back at the place where he had stood before. Again, he saw the old man, wearing a golden crown, dressed and mounted as before. The old man asked Shanxing: "Do you remember what I told you earlier?" Shanxing replied: "I remember it all." The old man said: "Go at once and report to the Emperor what I have said." Shanxing said: "I am poor and have no provisions for the journey, nor any official document. How can I reach him?" The old man said: "The way ahead will be provided; you need not worry." Shanxing said: "To see the Emperor is no small matter. How can I prove my words when I arrive in the capital?" The old man said: "When you reach the capital, there will be someone presenting a stone turtle. You may use that as proof." With these words, he disappeared.

Shanxing went to see He Ruo Xiaoyi, the Chief Administrator of the Jinzhou General’s Office, and told him what had occurred. It was the third day of the fifth month. Xiaoyi detained Shanxing, and on the eleventh day, he brought him before the Prince of Qin, who listened to the full account of the old man’s appearance and words. On the thirteenth day, the Prince of Qin dispatched Du Ang, Commander of the Left Imperial Guards, to accompany Shanxing to the site of the vision to perform a sacrifice. As they bowed and were about to rise, suddenly the old man appeared as before, amidst purple clouds. He raised his whip and pointed at Ang, asking: "Who are you?" Ang replied: "I am Du Ang, envoy of the Prince of Qin, come to offer sacrifices with respect." The old man said: "I neither drink nor eat; what need have I of sacrifices?" Then, pointing at Shanxing, he said: "All that was entrusted, this man knows." Ang returned and reported the divine man’s reappearance. He was then ordered to draft a memorial and escort Shanxing by courier to the capital.

On the nineteenth day of the fifth month, as they arrived at the gate of the imperial court, there was indeed Zhang Da, Attendant Gentleman of Zouzhou, presenting a stone resembling a turtle, inscribed with the words: "The realm is at peace, descendants flourish, for ten thousand years." Together, they entered to make their report. Emperor Gaozu was greatly pleased. On the sixth day of the seventh month, an edict was issued appointing Shanxing Gentleman for Court Audiences. The Court Attendant Liu Xian was ordered to establish a temple on Yangjiao Mountain. On the twenty-fifth day of the eighth month, Shanxing arrived at the temple site and again saw the old man between the two trees. A great canopy was spread, and he sat beneath a forest of ivory tusks, leaning on a jade table. A net of pearl flowers draped over him, and two young attendants in green came to serve. Behind him stood a yellow-robed warrior holding a horse. The old man asked: "Is the Emperor pleased?" Shanxing replied: "He is greatly pleased, but he wonders who the sagely one is." The old man said: "I am the Most High Divine Immortal, surnamed Li, named Boyang, known as the Old Lord. I am the imperial ancestor. In Guyang County of Bozhou, a withered juniper has revived—this may serve as verification. After the bandits are pacified this year, the realm will know great peace, and the dynasty shall endure." With these words, he suddenly vanished.

Shanxing again reported this to He Ruo Xiaoyi. Xiaoyi dispatched Xi Tong, the Registrar of Xiangyang County, to ride posthaste with Shanxing to present the memorial. The High Ancestor bestowed upon Shanxing an imperial robe and a bundle of silk. In the second year, in the fifth month, he ordered the Prefect of Louguan to renovate and rebuild the Hall of the Old Lord, the Hall of the Heavenly Worthy, and the Temple of Perfected Master Yin, ensuring all structures within the abbey were spacious and grand, befitting their revered stature. He also granted ten *qing* of land and the land of the Xianyou Supervisory Office to serve as the abbey’s estate. Furthermore, he established a supervisory office beside the abbey, appointing officials to oversee its construction, and designated the Prefect of Qiping as the abbey’s administrator.

In the spring of the third year, the Emperor personally visited the Old Lord at his shrine. Pingding led the Daoist assembly to welcome the imperial carriage and presented a feast for a thousand people. The Emperor summoned Pingding, the Dharma Master Lü Daoji, and the Fast Supervisor Zhao Daolong, granting them seats. He then ordered all officials to be seated and partake in the meal, saying: “Our distant ancestor has personally descended here. As the sovereign of the realm, how can We not undertake grand constructions?” Thereupon, he issued an edict renaming Louguan as Zongsheng Abbey and bestowed two hundred *shi* of white rice and one thousand bolts of silk for the abbey’s repairs.

Zongsheng Abbey was the former residence of Perfected Master Yin. According to the *Xuan Shi Zhi*: When Perfected Master Yin was about to ascend, he entrusted a stone casket to his disciples, instructing them: “This casket contains talismans and registers. Do not open it rashly.” During the *Dali* era, a man from Qinghe named Cui Jun served as the Prefect of Jianwei. Upon arriving at his post, he heard of Perfected Master Yin’s casket and laughed, saying to his officials: “The deception of Xin Yuanping is evident here.” He immediately went to the abbey and ordered the lock broken. The Daoist Yan Shenbai said: “The Perfected Master left instructions: ‘Whoever opens my casket will bring great misfortune upon themselves.’ I implore Your Lordship not to violate the Perfected Master’s covenant.” Cui Jun grew angry and said: “Perfected Master Yin has been dead for a thousand years—how could this stone casket alone remain?” He ordered the lock broken, but it would not budge. Cui then had leather straps tied to the casket’s handles and used several oxen to drag it, whipping them until the lock gave way and the casket opened. Inside were dozens of scrolls of talismans and registers, inscribed in cinnabar on yellow silk, radiant as if newly written. After examining them, Cui Jun said: “I initially thought the casket contained rare treasures, which is why I opened it. Now I see it holds only talismans and registers.” He then resealed and locked it before departing.

That very night, he died suddenly but revived after three days. His officials all came to pay their respects and inquire about his condition. Cui Jun said: “I was always clever and had never heard of divine immortals. After opening Perfected Master Yin’s stone casket, I was indeed seized by officials of the netherworld. At first, a purple-robed scribe told me: ‘I am a scribe of the netherworld, acting on orders to summon you. You cannot refuse—resistance will only bring greater misfortune. You must come quickly.’ Though I was alarmed and wished to decline, I found myself already departing with the messenger. After leaving the prefectural city and traveling fifty *li*, we arrived at the netherworld. The official there was the former Chancellor Lü, who said to me: ‘Why did you open Perfected Master Yin’s stone casket? Now, by the command of the Supreme Emperor, your official rank and lifespan are to be revoked. What say you to this?’ He then summoned the Record Keeper, who examined my register of rank and lifespan and reported: ‘He has held five offices and has seventeen years of life remaining.’ Now, by the Supreme Emperor’s decree, all five offices are revoked, and fifteen years of life are erased. Only two years remain.’ Thus, I was permitted to return. Two years later, I indeed died.”

In the third month of the fifth year, Ji Shanxing arrived at the temple before Yangjiao Mountain and saw the Old Lord seated in the hall, leaning on a jade table, with immortals arrayed in attendance, divine soldiers standing guard, and a procession of dragon and tiger riders, banners, and standards filling the sky. A herald summoned Shanxing forward and said: “The withered juniper at the temple in Bozhou has revived. Our grandson shall reign as king. I have already dispatched the Duke of Zhou, Dan, with divine troops to assist the state in suppressing Hei Kan. The victory is assured by the fourth month.” Xiaoyi dispatched Ma Jingqing, the Military Staff Officer, to ride posthaste and present the memorial. He then ordered Shanxing to travel by relay carriage to the army at Luozhou to deliver the proclamation. At the appointed time, Hei He was indeed pacified. (From the *Old唐书*.)

In the tenth month of the seventh year, the *jiashen* year, the Emperor visited Louguan to sacrifice to the Old Lord. Earlier, the High Ancestor had decreed that Wang Yuanzhi, a Daoist of Yuqing Abbey, be appointed Gentleman for Court Discussion and bestowed upon him a gold-threaded crown and a purple silk cloud-patterned robe, for Yuanzhi had once received the Old Lord’s decree foretelling the receipt of the Mandate. Yuanzhi was a disciple of Tao Hongjing.

When Emperor Yang of Sui was the Prince of Jin and governed Yangzhou, he established the Yuqing Xuantan to house Yuanzhi. Messengers arrived in succession, and Yuanzhi came to pay his respects. In an instant, his hair and beard turned white, frightening the prince, who sent him away. Soon after, his appearance returned to normal. When Emperor Taizong of Tang campaigned against Wang Shichong, he and Fang Xuanling visited Yuanzhi in disguise. Though they were unrecognized, Yuanzhi immediately greeted them, saying: “You shall become a sovereign of great peace. Take care of yourself.” After Taizong ascended the throne, he personally received Yuanzhi’s registers and methods, appointing him Grand Master for Splendid Happiness with Silver and Cyan Ribbon.

In the ninth year of *Zhenguan*, Yuanzhi firmly requested to return to the mountains. An edict was issued to establish Taiping Abbey on Maoshan and ordain twenty-seven Daoists. A jade-sealed decree was bestowed, saying: “When We were still a prince, We early gained the opportunity to seek the Dao from you. Your virtuous demeanor remains ever in Our thoughts, waking and dreaming. Recently, upon reading your memorial requesting to return to your old mountain, We have issued a separate edict to honor your lofty aspirations. The abbey We have ordered established shall serve as a testament to Our enduring devotion.”

After the Master returned to the mountains, he said to his disciple Pan Shizheng: “Recently, I saw the celestial register, which revealed that in a past life I had inadvertently harmed a young boy’s lips, preventing my ascent to heaven. Now, I have been summoned to serve as the Immortal Lord of Shaoshi. My departure is imminent.” The following day, he bathed, donned his robes and cap, burned incense, and passed away at the age of one hundred and twenty-six. An imperial edict posthumously bestowed upon him the title of Grand Master for Proper Consultation with Gold and Purple Ribbon, and he was honored as Master Yisheng of Ascended Perfection.

In the first year of *Zhenguan*, on the *bingwu* day of the seventh month, Emperor Taizong ordered the restoration of the Supreme Lord Lao’s Temple in Bozhou and the Temple of Master Yan in {Duizhou}, each granted two thousand households for sacrificial offerings. In the ninth year, on the *dingmao* day of the second month, the King of Khotan sent his son to pay tribute. When asked about his kingdom’s lands, the prince mentioned that to the west stood the Vimalakīrti Monastery, said to be where Laozi transformed the Hu barbarians. The Hu customs were thus softened, and Laozi ascended to heaven in broad daylight. The people, longing for him, built the monastery in his memory.

On the twenty-fifth day of the first month in the eleventh year, an edict was issued: “The Exemplar Lord bequeathed a model of purity and emptiness, while Śākyamuni left a principle of cause and effect. Though their teachings differ in approach, both guide seekers toward the same ultimate truth. Their doctrines, though distinct in method, converge in their profound benefit. The Great Dao’s practice originated in remote antiquity, arising from the nameless beginning, transcending form, governing the two modes of yin and yang, and nurturing the myriad things. Thus, it governs states and restores order, returning all to simplicity and sincerity. The rise of Buddhism, rooted in the Western Regions, spread to the Central Plains during the Eastern Han. Its principles of divine transformation are manifold, and its retributions varied. In recent times, devotion to it has deepened. People seek present blessings, while households fear future calamities. Thus, the vulgar mock the profound Dao, while those drawn to the strange vie to follow the true essence. What began as a ripple among the common folk has swelled into a tide sweeping the court. As a result, the teachings of foreign lands now overshadow the myriad wonders of our own, while the doctrines of the Central Plains lag behind the single vehicle. This drift has persisted for years, and We cannot but reflect deeply.

We, in reverent awe, have long pondered the Supreme Dao, seeking to rectify past errors and restore proper order. Moreover, Our imperial lineage descends from the Keeper of the Archives. The flourishing of Our dynasty relies on the power of supreme virtue, and the pacification of the realm depends on the virtue of non-action. It is fitting to make adjustments and propagate this profound transformation. Henceforth, in all matters of fasting, offerings, processions, and recitations, Daoist priests and nuns shall precede Buddhist monks and nuns. May the customs of our ancestral roots flourish throughout the nine provinces, and the reverence for Our origins endure for ten thousand generations.” The decree was proclaimed throughout the empire, and the responsible officials were ordered to implement it.

In the twenty-first year, an edict commanded the translation of Laozi’s *Dao and Virtue* into Sanskrit, to be bestowed upon the kingdoms of India. Earlier, when the Tang was destined to receive the Mandate, the Lord Lao had dispatched the immortal official Ma Zhou from the White Spirit Palace on Mount Hua to assist the Sacred Scion in founding the dynasty. Yet Zhou indulged in wine, drowning in worldly dust for twenty years, reduced to poverty and hardship. Hearing of Yuan Tiangang’s skill in physiognomy, he sought him out to divine his fortune. Tiangang gazed at him for a long while and said: “Your five spirits have fled, and your body lingers like a corpse. What fate could you possibly have?” Zhou was startled and asked for a method to avert disaster. Tiangang replied: “Go eastward at once. You will encounter an old man riding an ox. Do not approach or speak to him; simply follow him, and this calamity may be averted.”

Zhou followed his instructions. Before leaving the capital’s gates, he indeed saw an old man riding an ox exit the city. Zhou silently trailed behind, winding through village paths until they ascended a great mountain. At the summit, the old man turned, dismounted, and sat beneath a tree. He said: “The Supreme Lord commanded you to assist the Sacred Scion in founding the dynasty and saving the world. Why have you drowned in wine, bringing yourself to ruin and starvation? Your five spirits have scattered, and your vital qi has dissipated. You are on the verge of death—why have you not reflected and reformed?” Zhou, bewildered, did not understand. The old man said: “You were once an immortal official of the White Spirit Palace. Now, the Immortal King of Mount Hua has sent for you.” They entered the palace, arriving before a grand hall where the attendants stood in solemn array, as if before an emperor’s court. Approaching the curtain, a voice reprimanded him for his disrespect in failing his mission, casting aside his duties. He was ordered to return to his former post to reflect on his errors and submit a confession.

The old man and several officials escorted him to a separate courtyard in the eastern quarters. Upon seeing the gate, he found his name still inscribed there. Unlocking it, he entered to find the furnace, tripod, bed, and mat exactly as he had left them. After pondering for a long while, five figures in five-colored robes suddenly appeared before him, saying: “We are the spirits of your five viscera. You have wallowed in drunkenness, defiling your body. We have long since departed.” They instructed him to close his eyes, promising to return him to the divine chamber. Zhou complied, and in an instant, his mind cleared, and he recalled his past life—over twenty years had passed like a few days.

Returning to his former residence, he emerged into the Immortal King’s court and prostrated himself in apology. He received his mission anew and was sent to Chang’an. The next day, he visited Tiangang again. Tiangang exclaimed in surprise: “What encounter have you had? You are already transformed!” He added: “In sixty days, you will rise nine ranks; in a hundred days, you will attain the position of Chancellor. Take care of yourself.” During the *Zhenguan* era, military officials each submitted strategies for governing the state. Zhou, acting on behalf of the general Chang He, presented a memorial. Taizong was greatly pleased and appointed him *Shiyi* and *Jiancha Yushi* on the same day. Thereafter, he steadily rose to high office, serving as *Zhongshu Ling* for several years.

One day, a group of immortals descended into his chamber and said: “Your service to the state is complete. You may retire. The Supreme Unity has summoned you—do not linger.” The following day, he passed away without illness.

*(From the* Tang History.*)*

*The Extensive Records of the Taiping Era* contains an identical account.

In the second year of *Longshuo* during the reign of Emperor Gaozong (662 CE), the emperor visited the Luoyang Palace. Moved by sudden inspiration, he ordered an inquiry into any ancient sacred sites or numinous traces nearby. The elders reported: “North of the imperial city lies a mountain with an old shrine to Laozi. Whenever prayers are offered there, blessings manifest without delay.” The emperor immediately dispatched Xu Lishi, the prefectural governor of Luozhou, to rebuild the shrine as a pure temple. During excavation, a stone altar and an ancient stele were unearthed. The stele bore the inscription: *Stele of the Perfected One, Lord Bai*. It had been erected by the immortal Bai Zhongli, and was the site where the Celestial Master Zhang Daoling established the Northern Yu治.

The stone altar measured four *chi* in length, two *chi* in width, two *cun* in thickness, and eight *cun* in height. It had two legs at each end, and the surface was engraved with the characters *Tai Shang Lao Jun*. After the hall was completed, a grand Daoist ritual was held to celebrate and invoke blessings. Upon the conclusion of the *jiao* ceremony, a sudden white radiance enveloped the hall, illuminating the steps and altar. Laozi appeared within the light, flanked by two Perfected Ones, and lingered for a long while before vanishing. Thirteen witnesses—including Quan Dali, the Supervisor of the Palace Chambers, and Yang Hu, the Recorder of Luozhou—jointly submitted a memorial detailing what they had seen. An imperial edict ordered the event to be depicted as an auspicious portrait of Laozi. Officials presented congratulatory memorials, one of which read:

*“The numinous presence beneath the pillar scatters its light across the altar and hall; the immortal attendants at the rafters pour forth wondrous splendor upon the steps and courtyard. Truly, this response to utmost sincerity befits the sagely virtue of the emperor—how could it be compared to the glory of Yu’s reign, the auspicious airs of Han, the descent of sacred birds at sacrifices, or the flourishing of mulberry and catalpa in the royal domain?”*

Later, during the reign of Emperor Minghuang, Wu Daozi was commissioned to paint the portraits of successive sagely emperors within the hall and to adorn the walls of the corridor with murals. Du Fu’s poem *Visiting the Temple of the Mysterious Origin Emperor North of Luoyang* reads:

*“Mountains and rivers uphold the embroidered doors,
Sun and moon draw near the carved beams.
The immortal plum’s roots run deep,
The catalpa and orchid’s leaves gleam with luster.
The ancestral house preserves old histories,
The Dao and its virtue are entrusted to the present king.
The painter’s hand studies the masters of old—
Wu’s artistry stands unrivaled.
The forest of spirits shifts the earth’s axis,
Its sublime wonder stirs the palace walls.
Five sages arrayed in dragon robes,
A thousand officials in ranks of mounted riders.
Their crowns and tassels all flourish,
Their pendants and ornaments all soar.
Though he withdrew in humility beneath the Zhou,
His classics and teachings embraced the Han emperors.
If the Valley Spirit does not die,
Where else could one nurture simplicity?”*

This refers to the same temple.

Ouyang Xiu’s *Collected Antiquities* includes a postscript to *Visiting the Temple of the Mysterious Origin Emperor*, which states: “Emperor Xuanzong of Tang composed the stele inscription and wrote it himself. I have seen his calligraphy for *The Magpie and the Swan’s Neck* in the world, and the script is identical to this. The stele stands on a mountain north of Deng in Luoyang—locals call it the Temple of Laozi.” The postscript notes that Emperor Minghuang must have composed an imperial poem for the occasion, but it has not yet been found, so it is not recorded here.

On the twenty-eighth day of the second month of the first year of *Qianfeng* (666 CE), a *jiwei* day, the emperor returned to the capital after completing the Feng and Shan sacrifices at Mount Tai. He then traveled to Bozhou to pay homage at the temple of Laozi and posthumously bestowed the honorific title *Tai Shang Xuan Yuan Huang Di* upon him. The emperor’s own composition read:

*“The Great Dao, formless yet complete, predates the Two Modes and stands supreme. The Perfected One, emptying himself, speaks with wondrous subtlety of the myriad things. Consider Laozi: he is the root of Our lineage. From the time of Fuxi to the end of the Zhou, his numinous responses knew no form, his transformations took myriad paths. He ascended upon primordial qi, descended moved by the essence of the sun. At times, he roamed the cosmos, exhaling and inhaling wind and clouds; at others, he served as teacher and friend to emperors, his alchemical arts wondrously transforming. Like yin and yang, he is unfathomable; with the sun and moon, he endures. Yet when the celestial stems fell into decline and darkness neared the pillar beneath the hall, he greatly propagated his refined teachings, leaving a model for posterity. Though his heart equaled the Great Void, his principles returned to the True Sovereign.*

*As for transcending sageliness and abandoning wisdom, calming the spirit and diminishing desires, dwelling in the depths of stillness and obscurity, beyond the reach of sight and hearing—serene in non-action, at ease in self-possession—his was a draught that never ran dry, a use that never depleted. Grasping the Great Image to restore purity, cleansing the dark mirror to cast off burdens, he spans the cosmos in perpetuity, striding the potter’s wheel of creation to nurture all things. Perfect indeed! Truly, he defies all naming.*

*Moreover, the resources of the Great Sage ensured the flourishing of the imperial line; the footsteps of his supreme virtue marked an era of felicity. We, inheriting the numinous mandate, govern the multitudes. We unite the brightness of the Three Luminaries in reverent awe day and night, bear the weight of the Four Great Elements with solemn vigilance in waking and sleeping. We exhaust filial piety toward the ancestral shrines, extend harmonious grace to the seen and unseen, enact the transformation of purity and stillness, and inherit the enterprise of Great Peace. We ascend the sacred peak to perform the rites, sit in the Bright Hall to receive the records. Auspicious smoke gathers in celebration, repeated blessings descend. Cranes respond to the Nine Songs, mountains resound with ‘Long live the emperor!’ Surpassing all antiquity in felicitous omens, We stand foremost among the emperors of old.*

*After the grand rites concluded, We returned to the capital. Our carriage passed through Lai Xiang, and We personally offered libations of peppered wine. Gazing upon the auspicious wutong tree, We lingered in reverence; embracing the sacred spring, We sang in perpetuity. Our thoughts of ‘as if present’ run deep, and Our devotion to honoring the beginning grows ever more earnest. It is fitting to illuminate the profound mystery of the primordial origin and to manifest the merit of the Dark Sage. Let the posthumous title* Xuan Yuan Huang Di *be conferred, and let the Sage Mother be honored as* Xian Tian Tai Hou*. The temple halls shall all be repaired and rebuilt. A magistrate and vice-magistrate shall be appointed to oversee offerings and sacrifices.”*

Still, We decree that Guyang County be renamed Zhenyuan County.

In the fourth month, fourth day of the *yimao* year of the Yifeng era (7 April 678), an edict was issued dispatching the Daoist Zheng Yuanyin to the Pure Temple on Mount Yu in northern Luoyang, where he, together with Luo Wuxian and twenty-four others, established a fast and performed the Daoist rites. On the first day of the fifth month (3 June), at the hour of *chou*, as they reached the eastern gate of the hall, they beheld Lord Lao mounted upon a white horse with crimson mane, accompanied by two blue-robed youths, descending upon the altar. Auspicious light suffused the scene, and turning to the Daoist Kou Yidai, He spoke at length before vanishing.

The Supervisor of the Northern Palace Gardens, Gentleman for Court Audience Sun Rui, verified the hoofprints left upon the altar and promptly memorialized the throne. The hundred officials presented a memorial in celebration, declaring:

*“The August First Sovereign, the Dark Sage, manifested Himself at the temple precincts—His golden countenance and jade radiance outshone the brilliance of nocturnal luminaries; His white steed and vermilion mane left traces upon the celestial thoroughfare. The azure-clad youths, guiding at dawn, descended in accord with the jade chimes; purple clouds spread across the firmament, veiling the Silver River as He ascended on high. Thus is His legacy flourishing, His sacred mandate renewed, Our imperial sway expanded, and the light of His grace extended to all under Heaven. For this reason, the Dao surpasses the hundred kings, felicity abounds through ten thousand generations, the precious throne is eternally secured, and boundless prosperity is assured.”*

The Emperor was greatly pleased and decreed that henceforth Daoist priests should be placed under the jurisdiction of the Court of the Imperial Clan, ranking immediately after the imperial princes. Upon reviewing the memorial submitted by Court Gentleman for Ceremonial Spread Sun Rui.

In the third year of the Yifeng era, the fifth month, an edict was issued declaring that from that time forward, the *Dao De Jing* would be designated as the Supreme Classic, and all candidates for the imperial examinations must demonstrate proficiency in its teachings.

In the first year of the Wenming era of Empress Zetian, the jiashen year, Emperor Zhongzong was deposed and enfeoffed as the Prince of Luling, as the Empress sought to elevate the Wu clan. On the eighteenth day of the second month, a man from Yuzhang named Hao Yuanchong, bearing an imperial summons, arrived at the capital. When he reached Huangtianyuan in Longtaixiang, Que County, Haozhou, six immortals suddenly appeared, each riding a crimson dragon and clad in robes of varied hues. Two bore pearl-emblazoned banners, while four carried rainbow streamers and brocade parasols. Emerging from the multicolored clouds in the northwest, they leaned down and said to Yuanchong: “The Most High Lord Lao is coming.” With that, they passed by. Moments later, an extraordinary fragrance filled the air, and three azure-clad youths bearing incense burners glided past amidst the clouds. Then, two more azure-clad youths, each holding a red whisk and ruyi scepter, smiled as they passed.

Next, four immortals appeared, each riding a green dragon and bearing a tally of authority. They addressed Yuanchong: “The Most High Lord Lao is coming and wishes to speak with you.” No sooner had they spoken than they, too, passed by. Soon after, amidst the five-colored clouds, a Daoist appeared, clad in pale yellow robes, his hair and temples white. Upon his head rested a floral crown of golden hue, and he rode a white beast resembling a mule, its mane and tail aflame. Leaning down, he said to Yuanchong: “I am the Most High Lord Lao, the primal ancestor of your Emperor.” Yuanchong immediately prostrated himself, and the Lord Lao said: “Follow me, and I shall speak with you.” Before he knew it, Yuanchong found himself suspended in the clouds, several *zhang* above the earth.

The Lord Lao instructed him to convey this message to the Empress: “The mandate of my dynasty is enduring and far-reaching; you must not rashly enthrone a surname other than our own.” Behind him stood six immortals, each riding a dragon and girded with swords, their floral crowns resplendent and their robes of variegated hues gleaming brilliantly. They bore objects in hand, none of which Yuanchong could recognize, and stood in attendance to his left and right. Following them was a cloud chariot adorned with multicolored ornaments, upon which rested a great jeweled canopy with floral tassels cascading down, encircled by five-colored clouds. Countless attendants, some riding dragons and bearing tallies or banners, accompanied the procession, their forms towering and majestic. Music heralded their passage, and as they traveled, over five hundred officials, commoners, and residents of Que County witnessed the spectacle, all bowing in reverence from afar. In an instant, Yuanchong found himself back upon the earth.

Upon reaching Chang’an, he fully reported the encounter. The Empress was displeased and subsequently had him imprisoned.

Soon after, the temporary palace at Que County was established as Fengxian Abbey. Later, however, the Empress remained ever fearful of these words and dared not enthrone Wu Sansi. Ultimately, she restored the throne to Emperor Zhongzong, whereupon the reign era was changed to *Shenlong*. In the second year, it was again altered to *Jinglong*, and an edict was issued commanding every prefecture in the realm to establish a Jinglong Abbey, which was subsequently renamed *Zhongxing Abbey* and, in the third year, *Longxing Abbey*.

During the *Kaiyuan* era of Emperor Minghuang, an edict was issued stating: “In the *Wenming* years, Hao Yuanchong of Hongzhou transmitted the true instructions of the August Emperor of the Mysterious Origin to the Empress, declaring, ‘Our dynasty’s mandate shall endure without end, spanning ten thousand generations of rulers.’ For this, he was imprisoned and met his ruin. Had he not been a man of extraordinary loyalty and righteousness, who would not have shunned the penalty of death to defy the command of the divine spirits? He is worthy of posthumous honors to console his soul in the netherworld.” His son, Qiong, was also granted an official post commensurate with his rank.

The edict further proclaimed: “The late Hao Yuanchong of Hongzhou, in former times, responded to a propitious summons and arrived at Haolüe. There, he suddenly beheld the August Emperor of the Mysterious Origin, who lifted him into the clouded heavens and spoke with him, charging him to admonish the Empress and foretell the dynasty’s resurgence and the boundless span of its sacred destiny. He, disregarding his own life, came to transmit this decree, only to suffer imprisonment—truly a cause for lament. Had he not been one who exhausted his devotion to the court, who would have dared to confront authority in such a manner? He is fittingly granted posthumous honors to console his departed spirit.” He was posthumously appointed Prefect of Dizhou.

In the first year of the *Wansui Tongtian* era (the *Bingshen* year), the monk Huicheng of Fuxian Monastery in the Eastern Capital submitted a memorial requesting the destruction of the *Scripture of Laozi’s Conversion of the Barbarians*. An edict was issued to the Vice Minister of the Ministry of Justice, who, together with the Director of the Hongwen Academy, deliberated on the matter. The Grand Master for Splendid Happiness, Acting Vice Minister of the Ministry of Justice, and *Shangzhuguo* Liu Ruxuan presented his argument:

“The origins of Li and Śākya are one, never having been distinct. The Dharma-body and the Dao-essence manifest without limitation, descending in traces and bestowing numinous power, each enacting its own transformation. Moreover, Laozi emerged from the East and journeyed to the Western Regions. Though his ultimate fate remains unknown, his deeds are recorded in earlier histories. According to the *Book of the Later Han*, ‘Laozi entered the lands of the Yi and Di and transformed them into the teachings of the Buddha.’ The *Biographies of Eminent Recluses* states, ‘Laozi converted the customs of the Rong into those of the Buddha.’ The *Veritable Records of Our August Dynasty* recount: ‘Five hundred *li* west of Khotan lies the Vimalakīrti Monastery, built where Laozi converted the barbarians.’ There, Laozi ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Taking leave of the assembled barbarians, he declared, ‘Formerly, I wandered in the heavens, reviewing the registers of humans and spirits. Soon, I shall descend again.’ Thus, this monastery was established.
