Thus did the First Emperor of Qin dispatch alchemists to sea in search of masters, only to be deluded and gain nothing. Emperor Wen consumed a foreign monk’s elixir and succumbed to a sudden, incurable illness. As the ancient poem says: ‘Those who take elixirs seeking immortality / Are often undone by the drugs.’ How true these words are! For rulers, the sole duty is to govern with principle. When the Four Seas rejoice in their leadership and the altars of soil and grain endure, longevity comes naturally." The Emperor deeply assented.

On the sixteenth day of the tenth month in the tenth year of the Zhenyuan era (794 CE), the female Daoist Xie Ziran of Golden Spring Mountain in Nanchong County, Guozhou, having perfected her Daoist practice, was summoned by Lord Lao and ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Three months later, she descended from heaven once more and said to Prefect Li Jian: "In heaven stands the loftiest Jade Hall, where Lord Lao resides. Its walls are of white jade, inscribed above with the names of divine immortals in gold. At times, vermilion script appears beneath, noting those destined to descend as emperors or chief ministers. Whenever divine immortals enter to pay homage to Lord Lao, all bow four times. Fearing that the world would not believe in the existence of divine immortals, I have come briefly to tell you this." Having spoken, she ascended to heaven. Li Jian reported the matter in a memorial, and an imperial edict was issued in praise.

According to the *Taiping Guangji*, Xie Ziran was of exceptional intelligence from a young age and abstained from meat and blood. Her mother sent her to study under the nun Yue Hui for a year, but she fell ill and sought to return home. Later, she was sent again to study under the nun Ri Xiao for several months, but once more asked to go back. Her family lived at the foot of Da Fang Mountain, whose summit bore a stone statue of Lord Lao. Gazing upon it, Ziran turned to her mother and said, "This is where I wish to dwell." Her mother consented, and they moved to the mountaintop, where she constantly recited the *Daode Jing* and the *Huangting Neipian*.

After some time, at the age of fourteen, she ate newly harvested rice and saw it was infested with maggots. From that moment, she ceased taking grain. Her father, Xie Huan, had been away on official duties for many years. Upon returning and seeing that Ziran no longer ate, he grew furious: "Our family has long followed the Confucian way—how could such demonic delusion take hold?" He locked her in a room for over forty days, yet she only grew more radiant and refined. Huan was then struck with astonishment.

Later, the Lady of Mount Tai and various immortals frequently descended to her home, instructing her to bathe in fragrant water but forbidding the use of *rutou* incense. They also said: "Heaven has its true immortals, not the likes of ghosts or spirits. In the celestial realms, none shave their heads; after attaining the Dao, all don immortal robes and cap shoes." When Ziran ascended to heaven, she wrote on the eastern wall of the hall: "I leave this message for my kin: do not grieve. Diligently cultivate merit, establish fields of blessings, purify your fasts, and recite the Dao. After a hundred kalpas, may you have good cause to meet again in the Pure Source homeland." Her writing remained there.

Li Jian’s *Stele of the Jinquan Daoist Sanctuary* and its accompanying biography fully record these events. Han Yu, the Duke of Changli, wrote a poem on the matter:

In Nanchong County, Guozhou,
A girl named Xie Ziran, chilled by fate,
Knew nothing of the world’s ways,
Only heard of divine immortals.
Light of life, she followed their path,
Dwelling on Jinquan Mountain.
She spurned all worldly glory,
Abandoning her parents’ tender love.
One day, seated in an empty chamber,
Mists arose around her.
As if hearing the strains of sheng and pipes,
From the boundless heavens they came.
Daylight turned to twilight’s gloom,
A chill wind swept the scene.
Eaves and pillars flickered in light,
Five-colored radiance linked in chains.
Onlookers stood in awe,
Hesitant to draw near.
In an instant, she rose lightly,
Drifting like smoke in the wind.
Vast the eight directions stretch,
No trace to follow.
The village elder reported the event,
The prefect, startled, came to see.
He drove his carriage with officials,
Outpacing the curious crowd.
Entering the house, they found nothing—
Only robes and shoes, shed like a cicada’s shell.
All declared it the work of immortals,
A tale truly worthy of transmission.
This is the story it tells.

In the first year of Baoli of Emperor Jingzong, *yisi* (7th month, 7th year of the sexagenary cycle), the Left Vice Director of the Imperial Secretariat and Grand Councilor Li Fengji was appointed as Acting Grand Commandant to preside over the autumn offering to the Great Sage Ancestor at the Taiqing Palace. In the second year of Baoli, *bingwu* (1st month, 14th year of the cycle), the Emperor conducted rites at the Southern Suburban Altar and paid homage at the Taiqing Palace. As the imperial procession approached, the Lord Lao manifested as an elderly man in white robes, pointing to Zheng Wen, the Chief Clerk of Chang’an County, and saying: "There is a common well along this path—fill it at once." Soon after, the old man vanished. When the imperial carriage arrived, the matter was reported, and the assembled officials offered their congratulations. An edict was issued to the Vice Minister of War, Wei Chumou, to compose an inscription, and to the Gentleman for Court Audiences, Liu Gongquan, to inscribe it. The stele was erected beside the filled well, and the account was recorded by the official historians.

As the stele was being raised, a sudden gust of wind arose, swirling around the platform without cease. Gazing upward, the crowd beheld the Lord Lao clad in a purple robe and golden crown, his feet shod in golden slippers, standing upon a white lotus. In his right hand he held a Fivefold Radiance Fan, while his left hung down, and a golden light shone in the air around him. Liu Gongquan and the stonecutters gazed upon him for a long while, then traced his likeness upon the ground. As the drawing neared completion, the Lord Lao suddenly pointed his fan toward the sky, scattering streams of light in all directions before ascending into the void. All eyes followed him until, in an instant, he grew distant and vanished into the clouds. The event was reported to the throne, and an edict ordered the account to be inscribed upon the stele. The stele, inscribed by Liu Gongquan and composed by Wei Chumou, remains extant.

In the eighth month of the seventh year of Taihe, *guichou* (40th year of the cycle), Emperor Wenzong issued a decree: "The Sage establishes the ultimate, and the foundation of teaching lies in honoring the ancestors. The king aligns with Heaven, and in duty he reveres his forebears. Our Great Sage Ancestor, the Mysterious Origin Emperor, inaugurated the treasure of fortune and bestowed his blessings upon the Tang, bringing the Six Directions to great harmony and guiding all beings to the realm of longevity. In preserving this grand enterprise, we rely upon the legacy of his wisdom. We have heard that the Taiqing Palace in Bozhou has repeatedly suffered from floods, its structures appearing dilapidated. As this is the sacred birthplace of the Sage, how could we neglect our reverence? Let Li Cheng, Military Commissioner of the Xuanwu Army, concurrently serve as Commissioner of the Bozhou Taiqing Palace, and charge him with its gradual restoration, that due homage may be paid in season, fulfilling Our intent."

In the second year of Kaicheng, *dingsi* (54th year of the cycle, 5th month), the Drafter of Imperial Edicts Gao Yuanyu, serving as Prefect of Bazhou, suddenly noticed an unusual radiance upon a cliff face north of the city, nine *li* from the banks of the Jialing River. Drawing nearer, he saw upon the stone a naturally formed image of the Lord Lao, his brows, hair, headscarf, slippers, and robes all perfectly rendered. Beside him stood a figure in loose robes and wide sleeves, holding a censer to offer incense. Behind them, a young attendant with twin topknots stood in solemn attendance, as if awaiting commands.

All these were beyond what human craftsmanship—whether painting, drawing, or carving—could achieve. Whenever Gao Yuanyu made prayers, purple-black vapors would rise from the stone, and a sacred spring would well forth of its own accord. When local officials and commoners sought blessings, none failed to receive immediate efficacy. Thereupon, he had the site carved into stone and a hall erected to mark this auspicious sign. He also commissioned an illustrated report to be submitted to the throne, and by imperial decree, the event was recorded in the official histories.

In the first month of the *gengshen* year (57th year of the cycle), the fifth year of Kaicheng, Emperor Wuzong ascended the throne. In the second month, he issued an edict designating the birthday of the August Emperor of the Mysterious Origin as the "Descended Sage Festival," granting a three-day holiday.

On the first day of the seventh month in the fourth year of Dazhong under Emperor Xuanzong, the Lord Lao dispatched the Perfected One Han Zhong to bestow upon the Daoist Hou Daohua of the Daojing Cloister in Yongle County, Hezhong, the surname Li and the name Neizhi, commanding him to reside in the Shan Jin Cloister of the Supreme Clarity Palace.

According to the *Shi Zhi*: During the reign of Emperor Wenzong of Tang, the Daoist Deng Taixuan completed his elixir and left a sealed container hidden within the cloister. Later, Zhou Wuxian took charge of the cloister, where there was a certain Hou Daohua who served as a menial—sweeping, cleaning, and performing every lowly task—treated by the other Daoists as little more than a slave. Yet he was always fond of histories and philosophical works, never setting down his books. When others mocked him, he would reply: "There are no dull-witted immortals in heaven."

One day, he trimmed the branches of the ancient pine before the gate, saying, "These will hinder my ascension one day." The others were all enraged. This occurred on the twentieth day of the fifth month in the fifth year of Dazhong.

The next morning, Daohua left a poem for Zhou Wuxian, which read:
*"Within the wrapper, the Great Return Elixir lay,
For years its hue unchanged—yet last night stolen away.
Today, with hexagrams empty, I take flight and rise.
Ashamed, I bid you treasure well, treasure well the Heavenly Master Deng.
When in years to come you refine the medicine true,
Leave some for Neizhi—let it not be lost from view.
If my master knows this decree,
Hasten the alchemy, delay not thee.
The Three Clarities await me alone,
The Great Canopy’s destined throne."*

Below, in smaller script, he added:
*"On the first day of the seventh month last year, I received the honor of Perfected Han’s descent, who bestowed upon me the surname Li and the name Neizhi, assigning me to reside in the Shan Jin Cloister of the Supreme Clarity Palace. Now, beneath the ancient pine, I cast off my sandals, ascend beyond its boughs, remove my robes and hang them upon the pine, then ascend in flight."*

Governor Zheng Guang investigated and verified the account, reporting the matter to the throne. An imperial edict granted five hundred bolts of silk and a set of imperial robes, ordering the renovation of the halls and corridors, and bestowing the name "Ascension to Immortality Cloister."

In the second year of Dazhong, the year *wuyin*, the Tiantai Mountain monk Chen Huixu had long resided at Guoqing Monastery. One day, while wandering the mountains with fellow monks, they wished to cross the stone bridge, but the rushing waters and slippery moss made the others tremble and hesitate. Huixu alone leapt across with ease, scaling the stone cliff, and did not return by evening.

Huixu, having reached the far side of the cliff, saw a faint path that gradually widened into a level expanse. Before him stood palaces and pavilions, ten *li* in length, adorned with myriad flowers. Three gates stood facing one another like a tripod; the central gate bore the inscription *Hall of Meeting Perfected Ones*, the left *Palace of the Golden Court*, and the right *Palace of the Paulownia Terrace*. All were adorned with golden eaves and jade windows, towering over a hundred *zhang* in height.

Entering the western side of the right gate, he saw another grand tower with a wide entrance, inscribed *Palace of the Right Assistant*. It comprised thousands of interconnected chambers, their jade steps and marble terraces winding in graceful curves, with flowing channels of water coursing through. Everywhere was splendid adornment, yet no trace of human presence.

Proceeding into another courtyard, he saw five or six youths in black robes glancing at one another, laughing as they departed. When he repeatedly questioned them, they replied, "Ask Elder Zhang." Soon after, an old man appeared, leaning on a staff and holding flowers. Startled, he said, "How did a mortal like you come here?"

Huixu replied, "I have often heard that beyond the stone bridge lies the Arhat Temple, and the sound of its bells reaches the mortal world. Thus, I came seeking it. By what fortune have I arrived at this realm? I do not know where the arhats reside."

Elder Zhang said, "This is the blessed court of the true immortals, the lower palace beneath the Heavenly Emperor, called the Golden Court. It is the land of immortality, the numinous realm where the true are nurtured, spanning one hundred and sixty *li* in circumference. The Perfected King of the Right Assistant and the Paulownia Terrace governs it, with three thousand immortals in ranks, three hundred perfected ones, and ten thousand divine kings, warriors, heavenly emperors, and jade maidens—forming a lesser celestial assembly. The Most High Lord Lao descends here thrice a year to assess the merits and ranks of those who cultivate the Dao throughout the world. This is the capital of the immortals, not the abode of arhats."

Huixu asked, "Who is the Perfected King?"

Elder Zhang replied, "He is the crown prince of King Ling of Zhou, disciple of Fu Qiu Gong, and holds the rank of Perfected One."

Huixu asked, "Can one learn to become an immortal?"

Elder Zhang said, "Through accumulating merit and virtue, one may ascend to heaven in the flesh. It depends on steadfast resolve and enduring effort. That you have glimpsed this blessed court suggests you may yet have hope of learning the Way."

Huixu asked again, "By what path does one enter the immortal’s Dao?"

Elder Zhang replied, "Internally, preserve the spirit and refine the *qi*; externally, ingest the elixirs of cinnabar. All who become immortals do so through the power of divine elixirs. You cannot stay long—the Perfected One has just journeyed to the Eastern Sea, and if his mounted retinue returns, you may face unseen consequences."

He then led Huixu out. After ten paces, Huixu found himself back at Guoqing Monastery.

From that time onward, Huixu grew ever more devoted to the Dao, developing a fondness for alchemical minerals. In his later years, he took residence at Sun-Embracing Monastery on Mount Zhongnan, where, though his body gradually weakened with age, his heart burned all the more fervently. After lying ill for over a month, utterly emaciated and frail, an old man suddenly arrived at the temple bearing a medicine satchel, crying aloud, "I sell the Great Return Elixir!" The monks all laughed at him, then pointed to Huixu’s door and jestingly said to the old man, "This monk is rather fond of the Return Elixir—you might well sell it to him." The old man gladly approached, leaving behind several pills. Huixu promptly consumed them. The old man departed at once, and the monks gathered to ask what had happened. Huixu replied that he had bought and swallowed the Return Elixir, then gestured toward them from afar, saying, "Do not come near—I sense a foul odor about you all." Soon after, he changed into fresh robes, then suddenly flew up to the eaves of the hall. Waving farewell, he rose gently into the air and departed.

In the second month of the gengchen year of the Xiantong era of Emperor Yizong (860 CE), the rebel Chou Fu, leading a band of outlaws, raided and plundered Yuezhou, rampaging through its counties. Officials fled in terror as he seized the city of Kuaiji. An imperial edict ordered the assembly of troops from eight circuits to suppress the rebellion, and the populace scattered in panic. There was a man named Shen Ying whose family revered the image of Lord Lao with utmost sincerity. When Ying heard that the rebels were approaching, he hastily locked his door and fled, accidentally leaving a young servant boy inside. Upon returning to unlock the door, he found the area overrun with enemy encampments. Later, as the tide of battle shifted unpredictably, the marketplace was set ablaze. After nearly a year and more, the rebellion was finally quelled. Yet when Ying returned to his home, he found it entirely unscathed. Upon unlocking the door, he discovered the boy unharmed. Asked what had happened, the boy replied that after the door was locked, he had felt no fear at all. A youth in green robes, about thirteen or fourteen years old, had appeared and said, "Lord Lao has sent me to play with you." He then led the boy to a grand residence, where he was given food and fruit. After eating for what seemed like half a day, he heard Lord Lao command him to tend the incense. No sooner had he lit the incense at the stove than he heard the sound of the door. When Ying entered, the incense was still burning.

In the ninth month of the jichou year (869 CE), the rebel Pang Xun of Xuzhou advanced on Bozhou with over three thousand followers, intending to seize the Great Clarity Palace as a stronghold. On that day, over a thousand refugees—scholars and commoners—had taken shelter within the palace. Suddenly, they saw Lord Lao ascend into the sky from the palace grounds, moving southward. Moments later, a black vapor rose from the Nine Wells, and soon the entire valley was shrouded in darkness. The rebels lost their way and trampled one another in confusion, and Pang Xun drowned in the waters. When the border patrol arrived, the sky cleared, and not a single rebel remained. Li Wei, the military governor of Bianzhou, reported the event to the throne. An emissary was dispatched to the palace to offer thanks, and an imperial edict was issued, reading:

*"The ancient village of Ku County, the sacred homeland of our August Ancestor, is adorned with majestic palaces and revered temples. Recently, as remnants of rebellion surged forth, setting fires and spreading destruction, dark mists veiled the clear sky, and the frenzied bandits lost their way upon the roads, scattering like deer in the wild—until all was laid waste. In profound awe of this mysterious power and the manifest efficacy of the divine, We now issue this decree in blue-green script to offer solemn thanks. Let it be proclaimed throughout the realm and recorded by the historians."*

In the third month of the gengzi year of the Guangming era of Emperor Xizong (880 CE), Huang Chao ravaged the commanderies and counties. In the seventh month, his forces advanced into Huainan, intending to burn the Great Clarity Palace. Suddenly, a cold wind arose from all directions, turning the heat to chill, and the rebels scattered in terror, ultimately retreating without daring to attack. Later, another band of outlaws sought to plunder the palace. As they neared its precincts, dark clouds gathered, obscuring their path. They turned back toward the city of Bozhou and laid siege to it, pressing the attack fiercely. The prefect, Pan Chou, faced the palace, burned incense, and bowed in prayer, beseeching divine protection. Soon, a black mist emerged from the palace and swirled around the city walls, reeking of a foul and terrifying stench. Then, a dense snow began to fall, bringing an unnatural cold. Over half the rebels perished, while within the city, the sky remained clear and serene. Divine crows swooped down to strike them, and in fear, the rebels lifted the siege and departed, soon scattering and perishing entirely.

Pan Chou ordered the Daoists Ma Hanzhang and Sun Qiwu to submit a memorial, stating: "Since the jisi year of the Qianfu era, the Great Clarity Palace has repeatedly faced rebellious bandits, numbering from over a thousand to more than ten thousand. All have eyed the palace, seeking to burn and plunder it, or to attack the city, or to raid the surrounding counties—amounting to one hundred and eight incursions. Each time, Lord Lao has secretly manifested his divine transformations: suddenly raising thick clouds, or striking with wind and storm, or smiting with thunder and lightning. Without fail, the rebels were thrown into chaos and swiftly met their doom. The number of people thus shielded and protected is beyond reckoning. We humbly request that the county seat be relocated to the palace grounds."

The imperial reply stated: "We fear that relocating the county to the palace would bring much defilement. The county shall remain in its original location, but in accordance with the precedent of Wannian, it shall be elevated to the rank of a 'red county.' A formal memorial of thanks shall be presented." The emperor then bowed toward the palace and sent the emissary on his way.

On the twelfth day of the eighth month, an imperial edict proclaimed: "The Great Clarity Palace in Bozhou is the sacred site where the Primordial One descended, its name exalted among the Daoist patriarchs. Its blessings have nurtured the imperial foundation, its Nine-Dragon Auspicious Well brimming with celestial qi, and the immortal trace of the single deer lingering in the trees. The propitious omens of successive dynasties are worthy of record, and in recent years, its numinous responses have grown ever more manifest. How fitting that such a hallowed sanctuary should be reverently adorned—how could it be left to decay in a contentious world?

Magistrate Pan Zhou has long governed with virtuous policies, steeped in the true wind of the Dao. He has generously donated his official salary to restore the palace and its temples, ensuring that every wall, beam, and locust tree is meticulously renewed, while the painted statues and murals gleam with even greater splendor. Upon reviewing the plans and examining the work, We find it deeply gratifying.

The resident ceremonial Daoist Wu Chongxuan shall be granted the purple robe and styled 'Master of Congealed Mystery.' Daoists Ma Hanzhang and Sun Qiwu shall likewise be granted the purple robe. Pan Zhou shall be promoted to Grand Master for Splendid Happiness, with the concurrent title of Minister of Works. All other matters shall remain unchanged."

In the eleventh month, Huang Chao seized the eastern capital. The imperial carriage fled to Xingyuan. Jia Xiang, an official in the Bureau of Finance, possessed a portrait of Lord Lao, which he always carried with him. Wherever he stayed—even for a single day—he would set up an incense altar. When Huang Chao overran Chang’an, Xiang fled with his family to take refuge at the foot of Dragon Horn Mountain. Suddenly, a band of rebels surrounded his home. Xiang entered to pray before the portrait of Lord Lao, then emerged to speak with the rebels. They cast their blades to the ground and prostrated themselves before him. When Xiang reentered his home, the rebels remained kneeling outside, confessing their transgressions. Moved by pity, Xiang presented them with silk and offered words of comfort. They took nothing and departed.

When asked what they had seen to make them bow in reverence, they replied: "We saw that Lord Jia was surrounded by divine soldiers, each several *zhang* tall, their mouths agape and eyes bulging as if ready to devour us. We feared for our lives." Thereafter, no one dared disturb his household, and his gates remained unbarred.

In Chengdu, Yang Nao’er diligently worshipped Lord Lao without fail. Once, while defending Jintang Pass, he was captured and taken to a bandit stronghold in the southern mountains. Day and night, he prayed to Lord Lao, longing to see his parents again. Suddenly, he dreamed that Lord Lao bestowed upon him a cloud, and a youth led him away, depositing him on level ground. When he awoke, he had already escaped the mountain stronghold and made his way home. On the day of his return, his parents were holding a hundred-day memorial feast.

In the third month of the second year of the *Xin Chou* era, Wang Chongrong, Military Commissioner of Hezhong, memorialized: "According to a report from Jinzhou, on the side of the Hall of Lord Lao at the Blessing Tang Abbey on Dragon Horn Mountain, a wisteria vine had withered and regrown upon a parasol tree. This was the same tree where Lord Lao manifested during the *Wude* era, and later, a hall was built between two such trees. Over a year later, a wisteria vine spontaneously grew upon the tree, extending over ten *zhang* with extraordinary luxuriance. When the Prince of Qi usurped the succession, the vine withered and died. Ten days later, it revived, and soon after, the Prince of Qi was defeated. When Emperor Zhongzong was restored to the throne and Zhu Ci plotted rebellion, the vine withered again, only to regrow after a time. In the first year of the *Guangming* era, when Huang Chao threatened the capital, the vine withered that autumn. By the second year, its branches and leaves flourished anew. Moreover, another vine sprouted on a neighboring tree, growing over five *zhang* in ten days, its foliage exceptionally lush."

An edict was issued in praise, and the event was recorded by the court historians.

In the sixth month, the imperial carriage arrived in Chengdu. In the seventh month, the era name was changed to *Zhonghe*.

On the night of the sixteenth day of the seventh month in the second year of *Zhonghe* (*Ren Yin*), a crimson-black phenomenon like a rainbow stretched across the northwestern sky. The rebel general Shang Hu attacked Zizhou in Yijun, where snow fell a foot deep. The cold was extreme, and two or three out of every ten rebel soldiers froze to death.

On the twenty-ninth day of the eighth month (*Wuchen*), the imperial clansman Li Teli commanded Daoist Li Wuwei to perform a jiao ritual at the Mystic Center Abbey in the Green Ram Market of Chengdu. Suddenly, a red light like a sphere emerged from the bamboo grove southeast of the temple foundation, leaping and bounding until it vanished beneath a plum tree to the west. They dug at the spot and, after three *chi*, unearthed a bronze plaque. It measured one *chi*, one *cun*, and five *fen* in length; seven *cun* and four *fen* in width; one *cun* and three *fen* in thickness on one side, with floral patterns; and one *cun* and eight *fen* on the other, weighing twelve *jin*. Six ancient seal-script characters, each two *cun* square and three *fen* deep, were inscribed upon it, their carving so lustrous it seemed not of human craft. The inscription read: *The Supreme One pacifies the calamities of Zhonghe.*

On the fifteenth day of the ninth month (*Gengchen*), Chen Jingxuan, Military Commissioner of Xichuan and Grand Preceptor, memorialized: "Your Imperial Majesty reveres antiquity and aligns with Heaven, responding to the celestial mandate and nurturing the realm. Your heart is fixed upon the supreme Dao, and your ministers are in perfect harmony. Thus, You have journeyed to inspect the land, openly acknowledging faults. Your profound benevolence extends to the earth below, and Your supreme virtue ascends to the celestial realm above. Auspicious portents have appeared in abundance, and omens of blessing have manifested repeatedly. The Supreme One has bestowed upon Your servant the mandate to quell the calamities of *Zhonghe* and the scourge of rebels, revealing this divine inscription in ancient seal script. It is sufficient to show that the inauspicious vapors will soon vanish, that Your sagely longevity is boundless, and that the day of restoration and tranquility is at hand."

The emperor ordered the plaque to be shown to all officials.

The Vice Director of the Chancellery and concurrent Grand Councilor Zhang Zhaodu, the Vice Minister of Revenue and concurrent Grand Councilor Xiao Ran, the Vice Director of the Secretariat and concurrent Grand Councilor Zheng Tian, the Censor-in-Chief Zhang Xi, and the Duke of Cao, Prince Gui of the Imperial Clan, jointly submitted a memorial of congratulation: "We humbly observe that when rebels gather like howling jackals and the imperial carriage journeys to inspect the land, when natural disasters rage and the nation faces trials in every age, Your Majesty has issued an edict of self-reproach like that of Cheng Tang, and dispatched punitive expeditions as in the days of the sagely kings. The malevolent rebels shall soon be annihilated. The signs of peace and prosperity are already manifest, and the hidden blessings of this divine inscription are now revealed. How could this compare to the red bird bearing a book from Heaven, or the dark turtle bearing the hexagrams that heralded the end of calamity? Moreover, this auspicious light leapt and bounded precisely during the ritual performed by the imperial clansman—its manifestation could not be more emphatic.

They further memorialized: "Ever since the imperial banners turned southward and the Yellow Road divided to the west, when traveling through the mountains, six phoenixes have soared beside Your carriage, and when crossing rivers, twin dragons have borne the imperial raft. Swords have flown from sheer cliffs, and sacred mushrooms have flourished in the temporary palaces. Auspicious texts have floated upon the waters of Xiang River, and radiant suns have shone together over the land of Shu. All this is due to Your sagely spirit and divine protection, Your humility and self-effacement, which have moved the celestial vault and stirred the fertile earth."

At the ancient site of the Green Ram, a minister’s concentrated devotion was as steadfast as bedrock. By the fresh traces of the White Horse, the silent revelation of sacred talismans unfolded. The immortals’ secrets, vast as the enduring mandate, wove through the six characters to align with a thousand years of destiny. At the founding of the Wei dynasty, the jade seal manifested its auspiciousness within the ancestral temple; in the era of Emperor Shun, golden chariots graced the palace courtyards—such marvels transcend mere relief and defy emulation by later ages.

Grand Councilor Li Shunrong and Commander-in-Chief of the Twelve Armies and Ten Garrisons Tian Lingzi memorialized in celebration: "Now, from the imperial domain, they have come to this immortal sanctuary. No sooner had their utmost sincerity arisen than felicitous omens swiftly appeared. The crimson light now manifest upon the earth is none other than the purple-black radiance that once accompanied the crossing of the pass. Upon excavating the accumulated soil, ancient script was indeed uncovered—its soaring, dragon-and-serpent strokes confirmed the pacification of the realm’s turmoil. Emerging from the profound and secluded, its principle shines with clarity. Since the Most High has now quelled disaster, we know the harmony of the Central Realm shall perpetuate its mandate. All that has appeared stems from the sagely ancestors, and what was unearthed derives from the imperial lineage. These numinous traces make heaven’s will manifest.

Moreover, the Mysterious Primordial Sagely Ancestor has, in every time of great peril, displayed extraordinary portents—each a luminous sign, all duly recorded in the orthodox histories. Formerly, at the Vermilion Phoenix Gate, Tian Tongxiu was shown the date of the restoration of the Tianbao era; now, within the Green Ram Market, Li Teli has been revealed the joyous return of Your Majesty to the palace. Truly, the realm is most fortunate. We humbly request that these events be entrusted to the historiographers."

The request was granted. On the fifteenth day, Li Teli was appointed Collator of the Eastern Palace, and Li Wuwei was bestowed the purple robe, each additionally granted two hundred bolts of fine silk.

On the twenty-first day, an imperial edict proclaimed:

The Supreme Lord of the Mysterious Primordial and his disciple, Master Wen Shi, once expounded the True Scripture upon the terrace of Louguan, where they covenanted to meet again at the Green Ram Market before ascending together upon cloud-borne chariots into the Flowing Sands. Such transcendent records and transmitted accounts are marked even upon terrestrial maps. From the reign of King Zhao of Zhou until this day, some two thousand years have passed, yet the numinous traces have remained faint, their vestiges scarce.

Now, by virtue of the imperial progress, the numinous has become manifest in radiance: extraordinary light leapt and danced before the courtyard, while divine talismans gleamed bright beneath the trees. The ancient hues of the inscribed grain and cinnabar bore verification of hidden portents. As the calamities of the era of Equilibrium and Harmony draw toward their end, the propitious omens of the fertile earth emerge in full—sufficient to attest that the Mysterious Vault bestows its protection, the Sagely Ancestor confers his auspices, the weapons of the great rebels shall be annihilated, and the enterprise of the Middle Revival shall shine eternally.

It is meet that these events be inscribed upon bamboo and silk, proclaimed throughout the realm, and entrusted to the historiographers for compilation. Moreover, let the inscribed texts be engraved and disseminated to all circuits and before the armies. The abbey shall be renamed the Green Ram Palace, and halls and chambers shall be erected therein. The adjacent lands, approximately two *qing* in area and recently dispersed among the common folk, shall be redeemed for two hundred strings of cash, with a public certificate issued to ensure their perpetual affiliation with the House of Tranquility. The imperial kinsman Li Teli shall be appointed to office, and the Daoist Li Wuwei shall be granted the purple robe. Such promotions and rewards are fitting to celebrate these numinous blessings.

With reverence befitting one who stands foremost among the public ministers, let the edict’s influence spread through commanderies and states. Let all serve with devotion in this era of extended prosperity, cultivate their minds in the realm of the Dao and virtue, and thereby cause arms to vanish within the borders and treasures to emerge from the earth. Such felicity is doubly worthy of praise.

On the seventh day of the tenth month, the eunuch official Zheng Zuntai was commissioned to oversee the construction of the Green Ram Palace, with all labor and materials supplied by the Inner Treasury. From the time the numinous signs were received until the *guichou* day of this month, the bandit gangs near Shu were successively captured and executed, and within ten days, the land was restored to peace. The imperial carriage proceeded to the Green Ram Palace, where gifts were bestowed in varying degrees. Li Teli was granted a *fei* and appointed Recorder of Longzhou. In the third year, on the *guimao* day, an edict proclaimed:

The Supreme Lord has conferred his auspices, and the Green Ram has manifested its response. It is fitting that rites of veneration be enhanced to requite these extraordinary blessings. The Purple Ultimate Palaces in all circuits, prefectures, and commanderies shall be entrusted to the local governors for repair and adornment as circumstances permit, and Daoist priests skilled in liturgical observances shall be selected to perform the offerings.

On the first day of the fifth month, the *bingyin* day, Chen Jingyi, Military Commissioner of Jiannan West Circuit and Prefect of Chengdu, in accordance with the edict, conducted the offering rites. The prayer read:

We humbly submit: since the Supreme Sage was born to receive the precious mandate and grandly inherited the great pivot of heaven, from the time he emerged from the *zhen* trigram to the moment he ascended the *qian* throne, has he not ever borne the moats of the realm in his thoughts, toiled over the governance of the decrepit, spread beneficence throughout the world, and extended grace to all that moves and grows? Though his aspiration was for purity and quiescence, fate has chanced upon hardship. Recently, rebels seized the palace, their venom spreading through the officials and common folk, startling the ancestral temples and roiling the mountains and rivers.

Thus, we tread the celestial steps to dispel calamity, and in the royal capital of Shu, we confess our faults. Relying humbly upon the profound protection of the Primordial Ancestor and the descent of numinous omens, the Six Sacred Characters have been revealed in luminous script, manifesting the thousand-year mandate of the sagely lineage. Now, indeed, the chief villain has been routed, and the ninefold palace gates reclaimed—pointing toward the Phoenix Tower, the return of the imperial procession is not far off.

Your servant, in reverent observance of the imperial decree, meticulously follows the ritual codes: casting jade tablets to present our words, purifying the golden altar to prepare the offering. We humbly pray that the host of perfected beings bestow blessings, that the Great Dao extend its grace, sweeping away the dark perversities of the Six Heavens and purging the demonic miasmas from the Nine Provinces, securing the peace of the altars of soil and grain and perpetually stilling the weapons of war. Soon after, by the encompassing nurturance of the Great Dao, the capital was recovered, Huang Chao and his cohorts of evil were executed, the realm was pacified, and the imperial carriage returned to the palace.

The Hanlin Academician-in-Attendance and Vice Minister of War, Yue Penggui, composed the *Record of the Green Ram Palace*. In the ninth month of the fourth year, the Central Secretariat and Chancellery, by imperial edict, directed the Military Commissioner of West Shu to erect a stele at the palace, stating:

From the Golden Towers descended auspicious omens; the purple and black revealed the configurations of the Celestial Pass. From the distant altar, secret portents arose; a crimson radiance surged forth as an earthly omen. Behold the mysterious talisman, inscribed in ancient seal script, at midnight when dragons and serpents stirred—six characters divided in splendor. A year later, the rebellion of the wolf-like warrior was quelled, the eight directions knew peace, the numinous efficacy was made manifest, and the restoration of the dynasty was achieved. Examining the prognostic words of the Divine Ancestor, the divine merit was displayed in the resplendent fortune of the age—how could it not be commemorated in the Daoist abbey?

Here in Shu’s riverlands, it shall be transmitted for a thousand myriad years, marking the eighteenth generation of our lineage. Yue Penggui, whose office presides over the inner brush and stands foremost among the close ministers, wields his golden words with consummate skill, inscribing them upon the stones of other mountains. His exposition is flawless, surpassing even Cai Yong’s prescient wisdom; his composition leaves nothing wanting, rendering Cao Zhi’s solitary brilliance needless. It is fitting to engrave this grand affair to authenticate this text.

Regarding Chengdu: when King Hui of Qin first connected it to the Central States, on the day of the Primordial One’s western assembly, it still lay beyond the borders of the feudal domain. Since Qin’s unification, many ancient traces have gone unrecorded. Two *li* southwest of Chengdu Prefecture stands the ancient Green Ram Market, where ancient pines and tall bamboos stretch across undulating hills. Those who dwell there often encounter strange transformations, and none dare trespass. The locals built halls and pavilions, offering incense and reverence, naming it the Abbey of the Mysterious Center.

Since the Great Tang received the Mandate, the sagely ancestors’ legacy has been honored without exception. In the north, at Laozi’s birthplace in Lai Xiang, and at Tianshui in Qishan, abbeys were erected to glorify the sagely merit—yet the Green Ram Market alone remained unadorned. When Emperor Xizong took refuge in Shu and obtained the talisman of pacification, he first allocated funds from the inner treasury to construct this palace. The stele and its inscriptions remain extant, fully recording the origins and details.

In the first year of Tianyou of Emperor Zhaozong, Zhu Quanzhong relocated the imperial carriage to Luoyang. By the second year of Tianyou of Emperor Aidi, in the fourth month of the *yichou* year, the Superintendent of the Supreme Clarity Palace, Liu Can, memorialized the completion of the Upper Clarity Palace’s renovations and requested its renaming to the Supreme Clarity Palace, to which the emperor assented. The edict proclaimed:

The August Primordial Emperor laid the foundation of the sagely lineage, spreading his protection over his divine descendants. The jade-like stream of his numinous succession, boundless as heaven and earth, and the precious chart of his steadfast mandate, enduring as the sun and moon, have been reverently honored by our dynasty through successive generations. In observance of the rites of ancestral veneration, we present texts of reverence; though the affair does not seek the true essence, it diverges from the doctrine of emptiness and nothingness. Recently, it has been customary to establish a separate palace alongside the Nine Temples. Since the relocation, we have been unable to undertake its proper maintenance, and within the Northern Observatory, offerings have been made as circumstances allowed. Though grand music is prepared, it lacks the substance of ancient precedent. The invocators, in their pursuit of distant ancestors, resemble those who sought the elusive dragon; the chief ministers, bowing in solemnity, are like those who sought the formless. Jade tablets are presented in vain, for none can match the celestial rites.

Liu Can, well-versed in ancient protocols and diligent in his weighty duties, proposed this renovation as most fitting. May it not err nor be forgotten, drawing the gaze and offerings of the divine. Moreover, upon inspecting the Mysterious Garden, it resembles Lai Xiang: the ancient halls are serene, the bridge’s trees cast dense shade, befitting the contemplation of the Purple Palace—thus it is named the Pure Abbey. To abandon the inferior and follow the superior is the mark of a wise man; to trace the source and rectify the beginning relies upon worthy ministers. Our collective deliberation finds this most appropriate, and it is worthy of special commendation.

In the second year of Kaiping of the Liang, the *wuchen* year, all Supreme Clarity Palaces, Grand Tenuity Palaces, and Purple Ultimate Palaces throughout the realm were renamed Temples of the Venerable Lord. Qian Liu, Prince of Wuyue, memorialized to restore the Temples of the Venerable Lord in the Two Zhe and Huainan Circuits to True Sage Abbeys, personally composing the stele inscription, which read:

Of the teachings of the True Sage, the Great Dao is foremost—elusive and indescribable, empty and formless, originating before the separation of heaven and earth, acting before the coalescence of primordial chaos. It encompasses the Nine Provinces and arrays the myriad things. Vast and enduring, profound beyond profundity, it cannot be exhausted by astronomical reckoning nor contained by words.

The Most High Lord Lao, his spirit congealed in primordial simplicity, his *qi* accumulated in the undifferentiated vastness, though his birth was occasioned by a stellar essence, his exalted virtue truly marked him as the progenitor of the Dao. In the eras of Fu Xi and the Yellow Emperor, he appeared under different names; through the ages of Yao, Tang, Yin, and Xia, he followed divergent paths yet remained one in essence. He established laws for the age, serving as both teacher and minister, expanding the winds of emptiness and stirring the boundaries of the subtle and tranquil.

When the Zhou dynasty was founded, the immortal sage met his destined time. Entrusted to the womb of a mysterious maiden, he took form in the state of Chu, declaring his surname as Li and transmitting his name through a vision of his ear. The twin pillars and triple gates marked his extraordinary form; his arched brows and crane-white hair further manifested his venerable age. Yet he concealed his brilliance in the public court, withdrawing as the Keeper of the Archives. Confucius of Lu marveled at the "dragon-like" sage; Master Yin recognized the auspicious *qi* of his advent. None failed to expound the *Five Thousand Characters*, and his lifespan reached eight hundred years.

When the Tang dynasty was to establish its royal enterprise, it traced its origins to this pure source, broadly opening the mysterious gate and repeatedly honoring him with exalted titles.

Thus, across the realm, Daoist palaces were established everywhere, all named "Purple Ultimate" in hope of conducting fasts and offerings. The local prefecture had once built such a sanctuary, though years had passed. Later, when great bandits swept through, they wantonly reduced it to ashes. Reflecting on the long neglect of the true Daoist wind, we therefore revived it, that blessings might be prolonged.

Examining the records and tracing the origins, we find that Lord Lao, embodying the essence of emptiness and non-being, responded to the primordial *qi*, assuming a divine form and existing before heaven and earth. During the eras of the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors, he styled himself Yu Hua or Guang Shou; in the ages of Yin, Shang, and Zhou, he served as Keeper of the Archives and Royal Preceptor. Moving in accord with nature, he appeared in response to the times. Later, in the reign of King Wen of Zhou, he was reborn in Ku County’s Lài Village, his Dao shaking the Central Land and his teachings spreading to the Western Regions. Yet his station was that of the Great Unity, his honorific title "Imperial Lord"; he governed the ten directions, gave life to the myriad things, matched the Three Luminaries in universal illumination, and endured through ten thousand kalpas in eternal life.

The former dynasty sought to dredge the numinous source and ascend the immortal lineage, deeming it no mere bloodline but a sacred lineage akin to ancestral pillars. Thus, Lord Lao was none other than the supreme Perfected of the Great Dao, not merely an offshoot of the Tang house. The Great Sage Xuanyuan necessarily sought the Dao on Mount Kongtong; the Most Benevolent King Mu of Zhou likewise studied immortality by the Jade Waters. Therefore, the veneration of Daoist teachings and the broad pursuit of meritorious causes flourished. Crimson towers and vermilion terraces rivaled the Penglai realms; star altars and moon halls were grandly renewed as sites for burnt offerings, ever hoping that mysterious efficacy might attain spiritual fulfillment.

On remaining vacant land, we inquired and planted pine and bamboo. White deer and azure sheep roamed freely among the spring ridges; pure winds and bright moons further adorned the autumn rivers. We established the name "Hao Capital Palace of the Mysterious Teachings," destined to endure for a thousand autumns, and renamed the "Purple Palace of the Perfected Sage of Yue" to ensure its glory for ten thousand generations. Thus, the Great Dao would ever flourish, the true wind never decline, and the teachings of the Mysterious Origin perpetually sustain the nation’s prosperity. It is fitting to carve this in imperishable stone to propagate its radiant glory.

The Zhen Sheng Abbey of Qiantang was later renamed Tianqing Abbey during the Song dynasty. The stele recording these events survives from the second year of Tiancheng in the Later Tang, when Emperor Mingzong issued an edict restoring the old titles of "Palace" and "Abbey" to the temples of Lord Lao, which had been altered by Zhu Wen.

In the eighth month, Left Rectifier Zhao Mingji submitted a memorial: "I observe that Daoist abbeys across the land have long fallen into disrepair. Since Zhu Wen’s usurpation, many were destroyed or abandoned. I humbly request that all abbeys established in our dynasty’s former territories, recently damaged or dismantled, be ordered restored to honor our ancestors and promote filial governance. This would illuminate Your Majesty’s restoration of the realm, manifest the great source of the nation’s Dao, and revive our true lineage for all time. In the two capitals, abbeys possess public fields; I beg that these be exempted from taxation to provide for ritual provisions and support properly named Daoist priests, that they may devote themselves to burnt offerings and invoke the blessings of the Supreme Mystery."

The request was granted.

In the third year, the first month, the Chancellery reported on holidays: "It is decreed that the fifteenth day of the second month, the Descendant Festival of the Mysterious Origin Emperor, be observed with a three-day holiday." The decree was approved.

In the first year of Huichang, the second month, an edict ordered a one-day holiday, and the request was again granted.

In the fourth year, the twelfth month, on the day *renzǐ*, an edict declared: "Revering the Sacred Ancestor and renovating Daoist palaces, having restored their names, it would be improper to leave them without plaques. Let the authorities craft and hang the old plaque of 'Supreme Clarity Palace.' Additionally, the Jinzhen Abbey within the capital shall be renamed 'Venerable Dao Abbey,' and its plaque likewise replaced, as it had been altered during the Zhu Liang era."

In the fourth year of Tianfu of Emperor Gaozu of Jin, the ninth month, on the day *xīnmǎo*, Daoist Master Zhang Jianming, the Venerable of Mystical Perfection, was summoned and presented with silk. Jianming, a native of Yan, had been a scholar in his youth, traveling and studying in the Hebei region, where he delved into the works of Laozi and Zhuangzi, his nature thus harmonizing with the Dao. Later, clad in cloud robes and star crowns, he embraced the teachings of spontaneity.

The emperor, long devoted to the Mysterious Origin, frequently summoned him upon ascending the throne. The emperor asked, "Can the Dao govern the world?"

Jianming replied, "The Dao is that which subtly encompasses the myriad things. It embraces the Two Modes and is called *virtue*. Those who attain its summit become the Dao; those who grasp its middle attain benevolence and righteousness; those who grasp its lower aspects attain propriety, wisdom, and trust. To lose it is to cease being human. Those who attain its ultimate may govern heaven and earth while seated on their mats—how could it be limited to governing the world?"

The emperor then invited him into the inner palace to lecture on Laozi’s *Dao De Jing*. He summoned the chief minister Feng Dao to receive the scroll and listen. Feng Dao remarked, "Daoists lecturing on *Laozi* and monks receiving full precepts—these are ancient practices, not to be taken lightly."

The emperor thus revered Jianming as his teacher and honored him even more. Once, hearing the palace’s time drum, he asked, "Does Your Majesty hear the drum’s sound? It is simply the preservation of the One. Strings, gourds, bells, and stones produce twelve tones, yet only the yellow bell harmonizes with itself; the other fifty-nine do not respond, for they issue from many gates. The drum’s sound—why inquire further? It has no gong or shang, no jue or zhi, no yu or bian, yet it harmonizes and accords—because it is One. If one can achieve undifferentiated unity in the heart, one becomes one with heaven and earth. What worry is there that the world will not return to purity?"

The emperor thus cultivated a mind of emptiness and stillness, honoring the Dao and valuing *virtue*. Hence, each audience with Jianming resulted in generous bestowals.

In the fifth month of the fifth year, Zhang Jianming was bestowed the title "Master of Penetrating Mystery." He was ordered to carve printing blocks of the *Dao* and *De* classics, and the scholar He Ning was commissioned to compose a new preface for the volumes’ frontispieces, to be distributed throughout the realm.

In the fifth year of Xiande of Emperor Shizong of Zhou, the sixth month, the emperor donated imperial robes and money to renovate the Supreme Clarity Abbey. In the sixth year, the second month, the abbey was completed, and the emperor visited it. After paying respects to Lord Lao, he inspected the bells bestowed upon the abbey.

The abbey stood outside the Qianming Gate. Initially, the emperor had heard of a bell in Pu Prefecture whose sound was exceptionally resonant, audible for dozens of *li* with each strike. He ordered it moved and bestowed upon the Supreme Clarity Abbey.

In the first year of Jianlong of Emperor Taizu of Song, on the *gengshen* day, envoys were dispatched to Bo Prefecture to offer sacrifices to Lord Lao. Another abbey, the Jianlong Abbey, was established at the Wenhe Gate—this was the same Supreme Clarity Abbey renovated by Emperor Shizong of Zhou, to which the emperor now granted a new plaque while continuing the ancestral rites to Lord Lao.

In the seventh year of Taiping Xingguo of Emperor Taizong, on the *renwu* day, he addressed his ministers: "Reading Laozi’s *Dao De Jing*, where it speaks of 'fine weapons as ill-omened tools,' and that the sage employs them only out of necessity, I have repeatedly reflected upon it as a rule of caution. Though a ruler may achieve unification through martial prowess, governance must ultimately rely on order—this deeply accords with Laozi’s principles of preserving life."

In the ninth year, he said to his chief ministers: "My daily routine is fixed: after attending to court affairs between *chen* and *si* hours, I read until late at night, retiring only to rise at the fifth watch. Even in the height of summer, I never nap during the day, nor do I overindulge in food or drink. After long practice, I find this regimen greatly beneficial. When one eats to satiety, the mind inevitably grows dull; if the limbs remain idle and one retires at once, the blood and *qi* stagnate, and illness naturally arises. How can one seek clarity and vigor thus? Laozi said, 'My fate lies with me, not with Heaven'—it all depends on one’s self-regulation. You, too, should take heed and not neglect your well-being."

In the second year of Chunhua, on the *xinmao* day, the emperor sent eunuchs Li Shoulun, Li Tingxun, and Luo Huaizhong to the military prefectures of the Two Zhe regions to select skilled artisans for the extensive renovation of the Supreme Clarity Abbey in Bo Prefecture. Thereafter, envoys were dispatched to pray for rain in times of drought or flood, and responses were frequently received.

In the third year, the emperor recited the *Dao De Jing* and remarked to his attending ministers: "The profound essence of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi lies in governing through purity and stillness—I shall strive to practice this." He continued, "In ancient times, Ji An governed Huaiyang with effortless ease, and Gongye Chang played the zither to govern Shanfu—these were applications of the Dao of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi."

Minister Lü Duan replied: "Implementing the Dao of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi to achieve great peace yields swift results."

Minister Lü Mengzheng added: "Laozi said, 'Governing a great state is like cooking a small fish'—if the fish is stirred, it falls apart. Recently, many have submitted memorials urging institutional reforms. I hope Your Majesty will foster a culture of purity and restraint."

The emperor said: "I do not wish to block the path of remonstrance. Even the words of the foolish may contain wisdom for the wise to select—this, too, is an ancient principle."

In the fourth year, eunuchs were sent to oversee artisans in renovating the Supreme Clarity Abbey. The work was completed in the tenth month of the first year of Zhidao. Thereafter, two imperial commissioners were appointed to oversee the abbey and the Dongxiao Abbey (the latter dedicated to the Primordial Celestial Empress, originally established as a temple in the first year of Yongxing of Later Han and later converted into an abbey under Tang). These commissioners served three-year terms. Annually, on Lord Lao’s birthday, the Three Primes, and other sacred festivals, grand *zhai* and *jiao* rituals were held, a practice that continues to this day.

In the fifth year of Xianping of Emperor Zhenzong, on the *renyin* day, the eunuch Chen Yanqing was sent to extensively renovate the Supreme Clarity Abbey and the Dongxiao Abbey, each assigned soldiers for maintenance.

In the second year of Jingde, on the *yisi* day, a decree prohibited logging in the vicinity of the two abbeys.

In the second year of Dazhong Xiangfu, on the *jiyou* day, a decree ordered the selection of ten Daoist priests from the Left and Right Streets to collate and standardize the scriptures of the *Daoist Canon*. By the third year, officials from the Chongwen Institute were assembled to meticulously review the texts, with Chief Minister Wang Qinruo overseeing the project.

Earlier, Emperor Taizong had sought Daoist scriptures, amassing over seven thousand scrolls. He tasked the Gentleman Cavalier Attendant Xu Xuan and Drafter of Imperial Edicts Wang Yucheng with editing them, removing duplicates, and transcribing copies for distribution to abbeys—reducing the collection to 3,337 scrolls. Now, Qinruo followed the old catalog of the Three Caverns and Four Supplements, expanding the canon to 4,359 scrolls. Upon completing the compilation, he presented the catalog to the throne, and the emperor bestowed the title *Baowen Tonglu* ("Comprehensive Record of Treasured Writings"). The emperor personally composed a preface, which read:

"Before the primordial chaos was divided, this was called the Nameless. When written words arose, creation began. The subtle and profound teachings of the arcane and the wondrous, modeled on nature and born of the Supreme Ultimate, narrate the origins of the vast primordial era, expound the heart of Heaven and Earth, proclaim the words of utmost truth, and bestow blessings upon all living beings. Inscribed upon jade tablets, bound with golden cords, sealed in fine cases, and stored in precious caskets, they are guarded by the myriad spirits, enduring with the cosmos. For ten thousand generations, they shall be revered, their luster undimmed like strings of pearls. Over time, even bedrock may erode, and their marvels cannot be consumed by raging fire. Truly, they are the most divine of the divine, the greatest of the great.

Reflecting upon antiquity and extending to future ages, from the sage-kings who drilled jade and wore coarse cloth to the ministers who gazed upon them in the court, they cultivated purity and tranquility to attain great harmony. The ancestral temple and court flourished, achieving grand stability—all followed their teachings to govern, adhered to their instructions to propagate. Through conserving essence and concentrating spirit, purifying the mind and refining conduct, accumulating virtue and increasing goodness, penetrating the mysterious and communing with the numinous, one ascends to the Nine Heavens above or extends life to ten thousand years below. Within the realm of existence, none can fully fathom this; before the images of emperors and gods, we behold it here.

Our dynasty has received the great mandate, revering the primal axis. Our August Ancestor taught with profound silence, and the Divine Ancestor governed through purity and stillness. The jade halls of prosperity were venerated, the silver inscriptions of distant emulation were sought. The grand achievements of our predecessors surpass antiquity. As a humble successor, I humbly continue this blessed foundation, receiving the *Hetu* from above and guiding the celestial chariot below. Emperors of past dynasties ascended in flame, while Deng Yu compiled the *Zong* by the eddying waters. The Guo River followed the wind, the state flourished in yang, the people returned to simplicity, and all things were free from bitterness and sourness."

Should we speak only of the numinous protection of ancestral blessings to uphold grand principles? Nay, the aid of the Void and Nothingness has brought forth abundant auspiciousness. To propagate the true wind and exalt the wondrous essence, we must unfold it through the registers and genealogies, that all living beings may be enlightened.

Yet as the sacrificial years have shifted and the chapters grown vast—passed from the imperial glory of Yu the Great, is this not recent?—transmitted through the Dragon Han and Red Bright kalpas, how could distant matters be fully expounded in order, or reverence be fully expressed? Thus, We commanded the ministers of the Court of Imperial Accommodation and the Secretariat to oversee the task, selecting eminent scholars to contribute their offices. They compiled the treasured texts of the cinnabar chambers, sought the secret arcana of the purple terraces, corrected ancient seal scripts and examined regional dialects from the imperial carriages. Delving into obscurities and probing subtleties, they exhausted the refinements of textual collation; grouping and categorizing, they ensured no error in the order of scrolls. This far surpasses the ninety-three chapters of the *Han Annals* or the thirty-six methods of the *Records of Immortals*. The sequence of flying ashes spans three full cycles, the teachings of the linked blossoms achieve perfection. May these be transmitted to future generations, disseminated across the boundless cosmos, freshly inscribed with brush and ink, to be eternally preserved in the mountains.

It was at Qishou that the imperial lineage was inaugurated, and at Hangu Pass that the true ancestral teaching ascended. Truly, the Way’s orthodoxy endures through the ages. The words of composition and creation reach the utmost bounds between heaven and humanity. To mingle these with the various philosophers would be improper; to rank them among the treasured scriptures is most fitting.

From Our youthful reverence, We have upheld this imperishable luminous model. The Privy Councilor, Minister of Personnel, Acting Grand Preceptor, and Chief Councilor Wang Qinruo, preeminent in his office, ever mindful of harmonious virtue, has labored without respite in his duties, aspiring to transmit glory to posterity. Thus, he has memorialized and presented these catalogued texts, humbly requesting a preface to be offered in seasonal observance. Though the vast origins of the jade cosmos lie distant, We revere the shade of the Dao; following the *Luminous Net*’s precedent, this work may yet illumine human culture. May it guide future generations, ever increasing in reverence.

It is titled *The Comprehensive Record of Treasured Writings*.

Qinruo further petitioned that the *Scripture of Dao and Virtue* and the *Scripture of the Hidden Talisman*—composed by the Sage Ancestor Lord Lao—be elevated from the Four Auxiliary Sections to the Cavern of Perfection. Earlier, an edict had ordered the removal of Daoist and Buddhist scriptures that mutually slandered one another, but Qinruo argued that the *Scripture of Laozi’s Conversion of the Barbarians* was an ancient sacred relic and should not be expunged. The edict assented.

In the fourth year of the Great Central Auspiciousness (1011), on the *xinhai* day, the Emperor offered sacrifice to the Earth at Fenyin. As the imperial procession reached Hangu Pass, He composed the *Inscription on Crossing the Pass, with Preface*:

The heart of the sagely and enlightened knows no partiality; the traces of divine transformation may appear or vanish. If the luminous legacy endures, the people will ever look up in reverence. Hangu Pass is the path by which Lord Lao ascended to the West. As for the One of Perfect Virtue who concealed His true nature, expounded teachings under a forced name, dwelled in the hidden chamber to nurture truth, and crossed the Flowing Sands to conceal His form—Confucius questioned Him on ritual and received subtle words; Yin Xi received the scripture and divined the true darkness. These events are preserved in ancient records and transmitted to future ages. Yet the Way of the supremely divine, attaining the wondrous existence of the myriad years—how could it be easily grasped?

Here, where the borders of Hua Mountain lie, where the Yellow River flows, the immortal’s chariot once passed, and its tracks remain. The pass and bridge reveal the barriers of Xiao and Han; this county and town manifest the numinous blessings of Lingbao. Thus, though people depart, the teaching endures; though ages change, the land remains. As We reverently sacrifice to the Earth and undertake this seasonal progress, We ever cherish the silent and profound wind in Our gaze, and the governance of purity and stillness in Our ancestral teacher. We shall sweep away the dust of the mundane and inscribe this upon jade-green stone.

**Inscription:**
The Teaching Father concealed His truth,
His virtue matched the divine dragon.
In divine wandering, He left silent traces—
His sagely words shine forth,
A model for all living beings.
Gazing toward Hangu Pass,
His tracks endure forever.
Boundless in mystery, infinite in purity,
The cloud-script and jade tablets
Are eternally engraved.

In the fifth year (1012), the Three Departments’ Commissioner Ding Wei was dispatched to perform the official rites. In the autumn of the sixth year, an edict announced the imperial visit to Bo to pay homage at the Supreme Clarity Palace. By then, the *heaven-ascending cypress* had long withered, but at this time, it flourished anew.

In the first month of the *jiayin* year of the seventh year (1014), the imperial carriage proceeded to the Supreme Clarity Palace for the audience. The Emperor personally presented the ceremonial jade and bestowed the august title *Supreme Emperor of the Primal Origin and Supreme Virtue of Lord Lao*. He composed the *Eulogy to Lord Lao, with Preface*:

To precede the Two Modes and give birth to the ten thousand things—this is called the Perfect Dao. To govern the Three Luminaries and spread the primal mandate—this is called the Lofty Heaven. As for the greatness of dwelling in the Dao and the sublimity of accompanying Heaven, though near in scope, it is beyond thought and measure; though its works surpass creation, it is beyond speech and knowledge.

In its timely response, it acts without acting, yet nothing is left undone; in its hidden radiance, it goes without going, yet nowhere is unreached. Thus, it abides in numinous traces, roams in divine wanderings—now appearing in indistinct presence, now soaring in profound obscurity. It dwells in the Ninefold Clarity, equal in honor to the Primal Beginning; through countless ages, it is revered as the Supreme, bestowing abundance.

Our great ancestor, Lord Lao, is truly the Perfected One. Through Him, the vast realm knows no lack; how much more so for this luminous blessed land, the birthplace of the August Emperor, where the resplendent immortal shrine stands in the domain of numinous radiance? Here, the accumulation of essence naturally gathers, and grand blessings are sought. Following the people’s acclaim and revering the primal moment, We offer this homage.

The Supreme Clarity Palace stands among the lands of Qiao Prefecture, beside the vast abyss of the Wo River—a renowned region where the Sage descended, a pure realm where the Perfected gathered. The state of Cheng Tang lies in view, the ruins of Taihao are near. The local customs are pure and steadfast, the land fertile and rich. Layered hills and high ridges command the terrain for a thousand *li*; lush forests and fine trees display verdant splendor through the four seasons. This is truly a secluded court for the numinous and immortal, a dwelling for the transcendent and divine.

Herein lies the Most High Primeval August Emperor of Supreme Virtue and Primordial Power, who discerned the obscurity of the primordial chaos and embraced the essence of the profound darkness. Emerging from the boundless transformation of the primal origin, He stands as the source and beginning of all living beings. Thus, He existed before the Great Ultimate came to be, and persists beyond its dissolution; He concludes the years that came before and endures through those yet to come.

When moved by the sun, He was conceived; by pointing to a plum tree, He revealed His lineage. From birth, He could speak, and before childhood, His hair turned white—these were the signs of His utmost numinosity. Concealing His traces beneath a tree, journeying afar across the shifting sands, He answered Confucius’s rites and transmitted the scriptures to Yin Xi—these were the bounds of His teachings. From the eras of Fuxi and Haotian through the Shang and Zhou dynasties, He appeared in each age as a Perfected One, leaving wondrous traces across generations—such was the virtue of His divine transformations.

Riding the winds and driving the clouds, He ascended to the Pure Capital of the Supreme Clarity, governing the host of immortals and disseminating the harmonious *qi*—this was the accomplishment of His shaping and nurturing. As for the principles of quiescence and non-being, the essence of purity and stillness, they originated in spontaneity and attained the utmost subtlety. Applied to governance, they harmonized with the Great Mean; applied to self-cultivation, they ascended to agelessness. Bestowed upon the world, they restored its primordial purity; instilled in the people, they brought them boundless blessings. Truly, He is the ancestor of all things and the forerunner of all teachings.

The Han dynasty revered His words, reducing punishments thereby. The Tang dynasty honored His Way, achieving great peace. Rightly do His traces spread far and wide, transmitted through countless ages without end. Our dynasty has received the precious mandate of extended prosperity, unfolding the jade-like patterns of accumulated glory. The foundation of pure governance begins with this wondrous Dao.

Our August Ancestor, the Emperor, possessed divine martial prowess yet refrained from killing, wielding shields and axes to bring the Nine Regions into submission. Our August Progenitor, the Emperor, embodied clarity and brilliance, ruling with ease and establishing teachings. Their cultivated virtues surpassed the grand reputations of all sovereigns; their flourishing numinous power left a legacy of reverence for their heirs. How could We not treasure frugality, uphold reverent silence, honor the Five Thousand Characters’ teachings, and bring peace to the multitudes?

Wherever the written chariot’s tracks reach, We aspire to a realm of benevolence and longevity; wherever the sun and moon shine, may all be bathed in harmonious clarity. Through utmost sincerity, communion is achieved, and auspicious signs manifest in response. When the divine proclaimed its favor and the great rites were decreed, pure blessings converged. The celestial chariot descended from the nine heavens to the imperial precincts, and the felicitous omens of a hundred generations resounded through the imperial lineage.

In reverent compliance and gratitude for such boundless grace, We resolved to select an auspicious site in the sacred lands to offer grand repayment in the spring season. Mindful that the Supreme Teachings originated with the Father of the Dao, whose wondrous efficacy pervades the Three Powers, and that the outskirts of Bo’s capital stand tall while the numinous ruins of Jiuque endure, We personally undertook the rites of reverent visitation, seeking counsel. The people’s acclaim swiftly coalesced into unanimous hope.

Considering this harmonious accord, Our heart was deeply consoled. Thus, We deliberated on the imperial progress to fulfill the people’s desires. Thereupon, We issued the radiant edict, charged the relevant offices, chose an auspicious day under the Regulator Star, and journeyed to the sacred court on the left of Who. Then, with a thousand chariots and ten thousand riders, the ninefold imperial procession and eightfold dragon banners advanced. Halting the reins along the imperial thoroughfare, lowering the canopy at the abode of the Perfected, the myriad officials attended to their duties as the myriad states filled the court.

Before the appointed hour, We performed ablutions, adhering to the teachings of solemn preparation. At dawn, libations were poured, and reverence was shown as if in the presence of the divine. The harmony of bells and chimes resounded, and the offerings were immaculate. Presenting the precious tablets, We elevated His august name; ascending the altar and performing the dances, We awaited the descent of the numinous journey.

As the starry heavens stretched into the distance and the palace halls loomed in profound stillness, We awaited the arrival of the wind-borne chariot, imagining the proximity of Penglai’s isles. The grand ceremony proceeded without flaw, and the blessings bestowed were sincere. Then, ascending the Great Hall and assuming the southern position, We presented jade tablets and silk, offered sacrificial animals and libations, assisting in the sacrifices to the ancestral temples and uniting with the vastness of the lofty heavens. Thus, the pure excellence of the state and family was perfected, and the great blessings of the gods and spirits were reciprocated.

Reflecting on Our humble station, We have been granted the opportunity to study the sagely designs, reverently upholding the sacred words and solemnly following the mysteries of the Dao. It is not that We indulge in esoteric practices to seek immortality, but rather to honor the quiescent and transform the realm, to emulate the pure antiquity and shelter the multitudes. As the Six Ancestral Temples bestowed their favor and the Three Spirits flowed their blessings, We have been able to receive Heaven’s gifts and share in the gods’ joy. The grand rites of vast emptiness have been repeatedly performed, and the resplendent affairs have all been made manifest. Through the fasting and dwelling in the Hall of Union, the reverent obeisance at Mount Dong was accomplished. Having followed the Dao to propagate transformation, We further exalted virtue to instruct the people.

As the imperial procession returned, the rites and decorum were fully observed. The splendid and solemn moments were engraved, that the moral influence might endure unchanging.

**Eulogy:**
The lands of Qiao Prefecture, the banks of the Wo River,
Here lies the blessed realm, where the Sage-King was born.
Embodying all wonders, He protected the people in need.
In the hidden chamber, He questioned rites; Confucius relied on benevolence.
At the Han Pass, He was seen in darkness; Yin Xi recognized the divine.
Though His celestial chariot has departed, His vast responses remain ever new.
This blessed land endures, His moral legacy boundless.
Gazing afar at the sacred halls, We heed the people’s wishes.
With feathered banners and reverent visitation, the ancient rites are upheld.
With solemn offerings and pure intent, devotion is expressed.
May His glorious name be fully proclaimed, and mutual resonance of bright responses be achieved.
We pray His majestic spirit remains undimmed, and that boundless blessings be bestowed in succession.
To proclaim and propagate His abundant principles, We humbly inscribe this enduring record.
May the sequence of auspicious omens endure through three cypress trees, and His surpassing virtue shine through ten thousand springs.

Thereupon, We commanded the painting and adornment of the palace halls, and composed the *Eulogy and Preface for the Ancient Jade and Stone Image of Lord Lao*:

The nearness of the sagely and real pervades the boundless; the realm of the numinous and bright must manifest in exceptional splendor. Gazing upon the likeness, shaped since ancient times, the eyes behold what is observable, and wondrous signs are abundantly displayed. Radiant with brilliance, it aligns with the auspicious era; adorned with beams and rafters, it illuminates the pure intent. With cloud-like banners halted, all look up in reverence. This inscription is carved in jade, that it may endure forever.

**Eulogy:**
Vast is the Father of Teachings, born in this numinous ruin.
Proclaiming the Dao and its virtue, He concealed His radiance in emptiness.
His likeness remains here, a clear and trustworthy sign spread far and wide.

Here the numinous radiance emerges, aligning with these auspicious signs.
The solemn sacrifices are renewed; the imperial carriage halts in reverence.
Carved upon enduring stone, it forever extols the grand design.

On this day—the twentieth of the first month—
a double halo crowned the sun, sweet dew descended,
immortal cranes soared, and five-colored clouds appeared,
while yellow clouds like canopies descended below.
As the imperial procession returned,
auspicious vapors filled the vista, and purple clouds cradled the sun,
transforming it through five hues.
A great amnesty was bestowed upon the realm.

At that time, Li Shunian, the twelve-year-old son of Li Ruogu, Vice Prefect of Bozhou,
presented a eulogy to recount the grand ceremony.
Summoned to the imperial presence, he was commanded to compose a poem
on the theme of the imperial visit to the Grand Pure Temple.
Taking up the brush, he completed it at once.
The Emperor praised his talent and ordered eunuchs to escort him to the Chancellery,
where he was granted the rank of *Tongzi Chushen*.
Earlier, north of the Hall of Emptiness stood a great cypress,
its branches stretching southward toward the front hall then under construction.
The craftsmen, fearing it would obstruct the eaves, reported to the officials,
proposing to trim it. One night, a great thunderstorm arose,
and by dawn, the massive bough had turned northward.
When the Emperor saw this, he marveled and halted his carriage for some time,
naming it the *Imperial Beloved Cypress*.

Many had inscribed verses upon this tree,
but none surpassed the masterpiece of Shi Manqing.
Later, Lin Jiong of Futing composed an even finer poem, with these lines:
*When the ancient hall was first to be raised,*
*the gnarled cypress, tall and twisted, clashed with the eaves.*
*Men asked why axe and adze spared it—*
*heaven’s wind and thunder turned its branches.*
*Mist-laden hues deepen as spring grows heavy;*
*moonlight graces it most fittingly by night.*
*When the True Emperor paused his carriage to admire,*
*from then its fine name would be known for ten thousand generations.*
For this records the truth.

The Emperor composed the *Preface to the Supreme Clarity Scripture of the Heavenly Youth for the Protection of Life*, stating: The wondrous origin is unfathomable, and the utmost truth responds most perfectly. That which can be spoken of is not the constant Dao; that which is non-action yet leaves nothing undone. Thus, from jade tablets and precious caskets, sacred teachings are spread; from the scripts of the *leimai* and the seal-characters of the phoenix, esoteric texts are expounded.
